# Oliver_Dwellings

## S. 1

Dwellings

Paul Oliver

HTA Luzern

I

## S. 2

The architecture by the people represents in excess of 90
per cent ofthe world’s buildings, including some 800
million dwellings. Dwellings is about the types and forms of
vernacular houses around the world, traditional buildings
that are self-built by their owner-occupiers or built by
members of acommunity, from many countries.

Dwellings is an essential record of domestic buildings
byindigenous groups that still exist and thrive inthe
world today, as well as others that are sadly under threat or
disappearing. It is also a useful survey for understanding
how different communities cope with issues that affect
everyone who builds, eg. climate, migration, as well as
symbolic or cultural meaning in architecture.

Since its first publication in 1987, Dwellings: The
House Across the World has been the classic reference to
the world’s informal domestic architecture. This new book,
‚Dwellings: The Vernacular House World Wide takes into
account further scholarship inthe field and also
acknowledges theoretical developments in the areas of
cultural geography, gender studies, sociology and
anthropology. Each narrative chapter is highly illustrated
with drawings and photographs, largely by the author from
his research field trips over many years. For architects and
students, and for those involved in historical research, or
the field of vernacular architecture, it is afascinating and
informative document ofthe ways in which houses are
constructed, decorated and inhabited around the world.

## S. 3

Paul Oliveristhe Chair ofthe Master’s coursein
International Studies in Vernacular Architecture, atthe
School of Architecture, Oxford Brookes University, where
he has taught for many years. Formerly atthe Architectural
Association, hewasthefirst Head ofthe Graduate School,
DrOliveris a Fellow ofthe Royal Anthropological Institution
and an Honorary Fellow oftthe Royal Institute of British
Architects. He isthe author or editor of numerous books
on architecture, including Shelter and Society, Dunroamin:
The Suburban SemianditsEnemiesandthethree-volume
Encyclopedia of Vernacular ArchitectureoftheWorld.

To thememory of my dear wife Valwho,
for morethan forty years, shared with me
thetrips, thefield-work, the interviews,
the surveys, the photography, the
problems and the pleasures of research
in every continent, on which this book is
based. It was Val’s love, herwarmth and
friendliness, her interest in peopleand
places, her organizational abilities, her
wise counsel and her considered appraisal
that made it possible.

Architects andthe vernacular

‘Amistaken concept of universality would have led meto apply
my mental categories of history and progress outsidethe context
in which they developed, a grave error‘'Thesewerethe words
ofthe Italian architect Renzo Piano, highly regarded bythe
profession but best known to the general public as beingthe
co-designer, with Richard Rogers, of the popular and iconic ‘hightech’ building, the Centre Pompidou at the Place Beaubourg in
Paris, completed in 1977. He had been invited to design a cultural
centre for Noum&a, on the South Pacific island of New Caledonia,
a commission which revealed the different aspirations of
cultures well removed from his own. Renzo Piano was notthe
first modern architect to discover, and to acknowledge however
indirectly, the relevance of vernacular architecture to his design
concepts and practices.

Perceptions of traditional building by architects inthe
past, have ranged from the picturesque and paradisicalto.a
condescending awareness of the virtues of the ‘primitive”.
Approaches, which have a long history, gained new life and
relevance with the emergence of modernism earlyinthe
twentieth century. Adolf Loos, one ofthefirst and most
influential ofthetheorists of the new movement, guided his
readers in 1910 to an Alpine Jake where mountains and clouds
were reflected, as were ‘houses, farmyards, courtyards and
chapels. These do not seem manmade but more likethe product
of God’s workshop‘, he wrote. In his romanticimagethe
peasant’s aim wasto build a house for his ‘family and livestock,
andin this he has succeeded. Just as his neighbours or his
ancestors succeeded. As every animal which allows itselfto be
led by itsinstincts,succeeds.?

Recognition ofthe vernacular inspired others, though
Frank Lloyd Wright's statement ofthe same year was probably

made independently. ‘Folk building growing in responseto
actual needs, fitted into environment by people who knew no
betterthan to fitthem with native feeling’ he wrote, implying
like Loos, that intuition ratherthan intelligence guided the
builders. Nevertheless, he considered such folk buildingto be
“for us better worth study than allthe highly self-conscious
academic attempts atthe beautifulthroughout Europe'3
Shortly after, Wright made hisfirsttripto Japan, establishing a
pilgrimage routethat was traced several years later by Bruno Taut
and eventually, by Walter Gropius, the founder ofthe Bauhaus.
Wright’s junior by asscore of years, Charles Edouard Jeanneret
who became known as Le Corbusier, made hisjourneytothe
‘East’ the following year. Enthralled by Bulgaria and impressed by
the konaks, the large wooden houses of Turkey, he also ‘enthused
over the decorative powerthat peopleand thingstake on when
seen againstthe white of peasant rooms’that hehad observed
during his tour of Serbia, Romania and Greece.*The prevailing
whitewashed walls left a deep and lasting impression on his
work, and onthe design aesthetic ofthearchitects that followed
him, many of whom in the ensuing years made pilgrimages to
the ‘white villages’ ofthe Mediterranean.

These few quotations of Loos, Wright and Le Corbusier are
drawn from numerous observations made by architectsinthe
formative years ofthe‘modernist’ era, when an admission of
the existence of building traditions that owed littletothe
development of professionally-designed ‘architecture’ was not
uncommon amongthis generation of architects. Itisatheme
that has persisted in the utterances of architectsto the present
day, though some ofthe aspects which were of concernttothem
have changed, or have become more complex over time. From
thechallengeto the principle of ‘truth to materials’ that steel,
concrete and plate glass posed, tothe design implications ofthe

timber houses, or
konaks, line a street
in Istanbul, Turkey

axiom that ‘form follows function’, architects were searching for
new paradigms that would help givethem direction inthe
twentieth century. Vernacular architecture appeared to provide
these, though often the pursuit was one of self-interest. The
phrase ‘what can we learn from the vernacular’ or its equivalent,
seemedto imply humility and respect, but only occasionally
were the lessons passed on to others in publications or by
education. There were exceptions, particularly in the detailed
record of aspecificlocaltradition, orinthe classifications of its
typology. Customarily, the method employed included a survey
usingthe conventions of plan, elevations and perhaps sections,
and less often, measured drawings. Importance was placedon
the accurate record ofthe building ratherthan on its significance
to its occupants.

Listing the characteristics of the vernacular in orderto
rationalize the recognition and study of atradition became
customary, though Sybil Moholy-Nagy considered that ‘all
classifications appliedto anonymous architecture must remain
arbitrary and unsatisfactory. She based her ground-breaking
study 0f1957 in praise ofthe ‘native genius’ of American and
other builders ‘on the principal criteria of expression ofsiteand
climate, expression of form and function, expression of materials
and skills’ which, she hoped, ‘would answer at least in partthe
where, why, and how of anonymous architecture’Reflectingthe
position taken in architectural history ofthetime, cultural issues
were barely acknowledged in her classification.

Several architects claimed to have been influencedto
some degree by vernacular traditions, butthe nature ofthe
influence was one of economy inform and design, clarity and
simplicityandthe unabashed, undecorated use of materials.
Amajor change in approach was signalled by the Egyptian
architect Hassan Fathy. Though he came from a privileged

background and had no immediate experience of rural Egypt,
he became interested in traditional Nubian settlements and
technologies, in particularthe Nubian mud brick vault
constructed without formwork. Contracted in 1946to designthe
town of New Gourna near Luxor, heincorporated this system and
hoped that others would adopt his ideas and methods,
eventually to ‘establish a cultural barricade to stop the slide into
false and meaningless architecture that was gathering speed in
Egypt. New Gourna failed for anumber of unforeseen socialand
economic reasons, but Fathy’s attention to detail and, in Willim R
Polk’s terms, the ‘awesome if not overwhelming problems’ he
encountered were met with ‘a sensitivityto man’s social needs
andthe nourishing ofthe human spirit” which madethe
experiment one of lastingimportance. Hassan Fathy’s research
revealed to him how ‘the vernacular architecture ofthe Arab
World and neighbouring regions not only solvedthe climatic
problems but did sowith acombination of beauty and physical
and social functionality! He believed thatthe ‘result ofthe
human-environment interaction constitutes culture and has led
to the development of a multitude of cultures by different people
in different environments. Vernacular architecture isone ofthe
most concrete manifestations ofthis interaction. ®

Regrettably, Hassan Fathy did not publish his account of
the New Gourna project for over twenty years. Duringthistime
the exhibition ‘Architecture Without Architects’ was shown in
1964 atthe Museum of Modern Art, New York. Extremely
successful, the catalogue ofthis exhibition by the compiler,
Bernard Rudofsky undoubtedly drew attention to ‘Non-
Pedigreed Architecture’ which was ‘so little known we don’t
even have a word for it. Forwant of a generic label weshall call it
vernacular, anonymous, spontaneous, indigenous, rural, asthe
case may be.” Oftheterms Rudofsky employed, ‘vernacular’ was

probably the oldest, having been used by Giles Gilbert Scott in his
‘Remarks on Gothic Architecture’ in the mid-nineteenth century.
An extension ofthelinguistic analogy often employed by
architects and writers, itcompared the formal language ofthe
educated &lite with the ‘language of architecture’ andthe
language ofthecommon people with the ‘vernacular’ in
architecture. Backed by Jose Luis Sert, Richard Neutra, Gio Ponti
and Kenzo Tange among others, and illustrated largely from
museum collections, the photographic exhibition presented
an idealistic, aesthetic view: ‘Vernacular architecture does not
gothrough fashion cycles. It is nearlyimmutable, indeed,
unimprovable, since it serves its purposeto perfection’ ran the
caption to one ofthe views ofthe most illustrated subject, the
Greek island of Santorini, which represented paradoxically, the
‘Classical Vernacular’'°

Slowly, the cultural identities and societal needs of
builder-occupiers were being recognized and duringthe 19705
an increasing number of publications laid greater importance
on such issues. These were further elucidated bythose
anthropologists and geographers who acknowledgedthe
significance ofthe built environmenttottheirstudies and
research. Ifthe concept of ‘primitive architecture’ still persisted
itwas now more rare, and an increasing awareness oftherich
diversity of cultures and their buildings became evident inthe
writings of architects from several countries. Among tthefirst
ofthese wasthe Netherlands architect Aldo van Eyck, who
perceived that amongthe Dogon people of Mali ‘allthe material
objects from the smallestto the largest which support daily life
are charged with extra meaning and are identified in stages
with thetotality of creation‘, which hesummarized asthe
‘basket-house-village-universe”. This emotional affinity
contrasted with the western ‘frame of mind’ which was

‘impervioustothe spirit ofecology, anda ‘farcryindeed
from theway the Dogonttend the natural morphology of
their environment."

In theirobservations on vernaculartraditions other
architects commented on the aesthetic merits of simplicity
and harmony, on the responses oftheir builders to different
environments and climates. They approved ofthe understanding
displayed intheemployment of materials and resources, inthe
use of localizedtechniques and craft skills in construction, inthe
functionalism of traditional building forms, types and spaces,
and in otherfeatures which frequently paralleled their personal
concerns as designers. 'I still keep the precarious pleasure of
working with the marvellous artisans ofthe north of Portugal;
plasterers, carpenters, stone masons’ wrote Alvaro Siza, paying
homage to them in 1989. Feeling ‘privileged to have had direct
contact with the people’, he had been concerned ‘withthe
regional problems’ and had ‘discovered the very disparate
geographic, climatic and cultural conditions obtaining between
different regions.”

Duringthe period of uncertainty followingthe decline in
confidence in Modernism, the architect and theorist Robert Stern
reviewed ‘the Post-Modern continuum’ including a discussion
ofthe vernacular which ‘grows out ofa local soilthat nourishes a
local flavour; it offers simple solutions, simply arrived at. Atrue
vernacular is timeless in the very best sense ofthe word, but it
does not “develop”. Itisa “native” and a “naive” architecture,
in which there is common agreement on form! Reflectingthe
tendency ‘to idealizethat which is lost, the vernacular was
elevated tothe level of an ideal, comparabletothat of Classicism
itself; itwas assigned a moral value, that of “honesty”. Craft
was elevated into creed.' Stern’s comments perpetuatedthe
stereotypes of naive and intuitive building, and the ‘loss’ ofthe

vernacular which had been current a lifetime before. Demetri
Porphyrios was more pragmatic: ‘the idea of vernacular has
nothingto do with stylistics. It rather pointstothe universal
ethos of constructing shelter underthe scarcity of materials and
operative constructional techniques’ he wrote, arguingthatthe
essential meaning of the vernacular referred to ‘straightforward
construction, tothe rudimentary building of shelter, an activity
that exhibits reason, efficiency, economy, durabilityand
pleasure. Culture, it seemed, played no part in his definition."
Nevertheless, an increasing number of architects inthose
countries where the vernacular is active, have been aware ofthe
qualities of their national traditions. Asian architects have been
engaged in designing contexts where serviced and planned selfhelp and community building may thrive. Amongtthe bestknown Indian architects is Charles Correa, whose practice has
included low- and medium-cost housing. ‘Ifwelookatallthe
major concerns of humanists and environmentalists today;
balanced eco-systems, recycling of products, people’s
participation, appropriate life-styles, indigenous technology, etc
we find the people of Asia already have it all’ he observed. There
was no shortage of housing in Asia, except in the urban setting

which in his view was the responsibility ofthe Asian architect
to generate. To Correa ‘the old architecture -especiallythe
vernacular-has muchttoteach us as it always develops a
typology of common-sense."5 More specifically, the climatic
conditions prevailing in India called for appropriate design
responses, such asthe use ofthe courtyard inthearid regions.
Though he designed buildings of monumental scale, another
Indian architect, Balkrishna Doshi established the Vastu-Shilpa
Foundation in Ahmedabad ‘to research the vernacular
architecture ofthe region. Though not withouttheir problems,
Doshi’s projects were aimed at reconcilingthe gap between the
architect and the community in confrontingthe ever-increasing
world demand for housing. His work advanced understanding of
the motivations and achievements of vernacular builders.'

As yetthere is no clearly defined and specialized discipline
forthe study of dwellings orthe larger compass of vernacular
architecture. If such a discipline were to emerge it would probably
be one that combines some of the elements of both architecture
and anthropology with aspects of history and geography. The
need for a multi-faceted approach has probably accounted for
the limited number of comparative studies in the subject, for

## S. 10

14

Introduction

anthropological enquiry is not customarily a part of architectural
education, and architectural principles have rarely been considered
a significant aspect ofthetraining of an anthropologist.
Consequently, notes and records of indigenous building made by
architects in many countries are frequently short onthe use or
meaning of buildings, while anthropological writings will give
accounts of kinship structures and social customs in considerable
detailwiththe barest acknowledgement ofthe architectural
contexts in which they may occur.

The lack ofacommon graphic language oreven of a
common technical one, (all professions being inclinedto develop
theirown specialized jargon) means that very few instances can
be cited where interdisciplinary teams have researched buildings
and settlements to bringtheir various skills and perceptions
uponthem with aviewto a unified, inter-disciplinary study.
Slowly the position is changing as international conferences
consider aspects oftraditional building and environmental
behaviour. Occasional studies in architectural anthropology
have also appeared sincethefirst publication ofthis book in 1987.
The vernacular does not depend on the interest of architects or
academics, nor does it require theirendorsement to survive.
Nevertheless, moves to destroy buildingtraditions have been
associated insome countries with a driveto modernize. Swept
along by accompanying propaganda the residents of vernacular
dwellings have often been urged or directed to relocate in highrise blocks or other mass housing. Others have chosen to move
into suburban houses in the belief that this will be of benefitto
them. Thethreatsto vernacular traditions are many and
architects will be called upon to express professional opinions on
theirfate. Butthis is a matterthat should be of serious concern
not onlytoarchitects butto allwho valuethe inheritance ofthe
built environment.

Many studies add tothe knowledge of vernacular
traditions in numerous cultures. Yetthe use and application of
the knowledge acquired is much less frequently discussed. With
the rate of growth in the world population, the disparities of
wealth between and within countriesand the exploitative
activities of multi-national corporations, the poverty of billions
of people will necessitate the building of new housing on a vast
scale. Inthis, the involvement of architects and the building
industry as well as that of vernacular builders, will be essential.
But asymbiotic relationship between self- and communitybuilders of countless societies and the professional designers
cannot be achieved without a profound understanding ofthe
environments, needs and values of the diverse culturesthat
will beinvolved.

Dwelling: the vernacular house

Though theterm ‘vernacular architecture’ has been usedin a J
number oftheinstances cited above, it includes many types

of buildingwhich have not been professionally designed. Broadly,

itmay be defined, as itwas inthe world encyclopedia ofthe

subject, as ‘comprising the dwellings and all other buildings of

the people. Related to their environmental contexts and available
resources they are customarily owner-or community-built,
utilizingtraditional technologies. Allforms of vernacular

architecture are built to meet specific needs, accommodating

the values, economies and ways of lifeoftheculturesthat \
produce them.’ To which it could be added that ‘they may be
adapted or developed overtime as needs and circumstances
change’ For more convenient usethe importance of self-help
orcommunity-based building is emphasized in the simple
definition ofthe vernacular as ‘the architecture ofthe people,
and by the people, but not forthe people. Inmakingthis

8: Mbuti Pygmies
smoking outside ahut,
Ituri Forest, Zaire

9: An immensetimber

framed farmhouse

complex, nowinthe

open-air museum,

Cloppenburg,

Niedersachsen,

Germany. Today it

functionsasa

restaurant and hotel \

distinction it is contended that ‘popular architecture’ designed
by professional architects or commercial builders for popular
use, does not comewithin thecompass ofthe vernacular.
Suburban housing estates are popular-butthey are not
vernacular. All houses are dwellings; but all dwellings are not
houses. To dwell isto make one’s abode:tolivein, orat, oron,
orabout a place. For many peoplethis implies a permanent
structure, for some it means temporary accommodation, while
for others it issimply where they live, even ifthere is little
evidence of a building. Dwelling is both process and artefact: it
isthe experience of living at a specific location anditisthe
physical expression of doing so. There are peoples whose
dwellings are simply shelters of branches and leaves for brief
occupancy, and there are others whose dwellings are massive
structures, finely wrought in durable materials and centuries
old. Butthe dwelling is morethan the structure, asthe soul is
morethanthe body that envelops it. For countless millions of
peoplethe bond between themselves and the place where they
live transcends the physical frame of their habitation. Itisthis
double significance of dwelling- dwelling as the activity of
living and residing, and dwelling as the place or built form
which isthe focus of residence - which encompasses its
manifold cultural and material aspects.

No one can say how many dwellings there are; nottothe
nearest million, noreven to the nearest hundred million. With a
world population in excess of six billion people it is possible
thatthere are a billion dwellings. Of these only aminuscule
proportion were designed by architects; one per cent may
well be an over-estimate. Suburban houses and multi-storey
apartment blocks erected by speculative builders or housing
corporations can be added in, but still the total of dwellings
produced under these professional auspices may still account

for fewerthan one dwelling in ten. By farthe majority of
dwellings in the world, whatever the basis of estimated figures
may be, were built either by the occupants themselves or by
members of their communities, without external professional
intervention. These dwellings, the houses of, and by,the
people, are the subject ofthis book.

Types of dwelling, such asthe white-walled cubic houses
ofthe Mediterranean islands, thetimber-framed English
cottages orthe ‘log cabins’ of North America have been the
subjects of attention for several decades. Their aesthetic appeal
and theirechoes of long, ifromanticized, histories have been
captured bythe cameras of countless tourists. But these kinds
of dwelling have also attracted scholarly investigation and
detailed studies ofthem have been made. Tourist boards and
conservation lobbies alike have recognized their value as
symbols of the heritage of nations, and there are parallel
interests ina number of countries. Yet vast numbers of dwellings
have been demolished because they are officially considered
‘sub-standard', they hinder ‘progress’ and ‘development’, orthey
are inappropriate in ‘amodern world’. Some have been destroyed
by natural disasters; many more have been the casualties of
conflict, and millions of dwellings are underthethreat of
impending destruction. Singular examples have been saved in
certain countries, though they may be grouped in artificial
environments or ‘open-air’ museums. An emphasis on such
conserved buildings as historical artefacts has often been atthe
expense of understanding how they have been adapted over
time to meet changing needs.

Few human artefacts havethe longevity of houses, or in
the case of nomadic peoples, of dwellingttraditions. Boats and
bowis, ploughs and pitchers, tables andtools may last fora
century or more and some may be in use for longer. Nonetheless,

## S. 11

16 Introduction

the continuity of use ofthe dwelling usually exceedsthe
utilitarian life of every other artefact. Consequently, the
occupancy of innumerable dwellings has been by successive
generations of families, whose association withthe building, the
land and the settlement is intimately related to their own sense
ofidentity. People of all ages in many parts ofthe world still have
both the desire and the capacity to constructtheir dwellings and
to shape their environments in accord withthenorms ofthe
societies of which they are members. Traditionally, the sensibility
andthe know-how, the skills andthecompetenceto build
effectively in responsetothe land, the climate and the resources
to hand, have been passed on between generations. Embodying
particular values and needs, homes are built with integrityand
authenticity in ways that have frequently achieved beauty of
form and unity of design, andinturn aretransmittedto
subsequent generations.

Untold numbers of buildings in usetoday arethe products
ofthe past, whilemany more tthatare still being built perpetuate
the forms that satisfiedthe same societies in earliertimes.
Dwellings may outlast lineages and in some cultures may be
re-occupied and adapted, their survivaltothe present beinga
testimonytotheir responsivenessto changing life-ways. Changes
may be induced by economic conditions, some buildings also
serving other purposes such as the protection of produce, the
shelter of animals, or for use as workshops. When cultures
change, old buildings may be adapted and new ones modified in
form, whilethe depletion of some materials and the substitution
of others may require new technologies. All dwellings have,
orsoon acquire, a history which may sometimes be relatively
static, but which may also bearthe evidence of considerable
transformation. Astudy of vernacular buildings which seeks only
to discover the ‘Ur-type’ or progenitor ofatradition and notiits

many manifestations and adaptations over time, is by no means
uncommon, even ifitis unrealistic.

Many factors exert theirinfluence on dwelling form in
constancy and in change, which relateto family and culture,
whether these be matters of ancestry and inheritance, of gender
and age, established religion or other belief systems, customs
and mores, or group and personal values and behaviours. When
the vast range of locations, conditions, histories and cultures
which have ccontributedtotheshapingandthe use ofthe
world’s dwellings are considered, the difficulties inmaking
acomparative study, even of representative examples only,
become apparent. Takingthem into consideration has
influencedthe way in which the vernacular house is discussed
here. To have looked atthe dwellingtypes of each continent,
oreven ofeach cultural area or region, would have involved
unnecessary repetitions ofthetechniques of construction (for
example, in adobe) or of plantype (such asthe courtyard plan),
orofotherfeatures that areshared by many peoples acrossthe
world. Instead, this book reviews some of the basic principles,
both environmental and cultural, which have had bearing on the
diversity ofthe world’s dwellings. Among them are climateand
location, economies and settlementtypes; material resources,
andthe processes of construction. Types and forms of
dwellings are related to their spatial organization and utilization
in meetingthe demands of daily family living, whiletheir
significance forthe occupants frequently finds expression in
symbolism or decoration.

Although some of the examples cited are from Europe and
North America, many have been drawn from other parts ofthe
world. Aconsiderable amount of literature on the vernacular
architecture of Britain and the United States exists, andthere are
national surveys, open-air museums and publications readily

available in several European countries. Yet the traditions of selfbuilt and community-built dwellings which are the focus ofthis
work, ceased to be active long ago in much of Western Europe
and in virtually all ofthe British Isles, while only a few traditions
are maintained in the United States. Almost inevitably, thriving
traditions areto befound among cultures in the so-called
‘developing’ countries, though cultures do not necessarily
correspond with the boundaries of nation states. Large numbers
of vernacular buildings exist and are in use in many countries
where thettraditions of building them are no longer sustained;
some ofthese are included here. Nevertheless, the majority of
dwellings that are cited in case studies are representative of
traditions that are still active, or were being built atthetime
thatthey were recorded. This means that thetraditions were
comparatively recent, having been researched in the closing
decades ofthetwentieth century, upto the present. In most
instances they were personally studied, the years ofthe ‘field
notes’ being listed inthe references.

It has not been possibleto return to alllocations orin
some instances, to ascertain the present circumstances ofthe
cultures. Much has been threatened, damaged or lost within

recent memory as individuals, families or even whole
communities have been forcedto abandon therural areas and
often with nohomestogoto, have migrated tothecities.

With the migration from rural regions some ofthe acquired
knowledge and experience of living in the world’s diverse regions
and environmental conditions has been lost. Beyondthe
traditional aspects of dwelling, the impact of globalization and
its effect on rural economies, rapid urbanization andthe
unprecedented scale of housing problems which confrontthe
peoples ofthe world inthetwenty-first century bringa new
urgencytothe study ofthevernacularandtothe recovery of
much accumulated wisdom.

In a great many culturesthe dwelling isthe largest
artefactthat aman, awoman ortheir family may ever
construct; in many others itisthe single most important
item that they may ever calltheir own. The dwelling is more
than the site it occupies, the materials of which it is made, the
know-how of its construction, the labour that has gone into
building, the cost intime and money that has been expended
upon it. The dwelling isthe theatre of our lives, where the major
dramas of birth and death, of procreation and recreation are

## S. 12

18 Introduction

played out, and in which the succession of scenes of daily
living are enacted, and re-enacted in the processes of dwelling.
Yet plays can be performed without stages, theatres stand
empty while awaiting the performers and their audiences.
Dwelling is much more than this: there are virtually no cultures
in the world forwhom a form of shelter does not exist. If

some have delegated the responsibility fortheir design and
construction to architects and builders many more draw upon
their vernacular traditions, adaptingthem as social change
requires, to build their own dwellings. The commonalties and
differences between them are sometimes subtle, sometimes
profound, but by knowing more aboutthe qualitiesthat shape
the dwellings of different societies we may be more effective
in assisting in the creation of appropriate living conditions,

as cultures worldwide striveto meetthe housingdemands
ofthe future.

Anote on measurements and drawings

Vernacular architecture is seldom based on precise measurements and the use ofthe metric system is rare. Human
proportions are commonly the source ofthe measurements
used inthe ‘imperial’ system. Includingtheinch, thefoot, yard
and fathom, this system is largely of Roman origin, which was
derived from human dimensions. As given inthis book,
measurements are generally approximate but, where appropriate,
their metricequivalent is alsoiven.

Forthe same reason, precise scales are not employed
with referencetothe vernacular drawings, except where specific
buildings are recorded. As walls, roofs and other structural
features or details may vary within the norms of atradition,
fully-dimensioned plans, sections and elevations are not used.
Projections or perspectives are used where three-dimensional
impressions are appropriatetothe context ofthetext.

Shelterofthenomads

Ifthereis one exoticterm fora house that a Western child knows,
it is ‘igloo”. For an ‘igloo’, augmented by harpoon, husky dogs
and sledge, has long served as a symbol of anything cold from ice
creamto the acme of the salesman’s skills in disposing of
refrigerators. Such pre-conditioning may make it difficultto
examine objectivelythe habitat ofthe Inuit, asthe ‘Eskimo’
peoples are correctly termed. The Inuit are dispersed around half
ofthe polar world, from the Bering Straitsand Alaskato the
shores of Greenland. Nomadic hunters whose food and the raw
material for their clothing and artefacts comes mainly from sea
mammals, they are principally coastal dwellers with acycle of
movements and activities largely determined bythe length and
severity ofthe Arcticwinter, andthe conditions of iceand snow
prevailing over both land and sea.

Surviving inthe Arctic

Inuit territory lies mainly north of the 12°C August isotherm,
which defines the northernmostlimits ofthetree-lineandthe
territories ofthe forest First Nations (Canadian Indians). It loops
north ofthe Arctic Circlein the Yukon, embraces much of Hudson
Bay, sweeps deeplyintoLabradortothe east, andincorporates
the Bering Sea coast of Alaskatothe west. Fortheirfoodthe
Inuit largely depend on catchingfish-a family may consume
fivethousand in ayear-and hunting seals and caribou, and

by picking berries and fruits when the warmer, short summer
season permits. Themany months they spend in the confines of
the snow or sod iglu might seem circumscribed and confined,
butthe people are sociable and may travel hundreds of miles by
sledgeto visit relatives and friends. Inthe vast wastes oftundra
and snow, coastal fogs and winter blizzards obscurethe
landmarks ofthe indented coastline, so such travel away from

3: Inuit stilt houses 2: Map of Inuit

the minute humps ofthe dwellings demands extraordinary
navigational skills and spatial cognition. These they indisputably
have, as can be seen in the remarkable maps drawn by the Inuit,
collected and published as early as 1942, which demonstrate
theiracute observation and awareness of their inhospitable
environment.' Mastery of survival arts, includingthe invention
and making of everything from their harpoons and net gauges

to their snow goggles and visors, from their stone vesselsand
lampstotheir clothing and dwellings, also ensured their capacity
to livein the Arctic.

Acommon language, more or less mutually intelligible
among hunting bands dispersed over nearly 2,600,000 square
kilometres (about a million square miles), means that Inuit
share, broadly speaking, acommon culture. There are differences
between the ways of life ofthe Alaskan Inuit and those ofthe
Central Territories in the islands of northern Canada, and ofthe
people of Greenland, some of whom live 800 miles north ofthe
Arctic Circle, butallshare the experience of being exposedto a
most rigorous climate and a harsh and inhospitable
environment. There isevidencethatthe Inuithave evolved a
physical resistance to chronic exposure to extreme cold, with
hand blood-flow twice that of people from temperate climates;?
but even so, their main resistance to excessively low
temperatures was achieved with great ingenuity. Mummified
bodies were discovered at Qilakitsoq, Greenland, wearing clothes
of sealskin sewn with sinew, waterproofed with seal oil, and
undergarments made from the feathers of cormorant, ducks and
geese; garments ofa kind that were still serving their purpose
five centuries later.3 Such clothing protected the individual;
the dwelling origlu protected the family group.

Snow houses were seldom built bythe Alaskan Inuit and
infrequently by those in Greenland; they were, however, used as

winter dwellings by the Central Inuit and smaller ones were built
astemporary shelters by hunters. Although their use is now rare,
they were stillemployed by some Netsilik Central groups inthe
1960s.* Irrespective of their size, the method of building iglus was
consistent. A level site or, where possible, onethat was banked
slightly, was usually chosen for a new iglu, and suitable snow
located bytestingfor its consistency with a fine caribou antler
probe. Blocks cut from asnow-driftthat had been built upfrom
several falls were inclined to shear, so a snow layer from asingle
fall was sought. Itwas cleared oftop snow with a hide snow
shovel, andthe drift swiftly cut or sawn into blocks with a
slightly curved ivory snow-knife or orsulung. Ablock might beas
much as3ft (about a metre) in length, halfthat in width, and

8 inches (about 0.2 metres) thick, and each block was cut with
ascarcely perceptible curve.

After acircle had been scribed some sixteen ft (5 m)
across, the initial layer of blocks was laid in aring. The first block
wasthen cut backtotthesite level, and the subsequent blocks
were each cut atan inclinesotthattthis layer formedthe first
stage of a continuous spiral. The next blocks were laidsothat
the joints were ‘broken’ (or did not coincide) and chamfered so
that, as they inclined inwards, each one rested against the
preceding block. In this way the spiral continued to slope
inwards without the dome collapsing duringthe course of
construction. One man would work insidethe dome, and
another outside it, cutting and packing the blocks and filling
any gaps with loose snow. A keyblock ortwo would be placed in
position, supported bythe man within, who stood, if necessary,
onabench ofsnowtoreachthetop.

Oncethe vaulted dome was complete, an opening was
cut closetothefloor level, andatunnel, sometimes with storage
chambers, was made forthe approach. This was lowerthanthe

internal floor level sothat cold airwould remain trapped within
itand not enterthe iglu. Insome cases the entrance was
approached by a subterranean channel, in others by an angled
tunnel, but in every instancethe aperture was small, sothat.little
cold airwas admitted when the snow block door or bear-skin
curtain was moved aside. Inside, a snow bench, or iglig, the width
ofthe dome, was built upto a height of about one metre (2 or
3 ft), which served as the living and sleeping platform. In front
ofitatfloor level, thefirstthird ofthespace formed the natig,
orgeneral purpose area, which housed the stores offish, the
cooking utensils andthe household articles. Itwas also usedto
receive visitors. Some Inuit groups lined the roofs oftheir houses
with caribou hides to form an inner skinwhich acted as
additional insulation. With blubber lamps and bodyheat it was
possible fortheinternaltemperatureto beraised to 15.5°C or
more, when the outside temperature was down to-40°C. To keep
the interior ventilated a small hole, or gangirn, was madeinthe
dome wall, and others would be pierced when staleairhadto be
released. Some internallight was to be had from a blubber oil
lamp with amoss wick, and daylight was obtainedthrough an
arched opening cutthrough thesnow dome abovethe entrance.
Atranslucent layer of sewn seal intestine linings often served as
a window, or alternatively a block of clear ice was used, which
was formed from snow melted in a sealskin bag, theresulting
curved shapefittingthe surface ofthe dome. Sometimesa
window might be cut in the side oftheiglu, andthe block
removed usedasareflectorto catch and deflectthe last rays
ofthe sun intothe dwelling.5

Inside the igluthe family sat on the iglig, or platform, with
their backs closetothe wallwith their legs extended but not
overhangingthe edgeto ensurethatthey were outofthecoldat
floor level. The iqliq would be covered with moss and sealskins or

hides to insulate the occupants from the cold, while astove used
formakingtea would help raisethetemperature. Fish hadto be
gutted and cleaned, small animals from thetrap lines skinned
and quartered, clothes sewn, and hides prepared -although it
was forbidden to do so whilethe men were out hunting. Though
some proscriptions on what could be done within the iglu were
based on superstition, most clearly evolved becauseoftheneed
to live amicably in close contiguity. Honesty and calm were
valued, anger and extreme behaviour repressed. Inthe evenings
the family would engage in games, play withthe children,
smoke, drinkttea, tell stories or receive visitors, and eventually
undress and climb swiftly into fur-lined sleeping bags of sealskin.
Inthe early 19605 a band of Netsilik, the Utku, numbered
Just thirty-five people in eight households, the sole inhabitants
of some ninety thousand kilometres (about 35,000 miles) of
territory. In spite of their isolation, however, the Inuit, when
given the opportunity, are gregarious. Until recentlycommunal
club houses, karigi (gaggi or gaggig), were built bythemen and
were afocus for ceremonial life. Song-dances, in which bothmen
and women participated, gave opportunities for repressed
feelingsto be relieved through derisive song duels; and major
feasts and ceremonies were held in the karigiwhere protective
amulets, in which spirits resided, were hung. In some instances
a karigi might be made by joiningthree or more snow houses;
in others, a special, large snow house of about 20 ft (6m) or
more in diameter might be built, capable of holding as many
as sixty people. The motivation to construct large, communal
snow houses for community gatherings, ceremonials and
religious occasions was an outcome of Inuit values and belief
systems, but ultimately, the perpetuation of allthesetraditions
depended upon their continued, yet flexible, transmission
over generations.

7: Typical construction 8: Inuit hunters camp

Unquestionably an extremely efficient form of dwelling,
the snow house was devised from the most economical means:
the dome offered the maximum volume possible with the
minimum surface area, being constructed without formwork
through the use ofthespiral of chamfered blocks. Moreover,
the dome had no internal corners or areas where stagnant or
cold pockets of air couldform, and its contours permittedthe
conservation ofheat and energy and assisted in the circulation
ofair. Avolume of cold air could betrapped between a secondary
lining of hides andthe internal surface ofthe igluto preventthe
snow shell from melting as a result ofthe build-up of heat
within. Itwas also an adaptable dwelling, which could permitthe
addition of domical cells for storage orfor extensions when two
or more families wished to link up duringthe long winter.

Elsewhere in the 4,000 mile (10,000 kilometre) span of
Inuit dwellings, othertypes of iglu were built. On the Arctic coast
of Alaskathe anegiuchak was favoured, being made of snow
blocks, but was square in plan and with a gable roof over which
loose snow was piled.? Orthe killegun was made, which was a
temporary structure made of block walls cut from the ground
snow. The holetthis left was 3 ft (about ametre) deep, andthe
four walls rose a similar amount above the surface ofthesnow. A
ridge pole was used to support acanvas roof. Inside, atent was
pitched, thus producing a double-skinned dwelling so efficiently
insulated asto permit atemperature of over38°Cto bereached
with the aid of a camp stove, when thethermometer outside
droppedtto -28°C.

Itis possible thatthe snow house derives from the
temporary shelters built bythe Nunamiut of Alaska.® This
consisted of a domical framework of willow wands covered in
a layer of snow, heated stones being used to annealthesnow
into a shell. However, there is also a possibility that houses of

## S. 16

26 Shetterf

thenomads

whalebone rib frames may have preceded the snow house; as
the remains of ancient whalebone-framed houses, or quarmang,
have been found in all Inuit regions and some were still in use
earlyinthetwentieth century. Most others, includingthose

in Greenland, were replaced some three centuries before by
dwellings of stone and turf orsod. Many ofthese were cut into
ahillside, while others were party subterranean. They were
constructed out of heavy stones, which were corbelled out on
the cantilever principleto support a roof slab, over which turf
and stones were packed. The Greenland house was not dissimilar
to the snow iglu in form, but a rectangular stone and turfhouse
largely replaced it, sometimes providing accommodation for
several families.? Tothe west, in Alaska, where driftwood

could be obtained in considerable quantity, log houses were
constructed, and these were packed overwith sod. The internal
form ofthese houses was approximately rectilinear, butthe
snow platform or iglig, the narrow and low entrance passage,
and gut-skin windows were common to nearly alltypes. Where
possible, the karigiwere also constructed from log, plank or
stone and sod as in Alaska, where the club houses were
associated with specificwhaling crews.

Asthe winter ended the snow house became
uncomfortable, with snow blocks melting, roofs falling in, and
the smell of faeces outside becoming evident. Inthetransitional
period between seasons a gaqmagq might be built, consisting
of circular walls of snow blocks with atent rooforwith atent
pitched within the enclosure, its guy-ropes fixed with pegsto
the wall. Afterthe biting cold of the winter, the brief summers
brought the possibility of hunting game -and clouds of
mosquitoes and bitingflies. Nearly allinuit bands turnedto
making ttent dwellings or tupiq, constructed from slender poles
made of pieces of antler or willow wands lashed together. Two

pairs of crossed poles, the smaller attheentrance, the larger at

What can be learned from the example of Inuit shelter
types? Survival in the Polar Regions would not be possible for
extended periods without effective means of shelterto insulate
a family against the rigours of extreme cold, and exposureto
winds, fogs and precipitation. Having neither clothes not shelter
ahuman being would soon die when exposedto atemperature
of-5°C. Relativetothe landmass they inhabit, there are very
few Inuit, in some areas as few as one person to 2500 square
kilometres (about 1,000 sq miles). They are widely dispersed
alongthefrozen rivers and harsh, serrated coasts ofthenorthern
Polar Regions; in terms of human population as a whole, their
existence is marginal. The Inuit have been abletoliveinthe
severe conditions ofthe Arctic because they have developed
the clothing and the dwellings that providethemwiththevital
protection, andthetechnologies that have madethese
developments possible. Permafrost inhibits deep digging in
some Arctic regions and layers of ice 6 or more ft (approximately
2 m) thick may cover rock, although in some partsturf and stone
may be obtainable. It isevidentthat in such aregion, where
natural organic materials inthe form oftimber and grasses are
virtually non-existent, and where suitable structural members
may only be obtained from sea creatures such as whale or seal,
great inventiveness is required to devise and to adapt existing
resources to become building materials, asthe Inuit have

developed the unsupported vault ofthe snow house. For
another example of climate in the development of building
forms, we might consider thetraditionsthat have developed
amongst other Arctic peoples, such as the Sami, formerly
known astheLapps.

Spread overthe northern regions of four countries
(Norway, Sweden, Finland and north-western Russia) the Sami,
like the Inuit, also demonstrate that cultures are not defined by
national boundaries, indeed these often severthem ratherthan
preserve their integrity. The Sami, however, are a people divided
into numerous tribes, whose social behaviour and dwellings are
distinct, and into four groups: the Mountain, Forest, Coastaland
Skolt (eastern) Sami. Reindeer herding is common among most
Sami, andtheinstinctual migration ofthese animalsto their
mating grounds requires nomadic pastoralism intherutting
season, duringwhich thetraditionaltent is still widely used."
Although similarto the Plains tipiin outward appearance, the
ätnäris-kähtetent ofthe Sami was, however, constructed
differently. Four poles with curved ends were lashed togetherto
make a frame oftwo arches, and the smoke-pole, or pot hanger,
was inserted through a hole in this frame. Over a dozen supporting
poles were then placed against these arches, including a pair
which defined the entrance, to produce the conical form. A pair
of large, triangular tent cloths would be stretched overthe
structure andtiedtothe poles, asackingsmoke-hood covering
the peak, and a door-flap hanging from the door-frame. Athick
layer of birch twigs and brush was laid on the floor, except forthe
hearth, which was ringed with stones, and a path to the doorway
was defined by logs. Outsidethetent a pakkas oftwotripods and
aridge or platform was erected, from which bags and personal
belongings were hung above the wet ground. On striking camp
the kata cloths were folded into saddle cloths forthe pack

Whether they are swiftly erected forimmediate and
overnight stays, built for intermittent or more prolonged use, or
are intended for semi-permanent occupancy, the nature of each
dwellingwill beconditionedto a degree by its function withinthe
economic and social life ofthe group. For boththe Inuitandthe
Sami, expediency and the maximizing oftheir structural potential

28 Shelterof

shapes their response to materials; theimperativeto create
adequate conditions for domesticlivingand work necessitates
theirresponse to climate; and a perception ofthe benefits of
introduced technologies, againstthe maintenance of customary
methods and skills, influences their attitudetto change.

Itwas not until the sixteenth century, when they werefirst
reported as breeding reindeer, thatthe Sami became pastoral
nomads. However, we have records of nomadic dwellings from as
early as98 AD, when the Roman historian Tacitus had referredto
the ‘extraordinarily wild and horribly poor’ hunters, who lived in
contraptions of ‘branches connected together.’ People who live
offtheland by such wild life as they may kill or by collecting
roots and fruits, are broadly classified as ‘hunter-gatherers’ or
“foragers”. Customarily they build simple shelters of branches and
grass as did, until the 1920s, the most southerly counterparts
oftheInuit in the Americas, the Selk’nam (or Ona) ofTTierra del
Fuego, Patagonia.'® A few hunting bands ofthe muchdocumented San ‘Bushmen’ (now known as Basarwa) ofthe
Kalahari Desert, may stillmake similar kuas, or shelter of
branches, although the majority have become settled in recent
years.” Other peoples, likethe Punan of Sarawak, may cut down
forest palm fronds to make a temporary shelter;® whilethe Wai-
Wai hunters of Guyana cut long palm fronds, which they arrange
like a fan over a pole framework, to make what they poetically
term a ‘powis-tail‘, after the powis (curassow) bird ofthejungle.'

More carefully constructed arethe domed huts ofthe
Mbuti oftheIturi forest in northeastern Congo, and the Bakwa/
Akwa of the forest regions borderingon Cameroon andthe
Central African Republic, and othertribes of pygmies. Small in
stature and known since ancient times, pygmies have an
emotional bond with the ‘Mother Forest’, which is not shared by
other peoples oftheregion, many ofwhom fearthe spirits ofthe

Hunter-gatherers most frequently construct their shelters
from branches, withies or canes, together with grasses, leaves or
other such materials, which can be collected nearthe selected
site, to providethe required frame and cover. Nomadic pastoral
herders, onthe other hand, are more likelytotake many ofthe
structural components with them, usingsome of their herds, be
they reindeer, camels oryak, for pack animals. As nomads they
are alsoon the move, although their routes are usually planned
to follow agreed patterns which do not conflict with those of
other pastoralists, or asthey are determined by access to suitable
grazing or sources of waterfortheirherds. In doingsothey may
cover immense distances across the ‘wastelands’ ofthe world.

Living in the desert

Although we are accustomed to thinking of mankind in relation
to nations and territories, only thirty per cent oftheworld’s
surface is land. Ofthis, immense areas are very thinlyinhabited
orhavenno inhabitants at all: most of Canada, Alaska and
Greenland; much of Russia and Siberia; large tracts of Mongolia,
the Middle East, Arabia and North Africa; and most of Australia.
These regions are largely barren lands, from the short grass
steppes of Russia, the gravel plains ofthe Gobi, ortherocky
highlands ofthe Sahara, tothe moss and lichen-covered
wilderness ofthetundra and ice shelves ofthe Poles. Some are
arid, with high temperatures and low rainfall; others cold,

and subject to searing winds. Combining poor soilsandthin
vegetation, these territories are generally unsuitablefor
cultivation, although they may still be hostto many species
ofanimals, birds and hardy plants.

Inhospitable they might seem, but many ofthe world’s
more barren regions sustain dispersed nomadic populations
whose ability in adaptingto their environment is often
remarkable. This isthe case withthe Rendille, who liveinthe
semi-desert between Lake Turkana and the Ndoto mountains of
Kenya.?? They are nomadic pastoralists whose movements are
dictated bytheneedtofind forage and waterfortheir camels,
in adry region which has an average annual rainfall of lessthan
250 millimetres-and in some years, none atall.On finding it
they may plantto stay for a few days to pasturetheir camels,
whiletaking care nottto overgraze the land or pollute the water.
The pack camels are relieved ofthearcs of poles and wickerwork
frames and doors which arethe basic components ofthe
Rendille min or dwelling.

Looking rather like a largewhelk when assembled, the
min is not asymmetrical dome, but two-thirds of ahemisphere,
with an inclined front. The process of assembly isthework ofthe
Rendillewomen. Two pairs of long curved poles, which they have
previousiy formed into aflattened arc by heating, bendingand
considerable use, are lashed together; one pair providingthe
leading edge of the part-hemispherical frame, the otherasecond
curved bracetothe rear. Further lengths oftthin stick are bent
over and lashed to these stronger elementsto constructthe
domical part ofthe hut, while straight poles are inclined inwards
to form the frame atthe front. When the frame is complete, the
women cover it with ‘tiles’ of gathered lengths of sisal, sewn
togetheratoneendandtiedtothe horizontal wands. Attherear,
a couple of animal hides are hung, which can beremoved for
ventilation ifthe interior getstoo hot. Alongrope is bound
aroundthe whole structure, and although it is light and quivers
in the fierce winds that blow from Lake Turkana, itmakes a
resistant shelter.23

On plan, the min departsfrom a circle, beingwideratthe
frontthan attherear; andthetwoformsthat make upthe whole
structure differentiate the areas within. The forward space is
usedto storethe milking vessels, firewood and sandals; the rear,
domical section provides a sleeping area forthewoman and her
husband, with palm stem and skin sleeping mats on the ground.
Personal possessions such as stoppered calabash gourds, hide
containers and household equipment are suspended from the
ribs orhungroundthe back ofthe hut. Whilethe women build
the huts, themen definethe nabo, or council enclosure, and
lightthefirethat will provide the firebrands for allthe huts. An
enclosure of acacia branches is built round the settlement with
its entrance facing west. The huts all have their entrances facing
the main accessto the compound, and are ranged in an order of
seniority that reflectsthe subclan and status ofthe occupants.
Enclosures forthe animals are also built of acacia, and each
member ofthe camp has a well defined roleintheanimals’ care.

When the grazingiisthin the elders ofthe Rendille camp
debate their next move. Dismantlingthe hutstakes two orthree
hours, and the poles and mats of each min are carefully loaded on
to two orthree pack camels. With the long spars rising high from
its back likethe mast of adhow, the camel justifies its name of
‘ship ofthe desert’ asthetrain moves offtofind another campsite
by nightfall. Anders Grum, a Danish architect-anthropologist,
with the help of a venerable Rendille man traced the movements
ofonenomadic group. Over more than seventy years it covered
over nineteen thousand kilometres (some twelvethousand
miles), in long, zig-zagging routes, making use ofthewhole
Rendille territory and sometimes penetrating well beyond.?+

Many other East African pastoral tribes, likethe Boran of
Somalia, use similar shelters, transporting poles, woven straw
mats and wattle doors on their pack animals asthey migrateto

new grazing lands. Because the structures are covered with mats,
they are frequently termed and classified as tents butthey are
more accurately (although not more simply) described as frameand-mat dwellings. There are important structural differences
between thistype of hutandthe majority oftents, foritis built
by restrainingthe bent poles that form the structural frame in
tension, the mat covering being laid overthe surface, and only
lightlytensioned, if at all, bytyingittothe frame. True tents,
however, tension the covering itself, without placingitona
frame but by stretchingit over polesto keep it clear ofthe ground.?5
Probably the best-known tent isthe A-section form with
a rectangular plan and low ‘brailing’ walls, used by armies the
world over. But apart from those who have acquiredthem in
refugee camps or from relief programmes, few tent nomads use

## S. 19

32. Shelter of
thenomads

thistype. Quitethe most common is one or other variant ofthe
so-called ‘black tent”. Spread acrossthe map like a black tent
itself, the culture of pastoral nomads stretches from Mauretania
and southern Moroccotothe west, tothe aBrog of Tibet in the
east. In some respects, the conditions in which Tibetan nomads
live differ considerably from those of the arid-zone nomads of
the Middle East-thealtitude, the extremes of cold and heat,
the high pastures, the herding of yaks ratherthan camels or
goats. Butthey share with their lowland desert neighboursthe
use ofthetent, although the Tibetan type is much the heavier
being made from the belly-fringe hair ofyak steers, with pinsand
toggles made of yak-horn.?° Whilethere are many variations
ofform and detail inthetents ofthe differing peoples of Iraq,
Turkey, Arabia or Algeria, they have much in common,
suggesting perhaps a single source far back in time, beforethe
Song of Solomon was composed: ‘I am black, but comely, Oye
daughters of Jerusalem, as the tents of Kedar...?7

All blacktents are made from strips of cloth sewn edgeto
edgeto form a large surface. The strips, woven on ground looms,
are narrow enough forthe women to sit astridethem inthe
process of weavingthe goat-hair yarn. In essence, thetent cloth
isamembrane held in tension with guy ropes and stretched over,
or between, anumber of poles. East ofthe Arabian Gulfthetents
are mainly stretched alongtheir length, fortension acrossthe
coverwould separate the stripsattheseams. Tents in Arabia and
North Africa are strengthened by transverse reinforcingtension
bands ofwebbingwhichttaketthe strain across the membrane.
Many tents are strained over a curved ridge-piece supported by
one ortwo poles, oreven over a number of such ridge-pieces, to
give atypically humped effect;theridge preventsthe supporting
internal poles from piercingthe membrane. Althoughtthe black
colour ofthegoat hair might be expected to absorb the sun’s

rays and make thetent interior uncomfortably hot, the dark hue
ensuresthatthe cover casts a dense shade and insulates against
radiation heat. Natural oilsinthe goat hair are not washed out
by dying and consequently the open weave, which permitsthe
circulation of air, can contracttightlyto produce an impervious
shield in therare, sudden rains, retainingheat asthetemperature
drops. In Morocco, where rain, and even snow, fall quite often,
theridgeis high andthepitch ofthetent steep, but inthemore
arid regions the pitch ofthetent is flatter.2° Tents east ofthe Gulf
tend to be more box-like, with low-pitch covers and high side
walls. Ofthese, the khune ofthe Basseri is representative of how
thetents are used in practice.

One ofthe confederacy of tribes, the Basseri ofthe Fars
region of Southern Iran are herders of goats and sheep. Theirtribe
is divided into a dozen descent groups which are subdivided into
oulads, or family clusters, numbering about a hundred tents.
Every oulad has specificrightsto summer pasture, which have
been assigned bythe chiefs, towhich they migrate followingan
il-rah or‘tribal road”. This is atraditional routeon which they can
claim rightsto grazing, waterand camp sites at specifictimes
ofthe year. Othertribes may share parts ofthesame il-rah at
differenttimes sothatthe limited resources ofthe pasture are
utilizedtothefull. Fredrik Barth, who lived with the Basseri,
noted that it is necessary forthe health oftheiranimalsthatthey
are moved frequently, andthatthe migratory routes makethe
best use, at appropriate times ofthe year, ofthe different
ecological areas ofthe Fars region.??

Atent accommodates a Basseri household of five or six
persons, each household owning upto a dozen donkeys as pack
animals, and around a hundred sheep and goats in theirflocks.
Often they travel in large groups of as many as fortytents, but
when thewinterforage isthinthetents are spread out, with

only four orfivecamping near one another and other clusters
camping several kilometres apart. Becausethe nature ofthe
camp sites varies there is no strict ordering ofthetents, butthey
are often ranged in a rough semi-circle. When tthetent cloths are
raised wooden 'stay fasteners’ orloops alongthe edges ofthe
cloth takethe guy ropes, which are strained overthenotched
ends ofthe supporting poles sothatthey do not bear directlyon
the material. Five poles on the long dimension andthreeonthe
short sides ofthe membrane, which is some twenty by thirteen
ft (6 x4 m) in size, take the strain. In alargetentthelength ofthe
cloth is supported by a discontinuous ridge of T-shaped
elements, each made from a pole inserted into a ridge length.
Towardstherearthetent is further divided by ahigh screen of
personal belongings in woven sacks, which creates a private
space beyond. Skin vessels for milk and water line one side, and
a depression nearthe front ofthe khuneacts as afirepit.

When it is considered timeto move on, camp is struck
before sunrise and amounted advance guard rides swiftlyto
reconnoitre a new campsite. By noon the straggling line of
animals will have arrived and tthetents will be pitched again; the
women will have settled down to their domestic tasks, cooking,
spinning or weaving while the men obtain wood and water, do
the repairs, twist new ropes, andtendthe animals, astheir
predecessors have done for generations.?°

From tent to house

Although individual nomadic groups may use asingletype of
tent orframe-and-mat dwelling, larger complexes of peoples
spread over aconsiderable area may use a variety of structures.
These may be simple deviations from anorm orthey may have
fundamental differences which are specificto agroup or clan.
Obviously, differentiation contributes to a sense of belonging

Inhabitinga large area ofthe Central Saharathetribes
known as the Tuareg number around half a million people. In
Arabic, Tuareg’ is saidto mean ‘theabandoned people’-those
who have departed from the strict tenets of Islam. Tall, lean,
haughty and, in the past, warlike, the men wear indigo blue veils,
afactwhich has had a peculiarly romantic appeal for European
travellers. Strong class and caste distinctions between the
nobility andthe black harratin, the vassals, are observed through
complex rules and protocols. Centuries ago these people of
Berber origin moved southtto breedthe camels which aretheir
pride, and alsoto raisegoats inthe Algerian desertand even a
few zebu, or humped cattle, further south in Niger. They survive
in the mountains and gravel plains ofthe Hoggar and the Ayr
massif because ofthe presence of occasional oases, waterholes
and wells, andthrough thetenacity of desert acacias and fodder
plants like tullult grass and aramas.3'

Formerlythe Tuareg were raiders; today, many are traders
in camels while others transport salt from Bilma acrossthe
fearfulsands of the Tener£ desert, south ofthe Ayr mountains.
They barter for cereals, tea and cotton cloth, exchangingthem
for goods made of dyed goatskin - camel hide does nottake
tanning. In leather they are skilled workers, but neitherthemen
northewomen weave. Consequently, thetents ofthe Kel Hoggar

Tuareg are covered in membranes of sewn skins, and not of

Thetrailingends ofthe leather are made into tassels for

attach the guy ropes.?

Skin tents amongthe Tuareg may take many forms:
anthropologist Johannes Nicolaisen listed sometthirty support
systems over which the membranes are stretched. Ofthese, a
number consist oftwo or more arches made from tamarisk
roots, placed at right anglesto the long dimension ofthetent,
the ends ofthe membrane being supported by cross-pieces
resting on forked poles. Othertypes are supported bythree or
perhaps five cross-pieces, those inthe centre being higherthan
theends. Alternatively, a central pole is inserted into a notched
ridge-piece to form a ‘T’-member, clearly demonstratingthe
source ofthis system isthe Arabictent. Inthese and allother
methods the curved poles, ridge-piece, or transverse crosspieces spread the load ofthe skin membrane, which is under
tension. Asthe membrane is not attachedtothem there is
norisk of uneven strain, lateral shifting ofthetent in high
winds doingno damage asthey slide overthearchesand
other members. Polesattheedgestakethe guy ropes and
keepthe cover taut.

Daytime temperatures can be high in the desert, with
122°F (50°C) by no means uncommon and underfoottemperatures
reaching 70°C. Yet at night frost is frequent, and snow fallsinthe
mountains. With the sides open forthrough ventilation thetent
can bean effective shade cover, but it is uncomfortablewhen the
sides have to be closed in desert winds and storms. Mats woven
from azefu grass are used as screens aroundthetentsand
rough stone walls are built aswindbreaks. Exposedtotheharsh
extremes ofthe climatethe skins eventually deteriorate: they
are renewed a strip ortwo atatime, from the centre outwards,
the worn outer edge strips beingremoved. Itisa cyclic process,
exactlythesameasthat used bythe Bedouin and Berberwhen
they renew the strips in their goat-hairtents, further confirming
the Arab origin ofthetent.

Amongthe Kel Geres and other Tuareg peoples ofthe
Ayr massif inthe southern Sahara, another form of portable
structure is used-the frame-and-mat type employed by many
peoples ofthe Sudan and East Africa. Nicolaisen identified seven
types offrame, though the most common is made ofthreeor
four hoops and has central arches which are higherthan its
sides.3* Each hoop is made oftwo lengths of acacia root preformed by heatingthem when green and ttyingthem with cords
untilthey dry outtothe desired shape. One length is greaterthan
theotherin each hoop, that atthe front being longerthanthe
rear, sothat when they aretiedtogetherthejoins arenot atthe
crest. Transverse cross-pieces attheends oftheframe are lashed
to corner supports, while long poles are run acrossthe hoops
from endto end ofthestructure and are held in tension by being
tiedtothe frame andthe central arch. Thewhole structure
resemblestthe inverted hullframework of a boat supported on
slender poles. Overthe framework, mats made of plant stems
are secured with cordtothe horizontal cross-pieces and the

Itseemsthatthe Tuareg have been willingto borrow
sheltertypes and structural techniques from other nomads, and
to adaptthemtotheirown needs. Inthe south oftheSahara, the
types meet and, literally, overlap: both forms of dwellingmay be
found in onecamp, and sometimes the frame-and-mat structure
may be covered with a skin tentto keep out summer rains. Rains
however, have not been a problem for several years; ratheritis
shortage of water and hence of forage, that has had a profound
effect on Tuareg culture, and thousands have been forced to
migratetothetowns and refugee camps. Not all Tuaregare
nomads; increasingly, thetribes ofthe southern region have
taken up forms of agriculture and lead a more sedentary life.
Their semi-permanent dwellingtypes include the sturdy but
elementary square frame used by the farmers of Hoggar, the
ekeber, which consists ofthree poles aside, and a higher, central
forked post. Thistakes the burden of radiating rafters which are
lashedto the ‘wall plates’, the horizontal members ofthe wall
frames, over which bundles of bullrushes or hay may be placedto
form both the walls and the roof covering. Other Tuareg huts are
cylindrical in form, with low-pitched thatched roofs; and many
have adopted the dome structure, common among many of
thesavannah and Sahel peoples, of aring of polestiedtogether

permanent dwellings of stone or, underthe influence ofthe
French, of mud brick, while others used conical mud balls to build
the rectangular, flat-roofed house whose form is characteristic
ofthe Hausa of northern Nigeria. Such mud and stone houses
of Tuareg agriculturalists began to appear in settlementsin the
twentieth century, and have started to assume village forms
which reflectthe adjustment of caste and class systems based
on oldnomadictraditions to the more recent farming practices.3

Under the pressures of environmental deterioration and
political change many nomadic peoples have been obligedto
adopt a sedentary life. Sincethe 1950s they have hadto
abandon their pastoral, hunting or food-gathering ways of life
on the margins ofthe habitable world. To our loss, fortheir
exceptional survival skills, and their building techniques using
immediate resources for their portable dwellings, are soon
forgotten in the process of adaptation to alien patterns of
living and unfamiliar house types, especially when these are
imposed upon them. Nevertheless, many formerlynomadic
peoples have become ‘semi-nomadic', or have settled oftheir
own accord, such as the Fulani (Fula, Fulbe or Peul), a people
with a complex history whose migrations from Senegaland
the western Sudan began in thethirteenth century.

Slender and aquiline, the Fulani intermarried with those

with whom they came into contact orwhom they conqueredin
battle, while still retaining their own language. Many had settled
intthe Hausa states but, in the early nineteenth century, when
the Muslim Fulani chief Usuman dan Fodio led a jihad or holy war,
they overthrew the Hausa and assumed power. Other Fulani
groups have remained as nomads, likethe Bororo’en who are
independent cattle-herders; while those likethe Fulbena’iare
semi-sedentary (or semi-nomadic), theyoung men seasonally
migratingwith the herds of cattle or goats for a periodwhen

the grazing permits. Periodically they return tottheir sares, or
compounds, which may enclose within their surrounding fences,
pole-framed, circular-plan huts with wall cladding of woven
straw and domed, thatched roofs, and cylindrical earth houses
roofed with conical pole-and-thatch. After many ofthe dwellings
in one such sar& suffered some damage while its inhabitants
were absent, temporary repairs were madetothe walls with
encircling poles and reeds. Young animals were kept beneath a
shelterinthe centre ofthe sare, andtthe compound was further
protected withaafence.

Among some ofthe eastern Fulani a rulingand professional
class, the Toroobe, had considerable power, becoming able
administrators in Muslim society and exactingtaxes from their
less fortunate brethren, the Fulbessiire. By the eighteenth century,

both groups had established themselves 3,200 kilometres
(about 2,000 miles) from Senegal in the Adamawa and Benue
River region of what is now Cameroon. In 1949-50 avillageinthe
region was visited by a team of Parisian architects from l’Ecole
Nationale Sup£rieure des Beaux-Arts. They surveyed some of
the sares, or compounds, butfound them ratherformless, with
labyrinthine routes between their mud walls. Some ofthe
earthen houses had the square plan and flat roofs in the Islamic
style of northern Nigeria. Atypical sarecomprised anumber of
dwellings, including a reception hut and sometimes another hut
nearby, which had been built by a young unmarried son. Beyond
ascreen wall, an enclosure contained the huts ofthewives
arranged in aloose arcaroundtthecompound headman’s hut,
the fattuude, which was often ofthesquaretype. Granaries, a
kitchen hut, agrinding shelter, astable, awell and, attherear of
each sar&, latrinesand a dunghill, were all recorded bytheteam.3®
B& is another Fulani village, situated between Garoua and
Rei-Baba on the Mayo-Kebbi, atributary ofthe Benue, where
many ofthe Fulbesiireadopted the economy and oftenthe
dwellingtypes oftheindigenous peoples, with the fields around
the sar&producing alternate crops of sorghum and groundnuts.
Around thetime ofthe Beaux-Arts team’s visittotheregion, the
French agricultural economist Rene Dumont visited B&. Henoted
that a plot of manioc (or cassava) was situated closetothe sare,
fenced against goats, and ashort distance away sweet potatoes
were growing in ridged fields, the crop alternated with
groundnuts, sorghum or beans. Further afield onthehill-slopes
rice was growing in hollows amongthe sorghum fields, and below
the sare, nearertotheriver, red millet was planted. Rice was grown
atthe flood limit and quick-growing maize corn, orsorghum
seedlings transplanted from the upper nursery beds weresown

About twenty years later BE was again visited, thistime
by an anthropological archeologist, Nicholas David, who studied
the social structure and living patterns ofthe inhabitants,
making detailed records ofthirty-six ofthefifty-six Fulani
compounds in the village. In 1966 cotton had been introduced as
acash crop; the growing of groundnuts and sorghum continued;
andthe diet was augmented by fishing in the Kebbirriver-which
Dumont did not mention. Acompound, David noted, would have
a minimum oftwo huts, onefor a man, his wife and pre-pubertal
children, and anotherforakkitchen. Butifhetook asecond wife,
she would also require aminimum oftwo huts, and agrinding
hut would also be found wherethere were two wives.
Hearthstones were located insidethe kitchen hutsand in the
open for dry season cooking. Water vessels were sunk intothe
ground, baskets for storing groundnuts were suspended, and
plastered grass granary bins were set on rocksto keep away
rodents. Some larger compounds were agglomerates ofthe sares
oftwo or more friendly or related families, while guest huts and
bachelor huts were also sited in the sares.

When a member ofthe family died, the body would be
interred nearthe guest hut and the deceased’s own hut would
be abandoned. Soon the puddled earth would be used forthe

building of another hut nearby. The roofs ofthe huts were
frequently infested with white ants, which could consume one

Every otheryeara hut within acompound became uninhabitable,
andthe whole saröwould be replaced within aspan offifteen
years. Inthe process, functions often changed. As new huts were
built for new wives, old ones were recycled or used for other
purposes: deteriorating sleeping huts might become a kitchen,
and an old kitchen might be used as a storage hut or animal pen.
Althoughthe overall generalized appearance ofthecompounds
may not seemto have altered sincethe visit ofthe Beaux-Arts
architects or Ren& Dumont, in factthe village of B& as seen by
Nicholas David was a different one in every detail. Change in
this society was physically expressed inthe buildings, and the
presence of bicycles and transistor radios in the village was
evidence of yet further, and different, changes taking place.*°
Apart from what we may learn from these reports about
the Fulani compounds of B&, these three different accounts also
demonstrate how each observer, objective accordingto hisown
discipline, sees the settlement in a different way. Thearchitects,
seekingformal beauty and consistency found the variety of
forms disconcerting and haphazard; they were only marginally
concerned with the reflection ofthe social structure within
them. Tothe agricultural economist, the village demonstrated
atype ofhhorticultural land utilization which was efficient,
though mainly only fertilized by riversilt: he deplored the wasted
potential of ashes and manure in the prestige dunghills and
middens. Forthe anthropologist, the layout ofthecompounds
reflected social custom and family structure, andthe cycle of
labour. Each observer had his own purpose in conductingthe

the useofthecompound plan as research material, thattheir

From these studies we can only ever get an incomplete
picture of B&, and one which reflects the interests ofthe
researchers. But even ifthereismuch that remains untold, we do
still learn something ofthefactors that influence its buildings
and settlement, even ifthis is no less narrow a focus. B& however,
is a specific case. Other Fulani sares share characteristics with its
compounds, but the village has no mirror image. Although many
ofthe general features of climate, land use, economy, social
structure and history may have theirechoes in other societies,
this particular blend of interaction and evolution is uniquely its
own. Itisthis singular nature of settlements within societythat
has so often been overlooked when generalized characteristics
have been used to justify ‘mass housing'’ for diverse peoples- a
subject to which we shall return. And it must be borne in mind
now, as we consider some oftheconstituents, commonalities
and differences between various dwellingtypes and settings. In
theensuing chapters the aspects of dwellingthat are epitomized
by the iglu, the min, the Ehen orthe sare, such as location,
climate, settlement, economy, resources and technology, will
be examined through other examples and cultural case studies,
though regrettably not always atthe same length. They may
show how these various aspects of dwelling are integrated inthe
total context of a way of life within a given environment: how
dwelling as artefact relatesto dwelling as process and experience.
