Past and present

‘They have so used the premises from time when memory is not’
stated a reportto King Richard Il on the condition of farming on
Dartmoor. Six hundred years laterthe ‘commoners’ whofarmthe
“ancient tenements’ ofthe moors still defend their rightsto take
turves, to gatherwood and to pasture their animals. Dartmoor
isa dramatic wilderness plateau in the south-west peninsula of
England, wholly within the county of Devon. With relatively few
trees, its broad furze (gorse) and bracken-clad moors, scattered
with the ‘clitter’ of granite boulders left since the Ice Ages, riseto
heavily weathered ‘tors’, granite outcrops on the round-breasted
hills. It can be dry and parched intthesummer, but in winter
Dartmoor is often fog-bound and wet and subjecttto storms; and
there are signs of many hundreds of Bronze Age settlements and
hut circles which were sited to take advantage of such protection
as the forms ofthemoors could give.!

Inasaddle between two ofthe moors at Houndtoraarethe
remains of a cluster of granite-walled dwellings, which may date
fromthetenth century. They are an early form ofthe ‘longhouse‘,
atthe uphill end of which was the 'livier' or dwellingofthe
farmer and his family, whilethe cattle were winteredin the
‘shippen’ atthe lower end, with a stone-lined channel setinthe
floor so thattthe slurry could drain downhill. Hay was stored in
the ‘tallet‘, or overhead loft, from where it was pitchforked to
the cattle below. Livestock and farm family alike shared a crosspassage which gave access to both partsofthehouseandtothe
pasture, the livier gaining warmth from the heat ofthe animals.?
One-storeyed buildings with roofs of timber frames covered in
thickthatch, longhouses were constructed of granite boulders,
the larger of which were drilled and split into manageable blocks.

Although the Houndtor longhouses are now no morethan
an archeological site, an early form ofthe longhouse still stands

one farm, on thesite of agroup ofthree farmsthat were listed
amongthe thirty-seven tenements in the royal forest ofthe
Duchy of Cornwall as defined in the ‘Perambulation’ of 1240 AD.
Also nearby is athird farmhouse, whose occupiers in the 19705

thetraditional systems oftheirchildhood.> Many afarm hadaan

in the furze-oven. The ash was used to harden floors, to make Iye
soapandto helpfertilizethe soil.

Afew miles away in a hidden valley is Lettaford, where
farms and farmworkers’ houses face on to a plot still known
as ‘the green”. Sanders, one ofthe old longhouses, still has its
shippen almost intact, while the livier retains its fireplace and
bread oven, spanned by an immense granite lintel.*Most
longhouses- and there are over a hundred in the Widdecombe
parish of Dartmoor alone - have had their shippens converted
into further rooms, and some are rented to holiday-makers.
Though a barn on a Dartmoor farm may now house atractor,
its present use reflects atime when stone was abundant for
building ‘linhays’ and barns, while roof timbers had to be brought
in by waggon. The granite walls ofthese longhouses and barns
were sometimes constructed without mortar, and massive
timbers supported the weight of roofs bearingmanytons of
thatch or slate.

## S. 26

46 Settlingdown

Perhaps the most permanent reminders ofthe long history
of moorland settlements are the late Bronze Age and early
medieval field systems that can still be seen, with their long
walls of fieldstone granite which outlinethe strip fieldsandthe
‘newtakes’ claimed from the 'outfield’ ofthe moor. Smallthough
they are, the Dartmoor hamlets illustrate many ofthefactors
that influence settlement, ofwhich the kind of economythatcan
be sustained isfundamental. The capacity ofthelandto support
a populace is often reflected in the density ofthe population,
whilethe nature ofthe farming system is an indication of access
towaterandtothetype and quality ofthe soils. Formerly, the
farms of Dartmoor were largely self-sufficient-the owner of
Sherrill in the 1970s stilltaking pride in making her own butter,
baking her own bread, spinning and weavingthe wool from her
own sheep, and raising a mixed stock of cattle, sheep, horses,
pigs and fowl. The moors are not readily cultivated on alarge
scale and are more suitable for rearing cattleand sheep, but
there was an evident need to diversify farming activities asin the
winter months the hamlets could be isolated.5 As markets were
established, links between settlements became more important;
examination oftheterrain revealsthe communication routes
that gave accessto rural fairsand cattle markets, andtothe grass
‘keep’ ofthe South Hams of west Devon. When these fairs were
taking placeand the police were fully occupied, squatters often
exercisedtheirrightto keep a dwellingon the moor, which was
allowed them ifthey could builditand have afireburninginthe
hearth between sunrise and sunset; at least one such squatter’s
house, Jolly Lane Cot, still stands.? Today, horseboxes, tractors
and lorries have shortened thetime spent in travelling, butthe
narrow lanes still regulate the flow.

We know something ofthe backgroundtothese
settlements because of the long history of official records in

6: The village of Törbel, 7: Old and new houses,

the field plots. Valais,

Britain. Abureaucratictradition of keeping administrative
records, parish registers and official archives can makethe
unravelling ofthe skeins of settlement history possible.
Genealogies, accounts oftithes and taxes, court records of legal
disputes, the entry of births, marriages and deaths orthe sale of
plots and ‘parcels’ of land all contributeto an understanding of
the establishment, growth, stability or decay of acommunity,
especiallywhen augmented with details of methods of
cultivation, production, processing, storing and marketing.
Analysis of demographictrends andthe impact of wars, of
economic pressures, of political and religious power, can also all
be significant in the reconstruction ofthe past ofacommunity.3
Anthropologist Robert Netting used such recordsto
examinethelifeofthe mountain village of Törbel inthe Swiss
Valais, findingtheseto be infinitely fullerin documentationthan
were the oral sources for his previous studies ofthe Kofyar hillfarmers oftheJos plateau in Northern Nigeria. Perchedon a
south-facing Alpine slope at some 1,500 m (about 5,000 ft)
and within sight ofthe Matterhorn, Törbel is protected from
avalanches by larch forests above. It has had a longttradition of
common use of some lands and private ownership of others; and
isatightly-knit, vigilant, perhaps even suspicious community,
with family commitmentto the production of its limited
but healthy diet, and collective participation in the seasonal
transhumance of its cattle. Alternating the fallowing offields,
rotating crops of ryeand potatoes, compostinganimaldung and
gathering forest humus for its nutrients, the farmers overthe
centuries have developed a husbandrytthat is sensitivetothe
specific soil structure and climate of the Alpine settlement.
An irrigation system established as early asthetwelfth century
hasto be maintained in good repair, and farm families use iton
asixteen-day cycleto waterthe vines on thesteep mountain

terraces. Törbel maintained its equilibrium overthe centuries

through judicious farming and effective internal social controls.

But parish records revealed a population expansion inthe late
eighteenth century alongside a decline inthe mortality rate
which, Netting deduced, was the result ofthe beneficial effect
on health and fertility ofthe introduction of potatoestothe
mountain economy.?

Houses and land had been the subject of investment
since thethirteenth century and a document dated 1483,
concerningthe regulation ofland-use, bearsthe names of a
dozen ofthefamilies that still farm there. Records kept of
notarized transactions, and later, bills of sale, meant that from
the 16005 the exchange and ownership of property was well
documented. Young married couples in Törbel had difficulty in
acquiring property unless parental land was made availableto
them orthey gained itthrough the partition of the inheritance
onthe death of afarmer father.

Houses were built of horizontally-laid, notched larch
timbers, under local slate roofs. The earliest, datingfrom the
fifteenth century, are the Heidenhauser-literally ‘heathen’
houses -and these were made of split logs, although later the
baulks were sawn. Speicher, or storehouses, and field granaries
mounted on mushroom-shaped staddle stones to repel rodents
were similarly built. Each house had a Wohnstube, which
combined the functions of livingroom and bedroom, andaa
kitchen with an open hearth. Beneath the house a cellar kept
cheese edible for years and was used in summerfor keeping
meat. Clustered closely together with narrow paths between
them the houses allowed littleroom for expansion, and in large
families trundle beds were used for children who did not share
the parental bed. Because of their sturdy construction andthe
steady state of the settlement many ofthem have remained in

use for centuries. Recentlythough, changes have come to Törbel.
Chalets have been built on the outskirts ofthe village and new
houses have appeared on inherited lands. Many people commute
tothetowns but others, with evident satisfaction in theirway
oflife and pride inthenew amenitiesthatthey have received,
still farm in the village.’

With an emphasis on the ecology ofthe mountain
settlement, Netting depicted the life ofthecommunityin
historical depth, chartingits institutions and delineatingthe
problems and choices for farming families over generations. But
villages have a spatial as well as atemporal dimension; and his
study did not include plans of land holdings, dwellings, church
and workshops, which would have given a fuller picture ofthe
settlement in its location and the processes of change and
growth that influenced its form.

How longtthe site had been permanently settled is
uncertain, but a Celtic origin has been advanced. The seasonal
transhumance (literally ‘across ground’) of cattletothe upper
alp (meadows) for a short, ten-week period inthesummer
during which the hay in the lower alp is cut and stored, suggests
a Neolithic origin, borne out by archeological evidence of
deliberate forest burning, probably by nomadic herders, to
increase summer pasturage." Transhumance survivesasa
practice in other montane parts of Europe, such asinthe
Cevennes, France, where immense flocks ofsheep aretaken to
thehills for summer feeding, orinthe highland landscape of
extinct volcanoes in south-eastern Auvergne. Cow-herders from
the farms in the valley floors bringtheir cattletotherich upland
pastures while the fields about the farms recover. For several
weeks, the herders shelter overnight ina buron: arough but
weather-tight, single-roomed granite building furnished with
bunks, alarge table, astone cooking hearth and candles.

Outside, each animal has a bell suspended from its neck, helping
the location of strays and producing a gentle cacophony of sound
whenthe cows are herded together. Some burons have been
developed as, or replaced by, jasseriesthrough which the cattle
are steered sequentiallyto be milked before being guidedto

new pasture. Separation ofthe milk is begun immediately, the
Jasseries being essentially small cheese-making establishments,
thus bothtthe valleyfarm and its upland counterpart being
productive ."?Such transhumant practices are sometimes regarded
as nomadic survivals, though pragmatic pastoralism may also
account forthem.

In contrastto the agricultural societies who raise crops
andlivestock on farm lands, many of the peoples who dwell near
rivers, lakes orthe sea depend on fish astheir principal source of
food. Though it is an inland plateau, it is possibleto seethesea
from Dartmoor and to look further west tothe neighbouring
peninsula of Cornwall, with its crenellated coastline of bays
and estuaries. Many ofthese are fishing villages, such as Looe,
Polperro, Polruan, Fowey and Mevagissey, which lielessthan
50 kilometres (30 miles) south-west ofthe moors. These villages
were well established by the fourteenth century, when casual
fishing developed as a local industry and the first harbours were
constructed. Polperro, for instance, is situated in a narrow valley
with hills risingto 120 m (about 400 ft) on eitherside ofa
winding stream that flows south-east into a wide ‘pol’ (pool),

Its accesstothe open sea narrowed by a headland, the Peak Rock.
Originally divided between Talland and Llansallos, Polperro was
privately owned by lords ofthe Raphael manor for over six
centuries, until 1831.3 They built and maintainedtthe piers, and
exacted fees from fishermen and traders, whiletheimpoverished
Polperro fishermen engaged extensively in smuggling. Subjected
to devastating storms and floods, including a storm during

which twotthirds ofthefishingfleet was lost, they constructed

a further, defensive pier in the 18605, creatingan outerandan
inner harbour, to a pattern which is echoed in anumber of
Cornish fishing ports. To the south ofthe harbour, Fish Quay was
used for unloadingthe catch, whichthewomen gutted, salted
and stored in hogshead casks in factories opposite.'

Restricted by the narrow valley, the houses ofthe
fishermen were tall and narrow, frequently sharingcommon
walls. Built of flat slabs of local stone, under pitched roofs
covered in slate, they replaced the majority of the earlier grassthatched cob (earth) buildings, anumber of which having
succumbedtothefloods. Many had slate-hung external wallsto
minimize water penetration from the winter gales, while others
were cement-rendered, the texture of the slab courses enhanced
by drawing a trowel or bottle acrossthe renderto producethe
characteristic ‘Polperro ripple”. Partly because ofthe steeply
inclined valley wall, the houses were often entered by flights of
stepsto an ‘orrey’ porch, the irregular space below being used for
the storage offish orfishingtackle. Three storeys in height and
one ortwo small rooms in depth on their shallow sites, thebacks
ofthe houses on the Lansallos side were reached by steep stone
stairways and entered at higher levels. Houses on the north or
Warren side interlock to the water’s edge, with a few narrow
lanes threaded between them."

In 1894, by Act of Parliament, local trustees were
incorporated and empowered through public subscription to
buytherights, andto maintain and regulate the harbour. Today,
however, trawling for mackerel and fishing for pilchard by longline continue on a small scale, and although the quay spaces still
servetheir purpose, these villages have also become favoured
destinations for tourists in the summer season. Despite being
neglected by specialists in British vernacular architecture, fishing

villages like Polperro demonstrate the influential features of

Ifthey are careful notto exploittheir resources, fishing
communities can nurturethe shoals and produce a surplus
which can betraded with peoples inland. It would seem saferto
build on land, and many riverain and lacustrine peoples do; but
there is also a strong incentiveto live overthefish stocks in order
to protectthem. Whole villages, even entiretowns, areto be
found built over water, the dwellings being erected on piles or
‘stilts’ requiring catwalks and jettiesto connectthem. Among
the earliest European settlements of this kind of timber structure
known to archeologists, arethe lake villages of Switzerland.
Covered with water forthousands of years theirtimbers have
remained intact, though exposure to air rapidly destroys them.'
Many lake and coastal communities survive elsewhere inthe
world still, amongthem the many villages andthetown of
Ganvie on Lake Nokwe, Benin, the latter having a population in
excess 0f10,000. Using ingenious methods oftrappingfish, the
Toffinou people ofthis region have been prosperous, thewomen
smokingandtradingthefish caught bythe men with the inland
peoples of Benin (formerly Dahomey). Their rectangular plan
houses are built on stilt frames of mangrove poles, the floors
raised above flood level, and have gabled or hipped roofs made of
palm fronds, over which a thick grass thatch is laid.'® As enduring
is Kampung Ayer in the Brunei lagoons where once there were
pile dwellings of a similar kind, but whose thatched roofs have
now been replaced with corrugated iron. Including a modest
mosque among its many houses, with substantial walkways
between them and with bridges to the shore, Kampung Ayer has
resisted attempts to remove it; instead, aschool has been built

Heretoo, the economy is based on fishing, the inhabitants
selling large quantities on a banjo-plan market pier. Riverain
and lacustrine peoples can be endangered by seasonal flooding.
Coastal peoples andthose on tidal reaches of rivers are also
subjected to considerable daily variations in water levels, and
there are problems caused by the instability of silts and river
mud, and sometimes the undertow of receding tidal waters."
In spite of these problems, all such peoples identify with their
locations, and preferto pursuettheir traditional economies andto
build their dwellings in ways that are appropriatetothe conditions.
Like thenomads andthetranshumant pastoralists, the
lake dwellers challenge our notions of settlement. Between
coasts and uplands may be found, in differing continents and
contexts, themarshes and swamps, the rain forests, jungles and
woodlands, the savannahs, prairies and veldts, the undulating
foothills, andthe hot and cold deserts ofthe world. These are
where the majority oftherural populations dwell in farmsteads,
homesteads, villages and rural townships that may be numbered
not inthousands, but in millions: India alone is reckoned to have
more than half a million villages. Movements of nomads who
settled millennia ago, invading hordes and migrating families
who established their claims to land and water, defended their
territories and established their lineages, determined much
ofthe patterning of settlementsthat can be perceived today.
Beneath the foundations of untold numbers of houses are
thefloors and foundations of their predecessors; tothe
archaeologisttheland isa palimpsest on which can bereadthe
unfolding of man’s settlement history.?° But if generations have
built on ancestral lands, new territories are still being opened up,
virgin forests are still being felled to make new claims upon the
soil. In this review of houses across the world today, reference to

the past history of many dwellings must necessarily be brief;
nonetheless, itshould be recognized as being ofthe greatest
significance in establishing the vernacular traditions by which

Supporting life

Ecological factors profoundly affect population densities;
marshy regions which have been exploited forthe growing of
rice have permitted densities which have reached well overone
thousand people per square kilometre in some parts of rural
Asia; no other cereal enables so large a number of peopleto be
nourished bysosmalla cultivated area. What is often termed
the ‘carrying capacity’ ofthelandto support a population relates
to the form offood production andthe kind of society which
utilizes it. When the maximum population is reached, beyond
which the carrying capacity ofthe lands exceeded, either new
lands must be sought or expanded into, ortheremustbea
change in the intensiveness of the cultivation system. Eventhe
poorest of agricultural lands and the simplest oftoolscan
multiply by asmuch as forty times the number of huntergatherersthe land can sustain - but hunters can survive where
agriculture is not possible.?' The processes of agricultural and
technological change do not necessitate a corresponding change
in settlement, but'they certainly contribute.

Some ofthe examples discussed so far can be considered
to be ‘upland’ settlements, others being both inland and coastal;
the latter by definition, can be described as ‘\owland”. It has been
shown by the Polish geographer Staszewski, thatthe bulk ofthe
world’s population occupies lowland territories- perhaps fifteen
per cent live at an altitude above 700 m (about 2,270 ft) and over
half live below 200 m (about 650 ft). The factors that influence
whether communities live in high or low altitudes are numerous:

in much of South America it is healthierto livein mountain
regions wheretheairisclean and free ofinfestation and
pollution. In Colombia and Ecuador, eight out often peoplelive
in the Andes; in Bolivia, three-quarters ofthe population live
above 3,000 m (10,000 ft).?? Altitude and latitude interact, and
in northern latitudes more people live in the lowlands. Again,

it has been noted that the greatest densities ofthe world’s
population live closetothe sea, with three-quarters ofthe cities
of overfour million inhabitants being located on sea or lake
shores, and most oftherest by principal rivers.3 As yet, the world’s
population is farfrom wholly concentrated in these cities,
around half still being rural.

The economics of settlements relatetotime-distance
factors which affecttthe ability of a population to work its lands.
Those crops that demand constant attention orare required
most are closesttothe settlement, thosethat can be maintained
with less intensive use oflabourandtime are further away; and
atthefarthest horizonsthe land may be unexploited.?# Territorial
boundaries are frequentlythe result ofthe meeting of lands
under production; and when allthe available land has been
occupied, production increases to meet an expanding population
with more intensive agriculture. Acquisition by force of adjacent
lands has often been the most direct, if least peaceable, solution
to the problems of land and population pressures; hence defence
oftheterritory may be the most significant factor in settlement
organization and location. Frequently the boundaries of an agreed
territory are defined by topographical features - such as mountain
ridges, rivers or shorelines. But in thinly-populated forest regions
thetreeline edge itself may bethe boundary, the forest dwellers
apprehensive ofthose whose territory lies in the plains.?

There are manifold reasons why villages have formed, and
security is undoubtedly one. Isolated homesteads are vulnerable

to marauders, and the need for protection alongwiththeneed
to ensure the family line into future generations are powerful
imperatives for joining others. There is safety innumbers, but
there is a narrowing of outlook too; many villagers are secure

in their group but suspicious of, and hostileto, outsiders. An
Amazonian people, the Yanomamö (or Yanoama, Yanomami)

of southern Venezuela and north-west Brazil are a good example
ofthis and have been the subject of numerous ethnographic
studies.?°They have been described as both ‘fierceand
aggressive‘, and as ‘gentle.. and proud’; and their apparent
cruelty or indifference to suffering, including their own, has
attracted much attention. With no recorded history, they have
been subjected since 1960 to sociobiological research which has
attemptedto trace their origins. Anumber of genetic, physical
and linguistic features suggest that they may beamongthe
oldest of indigenous peoples in South America, whose way of life
may have changed little forfivethousand years.? Livinginthe
“low humid highlands’ of Venezuela, oralongthe Orinoco and
Rio Negro, their overallterritory isimmense, though theirtotal
numbers may only bein the region of 10,000.

Yanomamölive in teri, or household clusters of extended
family and kin groups, who share the same name, which will
have been derived from natural features or creatures. Each
household cluster dwells in a shabono, aterm which refers
tothe cleared centre of asite, but which is also used by
anthropologists asthe name ofthe collective structure itself,
which is approximately circular and about 100 ft (30 m) across.
Yanomamö hunting bands also construct temporary monopitch
(so-called ‘lean-to‘) shelters loosely arranged inaring;andthe
characteristic yahiform may derive from this, as each household
builds its own unit, adjacent to its neighbours, which may be
focused on one or more hearths. Afew Yanomamö groups inthe

Indians are hostileto

household within their

Orinoco and Tio Parima regions build a circular maloca form
under a single roof with acentral opening, suggesting an
alternative origin to the shabono. Links with other teri are
established for marital and security reasons, allies being
necessary when warfare occurs, andthus a ‘molecular’ settlement
pattern of connections is created, althoughitisonethatis
subject to change.?®

An average shabono may cover a score of hearths,
sheltering a total of seventy people. Construction varies, but
typically a pair of short polesattherearanda couple offrontal
poles provide the basic support for aroofframe. Obtainingand
raising the pole frame is done by the men, whilethewomen
collect andtiethe palm leaf thatch. Working from the lowest
level upwards, the palm leaves are attached to lianasthatrunthe
length of aring of shelters, each successive layer being partially
laid over its predecessor, to produce a weather-resistant roof
with afringe atthe leading edge. Some groups construct crossridges creating a double pitch; while others use a frame over
which long poles are cantilevered, to produce a steep pitch and
a very high front fringe. Other variants have also been recorded.
Thatch covering a shelter may be overlapped with that ofthe
neighbours, closing gaps and givingthe impression thatthe
shabono is one continuous structure. Domestic spaces may be
divided or left without internal separating walls. Supporting hut
poles are used for the suspension of hammocks, which may be
made of cotton thread or of bast strips. There are no surplus
items of equipment, and baskets and water gourds for specific
purposes are kept nearthe household hearth. Afence of poles
collected for firewood provides a back wall, and beyond this a
defensive pallisade may be erected around the whole shabono, all
within a clearing large enough to embrace the garden plots and
to expose any marauders.?

## S. 29

As both hunter-gatherers and horticulturaliststhe
Yanomamö have a mixed economy, their chosen sites for semipermanent occupation needing to support garden plotsforthe
growing of staples such as plantains and manioc. Maize, papaya,
bottle gourds, cotton andttobacco are also grown, as well as
crops such astomatos and beans which are used for barter.

No Yanomamö are specialists: each man makes his own long
hunting bow and arrows, and builds his own unit. He will have
opportunities to develop his skills, as the fission (or cleavage) of
a teri, orthe fusion of kin-related groups to form a new terilead
tothe building of new complexes. Infestation by cockroaches, or
lice and fleas harboured in the roofs will also forcethem to move
and rebuild a shabono within reach oftheir garden plots. The
decision to moveisa collective, if frequently disputed, one, for
there are no hereditary chiefs and no hierarchicalttiers, though
leaders may appear temporarily.3° It isevidentthatthe need for
protection and security is significant inthe Yanomamö's choice
of sites and in the preparation oftheforest clearingsandthe
erection of palisades. In all respects, from their social structure
to theircombination of hunting, gathering and skilfulfarming,
from their single shelters to the collective ofthe shabono, from
their site selection to their frequent moves, from their distant
contacts with kin to their plan-defying dispersal, the Yanoma
bringtheories of dwelling and settlement into question.

Defence has been a major factor in the siting of countless
settlements, in all parts ofthe world, including for instance, the
pre-Saharan southern slopes ofthe High Atlas. The landscape is
desolate, rock-strewn and arid, but it is broken by the Valley of
the Draa, which provides a fertile if unreliable strip which waters

the palm groves. Alongthe Draa are the settlements of both
Berbers and Arabs. Formerly under frequent attack from other
groups and from nomadictribes, they defended themselves in

## S. 30

walled towns or ksour. Comprising an agglomeration of densely
packed, ochre-hued earth and stone houses, sometimes several
storeys in height, these towns are interlaced with narrow lanes
and culs-de-sac which reach the dwellings by circuitous routes.
Arepresentative ksourmay have as its key nuclei a mosque, an
ancient soug or market-place, a kasba, ceremonial dance space,
shops and wells. Such a walled, nucleated settlement has no
room for expansion within its boundaries, so, as has also

been the case with many walled towns in Europe in times of
comparative peace, further markets and dwellings have
sometimes been built beyond the walls.3'

Ethnic minorities and other defined cultures may have
little defence against rivalry for resources, the exercise of power
through territorial claims, and conflicts of interest and religious
belief of dominant societies, which have forced the diaspora and
the relocation of countless communities throughout the world.
Inthe hilly central Black Sea region of Turkey isthe small,
nucleated village of Akyazi, where a number of lanes converge on
an open plaza, with asimple, austere mosqueto oneside. Inthis
respect it is not greatly dissimilar from other villages of similar

IL

scale in the region. Many of these others, however, have timberframed buildings with adobe infill, pitched roofs and, frequently,
symmetrical arrangements about a central doorway. While in
Akyazithe houses are quite different, being constructed on
massive posts driven deep into the ground. These posts support
the living quarters and define a large cellar area below in which
heavy domestic work is carried out and wherethe animals are
kept in winter. Most noticeable of the features of atypical Akyazi
house isthe extended structure before the domestic quarters,
and above part ofthe cellar, which forms a deep verandah. This
may be covered with mud and grass, and in some cases byathin
layer of cement. Here fruits and berries are laid outto dry, and
rugs arehungtoairin thesun. Sometimesthere may beagate
to one side ofthe house, which opens on to a houseyard where
chickens and dogs vie forfood. Access to the dwelling is by
wooden steps within the houseyard, andthese leadtothe
verandah. Here a visitor is welcomed with refreshments, forthis
isa culture where hospitality is customary. As small farmers
with many animals intheimmediate vicinity ofthe house, the
approaches and houseyards may be dusty or muddy accordingto

the season, but within the houses, which arethe domain ofthe
women, therooms, furniture and utensils are meticulously
clean. Another distinct feature isthat chairs, some upholstered,
are placed around the main living space, ratherthan the sedir,

or continuous and encompassing boxed settle, which is common
in Turkish rural homes.3?

Much more could be said of these houses, their
outbuildings, stables, apiaries, and even the flimsy bridgesthat.
run between the buildings, all constructed by the village men,
who are experienced in such building. Similarly, the settlement
pattern itself could be analysedfor its function, structureand
familial connections. Butitisthe residents’ pride in their
Georgian ancestry, their defence oftheir culture and reluctance
to relinquish the roots oftheir past, andthe slow, century-old
process of adjustment, which includes the adoption ofthe
Turkish language, which are significant here. For Akyaziisa
village that was built by migrants from Georgia inthe 18805, a
score of years afterthe Russians had defeated the Ottomans
and reinstated Christianity there. Its houses remain Georgian
in design, construction and use, and though four orfive
generations have passed since its birth, its present builders cling
tenaciously to their tradition, while erecting dwellings that are
still very varied within its norms.3

When speaking of the history of cultures and their
settlements, of uncovered artefacts and signs of ways of living it
is easy to ignore the passage of successive lifetimes that mere
dates represent. Particularly, we may simply failto recognize
thatthe dwelling is often the most enduring signifier ofthe
persistence of traditional values, as wellas a record of adaptation
to changing environmental and social circumstances.

Intheflat delta lands ofthe Sacramento River, California,
isthe small riverside town of Locke. It was built by Chinese

the needs of miners in the Sierras, was burnt down in 1915. As
Chinese were forbidden to own land the site was leased from
George Locke, afarmer who had a levee and pier on therriverfront
nearby. The land lay below the levee, necessitating raised
walkways to the upperfloors ofthe dwellings facingthe river

behind them. Alleys linked the parallel streets. While it has been
claimed that in this respect the layout was in accordance with
Chinese tradition, there is little evidence of feng shuiprinciples

Locke gives no immediate impression of aChinese
settlement, but in the engaging, if deterioratingtown, at least
one house, that built by Sam Lee around 1918, has a deep
plan which largely corresponds with the plans of houses in
Chuangdong. It has a large dormitory-like room which could
have served as a bunkhouse for Chinese labourers, forthe
occupants were bachelors, wives and families having remained
in China. In a succession of rooms on either side of acorridor, the
plan takes the deep linear form sometimes referredto in China
asthe‘bamboo tube’ house. Allthe houses were framed of wood,
some with gable fronts and lapped boarding, or batten-andboard construction, with balconies overthe covered sidewalks
in the tradition of Californian smalltowns. Once Locke was
flourishing but today it has fewerthan a hundred inhabitants,

## S. 31

though it remains asthe only complete surviving Chinese-
American ruralcommunity.

From such immigrant settlements as Akyaziand Locke
we may learn not to make swiftjudgements or evaluations
based on appearances. We may notethe importance of cultural
identity, social change, land utilization, economy and
settlement pattern and gain a fuller understanding of some
ofthe factors that might affect their physical organization.
However, questions still ariseasto the antiquity of many
established vernacular dwellings and settlements: how old are
their present forms? Which arethe earliesttypesand how can
they be identified? What evidence exists of their historywhen
no documentation can betraced? All are questions for which
there are no immediate answers, and which exercisethe minds
and interests of many students ofthe subject.

Clearly, the transition from nomadism to seminomadism or directly to sedentarism has been important in
the consolidation of many cultures, as has diaspora, orthe
dispersalofthemembers of a cultureamongthecommunities
of another, whether enforced, induced or voluntary. Targeted
migration involvingthe deliberate and voluntary resettlement
of communities, large or small, for anticipated economic or
otherimprovement, is no less important. But inthe pursuit of
documentation and in the recording of measurement and detail,
failure to consider the complexity of motivations and processes
of settlement such as these is by no means uncommon.

Settlementtypes

Humankind is social, and the needto beinthecompany of others
extends beyond safety and self-preservation. Companionship
and competition, argument and appreciation, the sharing of
problems and thegiving of advice, and discussion and dispute

about issues of mutual concern are essentialamong all social
groups. But a broadening of local knowledge and experience, the
news of events, the exchange of ideas and information andthe
expansion ofthe intellect andthe heart are made more possible
inthe social life ofthe village and smalltownship. Economically,
the advantage of barter, exchange and trade, the opportunity

to sell produce and to purchase, are incentivesto usethe village
and, for many, to be a part ofit. The village, likethe hamlet orthe
farmstead, has an economic base as its raison d’£tre. But, asinso
much related to dwelling, itisthe interaction of many elements
andthe dominance in specific circumstances of some over
others-the.advantageous site, the market, the feudal manor,
thesacred ground -which may account forthe quality, shape
and, ultimately, the size of the settlement.

While there is a clear relationship of economyto
settlement, the identification of settlement patterns is frequently
imprecise. Aksaris usually referredto as atown, though in size
and in the numbers who live within them, many ksourare ofthe
scale of villages. The shabonois customarilyidentifiedasa
village, though in size it is equivalentto a hamlet. A‘dispersed
settlement’ may be considered as elementaryandasurvivalof
atype which has existed since the beginning of human
sedentarism. Yet in contemporary Central America the dwellings
ofthe campesinos, thetenant farmers and peasants, are often
dispersed. Ontheslopes ofthe coffee plantations of Costa Rica
thesimple one ortwo-roomed houses, manywith a cantilevered
sinkattherear, nestleinthe deep green, densely planted coffee
bushes. Below, in the lush rain forest on the shores ofthe
Caribbean beyond Limön, neat wooden houses inhabited by
people of African and slave descent, are raised offthe ground,
some with thatched roofs, some under corrugated iron sheeting,
and many, brightly painted and bedecked with flowers growing

Ahuddle of houses brought together for mutual support,
the hamlet is often occupied by people with close kinship ties.
Many have no focus beyond a well, abend intheroad, orsome
other small but, fortherresident, distinguishing feature. Some
may have ashrine orchapel, and the size ofthe settlement might
range from two orthreeto.adozen habitations in which as
many asa hundred people may live. There is no easily definable
transition from hamletto village; the distinction is blurred and
evident more in the nature ofthe scale and complexity ofthe
relationships between members ofthecommunity andthe
world beyond, than in any numerical definition. Numbers of
inhabitants are likewise no reliable index to the nature ofthe
village or smalltownship: to the Scott Committee, which reported
on land utilization in rural Britain, any ‘compact grouping of over
1,500 people constituted atown’, whilethe geographer Dovring
notedthatthe median size of villages was above athousand in
the Balkans, and in excess of 5,000 in Italy and Spain.3 Moreover,
populations are not constant; villages grow intotowns, others
decline with the migration oftheir young peopleto the cities.
Therefore it must betheir rural situation andtheir dependence
onaliving from the land, lake or sea as much astheir scalethat
distinguishes villages from the manufacturing and marketing
economies oftowns and citiestoday.

Nucleated settlements generally require a sufficently
broad and uninterrupted area for their expansion, but linear
settlements may follow atopographical feature which defines or
inhibits their growth in depth -ahill ridge for instance, or a valley
stream. Generally they have developed alonga communication
route, dwellings on either side benefiting by their access to
passing trade. Villages ofthe Asante (Ashanti) of Ghana, however,

are not constrained by landscape features but are nevertheless
linear, traditionally sited alongthe main street, the abonten,
which runs either north-south or east-west. Even inthe minor
villages the abonten can be over 100 ft (30 m) wide and many
are far wider, with drainage channels alongthe sides and
acambertothered surface ofthelaterite road. The house ofthe
chief, the ahemfie, with the Queen Mother’s house and courtto
its left, may face a central clearing with large shadettrees, the site
fortraditional ceremonials and village meetings. Further along
the street is situated the abosomfie, or ‘fetish house‘, theshrine
where the priest dancesto summon theancestorstothe beat
ofthe paired ntumpan ‘talking’ drums. Between these principal
structures and paralleltothe road, are clustered the houses of
the villagers, grouped accordingto their clans. Often these are
single rectangular houses, but most have additional paito, or
open-fronted rooms, arranged around the gyasse, or courtyard.
Formerlythe houses were of wattle, plastered with clay, and
often whitewashed, but solid ‘swish’ walls of packed earth,
laid in ‘lifts’ of halfa metre (about 2ft), arenow customary and
theformerthatched roofs are covered with the ubiquitous
corrugated iron. Traditionally, all Asante houses were singlestorey, but some chiefs’ houses are now two-storeyed.3°

Onthe edge oftheforest settlement asmallshop,
open market stalls and a simple schoolhouse may be found.
Surroundingtthe village are forest clearings prepared bythe
men, where cassava, maize and vegetables may be planted bythe
women members ofthese matrilineal clans, and rotation cropping
is customary. To aconsiderable extent the forest is farmed for
plantains and cocoa, for local use as well as forthe market; and
these trees, interspersed with giant cottonwoods, also provide
the cover that protectsthe exposed laterite soil from hardening
inthe sun. Some villages may be dedicated to the production of a

particular commodity, such asthe making of large coil pots from
local clay, coloured with ared slip and subject to lowtemperature
firing. Orthe village may specialize intheweaving ofthe
symbolically patterned stripsto makethe kentecloth ofthe
traditionaltoga, orthe printing of motifs on adinkira cloth. Such
specialization ensures regular trade atthe markets of Kumasi,
Tamale or other larger, nodal towns.7

In many linear villages problems arisewhen the round trip
to basic amenities becomes inconvenient, orthe participation of
the inhabitants in village social life is restricted by their relative
isolation attthe limits of the settlement. Underthese
circumstancesthere isatendencyto develop parallel secondary
and tertiary streets, cross-linkedto the main communication
route. The resultant grid settlement, with its checkerboard
arrangement of dwellings, is characteristic ofthe larger Asante
villages. Among some societiesthe grid plan isatraditional form,
but itis.also frequently generated by the focus of some forms on
acommunal open space, the main square or plaza, forinstance,
of Spanish colonial origin. The Laws of the Indies, which
prescribed uniformidad en todo indiscriminately applied
throughout Central and South America, forbadethe building
of Native American villages to their own patterns, but imposed
arigid conventional design, which included a plaza mayor or
squarewith achurch or cathedral on oneside, andthe cabildo, or
administration building, opposite it. Houses of colonial families
facedthe square and the native buildings were constructed in
rows to agrid pattern behindthem, and were occasionally built
in a style compatible with a former tradition.3®

Ollantaytambo in the Sacred Valley ofthe Inkas has long
been regarded as one ofthe most important ofthe pre-Columbian
Inka sites in Peru. Situated on aledge abovethevalleyand
approached by a steep flights of steps, thetemple site is famous

forthe scale ofthe blocks employed in its construction and

working the stone. Less attention is paidto the present

village of Ollantaytambo that lies below it, on theeast side of
the confluence ofthe Urubamba River and itstributary, the
Patakancha. This village has a stricttrapezoidal grid plan, with
seven short lanes crossingthe four main streets. In former
times regular water supply ran through the village in stone
channels constructed by the Inka, which still surviveand
function. The simply dressed stone walls ofthe Inka period also
remain in much ofthe village, and many houses of rubble stone
oradobe have been built bythe indigenous Quechua onthe
foundations that they provided. Inka houses inthetown were
placed around open kanchas or courtyards, generallyfourto
each group, andacommon cooking area was placed ina corner
ofthecourt. Akancha and its houses may have traditionally
accommodated an extended family, as isthecasetoday inthe
Quechua houses that followtthese traditional patterns. Some
houses still havethatched roofs, and others are occasionally
constructed in adobe blocks and quincha cane.3 Atthe focus of
thevillage, closeto theriver, isthe Plaza Manyaraqui, evidence

oftheInka plan being readily adapted tothe proscriptions ofthe
Spanish Conquistadores. Inthis instancethe ancient and later
settlement patterns proved to be compatible, very possibly
because ofthe continuity oftraditions. Yet it may also be noted
that grid plans are most common in highly structured military,
colonial or conquest situations, wherethe control ofthe
populace and the facilitation ofthe movement of armed forces
are their prime function.

Frequently advocated for modern housing developments,
onthe economic grounds of efficient servicing, thegrid plan can
beintotal disagreement with the ‘organic’ growth of villages.
For villages may evolve in accordancewiththetopography ofthe
region and access to water and other resources, together with
the spatial requirements of communities and families, andthe
nature ofthe land utilization which definestheir economy.
Spatial and demographic aspects of settlement arethe province
ofeconomic and population geographers who have much to
say on these subjects, It is argued by exponents ofthetheory
ofthelocation of ‘central’ places, that settlerstend to dispose
themselves regularly over new and unbroken terrain. As land is
cleared for cultivation and utlilization expands concentrically,

the cultivated areas come in contact with each other, closepacked like hexagons over the field and hamlets may be formed.
With the establishment of hamlets and the making of roads,
trading begins between centres. Enterprising settlements exploit
thetrade potentialto produce a profit, expandingto become
villages that serve hamlets andthen towns that serve villages,
ahhierarchy oftrading centres being established.#° However, there
areso many variables related to terrain, climate, life support,
ecology, resources andtechnology, quite apart from those
related to belief systems, values and the aspirations of specific
cultures, thatthetenets of central place theory are inevitably
compromised. It seemsto assume an abstract ‘level playing field’
where competition for resources and territorial claims on land,
orthe use oft, are not applicable.

Argumentsthat have been made for a physicaland
environmental determinism, have been rebutted by those who
argue that culture determines building and settlement.# There
ismuch evidence to show that allthese factors are applicable,
as we have seen intheexamples above, andthat no single factor
determines built form. Traditional theories of land utilization
and settlement location have been enlarged by the study of

ecosystems and human ecology. Inturn, allthese factors have
considerable bearing upon social organization, andthe study
of social systems, therefore inevitably overlappingand
interweaving with other current trends in anthropology, and
with planning theory. Important asthese subjects are, they are
specialized inthemselves, and complex in their ramifications
upon each other. Inthese brief observations on the form ofthe
hamlet, village ortownship, insofar asthese can be defined,
broad generalizations have hadto be made here, which could
be extensiyely modified in many contexts and contradicted in
others. History, topography, resources, economy, technology,
social structure, security, opportunityand communications

all play their part. Such isthe diversity of settlementtypesand
locations that although some morphological similarities may be
identified which invite classification, the balance and relative
prominence ofthe multiplicity of factors which influence man’s
settlements and dwellings in indigenous contexts, are unique
to their specific circumstances.

Classifying buildings
“The poor people live in typical mud huts with grassthatch roofs’
-such a description is repeated countlesstimes inthe accounts
oftelevision, radio and newspaper reporters. Butthey arenotthe
only observersto make generalized statements concerningthe
buildings of a community: summaries of a building form as ‘of
the bee-hivetype’ (using an anachronistic comparison) abound
intravelers’ and anthropologists’ writings. That the buildings of
adjacent villages may differfrom one another is frequently not
perceived, or notthought to be significant when it is. Instead,
they arecommonly assumedto be of atype. Any discussion
relatedtto vernacular architecture however, even ifitis narrowed
to dwellings and excludes other structures bethey wash-houses,
wine-cellars or watermills, rapidly involves referencestotypes.
Inordertocommunicate and to impart orexchange
information we havetto classify, but for discussion to progress
we have to identifythe common characteristic(s) that enable
architectural phemomena to be considered in specific classes
or sub-classes. Reference to vernaculartraditions on a global
basis must involve terms that attempt to differentiate without
being ambiguous: the Dartmoor longhouse, for example, is a
very different kind of building from the longhouse of Borneo;
andthe sacred dairy ofthe Toda in southern India, has onlythe
production of milkin common with the dairy on aDutch farm.
Thus we see how confusion might arise as a result of loose
terminology, and from the use of atypology based solely
on the functions of buildings. The pursuit of typologies in
vernacular architecture is often driven by the desiretto identify
every variant within anamed class; and the classification of
buildings by function -theatre, airport, bus shelter, village
hall- is stillthe system most often used in everyday speech.
Among architects, buildings are often classified usingthe

Previous pages: Alarge
minka, or farmhouse,
ofthegasshotypein

terminology of plan-types, elevation-types, form-types,
section-types and structural-types.

Typological distinctions that may be made of domes or
tents have already been noted and we have seen how these apply
tothe traditional dwellings ofthe Inuit orthe Kel Geres. Plantypes are frequently discussed in architectural discourse, but
without other reference they are often of limited value in
vernacular architecture. Forexample, the circlemay constitute a
plan-type, butthe Inuit igluandthe Mbuti hut share a circular
plan, as dothe cylindrical dwellings ofthe Fulani; they are very
different in form, and also differ structurally, being variousiy
built of snow, saplings and earth. Notthat we should minimize
the difficulties in drawing up any typology of buildings, which
may vary considerably over quite a small area. Guinea-Bissau for
instance, is asmall West African state of lessthan 36,000 square
kilometres (about 14,000 square miles) with a population in 1974
of half a million. A Swedish team identified six major ‘formtypes’ of houses within the state, one of which, the ‘planta baixa
circular asthey termed it, alone having five sub-types within it.
They identified nine major planttypes and several section
variations in theirtypology, but their extensive illustrations
and individual plans of houses revealthatthese categories
compromised the many subtle variations of plan, section and
form, made more complex by a diversity of materials and details,
which wereto be found even within a single village.!

In view of this, one may well ask whetherthere are any
generic plan orform types? It seems that circular plan dwellings
may qualify, as circular remains have been discovered that may
be many thousands of years old. There issome evidencethat
buildings that consisted entirely of aconical roof may have been
widespread, andthe development oftheraising of cones onto
low walls would have given more living space. Similarly, forked

branches could have been usedtotakethe weight of aroof

oftheearliest citiesthat have been identified, from the Early
Dynastic in Egyptto Mohenjo-daro and Harappa inthe Indus
valley, did consist of houses with square or rectangular plans.
Moreover, inthe Harappan villages, circular plan structures have
been identified as granaries.? Although the subject requires
more research, there is evidence, which continuestothe present,
that circular plan buildings may be replaced bythose with a
rectangular plan, butthe reverse seems nottto occur.
Archeologists may continueto uncover remainsthat could
confirm certain generictypes of plan, structure and form.

Many vernacular traditions make use of ‘primary forms’
includingthe hemisphere, cone, cylinder and cube, or sections,
divisions and multiples of some of these, such asthe semicylindrical vault andthe double-cube with a triangular prism
roof.3 Perhaps we respond tothese forms intuitively, with
reactions induced by the countless millennia of the evolution of
humankind. It is possiblethatthe appeal ofthesymmetricthat
isto be perceived in many vernacular forms may echo tthe basic
symmetry ofthe human face, limbs and torso, whilethe subtle
asymmetry and inflections of anorm which are evident in
countless dwellings, may parallel the singularity of every
individual within each culture.

The diversity of plans, elevations, forms, structures and
details used in vernacular dwellings world-wide, areto be
witnessed in a remarkable variety of combinations. When they
are perceived and considered collectively as occupying and
enclosing space to meet the dwelling needs of specific cultures,
thecharacteristics and qualities of one tradition compared with
those of others become more apparent. These combinations are

frequently referred to as representing a ‘style, theterm usedto
identify the conjunction of features which are associated with
a particular people, tradition or period. Frequently the style is
distinguished by location; forexample some ofthe various
‘Allemanic’ or German regional methods of timber-frame
construction are known asthe Niedersachsen, Swabian,
Franconian and Schwarzwald styles. Orthe stylemay be defined
byaspecific detail-the ground floor external framing ofthe
Bohemian umgebindehäus for instance; orthe brick-walled
Frisian Gulfhaus with its powerful internal structure oftimber
posts and central corn store.4

In general usage the concept of ‘style‘, whether it refers
to rococco architecture orthecoachwork ofacar, isone of
applied forms, shapes or decorative details, in which fashion
is a significant factor. Mention is sometimes made of housing
designs in a ‘local style’, which approximates tothe appearance
of buildings in aregion, or even of architects working in ‘the
vernacular style’ with no reference to place, let aloneto cultural
traditions. Even ifitisacceptedtthatthe concept of ‘type’ is
abstract, that of ‘style’ is cosmetic. The question still remains,
however, asto how and why local and regional traditions have
occurred, and what distinguishes their particular character. The
Japanese term minka literally means ‘houses ofthe people’, but
itwas customarily applied to the large, timber-framed, basically
rectangular-plan farmhouses, which usually had a single storey
beneath a gabled or hipped thatched roof. These farmhouses
were once to be seen throughout the main islands of Hokkaido,
Honshu, Shikoku and Kyushu, and as might be expected there
were many regionalforms, which respondedtothe climate,
environment and economic requirements of their respective
areas. Thus, for example, the magariyahad an L-shaped planto
accommodate the stables in the wintry horse-breeding region

of Tohuku, whilethe nagari-tsukihouse which also had a small

At onetimethere were some 1,800 minka in what is
known as the ‘gassho-zukuri’ (‘praying hands’ style) ofthe
mountainous Gokayama and Shirakawa-go districts ofthe Gifu
and Toyama Prefectures. Clustered in anumber of villages on
terraced plateau sites flankingthe Sho river, gassho-zukuri houses
are notablefor their impressively high, steep roofs embracing
three orfour levels abovethe ground floorto accommodatethe
raising of silk-worms. The basic house structure consists of a
rectangular plan with side-wall post-and-beam frames. These
takethe curved ends of heavy cross-beams, the chona-bari,
which are further supported by a post-row carryinga central
ushi-baribeam.s

While the house frame construction was usually done
by specialist carpenters inthe region, the village residents
assembled the sasu roof structure. The massive, steep rooftakes
theform ofarudimentarytruss, the gasshoshape being created
by pairs of principal rafters, crossed and lashed attheridge, with
their pointed ends inserted and wedged intothe chona-bari, and
further stabilized with a purlin. Hagai, long diagonal pole braces,
were usedto counteract the longitudinal wind loads, and also
helped support the ridge pole; while short braces inthe roof
plane heldthe ends oftie beams which supported the light
attic floors. Most beams were secured by square and diagonal
lashings ofthick neso cord, which permitted some flexibility
inthe structure under seismic loads. A mutual system, yul,
involving villagers from all households, provided the necessary

Itwas in the attics, which were uninterrupted by any
vertical members, that the silkworms were nurtured on large
trays carrying rows of tripods wheretheworms fed on chopped
mulberry leaves. They were not domestic spaces; headroom was
lowandtheywere reached by ladders and hatches. The gable
ends ofthe house were fenestrated with amado shutters and
shojiscreens at each level, to providethe necessary light, cross
ventilation and even temperature required forthe raising of
silkworms.? So important was sericulturetothe people ofthe
Gokayama and Shirakawa-go areas in providing silk as a source of
income, that young women were required to devote alltheirtime
to the intensive production oftheworms and their cocoons.
Families were reluctant to permit their daughtersto leave home
ifthey married, and husbands visited them while being obliged
to remain with their own parents, providing the labourto remove
the eaves-height winter snows. The system generated large
extended families.?

Onthe domestic levelthe madori, or ground plan,
consisted ofthe earth-floored niwawhich containedthefurnace,
andtheraised, wood-floored yuka with the traditional fire-pit
andthe sliding screens which created four rooms. Onlyinthe
1960, when nylon and other synthetic fibres became universally
employed, didthesilk industry ofthe Gifu and Toyama prefectures
cometoan end. Small-scalefarming continued, but itwas
constrained by the narrow valley. Hundreds of gassho-zukuri
were dismantled, andtoday fewer than 150 of them survive.

In 1995, three of the ‘gassho-style’ villages were inscribed inthe
World Heritage List and they remain to be appreciated, having
been ‘recognized as cultural heritage of clear universal value’.
Whereverthey occurin the world, being built as essential
constructions within livingtraditions, or as part of asurvivingand
functioning inheritance from the past, vernacular dwellings areto
be appreciated.This can be a subjective and aesthetic experience,
such as is enjoyed in the gaze of untold millions oftourists. But
they are more valuably considered in relationtothe resources
employed in construction, thetechnologies availableto execute
thework, the environmental behaviour ofthe buildings, and
their appropriateness to their function. Most importantly, they
need to be understood in relation to the social norms, domestic
priorities, values and skillstransmitted over generations bythe
cultures that builtthem, rather than in romanticimages oftravel
brochures, superficial descriptions of style, or decontextualized
abstractions of types within ataxonomy ofthe vernacular, or
even as artefacts in an open-air museum or heritage site.

Physical fitness

All buildings, whatever theirfunction, haveto meet certain
physical constraints. Whetherthey arethe outcome ofalong
tradition of received techniques, assembled by trial, error, and
experimentation, or based on detailed mathematical calculations
andthe application of formulae, ultimately the basic laws of
physics will determine whether they will stand up or collapse.

If, for example, afluted marble column in Athens stands on a
carved marble base, and a house post in New Guinea stands on
alump of stone, the base in each case exerts an upward thrust.
equaltothetotal load on the post or column. Or again, though

a solid beam may be made of oak, steel or reinforced concrete,

if all its dimensions are trebled it will bethreetimes as long-but

6: Shear failure caused
bylack ofjointingand
of diagonal bracing of
the wall frame, the

totheroof,andby

the wall with polesand

the roof thrust. Swahili
house, Lamu, Kenya

it will betwenty-seven times greater in volume, weight and
strength. In other words, no matter how simple or sophisticated
a building may be, itissubjecttothe same physical laws.
Understandingthese principlesthat affect the ‘structure’ of

a building, and the means and methods of assembly ofthe
materials employed in its ‘construction’, enables usto further
appreciate the achievements of indigenous builders. It can also
help usto recognize why some buildings may lurch or collapse
through structural weakness or failure, and understand how and
why building methods and materials may be improved, inthe
interests of economy or safety.

All building materials have weight, and all buildings are
affected by the forces of gravity. Structural systems transmit
the gravitational ‘loads’ to earth while creating and enclosing
the volume that is needed to meet the functions ofthe
building. Every dwelling has ‘dead’ loads, which includethe
weight of the static elements such asthe roof, orthe weight of
the walls which also carry and transmit its load. In addition,
dwellings have to cope with ‘live’ loads -forces that are exerted
inconsistently and at different times bythe wind, the
movement of occupants, the placing of furniture, the swinging
of doors, orthe pressure of farm animals against the walls. The
unpredictability of the live loads has often led to ‘overbuilding',
with walls of a mass and weight greatly in excess of what is
necessary for ordinary domestic purposes. That some buildings
survive over many centuries is often attributableto this
overbuilding, which has enabled them to cope with unexpected
stresses. Conversely, some buildings are deliberately underbuilt
sothatthey will be less dangerous if destroyed. The collapse of
buildings in a Pacific cyclone, for instance, isthe result oftheir
having been deliberately built notto withstand the ferocity of
high winds, which create a sudden increase in the live loads.

## S. 38

70 Truetotype

In addition, therefore, to the stresses imposed bythe
components used in the building itself, there are other stresses
which may vary considerably accordingto climatic conditions,
therelative stability ofthesite, and the way of lifeofthe
occupants. Stresses on buildingcomponents can be broadly
classified as ‘tension‘, a stress which pulls apart the particles of
matterand has a stretching effect, such asthe strain on aguy
rope; ‘compression’, where the particles are pushed against
each other with a shortening effect as in a column or solid wall;
and ‘shear’, which causes the particlesto slide, as for example,
where afloorjoist meets a wall. Abuilding materialthat isideal
for copingwith one kind of stress is not necessarilygood for
another: adobe (earth) brick may behave well under compression
forces, but may be poor in its capacityto resist shearforces.
Most materials meet certain kinds of stresses betterthan others
and part ofthe essential skill in building lies in the selection,
preparation and assembly of materialsthat can resisttthe strains
thecchosen form imposes, orthatthe elements impose on it

Good builders know their materials and makethe best of
their properties. The ‘elasticity’ of a ‘tensile’ material which allows
itto deflect orto stretch andto return to anorm isa property of
green woods and saplings, such as those used in frame-and-mat
structures as used by the Kel Geres Tuareg (see p36). Deformation
beyond a certain point however creates ‘yield’ where a material
will not return to its former shape, as inthe preformed ribs ofthe
Rendille min (see p31). Bending is a combination of both tension
and compression: abeam which is bent has its lower surface in
tension and its upper plane in compression. Over a period of
time, poles and beams may deform permanently through
being held in one position: this isthe phenomenon of ‘plastic’
deformation, or‘creep’, which can even occurin a material
which would break or fracture if subjected to a sudden blow.

Itisthe roof structure which usually causes the greatest
problem in vernacular building, for the weight exertsthrusts
onthe walls which can force their centre of gravityto move
outwards, the bending stresses causingthe deflection ofthe
wall. In aconventional roof frame of ‘principal rafters’ meeting
atan apex, a ‘tie-beam’ is frequently usedto counteract this
effect by triangulation. To stabilizethe structure, buttresses
may be used to achieve equilibrium by creatinga counterthrust
against a bending wall. One way of avoidingthe problem is
simply to build an ‘undifferentiated structure’ where there is no
distinction between roof and wall-the building is all roof. Two
or more ‘A-frames’ of inclined straight poles may betiedatthe
apexto support aridge pole,andthegroundacts asthetiebeam. Additional poles or rafters can be laid alongthe length
oftheridge-pole, and horizontal purlins may span the space
between whatare, in effect, rooftrusses and rafters.

(Agassho roof is essentially this, and is believedto have been
a development from A-frame shelters originally constructed
above pit dwellings)." Shingles, or some other ‘cladding’
material which is not load-bearing, can then be laid overthe
roofframeto keepthe weather out.

Undifferentiated structures such asthese are used as field
shelters in Andhra Pradesh in India, where sprays of palmyra
leaves are used for cladding. Easy to construct and effective proof
againstthe weather, they are sometimes erroneously referred
to as ‘tents’ though they are not covered with atensioned
membrane. Onlythe centre beneaththeridge gives adequate
head-room, which makes them inconvenient for permanent
habitation. Inthe Malakwa area of British Columbia, a region
which has heavy snow precipitation, immense A-frame housebarns over 30 ft (10 m) high are constructed by local farmers,
some buildingthem single-handedly with the aid of pulleys and

levers. The steep slopes shed the snow, andtto gain more internal
space some farmers build their barns with a double-pitch
“Mansard’ profile.”

As we noted with reference totheiglu ofthe Inuit,the
dome is a most efficient structure, producingthe maximum
volume possiblefor a given surface area. Aconsiderable space
was also achieved bythe Wichita of Kansas and Oklahoma, who
used curved poles setinacircleand lashed atthe apex with bark
strips, to make a substantial dwelling. Horizontal ribs were
secured between the main supports, providing fixings forthe
layers of grassthatch that weretiedto them. Often called a ‘grass
house’ ofthe‘beehive’ type, it was essentially aribbed dome of
pre-formed curved members, which traced the meridiansthrough
its apex. These were constrained bythe horizontal poles parallel
totheground and, as adome at any meridian issymmetrical, it
was held in equilibrium. The loads, however, were not equally
distributed around the perimeter ofthe dome, forthe cladding
was not load-bearing; they were instead carried bythe polesto
equally spaced points of contact. Unfortunately, the Wichita
grass house is rarely seen today.'3

Aribbed dome can be conceptualized as a multitude of
arches sharingacommon centre. Inastonearch, thevertical
compressive action isexerted on the voussoirs, ortruncatedwedge blocks, which are shaped to bondtogether undertheforce
of gravity. Each stone transmits part ofthe compressive forcesto
the sstones adjacent to it, eventuallytransferringthe weight of
thearchtothe supporting columns or walls when the ‘keystone’,
thefinal voussoir, is put in place. While a ribbed dome may be
thought ofasarotated arch, a plain domeis special in that each
layer of stones paralleltothe ground may be conceived as hoops
which can act as horizontal arches. In addition to the vertical
loads, there are compressive forces horizontallyaroundthe

9: Corbelled dome seen
from within

10: The principle of
cantilever is applied
to stone orbrickin
successive layersto
produce a corbelled

method is widely

layers ofthe dome; and this fact enables itto be built of
successively smaller rings of stones, each carefullyshaped on
allfaces in contact with-other stones so asto produce rings of
tightly fitted wedges which transfer the loads evenly.

Megalithic builders solvedthe problem ofspanning a
space by using this method of ‘corbelling‘, with flat and often
irregular slabs of stone laid upon each other. Each successive
slab projected inwardly by a fraction of its depth beyond the layer
below, usingthe principle of cantilever, the overhanging layer
being stabilized by the weight ofthe stone above. When this was
done from acircular plan, a structure like half an eggshell was
created. Because headroom is immediately restricted bythe
progressive inclination ofthe stone courses, many buildings had
a corbelled parabolic dome springing from athick-walled cube
base. Often, as in the Balearics, thethickness ofthe outside walls
obscured theform ofthedome. An argument can be made for
the diffusion of this corbelled stone technique from the Middle
East, inan arcthatsweeps west and north as far as Iceland,
suggesting a connection with the migration of Celtic peoples.'*

In parts of France different kinds of corbelled stone
structures can befound, often with square plans and roofs with
capstones. Many were used for centuries as field shelters, or
cabanes, but the larger complexes, known popularly as bories
and found inthe Vaucluse and Quercy regions, were permanent
habitations. Low walls and high vaulted roofs were built of
layered slabs, graded in dimension and selected so that any
irregularities in the stone contributed to the run-off of rain water
onthe outside. Undulating over dormer windows and entrances,
the vaults were bridged by monolithic slabs. In many cases a
chimney flue was incorporated in the structure, and sleeping
platforms made of juniper or liveoak were inserted into the wall
ofthe dome and accessed by notched log ladders."

Basically, adome is not extensible without substantially
altering its form. Asimpler but more extensible structure isthe
vault, which can be conceptualized as an arch whose depth is
greaterthan itsspan. Arches and vaults are often constructed
over ‘centering‘, awooden framework shaped to the profile of
the inner surface which takes the loads untilthe ‘keystone’
isinserted and the arch is complete. Some vaults are ribbed
structures, with the ribs as semi-circular arches at intervals,
orwith opposing curved planes springingto meet atthe apex.
Atimber truss would of course span such a space equally well,
though this form of construction is morecommon in regions
that have plenty of wood. Vaults made of ahomogeneous
material have their loads distributed alongthe lengths ofthe
supporting walls. As they still exertthrusts on the walls, edge
parapets are often built, their weight helpingto containthe
thrust. Parapets ofthis kind can be seen inthe complex of stonebuilt and gypsum-plastered barrel-vaults and intersecting crossvaults of the Berber village of Takrouna, in Tunisia.

Situated on a rocky, peninsula-like outcrop, the village
consists of single storey houses grouped around courtyards.
Most ofthese yards are square, while afew are long and narrow,
and serve anumber of household units. The outside walls ofthe
houses are without windows, a single doorway to each complex
faces the narrow streets, and at this level they seem somewhat
monotonous. But infact a considerable variety of form and
accommodation is realized in these buildingsthrough the use
of barrel-vaults, which span the parallel walls, one room in depth,
and are intersected by many cross-vaults to produce T-and
X-form roofs. Some are used for dwelling spaces, but others are
stablesforthe donkeys and stores for the olives from the groves
inthe plain below. Though many ofthe external walls may
expose their rubble construction, most internal walls andall

Such knowledge of their materials, and skillinthe
assembly of their buildings, was only acquired after years of
apprenticeship and many bruised knuckles from the adzehandles of master masons. With much practiceand long
experience, skilled craftsmen develop a ‘feel’fortheir materials
anda sense of appropriateness in their usewhich becomes
almost intuitive. But itwould be incorrect to believe, as many
have, that vernacularforms are arrived at by evolutionary
processes, in which the conscious decisions of their builders have
played little part. The abstract structural principles may not be
known tothem, butthey are far from unaware ofthe expression
ofthese principles in their work. They merely conceptualizethem
differently, basingttheir practice on their knowledge of what
structures succeed or fail.”

Naming the parts

As much a stereotype of vernacular shelter asthe ‘beehive’ isthe
‘cone-and-cylinder’ hut. However, describingthe buildings in
such elementary morphologicalterms, the phrase ignores

the range offorms, details, methods and materials which the
multitude of dwellings with a circular plan represent. The
Kipsigis, a people ofthe Kelanjin cluster of south-west Kenya,
built ahouse which loosely correspondedtothistype.Ithada
wall made from aring of posts daubed with mud, andathatched
roof from whose peak a narrow pointed pole extended. Such a
description is similarto those summarizingthe dwellings of
many other peoples, but itwas the outcome of a process which
hadto be learned and its details, each of which was named, were
specifictothe Kipsigis.' Asite was usually chosen close enough
to water, but on the leeside of a hill-slope. The doorway faced
downhill so that run-off water would not penetratethe house

Long and slender poles, chagaiik, were forced between themain

Atight ring of lianas wastied aroundtheends ofthe poles, and

thethatch and protect the walls from rain. Therefore, to provide

attheshoulderto produce a strong fringe.

Asyet there was no door. The openingwas made by cutting

asection of side wall poles uptothe second ring, strengthened

the door could be slidto seal the entrance from within. Inside a
large house, as many as four substantial forked poles, orttolaita,

More sticks had to be gathered for internal partitions,
beds and bed screens: as many as 800 sticks and poles might
be used in the building of one large dwelling. Men carriedthe
heavier poles and did most ofthe construction, whilethewomen
gathered the sticks and longthatching grass, which was carefully
laid from the eaves uptothe apex in successive layers overan
underlayer of bush leaves. Accentral pole attheapex several
metres in length, the kimonjogut, was tied in position abovethe
loft floorto form a finial which was decorated with bands of
colour. Thethatch terminated at the kimonjogutto which itwas
tied over a thick layer of leaves; and sometimes a broken pot,the
rokiet, was slid overthe poleto completethe crest. Butthe house
was still not finished - there werethe walls to be plastered by
the girls with a mixture of cattledung and mud, and laterto
be decorated; the house to be smoked to inhibitthe rotting of
the roofing leaves; andthefire-stones hadto be put in place,
amongst other details."

Further particularization is unnecessary here; thelist of
precise names for allthecomponent parts- which could be
expanded by addingthetermsforthetechniques and means
employed in their use-is evidence enough of the deliberate
nature ofthe building process. An established terminology of
this kind ensures that the necessary materials are obtainedand
appropriately prepared inthe required numbers as building
components, andthatthe methods used can be described and
passed on to succeeding generations. Such detailed naming of
the parts maintains the stability ofthetradition; changeina
particular element either requiresthe extension ofthe meaning
oftheterm that describes it, orthe need for.a borrowed, adapted
orinvented term to explain a new feature. Change isttherefore
slow, but it. does occur. In recent yearsthetraditional house type
described above, which the Kipsigis shared with the Pokotand

the Kuria, has altered in some respects. Interiors are now divided

Accurate reporting oftheterminology istherefore a key
to identifying building elements and traditions, andtothe
understanding of forms and processes. It is also an indicator of
modifications and variantsthat havetaken placeand hencethe
constancy and change in building practices, which may reflect
aspects of values and influence.?' But questions remain asto
howtthe changes take place; how innovations are adopted, and
how tradition and standard practice meet with individual and
family needs. Though it is customaryto assume that in such
contexts individual requirements count for littleand tradition
dominates, in fact, most traditionally built forms appearto
be modified in some measure by the owner-builders to suit
their own requirements. What, we may ask, arethe design
processes involved?

Design and build

Acknowledged enemies of the Kipsigis werethe Maasai, with
whom there existed codified, but lethal, rules of conflict.
Whereas the Kipsigis were intensive agriculturalists, the Maasai
in the past have been cattle herders- although recent pressures
have induced sometotake up a measure of agriculture. Their
respective societies differ in many ways. Kipsigis could build
wherever they wished within thetribalterritory, and their
settlements were consequently dispersed; by contrast, the
settlements ofthe Maasai were always clustered roundthe

## S. 41

hut ofa headman. Low-Iying, brown-coloured and with
theappearance of aring of loaves, the Maasai enkang, or
domestic kraal, comprise a circle of dwellings. Each enkang

may accommodate the members of one extended family orthe
combined homes oftwo orthree. The enkang is approximately a
circlesometwo hundred feet across, with a dozen or so dwellings
plannedto accommodate the headman, his wives, their children
and elderly relatives. Once assite has been selected bytheelders,
eachwife ofthe headman is given her site for her own dwelling;
thefirstwifetotherightoftheentrancetotheenkangring,the
second wifetotheleft,thethirdtotheright beside thefirst;

the fourth second from the left. Anotherform, the manyatta,

is constructed bythewomen forthe moran, theyoung warrior
age-sets atthetime of their circumcision and initiation. In
accordance with Maasai custom theyoung men liveinthe
manyatta on the plateau some distance away.?? Maasai move
their locations every seven oreight years asthe houses ofthe
enkangrot or collapse due to attacks bythe beetles and white
ants which infestthem.

Itisthe Maasaiwomen who buildthe enkang, the children
assisting in carryingthe materials. Leleshwa branches and sticks
are used where possible, asthewoodis relatively resistantto
termites, ants and beetles. Where acacia predominates, the
women have to walk several milesto find suitable leleshwa and
haulthe branches back; the narrower twigs and leaves being
saved for later use inthe building process. Before commencing
construction, each woman designs her house, using long, thin

poles of green wood to lay out her plan. Awife would spend FOREN Be, RS 0 Be | EN | ni TOR eis =.
