Maasai have a distaste for cultivation, or even for digging;

the position ofthe house is marked out with wet cattle dung

which also serves to soften the earth and permitsthefirst ring

of stout polesto beinserted. These arethen stabilized by a ‘ring

18: Typical plan of

a Maasai house.
Though their general

each house is designed
with partitions and

ofthewoman

chooseto drivea closerring of cedar stakes and place her entrance
flushttothe side wall. Other variants were common, particularly
in their internal arrangement. Slender leleshwa wands form the
roof ofthehut, and as with the earlier Kipsigis house, these are
forced intothe restraining ring between the principal polesand
bent overto produce a shouldered profile. Instead of meetingaat
an apex, however, they are pressed intotheringonthe opposite
side ofthe hut. Gradually a lattice is created and affixedtothe
internal support poles, and allthe elements aretied and bound
with leleshwa bark strips to produce a mesh which is strong
enoughtostand on.

Some houses have a double skin of poles between which
an insulating layer of leaves and thin branches may be packed.
Oncethis construction is complete, the houses are ready for
plastering. AMaasai enkang serves as a corral into which allthe
animals are herded back bythe boysto spend the night. The
ground becomes thick with cattle dung and, inthe course of
afew years is aknee-deep mire, which provides a ready supply
of material for plastering walls, roofs and interior partitions.
Pungent when wet, the plaster loses at least some ofits smell
as it dries and provides an effective protective layer aroundthe
house and, to some extent, repels insects. Sleeping platforms are
constructed against the internal posts made of a wattlemesh
over which a cow hide is laid. There are no window openings but
small vents in the walls, only a few inches wide, give a glancing
light across the sleeping platform. Three hearth stones are brought
in and sited; and afire is lit, filling the interior with smoke which
hardens the dung plaster. Wattle store boxes for calabash vessels
and tin cups, and pens for young goats orachild’s pet dogare
also constructed in some huts. Within two weeks allis ready for
occupation, andthe old enkang is abandoned.3

Undecorated and gaining its only colourfromthered

blankets ofthe occupants, the enkang seems dull atfirst, but as
we have seen, much care and craftsmanship have gone intothe
making oftheframe, and the plastered walls hide a variety of
internal arrangementes. Initiallythe dwellings appearthesame,
but within the uniformity ofthetype each Maasai woman may
design her house as she wishes, and she may also adopt new
techniques -the cedar stockade for example, which is almost
certainly a result of Kikuyu influence. Changes and innovations
willthen be observed, and either incorporated or rejected by her
companions in future building.

This is the way with most forms of indigenous building:
thetradition establishes a broad matrix, the individual builder
designs and constructs to suit his or her requirements within it.
Such dwellings are neither slavish copies of their predecessors,
nor wilful deviants from them. Construction is not a matter of
intuition as ifthe builders were like birds makingttheir nests, but
the result of deliberate decisions taken to meet perceived needs.
Asingle tradition may be described in general terms, but it
should be understood in specific ones; the differences are subtle
but significant, eventually contributingto the slow changing of
traditional form as innovations are introduced and influences
assimilated. The process of design is different from that used
by the Western architect, who may isolate problems and seek
solutions ttothe briefthrough the abstraction of the drawing
board and the building specification. In the indigenous dwelling
there is design too, but it is carried out on a one-to-one scale;
problems are perceived principallywhen the established norm
failsto meet them, or a customary solution is unsuitable forthe
prospective builder and occupier.

participating member of many societies, and thetransition from

stone, moulding and firing bricks, working and carvingtimber,
weaving mats and layingthatch all haveto be learned. Like
children the world over, the Ijaw children of the Niger delta
mimictheir parents, the boys climbing house posts as ifthey
were coconut palms, thegirls playingatfishingand cooking. They
build miniature houses of their own from discarded slivers of

real mats from bamboo that she has gathered herself, while her
brother, though he may stillbetoo young to obtain palm leaves
from thetreetops, learns to weavethem into roofingthatch.
Boys are giventoy machetes at an early age, and this prepares
them for handlingthe full size blade. Small fingers may fumble at
first, butthey soon become adept.?+ Ngoni girls in Malawi have
been observed mudding the walls of newly built huts without
any apparent interest in theirtask, while olderwomen and
grandparents criticized and advised. They were also inept at
beatingthefloors hard with flat flails, and found polishingthem
with damp ashes and cowdungfinished with clarifiedfat, hard
work, butthrough this socializing process they learnedto
develop their abilities.25

In such societies allmembers ofthecommunityare
expectedto havethe knowledge andtheabilityto construct their
own dwelling. When the help of several hands is necessary, as for
example in raisinga roof, members ofthe same lineage or clan
may be called upon to help or, in some cases, thewhole village
may be expected to participate in return forfood or labour. This
was the case in much of Anatolia, Turkey, wherethe principle of
emege, or mutual aid, applied in building and in other community
activities.26 As we have seen, this also applied with the yuisystem
of mutual labour exchange in Gokayama, Japan (see p68).

Astheabilityto build a house, or atleastto contributeto
the process, is required of every man in some societies, or of
every woman and child in others, the builder’s craft often carries
very littlestatus. Because each individual is required to build his
own housethe capacity to do so isno more highly esteemed
than is preparing food, and often agood deallessthan beinga
successful hunter. Frequently, therefore, work on house-building
isrelegated to therank andfile; as it was, amongthe Ngoni of
South Africa, who considered such workto betoo dirtyand
exhaustingforthe ‘women of good family”. This low status has
become institutionalized in some societies; in many parts of
India, for example, house carpenters are of alower middle caste,
abovethat ofthe dhobis (washer-men) andthe unclean sweepers,
but below that ofthe cultivators. Their relative status differs
marginally in every region, and often between villages. Inthe
traditional village of Madhopur on the Ganges plain of Uttar
Pradesh for instance, the ‘lords’, or Thakurfamilies, arethe patrons
of members ofthe lower castes who work forthem, among
whom arethe lohars, the carpenter-blacksmiths. But when
brickmaking, house building orthe digging of wells is required,
agang of camars, or members of the lower caste, will be recruited
forthe purpose.’ Onthe other hand, in the Brahman village of
Kumbapettai (in the Tanjore district of Mysore State), blacksmiths,
goldsmiths, stonemasons and carpenters were all kusavans, or
members of amiddle caste, who intermarried and were ableto
choose which ofthesetrades they wished to follow.® Yetthe
stratification ofthe castes is often much more segmented and
rigid: thus in the old city of Baghdaon (Bhaktapur) in Nepal,
for example, there are some nineteen castes ofwhichthe
brickmakers and masons rank seventh with the potters, a level
higherthan the carpenters and stonemasons, and fiveabovethe
blacksmiths and untouchables.? The caste system inhibits any

Any advance in the quality of building construction or
crafting largely depends on the range ofttools available, orthe
toolsthatcan bemadewith the resources at hand, and also on
the knowledge and skill in handlingthem. Phrases such as ‘heis
good with his hands’ or ‘she has an eye for it’ may beccliches, but
they are also a recognition ofthefact that abilities in perceiving
dimensions, usingtools and achieving high levels of quality in
doing so are thegift of certain members ofacommunity only.

All people in a village may have been involved in building at some
time or other, and most will have acquired the basics of ashared
tradition. However, this does not mean that all are equally adept,
northatthey have an equal willingness to learn more. Moreover,
it is not always simply a matter of being skilled with tools; many
inhabitants ofthe Polynesian islands, such as Nauru orthe
Solomons, have great skill in using pandanus leaves or stems to
makefibrous cords and ropes, sleeping mats, woven wallsscreens
and much else, andtools are scarcely used. The cords, for example,
are made by rollingthe fibres in opposing directions onthe
naked thigh. If access to a wide range of materials is available,
the most suitable can be selected.3°

Many northern European countries are particularly wellendowed with a variety of timber species, clays and rocks. But
some parts ofthe world have a limited range of materials with,
for instance, heavy timber being in short supply. Similarly, some
peoples are withoutthetools needed to work the materials:
California redwoods were of little useto the Miwok, an indigenous
Native American people, who could only build with strips of bark
and fallen branches before they had metal axes.3'To a great
extentthe potential of a material can only be fully exploitedwhen

asocietyhasthetechnology to work it. Heavy gauges oftimber
and boulders or blocks of stone can be used structurally with the
use of basictools, such as levers and rollers, alone. Nevertheless,
innumerable cultures have developed exceptional levels of

craft skills in makingwood or stone joints, and in carvingthe
materials forfunctional, structural or decorative purposes. Often
the work needs ‘roughing out’ orthe coarse removal of surplus
material, and this may be achieved with the percussive tools, like
hammers and mallets, which are also employed for driving nails,
pegs and wedges. Some edge tools, which include knives, chisels
and planes, may be worked by pressure; others, such as axes and
adzes, require sharp impact. Pitsaws and hand saws are cutting
tools that are worked by friction, asarethe abrasivefilesand
rasps, drills and awls, though the latter require rotation when
perforatingwood or stone.

Rotary motion is developed in the strap drilland the bowdrill, and both were known to the Egyptians over fourthousand
years ago. Alaskan Inuit are also among the peoplesthat have
used a form of bow drill, employing an ivory socket and a flint bit.
Their isolation suggests eitherthatthey brought thetechnology
with them from Asia thousands of years ago, orthatthey evolved
it from the use ofthe hunter’s bow-which was also theinspiration
forthe musical bow in many parts of the world. On Nias island,
Indonesia, a pump-drillwas developed, with a fly-wheel made
ofacoconut filled with small stones. Pump-drills were unknown
beforethe Romans, who used virtually allthetools mentioned
here and may have been largely responsible for their diffusion.®

Other commonly usedtools include clamps, pincers and
tongs which, with vices, are used for holding items while working
on them. Nails, bolts, ropes and lashings might also beregarded
as tools, but when these are used for permanent fixings they may
be considered as part ofthe finished work. Many more tools are

## S. 44

82 Trueto type

also employed, including those used for measuring, squaring
and, likethe peg and cord, for ‘setting out’ acircle. Some have been
specifically devised for working with certain materials, such as
cutting ceramictiles, and others for particulartasks or details
such as dressing ashlar stone or peckingtufa. Hewing, sawingand
carving are ttransformative processes andassuch have a certain
magical quality about them as they achieve irreversible change.
Itis perhaps for this reason that rituals sometimes accompany
the use oftools, as isthe case in Japan.3* Until the introduction
of power tools, theJapanese daiku, or carpenter, would havetwo
hundred tools in his kit, of which a quarter were essential chisels
and as many as forty were planes. Daiku predominated in Japan,
indeed there were ten times as many carpenters as stonemasons
inthe country in the seventeeth century, andtheymadeupa
third ofthe artisan class. In the course of buildingthefirst use
of any tool was accompanied by rites of blessingwhile rites were
performed at various stages, including a ridge-pole raising
ceremony.Inthisandthe final ceremony the master-carpenter
assumed the role of aShinto priest.

What may be seen as a form of voluntary segmentation
occurs in some societies, where particular families have secured
forthemselves the reputation for high standards of knowledge
and workmanship in a particular field, and have made others
dependent on them. In a society with amonetary base, skilled
masons, carpenters or housebuilders can sell their services,
gaining employment and commanding more pay by the quality
oftheir craftsmanship. This was the case on the Pacific island of
Palau, where the people had a complex monetary system and a
materialistic outlook. Persons of rank would not make theirown
canoes or build their own houses but employed specialiststo do
this work. Obligations of members ofthesame lineagetothe
community in, for example, the building of acommunal

24: Aglass-cutter
preparing pieces of
coloured glass fora
mosaic decoration.
Tunis, Tunisia

meeting house, were exceptions to this principle of work for
payment, but even in the latter circumstance the competitive
spirit among the Palauans meant that craftsmen soughtto
outdo others and so improve their status. This would enable
them to attract both more work, and the fees of apprentices
anxious to learn their trade.36

Specialization leads to esoteric knowledge, which is
available onlyto the ingroup of workers; the naming of parts
and processes becomes their propertyandthetechniques and
“tricks ofthetrade’ are carefully protected. In medieval Europe,
craftsmens ‘guilds’, or associations, were founded in partto
guard the interests ofthetrade, to establish the levels of
competence required, andto give statusto the master craftsman,
whether carpenter, tiler, stonemason or blacksmith.? Often
membership was only availableto aman whose father was a
member, but research has revealed that many women were
employed inthe building trades, and also functioned as
contractors.3® Apprentices were taken on by master craftsmen
to serve in asubordinate role for a number of years before being
accredited and ableto operate on theirown. Ratherthan work
for a master at regulated rates of pay, some preferredto work as
‘journeymen’ paid by the day (journee), and later astravelling
independent craftsmen. The extent to which vernacular buildings
were constructed by masters, apprentices orjourneymen, or by
the unemployed ‘travelling brothers’ ortramps,?? or by none of
these, has not been ascertained.

Although more has been published on the medieval
guild systems of Europe, guilds have had a long history, having
existed in ancient Egypt, Greece and Rome and India. They also
flourished in the Byzantine and Ottoman worlds and were
well established in the Middle East. In ancient Persia (Iran), for
instance, the banna (mason), the kashisaz (tile-cutter) and

25: Stone masonin
Indonesia, ‘squaring’
and ‘dressing’ astone
blockto effect a repair

their fellow skilled workers in wood, mirror glass and plaster
relief were members of specialized guilds, each with its own
ustad, or master craftsman; and in modified form the guild
system persisted in Iran until recently.#° The guilds were

not solely the province of literate societies, with written
regulations, contracts and formally agreed conditions of
service and rates of pay. Guild-like systems have been reported
in non-literate societies as widely dispersed asthe Nupe of
Nigeria, where craftsmen were organized in labour units
based on kinship; or on the Micronesian island of Truk where
specialized craft families jealously protected their secrets
and skills by exclusivity and ritual.#' In these latter instances
much of the vernacular building was carried out underthe
control of alocalized guild system, butthe degreeto which
these examples may be representative of others, needsto
beresearched.

Whether they live in asociety inwhich, asamember ofa
special group, lineage, village, caste or guild, they are associated
with a particular craft, orwhether they live in a society where all
people participate, vernacular builders have much to learn. They
need to know the properties ofthe materialsthey will use, how
to obtain them, and howto gain the skillsto fashion them with
the appropriate tools. As builders they must learn howto
construct accordingtothenorms, values and aspirations oftheir
communities, acquiringthe relevant knowledge from the
traditions ofthe past and, inturn, transmittingitto succeeding
generations. Conscious and physical memory training may be
inculcated and reinforced through song, proverbs, axioms,
repetition by rote and, above all, by sensitivity to the lessons of
theirown experience. Bysuch meansthe vernacular builders of
the world learn to betruetothetypes and conditions ofthe
dwellingsthatthey not only construct, but also occupy.

86 Built from

Carvingahome
Whether early man habitually inhabited caves, or whether caves
afforded better protection forartefacts and detritusand so
provided the best record of primitive culture, is still an open
question. They may bethe oldest form of dwelling, even today
some fifty million people live in caves; though most modern-day
‘troglodytes’ (cave-dwellers) have modified or adapted them for
habitation. Untilthe 1970s, anumber ofthe cave dwellingsinthe
sandstone of Kinver Edge in West Staffordshire, England were
still lived in, butthough there is a long history oftheir use in this
and other parts of Britain, it isnot comparable with the extent
of cave occupancy in regions of Europe.!

Many caves are still occupied in France likethose onthe
Loire near Saumur, or at Brantöme on the River Dronne. Rooms
have been carved out ofthelimestone, quite often on two floors,
and many have theirfacades made ttrim with doors, the upper
windows with shutters, whilewashing flutters on the rock-cut
balconies.? In Almanzora, southern Spain, the rock faces of low
and craggy hills open onto atight amphitheatre oftroglodyte
villages. Honeycombed at many levels, the dwellings are often
one ortwo storeys in height, their ownership proclaimed by limewashed fronts. Internally, the rooms have been peck-carved with
a metal adze to claim space, and barrel-vaulted ceilings transfer
the loads so thattthey are less likely to collapse. To create anew
caveatunnelwould becarvedtothe required depth ofthe
dwelling and the rooms excavated from the inside, sothatthe
progression was towardsthe front. Thefacade wallofthecaves
are intact limestone, though the external surface would be
smoothed and whitewashed.3

Sixty milestothe west alongthe sandstone escarpment
ofthe Sierra de los Filbares, is Guadix, wheretheravine ofthe Rio
Fardes and the slopes ofthe mountains are sculpted into villages

From the main vaulted chamber of these dwellings, hewn ovoid
and curtained entrances leadto the dormitorio (bedroom), the
comedor andtthe cocina (kitchen), the latterwith recesses for
pans and vessels and a stove with a chimney flue. These rooms
were chipped from the grey-brown stone with scutches, pecking
tools of adze-like form, and subsequently white-washedto
maximize reflected light. Beyond the village are steep ascents
to stables and stores and to rudimentary shelters, hewn out of
coarse and unstable limestone, leavingthick pillarsto support
the vaults. Many ofthese are of recent date but asthey were
carved by inexperienced immigrants and refugees from central
Europe, some have collapsed in heavy rains.5

6: Interior ofa kitchen
inaCappadocian

7: Tufa pinnacles in
avalley, Cappadocia,
which have been carved
to make dwellings,
dovecotes and other

slack periods ofthe farmingyear, as many have done for centuries
in thestrange moonscape of Cappadocia, Turkey. Like giant sugar
loaves, volcanictuff pinnacles of ancient compacted ash from
the volcano Ergiyas Dagi, have weathered into shapesthatrrecall
a Flemish master’s dream of mountains. In Ürgup and Göreme
and numerous small settlements, peasants chip out multistoreyed dwellings, honeycombingthe rock with their narrow
adzes. Apeasant couple maytakethreemonthstocarvean
upper-storey room. Softto carve, thetuff hardens on exposure
to air, asthe calcium carbonate reacts with carbon dioxideto
produce a hard crust. Carving out dwellings is an excavating,
hollowing procedure, essentially sculptural, exceptthatthe
carver works around himself, turning solid into void, rock mass
into room. To dotthis requires amental map ofthe section ofthe
rock pinnacle sothatthe sides are not breached, and the position
and number of steps in aflight of stairs, as well as an awareness
ofthe necessary thickness of floorthat must be leftabovethe
room below. There is no latitude for mistakes.$

Most families carve outtheirrooms onthe south or
south-east face ofthe Cappadocian pinnacles, to getthe benefit
ofanysuninthehardand cold plateau winters. Three or more
rooms may be carved, with short inter-linking passages and
balconied access to the outer faces, where steps lead down to
cultivated plots. Cupboards, tables, benches, sleeping platforms
and even decorative imitation fireplaces, are left in reliefasthe
internal voids are carved backtothe wall plane. Niches for water
pitchers and grain sacks may be cut deeper intothe rock. Ifthe
internal forms sometimes reflectthose of constructed dwellings,
they are arrived at by a negative process, subtractive ratherthan
additive. Many peasants favour conventional house interiors,
squaring offthecorners of rooms and carving flat ceilings. As
painting with whitewash does not obliterate smoke blackening

onthe ceilings, itis removed by chipping away thestone,
although this threatens to weaken the house overtime.’ The rock
spoil from the initial rough carving process, when large pieces are
removed, is used for internal wallsand for sealingthe cavesthat
are used as pigeon lofts, the nesting boxes also carved intothe
rock. Where the situation permits, aforecourt may be levelled,
sometimes with surrounding walls, to create social and play
areas andto pen livestock. In many instances additional buildings
are constructed of surplus stone, extendingfromtherock face
cave openings, orstanding free. Successive generations, with
faithinthe strength ofthetuff, have carved some pinnacles until
they have become mere shells, perilously closeto, and sometimes
beyond the point of, collapse. Butthough many rocks have
sheared from excessive carving, the complexes of rock dwellings
over some two hundred square miles still house tens of
thousands of people.®

Cave dwellings are not necessarily carved in limestone,
sandstone or volcanic tuff. In fact, by farthe greatest number are
excavated in loess, which is fine, yellow-grey loam, rich in salty
minerals. Loess was laid down by winds in the late and middle
Pleistocene periods. Inthe middle reaches ofthe Huang-he
(Yellow River) region in China, countless generations have riddled
the loess, up to 1,000 ft (300 m)tthick. Around forty million
people still livetoday in wholly or partially carved cave dwellings.
Subject to intense erosion, loess surface soil is unstable for house
building, but highly fertile for agriculture. By living in cave
dwellings, the people ofthis region consume none ofthe surface
land, and make good use ofthe stronger, more dense and less
moist deeper layers. When dry, the loess is hard and has an even,
stone-free texture. In North Shaanxi, caves are cut into southfacing cliffs of yellow loess, pairs of farmer-builders usingtheir
customary hoes and shovels to excavate the soil, whiletwo

## S. 48

90 Builtfrom

theground

companions removethe spoil in wheelbarrows. To ensurethat
exposed soil hastimeto dry the process is undertaken in stages,
andtakes many months to complete.? As described by Jan
Myrdal,'° after a vertical plane had been established asafacade,
aslotwhich definedthe entrance was cut back to wallthickness,
before the inner loess was opened outto form a vaulted room
some twenty feet deep, ten feet wide and ten feet high. Thenthe
doorway slot would be enlarged, awindow cut and the interior
smoothed and plastered with loess mud. After it dried outthe
cave made a sound home fortwo orthree generations, its life
prolonged by springing curved planks which providetensile
support undertheroof. Nearthe doorway the kang, ortraditional
brick bed heated by a charcoal fire, is usually constructed. Its
corner position generates an asymmetric facade, which may

be spanned by a semi-circularfanlight. Tothe front ofthecliff
dwelling a levelled and walled patio area provides an outdoor
social but private spaceforthe family, with access by way ofa
service lane orflight of steps.

Inregions where there is more open land but few suitable
cliffs, other farmers sacrifice some ofthe surface areato carve
out a square pit, about six metres into the loess and deep enough
to permit excavation ofthe side walls. The process of excavation
that followed was not dissimilarto that ofthe.cliff face caves.
Onthenorth side ofthe pit, which provided a family courtyard,
the excavated caves faced south, and were occupied by the older
generations, whiletherooms ofthechildren faced easttowards
therisingsun. Oppositethese were the kitchen and storehouse,
whiletothe south and always in shade, were the pigsty and
latrine. Access to the courtyard is commonly by a separately cut
ramp or stairway.

Farmers in Shaanxi excavate caves to make dwellings but
they also buildthem; vaulted houses that are similarto caves.

Stone upon stone

Thoughthe ‘built’ cave ultimately resemblesthe carved dwelling,
itwas arrived at by an additive process; thetight-fitting
assembly of voussoirs, each cuttofit.Insomeregionsthetools
neededtotrim stone have not been available, orthe accessible
stone has not been suitable for fine shaping. From Tibet to
Ethiopia or Italy, stone is often utilized as it is found, largeand
small pieces interlocked to make atight, compressive structure.
Such mass walling can be held in place by itsown weightand can
takethethrusts of atimber roof, but problems arisewhen wood
isscarceandthe space hasto bespanned with stone. Wehave
seen inthe previous chapter how Megalithic builders overcame
this problem by using.a corbelling system to make adome,
sometimes building it overathick-walled base structure. Among
the many dwellings usingthis method of construction arethe

ofthethree longttufa plateaux of Apulia (Puglia) inthe southern
‘heel’ of Italy; the builders of farms, villages and thetown of
Alberobello usedthe corbelling method extensively. Theword
trullireferstotheroof, which is in effect, aconical dome, but

though this implies a circular plan building below, it is seldom
the case. The majority ofthe houses built bythis method are in
fact, ‘cubic’ in form, though a farm or dwelling complex may
have many trulliroofs over a number of linked living spaces with
a near-square plan. Whentthe whole region is examined, agreat
many variants areto be found, including numerous stepped
structures on a circular plan, which are of uncertain date. Insome
cases flights of stone steps connect thethree or four levels, but
though these structures appearto have been used inthe past
both as dwellings and field shelters, little is known ofthetradition
untilthe early eighteenth century."

Atthistime, the ‘One-Eyed of Puglia’, Gian Girolamo, Duke
of Nado, promoted the building of trullidwellings in farm plots
on his extensive land-holdings. Subject to taxation on each of
the buildings on his land, he encouraged the raisingofthe
unmortared corbelled domes, asthese could be dismantled
overnight when thetax investigators were due, and re-erected
aftertheir departure. A particular concentration of trulliwas built
in the village of Selva in the duke’s domain; later in the century
when the inhabitants gained their freedom, they renamed it
Alberobello afterthe ‘beautifultree' beneath which they first
held council. The trullibuilders could now use lime mortar ifthey
so wished, and it was probably from this date, as construction
became more permanent, that specialist roof builders, the
trullisti, emerged. When commencing a new house tthe builder
would often excavatetherockinsitu, andwould then build a
dome overthe excavated space, which would be used asa cellar
orasa cistern, to support the floor ofthe house above. Ifthe trulli
was to bethe home of a field worker it might have only asingle
dome overa cell below. But most were constructed in clusters
oftwo, three orfour rooms, which were connected by doorways
or corridors through theirthick walls. Such groupings required

linking saddles or valleys between the domes, arrangementsthat
were further influenced bytherough undulations ofthe land,
necessitating a variety of solutions."

Stone walls in a cubic structure presented no difficulties
to the builder, butthetransition from squareto circle did.
Thoughthe earlier structures solved this by using very thick walls
to support the circular base ofthe dome, the skilled trullisti used
pendentives to achievethetransition, convertingtheroof
aperture from asquareto an octagon, on whichthedome could
bereadily placed. The pendentive allowed the buildertto ‘tail’the
dome intothe corners ofthecubic room, producinga curvilinear
triangle in each, a more difficult if more elegant solution.
Although the layers of carefully trimmed thin slabs of tuffinthe
domes suggest a corbelling system of successive rings, they
were frequently constructed in a continuous spiral of blocks,
with an ashlarface on the inside, and stone slivers wedged
tightly between voussoirs. To ensure regularityinthespiral, a
cordwastiedto apoleatthe centre, successive revolutions round
the pole shorteningthe cord and describing the inner contour of
the dome. A core of rock fragments and soil wastamped over the
outside ofthe dome, beforethethin-slab rings ofthe external
cone were laidtotthe desired, sometimes curved, profile. Because
thesslight gradient ofthespiral caused acompressive effect, it
was possibleto omitthetop ofthedome and leave an opening,
althoughthis was usually closed with a capstone supporting a
cucuerneo, a lime-plastered decorative finial.'*

Windows would have small lintels, but doorways were
spanned with arches, often in gable form, whilethe valleys and
eaves ofthe roofs directed rain waterto achannel which led
down tothe cistern below. Inside, arched alcoves, recessed ovens,
and conical chimney flues were skilfully built into the structure
with a consistent unity ofform. Lime plaster was frequently used

94 Builtfromthe ground

onthe walls, which today are customarily whitewashed both
inside and externally. In Alberobello the houses were placed
contiguously, the layering ofthe stone accommodatingthe
different levels occasioned bythe gradients ofthe streets.
Inthe countryside of olive groves and vineyards, many ofthe
innumerable trullifarms incorporated a group of domes overthe
living spaces and further rows of domes over stores and stables.
Even today such domes are being built or carefully repaired by
local trullisti, for although house-owners can raisethe walls
without difficulty, the maintenance or extension of trullineeds
more specialized skills. The example ofthe trulliillustrates not
only the quality of an apparently simple vernacular tradition,
but also how specialists may arise within the community.’
Stone building is by no means universal and even where
rock is exposed it is not necessarily used. Some rocks are difficult
to shape, others soon deteriorate; others are intractable.
Anigneous rock of a crystalline composition, such as granite,
ishardto work, even with strong and heavy tools. Millennia of
weathering and natural faults can cause graniteto split into large
slabs, and the resultant monoliths have been used for housebuilding. Inthe north of Portugal, for example, in particular
the Beina Alta region, much ofthe country comprises granite
mountains and plateaux, rough granite hillsand rocky stream
beds. Here, where the arable land is scarce and jealously
protected, the small granite houses are clustered togetherto
occupy aslittleofthe ground as possible while still being close
to the crops. Many of the houses are two-storeyed, the lower
level containingthe farm implements and housingtthe animals.
Aboveisthe single living space, with awindowless alcove for
sleeping, and a deep recess into the floor forthefireplace, with
iron cooking pots but no chimney. Massive granite blocks span
the windows and doorway openings, and monoliths framethem;

exterior steps and porches are constructed of huge granite slabs,
levered into place. In some villages, such as Monsanto, immense
boulders may be incorporated inthe walls, while in othersthe
stones may be smaller, and the roofs made of thinner slabs,
laid over rafters of fir from the pine foreststo the west. Tough
and uncompromising, itis an architecture with littleroom for
embellishment, though the openings may be whitewashed
followingthe shapes ofthegranitejambs and lintels, andthe
contrast ofthemonoliths and smaller random infill has a rugged
variety. Itisthe pervasive scale oftheslabs, andthe closeness of
houses, field walls, terraces and cobbled streetstothegranite
shoulders ofthe mountainsthatgivethe region a powerful unity.'
Apart from the availability of appropriate and workable
rock, and access to suitable tools with which to ‘dress’ it, building
in stone also requires time: timeto quarry, carveandshapethe
blocks, and time to assemble. Time to build implies long-term
residence; stone buildings are constructed by sedentary peoples
and are extremely durable. Peasants do not necessarily havethe
timeto spare on building and itiscommontofind stone reserved
forthe houses ofthewealthy, and for palaces andttemples.
Oritmay be used forthe engineering projects, road and bridge
building, defences and fortresses where its strength can be
exploited. Such major works have encouragedthe emergence of
specialized builders and craftsmen, whose skills have become
both developed and protected. Prestigious buildings are built
in fine ‘ashlar’, cut square and laid in regular courses, butthe
houses oftthe populace are typically in rubble masonry, held firm
by ‘quoins’ or corner-stones and, where necessary, levelling
lengths of timber. Houses of this kind are found in the villages
ofthe Tajiks inthe Salang mountains of north-east Afghanistan.
Herethe dry-stone buildings aretapered slightlyto increase
stability, andthough a house may have several storeys, these

In many contexts, entire walls may be made of random
rubble, irregular pieces fitted together where possible and held
with a binding material which, in the simplest dwellings, is
usually mud. This is a basic ‘wet process”. Wet process stone
walling involving mortar, requiresthe mixing oflime and
cement, orlime and pozzolana (volcanic soils) with sand. As
pozzolan soils are often found close to lime-stones, tufa caves,
dry limestone and mortared building stone may all occurinthe
same region, asthey do in Apulia. Lime mortarwas known ttothe
Greeks, andthe Romans developed the construction of lime kilns
as early as300 BC. Countriesthatfelltothe Roman Empire often
retained the techniques of lime mortar, or regainedthem in
medievaltimes. In China too, their use was well known, andthe
sections ofthe Great Wall built duringthe Ming dynasty have
lime and sand mortars. The lime was burned in temporary kilns
built on-site, amethod which survives in some parts of China,
such asthe Anhui province, to the present day. Cones of clayand
coal dust are layered with lime blocks and bound into atapering
truncated kiln, under which an intense, slow-burning fire of coal
blocks islit. This fire is allowed to burn for a week, afterwhichthe
kiln is cooled and broken apart andthelime is claimed, theash
being re-used to form new fire cones."? Though lime, gypsum,
and pozzolan soils areto be found in many parts ofthe
developing world, they are seldom used for building. This is due
to many factors, including the lack of knowledge and technology

necessary either for burning limestone chunksto makethe
quicklime, orforthe slaking ofthisto produce hydrated lime
suitable for mortars or plaster. Even so, immense lime kins
are constructed and used in Java, Indonesia, a region more
frequently notedfor its building in wood and bamboo.”

In spite ofits availability and proven durability, stone is
less frequently used now than it was in the past. This is largely
duetoageneral declinein the learning of the necessary skillsto
‘work’the material. Consequently, the costs of employing
specialized masons have risen beyond the means of most people.

Moulded in clay

Whetherit is bonded with mortar, orsimply used dry, stone is not
the most extensively used material for wall building, common
though it is in Western Europe and parts ofthe Middle East.
Throughout Africa, parts of Central and South America, much of
India, China and southeast Asia, one of the principal building
materials is mud. Soils are decomposed rocks, occurring over
much ofthe world’s land surface, and are used for building in a
variety of conditions. Chemicallythere are many soiltypes, and
as any gardener or potter is aware, some soils are alkalineand
some clays cannot beturned on a potter'swheel. Itisthis
variability that makes it possible for multi-storeyed buildings to
beconstructed of earth in the High Atlas or North Yemen - but
not in many other regions.

Podzolic soils cover much of Northern Europe, Russia, and
Canada, buttthose in coniferous forest regionstendto be
extremely acid and unsuitable for building. Soilsthat were
formed under deciduous forests on the other hand, as in Western
Europe, orthe grassland soils ofthe prairies and steppes of North
America, are widely employed for construction. In thinly-watered
areas, desertic soils of broken rock and sand have resulted in

some ofthe most notable uses of earth for construction, as have
thelaterites and red soils of India, Africa, and South America.
Laterites are low in fertility- paradoxically, forthey occur
beneath tropical rain forests- and rapidly leach out whenthe
tree cover is felled. They retain many ofthe characteristics of
rock, becoming hard and encrusted when exposed to air, which
makes them good for building, but poor for agriculture, except
when carefully nurtured.2°

Building soils should contain large pieces of coarsesand
as well as smaller sand particles, silt and fine clay. They do not all
have to be present in one mixture, and other elements may serve
similar functions, likethe naturally occurring shale fragments
called ‘shillet’ which makethered clays of west Devon, England,
ideal for ‘cob’ construction.?' The clay is mixed with water,
chopped straw or asimilar building material, andtrodden ina
pit until it has the appropriate consistency. Butthe method of
preparation depends onthe kind ofsoil, and each region has
developed its own. In its plastic state mud is malleable, capable
ofbeing mouldedto forms that are scarcely achievable by manual
means in any other material. Closetotheart ofthe potter,the
huts and compounds of sub-Saharan West Africa sometimes
display a remarkable aesthetic sense of form. From Senegalto
Cameroon a wide variety of compound dwellings is built bythe
application of similar principles.??

Where the land is intensively cultivated butthe ecology
is fragile, as it is in the savannah regions of West Africa, itis
importantthatthesitingofthetraditionalcompounds is carefully
considered. Responsibility for this does not rest with the village
chiefs, such asthe headman of Nangodi, Ghana onthe borders
of Burkino Faso, but with the priests. Many ofthe peoplesinthis
region, including the Fra-Fra of Nangodi, were conquered by
Mossi invaders from the north. A village of compounds forming

a widely dispersed settlement connected by footpaths through
thestands of maize and elephant grass, Nangodi is over 20 miles
(30 km) in length, overall. Each compound was encircled by
walls which linked the huts within and afforded some measure
of defence; however, the dispersal ofthecompounds had
undoubtediy madethem vulnerableto attack inthe past.

In more peacefultimesthe Mossi chief leftmuch ofthe village
management in the care ofthe male elders ofthe Fra-Fra,

who met regularly under ashade-giving counciltree.

Allocation ofthe land where anew compound could be
built was determined bythe Tendaana, or land priestwho
interceded between thelivingand the ancestors by appropriate
rituals and libations. In every compound conical mud columns
wereto be found, which contained the ashes oftherecently
deceased who were honoured daily with liquid food. Slowly,
the columns were permittedto collapse, andwhen the earth of
the column joined the soil below it was an indicationthatthe
departed hadjoinedthe ancestors whose domain isthe land.
The Tendaana was responsible for directingayoung family inthe
choice and allocation ofthesitefora new compound. The siting
could be closely relatedto the amount of arable land a mature
extended family could be expected to work, to their relationships
with kin occupying other compounds, andtothere-use of
ground consecrated for building. But forthe Fra-Frathe guidance
ofthe Tendaana was the result ofthe voice oftheancestors
speakingthrough him; land allocation was a matter of religious
belief and not one of pragmatic decision-making. This expression
ofthelandasthehome oftheancestors emphasizedthesacred
significance ofthemud from which the compounds are built.?3

constructed bythemen. Thewomen obtainthewaterandmud

Amixture of mud and chopped straw is left for several daysto
cure. Later this is mixed with waterto a plastic consistency and
moulded into balls. The wall builder presses these balls obliquely
inasspiral sequence, expellingthe airand compactingthe
material. A lift’ of perhaps eighteen inches is completed and
allowedto dry before another layer is added, often with alapped
Joint between layers, which assiststhethrow-off ofrain and
lessensthe risk of cracking and penetration. The structure may
berequired to support transverse wooden beams at roof level,
which penetrate halfway intothe wall; alternativelythe beams
may rest on forked columns which rise inside the dwelling
independent ofthe walls. Aflat roof of layers of sticks laid
crosswise and plastered over with several inches of mud may
havea parapet, andthe entire external wall is often rendered with
a mixture of cattle dung and oil. A preparation made fromthe
fruit pod ofthe locust bean orthejuices of boiled plantain stalks,
produces an effective water-repellentwhen addedtotheearth
mix. Though rain occurs only in brief periods it is extremely heavy
and potentially damaging. U-section half logs are employed as
waterspoutstto drain offtheroof water, and corn cobs orthe
fingers are used to produce looping or V-shaped markings on
the external walls, which appear decorative but which havethe
important function of retarding water run-off and sheet erosion.
Because they have curved walls, thecompounds of many
African peoples are structurally extremely efficient. Like fired clay
potsthey can be relativelythin becausettheir curvature hasthe
effect oftransmitting any external shocks, such asthe swinging
bulk of animals, alongttheir length, and so dampeningthe impact.

elephant grass, a store of which is kept for necessary repairs after

has her hearth, granaries and storage vessels, there are seatsand

between huts, plastered and baked rock-hard inthesun. Moulded

edges asthe human form, the buildings have an anthropomorphic
qualitythat is reflected inthe names ofthe spaces they define.+

When the successivelifts, or layers, in an earth wall dry by
evaporation, thesoil shrinks and the particles are drawn closer
together. Asthe plastic material becomes a dry solid it loses
most of its elasticity. This means that earth is effectively used
in compression: as for example, when it is usedto form loadbearing walls. Alltechniques seekto compress the particles, and
rammed earth, also known by the French term of pise deterre, is
an ancient method of doing so. Known by the Chinese for more
than 3,000 years, it diffused throughout the Middle East. All
versions of the method are similar; an open-ended frame inthe
form ofa box closed at one end is constructed, some eighteen
inches wide with planks on itstwo long sides. These overhang
that part ofthe wall which has already been tamped, to give
greater stability. Aframe is placed overtheopen endto hold
the planks secure, and a spindle attached tothe frame passes
through the built part ofthe wall to lock it in position.

With the ‘formwork’ in place, the box isfilledto a depth
of.about four inches with loose earth, which isthen pounded or
rammed with heavy poles. The compacted earth is further mixed
with straw bonded by seed oil or with a loose strawpaper mixed
with lime, which is soaked in water and pounded in with thesoil.
More soil isrammed untilthetop ofthe box frames reached,

the section of wallthus built being between oneandtwofeet,
accordingtothe depth ofthe shuttering. Itis leftto dry, though
not completely, before another layer is built on top; when it is
finished the wall can be given a mud plaster orwashed downto
produce a skim, which, when dry, takes awhitewash or other
suitabletreatment. Corners in a pise building are either
overlapped with successive layers, in similar fashiontothe
technique of building stone quoins, or lengths of board are
embedded in the layers sothatthey make a firm bond. Although
the pise method produces strong and solid walls, it is not usable
as aroofingtechnique. Awooden ring beam, orfired bricks, can
beinsertedinthetop ofthewallto.actas awall-plate, andthe
roof truss, with strong tie-beams to reduce outward thrust,
is rested on itand made secure.26

Soilsthat are extremely dense sometimes occur naturally,
and vernacular builders make use ofthem. Pioneer homesteaders
in the prairie states of America in the nineteenth century were
short oftimber but had soils in abundance. Seeingthe shoulderhigh prairie grasses, they turned the virgin land with their
breaking, or‘grasshopper‘, ploughs and used the sods to build
their homes. Sods cut from the low ground where the roots were
thick were some sixteen inches wide, the furrows being kept
ofeven width. They were cut into ‘bricks’, 2to 3 ft (1.5 m) in
length, with a spade. Rising from a shallow trench they were
laid in bonds, often with two rows of sods laid in parallel fortwo
layers and athird layer across them, to make a rectangular-plan
buildingwith a single dividing wall. Precautions were takento
‘break’ thejoints, the interstices beingfilled with fine clay. The
sods would often be laidto ridge height atthe gable ends, asthe
ridge pole, rafters and wall plate would be broughttotthesite by
wagon where possible. If available, saplings were used, the ridge
resting in forked poles at each end.

As always, the roof presented the biggest problem. Itwas
covered with corn-stalks, thick hay, brushwood or, when available,
planked with slats from a wagon. Laid with grass and then with
thin sods, which the fresh shoots in the spring bonded, theroof
was serviceable; but an intervening layer oftar-paper was an
added protection when it could be obtained. The customary
width ofa sod house was 16 ft (5 m), and acommon house would
be some 20 ftin length, though this was dependent on thesize of
the settler’s family. The floor would be of beaten earth, the
windows made of oiled paper and shoe-leather used for hinges
onthe plank door. Such a house mighttake onlyaweek
orten daystto build, afterwhich the homesteader devoted his
timeto digging a well. After afew months, when they had dried
out, the walls could be plastered with mud.? In the remote and
dispersed settlements ofthe prairies, many sod houses did not
receive electricity untilthe 19305. Forty years later, asmall
number of ‘soddys’were still occupied in Nebraska and West
Kansas, theirowners complaining only ofthe maintenance
against winter snows, andthefine rain of earth granules which
persistently fellfromtheroofandhadto be caught in makeshift
ceilings of old sheets. Many people recalled buildingthem, the
environmental comfort of their interiors in all seasons and, with
nostalgia, their education inthe sod schoolhouses of Nebraska.2?

Turfs used by the Sami semi-nomads in Norway andthe
western settlers in Kansas were cut from the ground, but river
bottoms also have compacted soils. Bogs in the river lands of
New Mexico and many parts of Hispanic-speaking America, where
the root systems of river grasses have created athick, matted
texture, are cut out in blocks called terrones. Once dried, they
can be used as bricks. A similar method of cutting bricks directiy
from the soil is periodically used in Kiangsi province in China
when theriver silts become too great forthe cultivation ofrice

Adobe abodes
Local desertic soils with their relatively high sand content
make an effective building material, whilethe general heatand
aridity greatly assistthe drying ofthe bricks. Earth bricks are
common in the Middle East, North Africa, the Americas and
parts of Europe, notably Spain. In Iran, the use of sun-dried
mud bricks, or khesht, is widespread. These are moulded iin a
simple wooden form and are usuallythin and square, around
8x 8x3 inches (20x20x7 cm). Knocked intothe hand from
the mould, the bricks are stacked on end to dry in rows, or like
dominoes in a continuous winding line. They are bonded by
a mortarofwetmud ofthesame consistency asthe bricks,
which consequently dries and weathers atthe same rate.
Laid with swift dexterity, in simple bonding or diagonal
and herringbone patterns, the walls may be one brick in depth.
Exposuretothe weather necessitates that adobe is protected
to prevent ‘spalling‘, orthe shearing of brick layers. In Isfahan,
as elsewhere in Iran, the adobe blocks are covered with kar-gel,
a plaster made of mud and straw (sometimes containing
animal dung as an emulsifying agent), which is spread thickly
over walls and roof domes. Some house roofs are so thickly
protected with kar-gelthatthe domical structure beneath is
scarcely evident, and a flat roofthat can still drain is produced.
Details, such asthe edging of ivans, or vaults closed at oneend,

Arabs broughtthetechniqueto Spain wherethe word
atobewas adapted to adobe, theterm by which itis known
throughout Hispanic America. Adobe blocks may be handmoulded, butthe use of amould produces a sharperedge and
standard dimensions, which togetherfacilitate bonding. Gangs
offour orfivermoulds may be used thoughthey are heavy to carry
when the clay is wet. Asthey dry out, adobes behave more like
stone, though they shear and crack easily if they are not wellcompacted. Consequently adobes are mainly used for loadbearing walls in compression, but itisnot uncommon to see
them employed in ways comparablewith stone, eventothe
construction oftimberless domes by the corbellingtechnique.

In northern Syria for instance, parabolic corbelled domes
are constructed above cells of a square plan, with layers of
sun-dried brick regularly laid and gently cantileveredtothe
apex. These domes have an egg-like section which efficiently
distributes the stresses on the shell surface and isttherefore very
strong. One ortwo bricks may be omitted from the shell form to
permit ventilation andthe escape of warm air, and stones are
inserted at points in the curvetto facilitate climbingthe dome
for repairs. Thewhole domeis finished with a plaster of mud
and strawto giveasmooth, rendered surface which reduces
wind erosion. Half a dozen cupolas may run side by side beneath
the unifying layer of mud plaster, sothatthey appeartobea
continuous structure. However, the cells are not linked
internally; each opens separately on to an exterior courtyard
space where other domes accommodate animals and grain.
Inform and method of construction, if not in materials used,
they are closetothe French bories (see p70).3'

Avariety offorms can be built using adobe blocks, but
the commonest use is for buildings with rectilinear plans. Where
palm, bamboo ortimber are availablethey will be usedtoform
the roof structure, resting on the mass walls, often with a low
parapettto give added protection atthe meeting ofthe materials.
Forthe problem of all earth construction isthat it is subject to
erosion, and is particularly susceptible to rain. Plasters of mud,
straw and dung, or macerated okra and leaves, may repel water
and protect the joints from seepage. Itisthesmooth surface of
the plaster which is ultimately visible, softeningthe contours
ofthe building as successive layers are applied in maintenance.
These weaknesses are considerably reduced or even eliminated
when fired or ‘burnt’ bricks are used.

Bricks and mortar
Fired bricks are more expensive than sun-dried bricks; producing
them isa specialized process and hence leadsto craft
differentiation, whilethe heat necessary to firethem consumes
large quantities of fuel. There are other disadvantages-the
physical change in the brick that firing creates means that brick,
unlike adobe, cannot bereduced and the material reconstituted.
But their durability, fire-resistance and relative imperviousness
when used with suitable mortars, means that fired bricks are
attractiveto those who can afford them. This was evident inthe
early development of brick in England. The Romans used brick in
Britain, but it was probably re-introduced by Flemish weavers
who settled in England inthethirteenth and fourteenth
centuries, and bythetrading relationships with the Hanseatic
League of European cities.3?

When wethink ofthe skilful use of brick in domestic
architecture it is probably the great canal-side houses of
Amsterdam that cometo mind. Inmanytowns ofthe Netherlands

there are striking examples, such as in the old Friesland seaport
of Hoorn, with its city gates, town hall and other public buildings.
It also had the large, stepped-gable five-storey warehouses ofthe
Dutch East India company; and in its backstreets, the simplertwostoreyed houses ofthe seamen, with their upper-level loading
bays, narrow frontages, side doors opening on long passages, and
agile dog-leg guttering.® From the fourteenth century untilthe
mid-seventeenth century in Britain, brick was exclusively used
forthe castles and halls ofthe gentry and a few public buildings.
Bythe eighteenth century, itwas used for vernacular buildings,
the bricks being made from local clays on site. Asthe colours of
the clays differed from area to area, largely duetothe degree of
iron inthesoil, this gave a distinct regional qualityto the buildings.
Over-burnt bricks could emerge from thekiln with a different
coloration as didthe bricksthat had been affected bywoodsmoke, differences which the bricklayers semed to have enjoyed
by exploitingthe colours of the ‘headers’ in patterns on a wall.
Bricks are laid in ‘courses’ (or layers), in various ‘bonds’
of‘stretchers’ (bricks placed lengthwise), and ‘headers’ (bricks
placed across the depth ofthe wall). One ofthe.attractions of
fired brickisthat as it does not need to be plastered, it can be
used decoratively. Bricks may vary in size from 2to 4inches
(5to 10 cm) in thickness (thin bricks fire more easily) to 8to 10
inches (20t0 25 cm) in length and sometimes halfthat breadth.
By usingthick mortarjoints, by contrasting horizontal courses
with vertically laid bricks, andtthe inventive use of herringbone
and diaper patterns, a diversity of bonds is possible. These may be
enriched still further by the use of glazed headers, recessed bricks,
staggered courses, and raked mortar joints.3*Such virtuosity is
occasionally displayed in vernacular traditions, but generallythe
patterns are simple combinations of stretchers and headers.
‘Flemish bond’ consists of alternating stretchers and headers on

each course, with a header placed centraily above and below a
stretcher; ‘English bond’ is made of alternating courses of
stretchers and headers. There are many variants, such asthe
English ‘garden bond’ in which three courses of stretchers are laid
between single courses of headers. Whilethese names suggest
national differences, the bonds were, in fact, as popularamong
Dutch and Flemish builders as among British ones. Undoubtedly
immigrants broughtttheir favoured techniques, though the
corner detail of‘tumbled’ bricks used by Dutch builders on gables
was little used in Britain.

Buildings of fired bricks are frequently to be found in
communities where mud bricks are generally in use, and aresome
indication of relative prosperity. They may also bean indication
to caste, as well as class distinctions. In many parts of India,
where bricks have been fired fortwo millennia, only the Brahmins
andtheJats can afford pucca (or pakka) houses in brick. The
poorer members of even these higher castes may have unfired
brick kucha houses which have been rendered with thick layers of
mudto make them somewhat less vulnerableto monsoon rains.
Kucha houses predominate; in some villages only one house in
ten may be a puccafired brick dwelling, in other villages there are
noneatall. Nevertheless, fired brick is distributed overthe whole
country, made in an estimated 10,000 rural kilns which account
for allbut one per cent ofthe nation’s calculated output of 24
billion bricks (1974). In all probability this estimate ofthenumber
of kilns was low; in 1978, there were some 3,000 recognized brick
kilns in Sri Lanka alone, each producing an average 150,000 bricks
ayear, sufficient to build over 50,000 houses. But not without
costtotheconsumer, andaheavy onetothe environment: a
firing of 25,000 bricks can consumeforty foresttrees, arate of
deforestation in SriLanka alone of around 750,000 trees a year.’

trees, occursinthe Sudan, where fired bricks were introduced

One ofthe most common forms ofkiln isthe ‘clamp kiln’, or
pazawah, where the bricksthat areto be fired form theirown
kiln. They are stacked in ways that accommodate the fuel, permit
igniting and stoking, and circulatethe heated gases. The entire
structure is covered with athick layerof mud which, afterthe
firing, is broken offto expose the burnt bricks. Because ofthe
heavy consumption oftimber other fuels are also used: from
large quantities of rice husks in Indonesia to a mixture of cow
dung and coal cinders in parts of India.3°

The more efficient and much larger Bull’strench type ofkiln
is extensively used in India, where as many as four million people
are believed to be employed by the brickyards. Unlikethe clamp
type which often needs re-firingand hasto betotally dismantled
to obtain the bricks, thetrench kiln operates continuously. A
deep channel, roughly circular in plan, takes the bricks, which are
stacked so asto permitthe flow of air. Bricks are removed asthey
are fired, and fresh ones ready for firing are stacked in their place.
Fuelled from abovethrough holes in the covering clay layer, the
firetravels slowly around thetrench, a light chimney, made
perhaps from oil drums, being manually moved above it. Asthe
trench isin effect aring, this can be an unending process. A daily
output of 10,000 bricks is customary, and some brickyards inthe
fine clay areas of northern India cover many hectares.37

Although fired clays may still be porous, bricks are
resistantto water penetration and erosion bywind andsand so
thatttheir use is particularly suited to regions where these forms
of severe weathering are a problem. Even more susceptiblethan
thewallsto the effects ofthe weather istheroof. Fired clay, in

the form oftiles, is a widely distributed form of roof covering.

It is believed that, like so much in buildingtechnology, the use
of clay tiles stemmed from China, andthattheRomans were
responsible fortheirintroduction into Europe. Roman tiling
involved the use offlat, edge-lipped tegulae, overlapped in layers,
with half-cylinder imbrices covering the joints. Asimpler version
ofthe same principle employs half-cylindrical but slightlytapering
tiles laid with the concave surface uppermost, overlapped by
corresponding tiles with their convex surfaces visible, to give a
ribbed textureto the roof. Beingtapered they can slide over each
other, but only sofar; the close fit can even eliminatethe need
to use mortar, whilethe space between the curvatures inhibits
creeping action. They can bethrown on a potter’swheelasatall
truncated cone, which isthen halved, or even shaped, it is said,
on thethigh ofthe maker. Such ‘Spanish’ tile roofs are used from
China by way of India andthe Mediterranean tothe countries of
Latin America. They are sometimes made oftheyellow andred
terracotta clays, which are mixed with ground fired clayto reduce
shrinkage when they are fired.3® Like roofs that are clad in slabs
ofstone orslate, the claytileroofisaheavy one, necessitating
the use of substantialtimbers in the structure, even ifthe lowpitched roofs of Spain, Italy or Greece do not requirethe amount
oftimberthatthe steep pitches of northern Europe with their
flattiles demand. Many ingenious ways of spanning spaces

with the same inert stone, clay or brick from which the walls

are constructed have been developed in vernacular traditions.
However, the majority of dwellings are not built of asingle
material but of several, of which the many varieties ofwoods,
with their elasticity andtensile strength are especially important,
andthe organic, fibrous and widespread palms, bamboos

and grasses which, by reason oftheirsheerabundance and
renewability, are extensivelyemployed.

Resources that grow

Although they do not reflectthe evolution of architecture from
a single source, there are marked correspondences between
thearchitectural forms of many widely separated cultures.
Sometimes these similarities may be attributed to the dispersal
of peoples and their carrying of buildingtypes and technologies
with them; in otherinstances, to the diffusion ofthe building
types and methods themselves, through the influence of
contiguous or dominant societies. But, aswe have seen in the
widespread use of corbelled stone domes, which can possibly
be attributed to both ofthese forms of diffusion of material
culture, there is also evidencethat correspondingtechnological
solutions may evolve from the acquisition of similartools and
the use ofthesame materials.

Buildings do not inevitably take specific forms fromthe
presence of common traits orresources; as any woodworker is
well aware, there are many ways in which a couple of lengths of
timber may betrimmed, fashioned, smoothed, butted, socketed,
orjointed, and many means by which the qualities ofthe
material can be exploited. But whilethe nature and texture of
organic materials may vary widely from softwoods to hardwoods
and from resinousto fibrous, and whilethe dimensions in which
they are available also differ greatly from one contextto another,
certain common characteristics may befound. These may be
governed by a material’s growth, elasticity, mass and grain, not
excepting its porosity orresistanceto rot, heat orrain,andtothe
activities of animals or fungi. Ultimately, these properties will
influence the material’s effectiveness in use and will help shape
the ways in which they are employed to the best advantage.
Asaresult, there are techniques of preparation and assembly
that areto be found throughout the world, with many
fundamentals incommon, even when their details are localized.

1: An early photograph
ofthetraditional
Vaitupu house.
Pandanus thatchedroof and gables with
walls of woven coconut
palm pinnules. Tuvalu
(formerly Ellis island)

Pole structures

So we mayfindthatthestructural principles on which a building
iserected in aremote island in the Pacific are not necessarily
greatly dissimilar from those in other parts oftheworld. Takethe
tiny island of Vaitupu in Tuvalu (formerly the Ellice Islands) for
instance. Herethe principal building material isthe pandanus
palm, though milo and coconut are also employediinthe
traditional Vaitupu house. First, six oreightforked pandanus
posts would be deeply bedded in the soil, or ‘earthfast‘, with two
corner posts ateach endandthe remainder at six-foot intervals
onthesides. These provided the basic support for atypical
house, which would be about 24 ft long and 18 ft wide (7.5 by

5.5 m). The forked, or round-notched, poles received the
horizontal side poles, or wall-plates; tie-beams and end-plates
werethen placed across to complete the framework and were
lashed securely in place.

Rafter poles were cut soasto meet ataridge angle of
about 80 degrees, five or six on each side beingnotched and
pegged wherethey crossed, sothatthey could rest onthe wall
plateand extend beyond the wallto produce deep eaves. Aridge
pole, somewhat shorterthan the fulllength ofthehouseand
held in position by temporary scaffolding, was lashed beneath
the meeting oftherafters, and inthe larger houses was supported
by kingposts restingonthetie-beams. The shortened ridge
received rafters at each end, which consequently inclined inwards
below the apex ofthe main rafters, to produce an inclined gable
end. At intervals horizontal purlins were placed in position on
theroof slopes and a lattice of ribs of split pandanus laths was
then affixedtotherafterstotaketheroofthatch, a similar lattice
being madeforthe gable ends. Platforms were constructed
which rested on thetie-beams; they provided valued storage
space but reduced headroom attthesides.

Lashings were made from sennit, grass which was twisted
and plaited by the old men, as much as nearly 6,000 ft (2,000 m)
being used inthe building of one house. Young men would cut
and strip offthe bark from the poles, whilethewomen prepared
the materials forthatching. Fallen pandanus leaves were
gathered and pulled around a stickto smooth and flatten them.
Coconut leaf ribs were collected and split into wandsto which
the pandanus leaves were attached, by drawingthe stem end
overthewandandattachingittothe leafspearwith.acoconut
spine. Soon a deep fringe of pandanus leaves was fixedtothe
spineto make a lau. Thatchers on theroof sewedthe lautothe
purlins with barbed wooden needles, working from the eaves in
successive layers, with the shiny and rain-repellant surfaces
uppermost, the greaterthe overlapthe denser and more durable
wasthethatch. Although the scale and the materials were
different, it will benotedthatthe method of preparing and fixing
the layers ofthatch was similarto that used bythe Yanomamo
of Venezuela. The end gables were similarlythatched andthe
roof ridge protected by plaited mats of coconut leaf pegged
into position. Meanwhile, thewomen gathered coconut palm
branches with their leaves, or pinnules, attached. Split down
the middle and laid side by side, they produced a double layer
of pinnules which were woven to produce a mat with abroad
herringbone pattern. Strung in several rowsthe mats cladthe
external walls, the earthfast forked poles bracingthe mats
againstthe pressure ofthe winds.?

In some respects, we can compare the Vaitupu house with
that built bythe Maya, a Native American people halfway across
the world, in Yucatan, Mexico. The Maya build a dwellingthat is
supported bythree or four pairs of substantial earthfast posts of
oxcitinche orsome other Yucatan hardwood that is as resistant
to rot. Thesetakethe pachna or wall plates, the end-plates, and

Neither the Vaitupu northe Maya houses were elaborated:
the raffia palm poles were not squared in section, trimmed down
their lengths, orjoined with precisely chiselled morticeand
tenon joints. The nature ofthe building material inhibits such
refinements, forthe strength of the poles depends on their
cylindrical section; trimming would have weakened the elements.
Similarly, the poles were lashed, notjointed, though cusps and
hollows were sometimes cutto receivethe curved section ofa
pole cross-member. Lashings are strong and flexible, affording
resilience in high winds and in light seismic activity, without
threatening the stability ofthe structure. Morticejoints would
weaken the poles, while nails would have splitthem.

Though details ofthe construction may be different, the
builders oftimber houses alloverthe world would find much
inthe building ofthese two dwellingtypes, particularlythe
structure ofthe roof, which correspond with their own methods.
The wall plates, roof trusses, tie-beams and collars, rafters,
purlins and ridge arethe constituents of roof assemblies among
hundreds of societies. Specific peculiarities, such as inclined
end-gables, have been developed with ashortened ridge as
hipped roofs in some contexts, and as projecting peaked ridges
over more steeply inclined gables in others. Tothe casual
observerthe building might seem unsophisticated but building
craftsmen would recognize the skill exercised in optimizingthe
potential ofthe available materials.

Timber technologies
Wood in its various forms provides one ofthe most important,
longest-used, and most durable kinds of building material,

which combinestensile and compression strength with elasticity.

That so much house construction is in timber reflectsthe
widespread distribution offorests, which still coverathird of
the land surface. Tropical hardwoods occur in regions with an
annual rainfall in excess of 20 inches (50 cm) and a mean annual
temperature of 24°C, orabove. They are found inthe Central
American Amazon basin andtthe northern half of South America,
the coastal rain forests of West Africa and western equatorial
Africa, and extensively in India and southeast Asia. Many timbers
are capable of being intricately carved and most can beworked
with relatively simple hand tools. But in some ofthese regions
timbertechnologies have not been well developed, as forest
dwellers lacked the appropriate tools, but in southeast Asia
buildings of substantial construction in tropical hardwoods
areto be found in Thailand, Malaysia and Indonesia.

North ofthearid zones, temperate hardwoods, such as
birch and hickory, areto befound inthe eastern United States, in
both western and eastern Europe and the Black Sea region and in
Russia north ofthe steppes, thriving in areas of moderately high
rainfalland atemperate climate.*Coniferous forests lietothe
north, in the colder zones of Canada and the northern Pacific
coast of North America, central and northern Europe where, in
countries like Sweden or Finland some 60 per cent oftheland is
forested, and in a broad belt across northern Russia to the Bering
Sea. With immense stands offir, pine and larch meetingthe
birches at the southern limits, Russia has the largest timbered
regions of any country.5

Timber-walling using a horizontal log construction, or
blockbauto usethe German term, is one ofthe principalmethods

## S. 59

of building in wood, using its mass and dead weight, but not its
tensile properties. It consumes much more woodtthan timberframing, relativeto the volumes encompassed, but itmakes
good use ofthe insulatingand defensive advantages of solid
wooden walls. Naturally, timber-walling is to be found in heavily
forested, often mountainous regions, with low temperatures.
From Scandinavia traditional blockbau sweeps in abroad arc
through central and eastern Europeto the Balkans and the Tyrol;
another blockbau arc takes in vast regions of Russia, from Karelia
tothe eastern limits of Siberia. Bythe Black Sea and eastto
Nuristan and Tibet, timber-walled houses are also numerous;
afew even survivein)apan.

Whether thetimber wall is made of logs with or without
the bark, orsawn into thick planks in the saw-pit, or split
lengthwise and finished with a broad-axe or adze, the same
principle of ‘locking’ the layers oftimbers is applied. This ensures
thatthey are notthrust apart bythe pressure and weight of
succeeding layers, as is possible with lap joints. Each log or
timber ‘baulk’ (squared log) is ‘notched’ attheends, either above
or below (or sometimes both)tto receive othersthatare placed
atrightanglesto it, theirends projecting and crossingatthe

corners. Amongthe simplest ofthelocking methodsisthe
‘saddle’ notch by which each round log is seated in matching
round depressions. The ‘V-notch’ is similar, the objective being
to make atightfit, so straight pine logs are welcome. Thesill
beams ofthe loghouse which took the floor joists were normally
supported by stone piers or a plinth of stone but wherethis was
not available, heavy logs were used instead. Asthe logs were laid,
carehadto betakenthatthe naturaltaper ofthetimber from
the wider base ofthetrunk matched thetaper ofthe next log, by
being laid inthe opposite direction. Iftime permitted, the logs
would betrimmed until their diameterwas consistent. In Russia,
logs ofthe same size would be selected for any one building,
small for a single-roomed izba; larger and carefully adzed, fora
two-storeyed farmhouse. Many ofthelatter had an additional
storey intheatticroof, the walls being protected from snow by
deep eaves and a projecting peak. The purlin ends were protected
by decorative bargeboards below a sulptured terminaltothe
ridge-beam. Elaborately carved nilichkini, or window frames
often with shutters, were also displayed. The Russians were not
alonein this: even more windows and decorations wereto be
seen in Switzerland, particularly inthe Simmenthal region.7

This was less so oftthe Valais, however, where the Törbel farmers
appearto have had notimefor decoration.

The halving ofthetimbers with lap-joints permits atight
joint, and ifthe notch is literally halfthe width ofthe baulk -
which cuts into the heartwood - adjacent baulksfit closely,
though they could be knocked apart. Other, more complex joints
were possiblewith theflat baulksthat had been sawn in the sawpitoradzed and trimmed. Amongthese werethe half dovetail,
orthefull dovetail, thejointtakingthe form used in furniture
joinery, but on a large scale. Lockingjoints with arisingterminal
toeachnotch ensured that no logor baulk could beremoved or
could roll out. Wooden pegs were also used forthesame purpose
in this fundamentally nail-less construction. Many other joints
exist, one researcher identifying some seventy edge-joints used
by Finnish carpenters.® But even so there were gaps between
logs which had to be ‘chinked’ with wood shavings, earth, small
stones or moss. Notched joints produce a projecting cross-over
oftheends of each log; an advantage ofthelap and dovetail
jointsisthatthe ends ofthe baulks can beflush withthe walls, as
isevident in Moravia and Southern Bohemia, though in instances
wherethe interstices between the baulks have been packed and
plastered over, some smooth wall planes have been painted.?

Roofs of log buildings are varied. In Norway for instance,
there were basically three types: the rafter roof where pairs of
rafters were notched intothetop wall log, which acted as a wallplate; another form wheretthe logs ofthe gables receivedthe
ends of regularly spaced purlins and there were no rafters; and a
third method which employed elements of both - rafters resting
on a main ridge beam that was supported by the gable logs.'
These roofs were shallow in pitch and were plankedtotakesods.
In many areas inthe ‘blockbau’ arc, the majority ofthelog
houses are single storeyed, frequently under steep pitched roofs,
