Systems of logconstruction bytheir nature, tendto
produce rectangular plans buttthis does not result in monotony
becase there are so many variants, often in a single village.
Double cottages under one pitched roof areto be found in Poland,
while rows of two orthree long and narrow houses linkedtogether
areto be seen in Romania. Three-room houses with entry on
along side are frequent, but houses in many regions inthe
blocbau arcmay have arcades atthe side or gable end, occasional
L-plans or large entry porches. Within, the peasant houses
tended to be furnished sparingly, with a long table, upright
chairs, blanket chests and formerly in Scandinavia, woodenframed box beds, with sliding doors. Houses were heated by
large stoves, often fired and fuelled in the ‘black kitchen’ while
cookingtook place inthe ‘white’ kitchen, heated bythesame
furnace. Colour, texture and warmth were provided by cushion
covers, draperies, bed covers and curtains, rugs and carpets,
which were embroidered, sewn or woven bythewomenfolk."

When millions of people emigrated to North America in
the eighteenth tothe earlytwentieth centuries they brought
many ofttheirtraditions with them. Timber-walled construction
ofboththelogandthetrimmed plank types were introduced as
were several notching techniques. The source of logconstruction
has been tracedto the settlement ofthe New Sweden colony in
the Delaware Valley by Finns and Swedes in 1638. English and
Scots-Irish immigrants had no former experience ofthe method

## S. 60

114 Resources
that grow

butreadily adopted it in the forest regions of New England as

did German immigrants and many other ethnic groups in
Pennsylvania and further west inthe early eighteenth century.'*
Thoughtthethree million square miles of forest intheeastand
south produced untold numbers of log structures as settlers
cleared land for cultivation, there were few states in USA and
Canada that did not see some log construction. Whilethe
rectangular plan remained the norm, original types developed
including the so-called ’saddlebag’ house with a central chimney
anda‘pen’ on either side; the ‘dog-trot’ house, single storeyed
but with a central, dividing open breeze corridor betweenthe
living and kitchen quarters; and the I-house, with achimney each
end ofatwo-roomed rectangular plan."

Apart from the log structures of simplified European types,
other methods, such asthe French Canadian poteaux aux terre
earthfast technique were used. Vertical log construction was
occasionallyemployed though this, while using similar amounts
of timber, has some structural disadvantages. In Canada a
method known asthe Red River system was developed. Squared
posts hadeither achannel rebated in them orflanges attached
on either side, which took the ends of squared logs dropped into
them." This technique was also developed by Australian settlers
where itwas known as the ‘dropped log’ method, theends ofthe
round logs being adzed to fitthe rebate. Migrants to other parts
oftheworld-such as Polish settlers in Brazil, also brought log
construction with them, making this among the world’s most
widely distributed building methods.” Dependent asitison
considerable reserves of timber, and useful when clearing land,
it has lost favour among self-builders, though many loghoues
are still being constructed in the United States. Instead, the
‘balloon frame’ technique of light 2x 4 in machine-cut wooden
structures capable of beingtransported and speedily erected

largely replaced the log house. Light in weight, the balloon frame
is a development from thetradition oftimber framing which
dominated in those parts of Europe where log construction was
not employed.'

Timber-framed dwellings are constructed onthe ‘postand
beam' principle, in which the beam transfers vertical loads along
its lengthsto its supporting posts. Sometimestermed ‘“trabeated
architecture’ -from the Latin word trabs for beam -it exploitsthe
tensile strength oftimber. Doubling the length ofthebeam will
halvethe load that it can take; doubling its width and depth will
quadrupleits strength. Hence, beams of considerable thickness
are employed for larger spans, butthese inturn need strong
supports, eitherthicker and firmly connected with the beams, or
braced to reduce the bending effects. This requires joints which
will notweaken thetimbers but which are secure and will not
tendto come apart. Alongthe side walls ofaframed house a
number of ‘studs’ orintermediary vertical supports will often be
placed, the spaces between the studs being spanned by shorter
members. This can be seen in the houses of villages in many parts
of South Germany where thetimber-framed walls may have
studs at intervals ofthree feet or so. These may be spanned with
horizontal lengths oftimberwhich together produce a regular
cellular pattern into which the window frames fit.”

Many fine buildings have been preserved, some dating
from thefifteenth century, with tiers of floors each slightly
‘jettied’ over the level below. Whilethe technique may have been
one of cantilever on theends ofthefloorjoistswhere they
rested on the wall plate, the projecting upper level also helped
to protectthetimbers and exposed wall surfaces from the
weather. Aside from these grander examples, humbler farmhouse
dwellings offer many instructive examples offolk building, and
are still used today for their original purposes, even though

centuries may have passed since they were erected. Large spans
between thicker posts at wide intervals marked a succession of
bays in many farmhouses. Horizontal elements linked the vertical
posts and any tendency to lurch was counteracted by diagonal
bracing. Often the brackets and braces exposed on the walls
gave a lively sense of movement, and fanciful names such as
the Wilder Mann orthe Schwabische Weible (the Schwabian wife),
were invented forthe shapes they made. Itis possibleto see in
these modest farmstheeccentric orindividual work ofa carpenter
who had devised a system of his own, sometimes including
curving braces, apparently employed fortheir aesthetic effect as
well astheirfunctional purpose. Joints were frequently ‘halved’
(lapped), especially in Schwabia, but tenon joints were common
in other parts, and in both methods thetimbers were secured
with wooden pegs. Leftto project, they were driven further into
thejoint asthetimber shrank, so asto keep the structuretight.
Inthese systems the load-bearing structure istheframe andthe
building will stand, and can be roofed, even without solid walls.'
Ofitselfthe frame provides support, but no screening
from wind and weather; the spaces between the framing elements
must therefore be closed with an ‘infill’ which may be made of
wattles, interlaced around slender poles and strained against the
frame, or inserted into specially cut grooves and augered holes.
Awattle infill can be made weather resistant with mud ‘daub’
forced into the interstices, orthe spaces may be packed with
‘clay lump’, the gaps that open in the drying process can befilled
with wet clay and the whole mud-plastered over. Again, random
rubble-stone or brick 'nogging’ can be stacked in the boxes,
sometimes laid in short courses, but often diagonally orin
‘herringbone’ pattern sothatthe settlement ofthe stone only
leaves aspace atthetop ofeach boxthat can easily be closed
with clay or pebbles. Alternatively, the building may be ‘clad’

with an external covering of slats or planks nailed onto the frame
and, when time and materials are available, over the interior also.
Such cladding was often applied, though with less reason, to
timber-walled buildings as well, sothat external appearances are
sometimes deceptive, the surface treatment not necessarily
being an indication of the structural system.?°

Fachwerk, the German tradition oftimber-framed
building has many regional forms as has been noted before; in
Niedersachsen (Lower Saxony) the construction is more rigorous.
Even so, localized traditions are evident, from the ‘round villages’
ofthe Wendland, tothe Altes Landtothe north, ortheopen
plains, moorland and woods of Luneburg Heath. Farmsin the
latter region may be clad in board and covered by giant hipped
roofs meticulously thatched to protect the buildings inthe
winter. In most parts of Lower Saxony the farms have an axial
and rectilinear plan, with symmetrical gable facades and roofs, a
90-degree angle atthe ridge, sweeping in an unbroken linetothe
eaves. From the ground floorto the eaves may be 10 ftin height,
and30fttotheridge above. At one end wasthe farmer’s house,
which would includethe kitchen, a dairy, bedrooms and living
spaces, but an open hearth in the main aisled hall ofthe building
was often used by the family and farm workers duringtthe day. At
theopposite end ofthe building a pair of great doors, centrally
placed, opened on tothis main volume. Essentially a barn, it was
usedasa cart store, stables, access to the hay lofts or other
functions, according to the kind of farming pursued.

In Niedersachsen as in other parts of North Germany the
structural system was strong. From a base of stone-levelled
timber sills a range of posts was placed equidistantly acrossthe
gable ends and alongthe side walls. Short ‘rails’ orties placed
between the posts at identical intervals combinedtogivea
squared grid, throughout, An upper storey ortwo.andaloft within

thepitch oftheroof was slightly cantilevered atthegableendto
produce a ‘jetty', each joist being supported by a post. The great
span ofthe internal structure was carried by internal post rows
on either side, with an arcade plate on which the great tie-beams
were placed. This created aisles in the barn section subdivided
by bays which could be used, for example, for cattle stalls or feed
stores. Theends ofthetie-beams took the rafters which were
furthersupported by the posts of the outside walls. Within
thegrid of posts and short beams, wattle panels could be set,
daubed with mud and whitewashed, but ifit could be afforded
the spaces were filled with brickwork, laid in simple courses or
with occasional patterns. Most impressive-and intendedto
impress - werethe gable facades of successful fruit farmers.
The panels inthese displayed a variety of brickwork designs,
symmetrically arranged in basket-weave, chevron, stellarand
other patterns; as many as a dozen different shapes being
employed at least twice in a single facade, off-set in some
instances by paintingthe fachwerk white. On most farm buildings
ofthis multi-functional kind an inscription withthe date ofthe
building, thenames ofthe owner andtthe builder, and often a
Biblical quotation or other text, was chiselled and painted.
Compared with the North German timberttraditions, the
so-called ‘half-timbered’ buildings of England and Wales may
seem less ordered. This impression is duetothe considerable
variety in scale, forms, plans, roofs and wall framing patterns
that may be seen. In hundreds of small towns, villages, hamlets
and farmsteads from East Anglia through the ‘heart of England’
to the Marches and north-west Wales, building was traditionally
in oak or elm. This was worked while it was still ‘green’, or soon

after felling, when the material could be sawn and joints chiselled.

After asssembly thetimber occasionally twisted slightly or
warped, but oncethis had happened it hardened in position and

rooftruss

purlin
collar

stoneplinth

made no further movement, ensuring the stability oftheinfilling
of wattle-and-daub or brick ‘nogging’ in the ‘box’ frame. A boxframed house was one in whch allthe posts and beams, and
intervening corner braces and horizontal rails were jointed to
produce a firm and stable structure, but one inwhich the
elevations appeared as a loose grid. Alternatively, the walls might
have ‘studs’ orsupplementary posts at regular intervals often
just abrick-length apart (see p17).* Possibly introduced from
Normandy, ‘close studding’ isthe one system that might
justifiably be termed ‘half-timbered'.

Early box-framed houses were ofthe halltype, opentothe
roof, but laterthe majority of such hall-houses had additional
floors inserted inthem to produce a two-storeyed house.
Theflexibility ofthe system permitted the building of wings,
extensions and ‘outshuts’ with monopitch pentice, hipped or
gabled roofs. Roof structures were principally ofthetruss kind,
of principal rafters and tie-beams using triangulation principles
to provide stability. Acentral king post, a crown post or pairs of
queen posts risingfrom the tie beams may give further support,
butthe variety of gabled, hipped and half-hipped roofs, with
added dormer windows and other detailswere a challenge to
the carpenters, which they met with ingenuity and skill.

Inthewest ofthe country, but not in East Anglia, an
earlier, but virtually unique structural system was widely used.
Related to the A-frame, the ‘cruck’ consisted of a pairof wooden
blades, in many instances derived from a single tree-trunk split
in two, one blade being the mirror ofthe other. Joinedtogether
atthe apexes to support the ridge, two pairs of crucks defined
a‘bay'. Abenttree could provide curved blades which, when
opposed, gave more head-room in a house. Alternatively, jointed
crucks could beconstructed, but both types generally hadatie or
‘collar’ beam which helped stabilizethe structure, the ends ofthe

Theoretically, organic materials such aswood are
renewable; good management ensures that stockstto replenish
resources are grown ataratethat provide a continuing supply.
Many oftthe earliertechniques have declined in the industrialized
countries; coppicing for example, inwhich smallwoods of young
trees-hazel, oak, chestnut, orash -are planted closely so asto
produce long straight poles, cropped, cut, sorted and stacked for
different building purposes. Though the practice has declined
in Britain, coppices are carefully nurtured in eastern Europe and
the forested parts ofthe Middle East.?7 There are, however, many
desert, steppe, prairie and veldt regions where there are few or no
trees. Yettrees do not constitutethe only organic and renewable
natural resource for building and many societies inthese regions
have discovered the structural possibilities of the grasses.

Building in bamboo

Popular usage distinguishes bamboos from grasses or palms,
butthey all have fibres embedded in pithy tissue, the bundles of
tissue developing fromthegrowingtip. Nevertheless, for practical
purposes, the bamboos constitute a recognizable group notable
forthe convenient size and weight ofthe‘culms’ ornatural
lengths, that are divided by nodes making cross-walls which give
them a high strength-to-weight ratio. The hardestttissues are on

19:Acommon bamboo 20,22: Thetriangular

detail. The supporting elements ofthe
post has been cusped Bafut roofframe are
above the culmtotake prefabricated onthe

aroof purlin. Rafters groundonthehouse
areheldbytheridge platform (20) and
ofthick bamboo raised into position

the outside, producing a strong, cylindrical shell which can be
cut up, sawn, or chopped into suitable lengths, but which can
also be splitto produce half culms, and split-peeledto make
strong binding materials. There is no barkto remove, virtually no
waste, and bamboo can be worked with a machete. But bamboos
vary: there are some 700 species throughout the world-over
200 in Latin America alone and they may grow in tropicaland
temperate climates from sea-level to over 13,000 ft (4,000 m). The
range means that some are stronger than others. Generallythe
thicker culms are good for compression, whilethethinner culms
have moderate elasticity and considerable strength. Starch and
moisture on theinside pith layertendtto attractfungiand
damaging insects, like the so-called ‘shot-borer’ beetle in India,
orthe ‘powder-post’ beetle in Puerto Rico. Buttthis is not always
the case; a giant bamboo, which grows upto 90 ft (27 m) in
height in Colombia, Ecuador and Peru, is very resistantto both.
The 6 in diameter culms, with their nodes at frequent intervals,
provide a substantial building material for supporting house
posts; narrower bamboo culmsaare used forthe rest ofthe
construction of houses.

Half sections of culms can be used as roof coveringin a
fashion similartothat of Spanish tiles. It is customary to lashthe
jeints, passingthe rattan or bamboo strip splinesthrough holes
bored just beyond the node; nails can be used but are inclined
to splitthe bamboo lengths. Pegs and dowels of narrow-gauge
bamboo or sections ofthick culm-wall are also used in jointing.
Harder woods may be used fortheflooring, asthey are less
inclined to spring, but they are more liable to attack by insects
than bamboo which, in some houses, may last thirtyor more
years.2?Though bamboo houses can be plastered with a mixture
of mud, dung and chopped grass, in many parts oftheworldthe
side walls are of woven bamboo splines, which have been split

from the culm andtthen beaten flat before weaving. Bamboo is
extremely strong, and makesfor a relatively light construction.
Where other woods are available, it may becombined with a basic
frame of heaviertimbersto make a more durable structure and
onethat can assume greater height, the bamboos or raffia palms
being used as studs, cladding or bracing, and stiffeningthe
structure in the process.

Amongthehilly slopes and grassland pasture of Western
Cameroon, which rise from a plateau region some 4,500 ft above
sea level, lives acomplex mix of related cultures numbering well
over half a million people. Efficient farmers, they raise small
livestock and grow a wide variety of food crops includingyams,
okra, watermelons, millet, bananas and, more recently, potatoes,
maize and beans. Bamboos, raffia palms and many slendertypes
oftree providethe materials forthe substantial houses built by
most ofthese peoples. Many livein compact villages, often centred
aboutthe courts of divine kings. Inatypical example, the Fon of
Bafut, chief of some 20,000 subjects, lives in alargecompound
with his many wives, each wife having a separate dwelling house.
Inthe main village and in others nearby, the inhabitants live in
similar houses, square in plan with pyramidal roofs.

When a new house iisto bebuilt,themalehead chooses a
site on his land and seeks the assistance of others in his lineage
to clearthe land and prepare a 12sq ft (3.5 sq m) site. Raffia poles
are brought fromthe bush and trimmed with a cutlass to
gftlengthsforthe uprights and 12 ft lengths for horizontal
members. Anotable feature ofthe buildings isthe extensive use
of prefabrication, orthe separate construction of component
parts ofthe building prior to assembly. Walls are preformed on
theground with a double layer of vertical uprights at intervals
ofafew inches clenchingthefive or six horizontals which are
drilled, pegged and lashed with raffia into position. Corner poles

are erectedandthe wall panels raised by communal effort and
lashed securely. Above the walls a large, prefabricated platform
is raised by ateam of eight men, its deep eaves extending2 or3 ft
beyond the walls.3°

An opening is cut intothe eavesto permit accesstothe
loft space, later used for storing fuel, the ceiling being solidIy
made of bamboo poles. Four triangular elements arethen
constructed on the ground, with sides of 16 ft (5m), the poles
tapertothe apex at which point purlins arethreaded between
two layers of rafters. Hoisted into position they are lashed
securelyto make a massive pyramidal roof which isfurther
supported by perimeter poles. Other members of the lineage are
invited to participate in the gathering of grass and the making of
sheaves forthatchingthe roof. Thethatching process itself, with
the sheaves packed tightly in layers between the purlins, is done
byafull-timethatcher. The grass is beaten until athick watertight shield isensured andthe edge istrimmed with a cutlass.
Withtheroofthatched, the walls are heavily plastered with
puddled mud, the heat from a central fire in the house drying
them whilethe owner builds pole-frame beds and storage
shelves. Later, asecond, lower ceiling is constructedto take dried
corn and palm kernels. Throughout the process of building, the
house-owner plies the helpers from his lineage with food and
palm wine, holds feasts forthethatch-gatherers, and forthe
blessing ofthe house when the work is completed. The grand,
even extravagant buildings ofthe Bafut, Bamilik&, Bamoun,
Tikar and other related peoples of Cameroon’s grasslands and
highlands, are only possible because there is an abundance
of suitable resources. The layers of vertical, horizontal and
diagonally lashed bamboos and raffia poles, together withthe
carved timbers and deeptthatch on the buildings, consume
considerable quantities of materials. These ensure, however,

the relative durability and stability of the structures; rebuilding

Fast-growing reeds, canes and bamboos are readily
replenished, some bamboos growing atthe rate ofas much as
2.r3ft(nearlyametre) a day in the wet seasons. Bamboos grow
widelythroughout the world, with high concentrations in Central
and South America and in India, Burma, China, Japan, and
Indonesia where stands of 100 ft (35 m) in height are far from
uncommon. Villagers may grow sufficient bamboo for
theirbuilding needs on their own plots and with yields as great
as twentytonnes a hectare, the problems of durability and
maintenance are largely offset by the fertility ofthe bamboos.?

Reeds and grasses

Because oftheir attenuated form and length-to-width ratio,
grasses alone are not suitable as structural materials. They have
great flexibility and elasticity, but little compression strength and,
when severed from their roots, insufficient rigidityto support
theirown weight. Bundled and tied, however, certain grassescan
be used structurally when other materials are notto hand, asthe
reed dwellings ofthe Marsh Arabs dramatically demonstrate.
Technically and botanically classified as grasses, reeds which grow
in water or marsh have strong straight stems and are used for at
leasttemporary shelters in parts of Africa, India and Bangladesh.
Inthe broad marshes between the confluence oftheTigrisand
Euphrates, north of Basra in Iraq, the qasab reed grows to over

20 ftin height. Qasab provided the basic building material ofthe
Ma’dan, whosettribes are scattered through some 6,000 square
miles of marsh. Though buildingtechniques differedto some
extent between the variousttribes ofthe central and eastern
marshes, a house might be raised on an island iftthe builders were
fortunate, or on an artificial one ifthey were less favoured. Inthe

latterinstance, a stockade of high reeds would beforced into

the area of marsh water chosen forthesite, and morereedsand
rushes gathered and transported in the ubiquitous high-prowed
mashaf canoes. Reeds and mud gathered at low water were
packed intothespace untilthewater ceasedtoriseabovethereed
mattress thus formed; the process was repeated until eventually
afirm base, or dibir, forthe dwelling was established and fresh
reeds of 20 ft (6 m) in length was securedtoform the basic
structure. These were thrust singly intothe reed mattress atan
angle of30 degrees away from the intended enclosed area, forced
into it until they were secure. Clusters of reeds werethen bundled
together and bound with straw ropes at frequent intervals along
their length. An irregular number ofthe bundles, from fiveto.as
many astthirteen, were arranged at around 4to 6ft (1.5to2m)
intervals, liketheribs of a skeleton.

The differing lengths ofthereeds produced a naturaltaper
in the bundles which were drawn togetherto produce a graceful
parabolicarch held in tension. Standing on atripod ofreed
bundles, one ofthebuilderstiedthe overlapping bundles with
straw lashings. When the row of arches was complete, more
slender bundles of reeds weretiedto the archesto act as purlins
at 2ft 6in (0.5 m) intervals. Straight columns of bundled reed,
two orfour innumber stood acrossthe openings at each end,
on which were hungthe reed matsthat closethe gables. Large
woven mats made from split gasab stalks cladthe whole
structure in overlapping layers secured by narrow bundles tied
through to the purlins. The house was now complete, but some
had more elaborate doorways facing Mecca, and open screens
to admit light but retain privacy. Amuch larger mudhif, or
guest house, was built in the village settlements. Some ofthese
reached considerable proportions; an 18 ft (6 m) high mudhif
ofeleven arches and 60 ft (18 m) in length would be common.

Wilfrid Thesiger reported atwenty-one arch mudhif 120 ft (36, m)
in length. Such mudhifs were fastidiously built, with as many as
seventy rafters and overfifty overlapping covering mats, the ends
elaborately patterned with open-weave mats.

Tending their herds of water buffalo, raising cereal crops of
rice, making reed mats for sale in the markets of Basra, the Marsh
Arabs maintained a way of life that lasted millennia. The sitras,
or winter shelters for their buffaloes, the raba, part dwelling, part
guest-house, the merchants’ houses and othertemporary or
permanent structures ofthe marshes were also built. Among
the most striking examples of ingenuity and skill in building,
they were developed intheface ofthe severest limitations of
resources. The marsh settlements were not without problems;
the lack of sanitation meant thatthe water was contaminated
and dysentry was rife. Bilharzia affected most ofthe population,
and seasonal flood waters which could rise within the houses,
added to the discomfort of a hardy but independent people.3
Since the Irag-Iran war, plansto drain the marshes have been
implemented and littleremains ofthis ancient tradition.

Living at a very different altitude but in equally harsh
climatic conditions the Aymara of Bolivia make their living
from fishing in Lake Titicaca, at 13,000 ft (3,800 m) the highest
lake in the world. Best known fortheir banana-shaped boats
made from tapering bundles of tutora reed, skilfully bound
together with grass ropes, they also make reed dwellings in the
dense tutora beds. Afew supporting poles hold uptherroof,
which is spanned with wide mats of reed stalks, and takes a keellike shape over which the cold lake winds blow. Reeds grow in
marshy, well-watered places and therefore, can only be used in
limited circumstances. Grasses, however, areto befound in
profusion in most parts oftheinhabited world, luxuriating
even where other vegetable materialsthat could be utilized for

building are in short supply-the veldt, steppes, pampas and
savannahs amongthem. Grass, like reed, is an excellent cladding
materialwhich can be bunched or sewn, or even pulled up bythe
rootsto provide athick cover. In consequence, it is probablythe
most extensively used of all roof cladding materials, thatch being
relatively lightto handle, resistant to water penetration, and cool
because of its poor conduction of heat.

In Western Europe changes in ruraltechnology have
adversely affected the production of resources used in building,
such asthatch. Until the 19505 the cutting of wheat byhand
with sickleand scythe, and itsthreshing over a frame so as not
to flatten and bruise the straw, ensured that it could be used for
thatching. But, with the extensive, and eventually universal, use
of mechanical combine-harvesters the wheat straw was broken
and of no usetothethatcher. Nevertheless, in peasant societies
in Europe and in many other parts of Africa, the Middle East,
and Asia, cereals and grasses are carefully grown, harvested and
stored for roofing, while marsh reeds are also widely used.

In Rajhastan and Uttar Pradesh in northern India,
temporary shelters are made by grass and straw gathererswho
supply materials forthethatching of mud-built houses. By

bundlingandtying canes and thick grasses, a light framework
is constructed against which thatching grass is secured with
straw.3' For more durable structures, thin sticks or light poles
provide the necessary rigidity. This isthe case withthe domical
indlu or ‘grass hut’ of many ofthe Southern Bantu peoples, such
asthe Zulu, Nguni, and Xhosa who migrated south from the
lakeland regions in the sixteenth century.®>The Ngwame, a Natal
Nguni sub-group living in the foothills ofthe ‘Little Berg’ closeto
Lesotho, are cultivators of mealies. Living at an altitude of 4,500
ft (1,400 m) and in extremes of temperaturethat range between
95° F(35°C) to below freezing, they endure a difficult climate
made more hazardous by sudden gales. Their huts aretherefore
very expertly, if laboriously, constructed. Insummary, the hut
comprises a close framework of overlapping arches, a layer of
mats which keepstthe inside clean, an insulating grass layer,
another layer of mats, a water-repelling layer of grassthatch and
a protective net.

Its external appearance gave littleindication ofthe
complexity of its construction, but this dwelling was not built
without cost in time. Werner Knuffel, who carefully recorded the
whole process, calculated that the building took sixty-five work

daysto build, two-thirds ofthework being done bywomen, and

was used.3° Few techniques of building so ingeniously exploit the

Coping with climate
Dwellings are builtto serve a variety of functions, but one ofthe
most important isto create living conditions that are acceptableto
their occupiers, particularly in relation to the prevailing climates.
Buildings do not control climate, which, apart fromthe wind or
sunshadowthatthey may cast, remains largely unaffected. But
from within the dwelling can modify the internal climate, even
though itis affected bythe external conditions. The materialsthat
are used, theformstheytake, thevolumesthey enclose, andthe
services that are installed may all contributeto the ‘micro-climate’
thata house generates. This is not always preciselywhatthe
occupants require intemperature, ventilation or relative humidity.
When wellight afire or, ifwe are amongthe privileged
minority, turn onthe central heating, we are in one way or another
respondingtothe external climate, but we are also attemptingto
create acceptable internal conditions. Though we spend much of
ourtimeliterally ‘out of doors’, we also spend a great deal oftime
inside; more in some weather conditions than in others, forin
some regions the climate changes not only seasonally, but
daily, even hourly. Seldom are both nighttemperatures and day
temperatures equallyto our liking: evenings are chilly, mornings
are fresh, the noon sun too hot. Virtually every type of dwelling
serves to assist us in adjustingto one or many aspects ofthe
climate. Onetypeis effective in keepingthe wind off, another
for keeping its occupants cool in blazing heat. Dwellings may
not always be equally good atcoping with a variety of climate
conditions; butwhentthey do it servesto emphasizethe old
adage that the house is ‘warm in winter and cool in summer.

Internal environments
Often internaltemperatures or relativehumidity are described in
terms of ‘comfort’; though comfort values differ widely between

cultures. Some arctic or desert peoples survive, apparently
contentedly, in conditions which would be exceedingly
uncomfortableto asouthern European. Immigrants from

the Caribbean or East Africa maytakealongtimeto adjust

to the ‘damp cold’ of northern latitudes. These differences in
attitudesto comfort are partly physiological, butthey are also
psychological and cultural. As cultures change, attitudesto
comfort conditions change also: today’s descendants ofthe
nineteenth-century pioneer familieswho braved the rigours of

a Kansas winter orthe stiflingheatofasummeronthe Great
Plains, insist on centrally-heated and air-conditioned officesand
houses. As previously noted, many homesteaders in Kansas and
Nebraska recalledthatthesod houses and schools inwhichthey
lived and learned were climatically acceptable.'

Itwas principallyduetothethermal properties ofthe
turves themselves, thatthesod houses maintained acomfortable
temperature. As buildings constructed of soilthey sharedthe
characteristics oframmed earth, adobe block and clay buildings
alloverthe world. Exposedtothessun’sraysthe external wall
surfaces absorbed heat, but as soil transmits heat slowlythe
interiors were likely to remain cool duringthe day. Laterthe walls,
acting as a heat-store, gently transmittedthe warmth intothe
interior, keepingthetemperature pleasant enough at night. While
this may not convince anyone to return to livinginthe ‘soddys’,
there is still a lesson to be learned. The micro-climate of aturf
building was frequently achieved by ‘passive’ means, without
theconsumption of fuel and the expenditure of energy that many
modern technologies necessitate, though when schoolrooms
needed heating in winter, hay-burning stoves were used.?

This is notto say that traditional systems of climate
modification do not use solid fuels; obviously, many inthe cold
andtemperate regions do, at least seasonally. The fuel was, and

in many regions still is, readily available as fallen logs, off-cuts
and reclaimed building materials, as may be seen stacked under
shelters and lean-to roofsthroughout Eastern Europe. Mention
has already been made ofthelargetiled stovesto be found inthe
Russian izba, and also in most regions in Scandinavia and central
Europe wherethey were in widespread use bythefourteenth
century; and ofthe hugefireplaces with their bread ovens and
meat-curing cupboards to be found in Dartmoor and many other
parts oftthe British Isles two centuries later. There was a revolution
inthe vernacular with the introduction ofthefireplaceandthe
chimney, which were sometimes placed in the centre ofthe
dwelling and sometimes, as inthe North American I-house,

at both ends. Other forms of heating systems have also been
mentioned, such asthenorthern Chinese kang, or heated bed
plinth, to which we might add the huogiang, or heated wall. This
issomewhat akin to the ondol or hypocaust underfloor heating
system used iin Korea.

Building materials selected from the natural resources
availableto different culturesto build their houses can be
examined asto the structural properties of materials selected,
and the methods employed in usingthem for construction. They
may also be considered in terms of their climatic suitability and
performance, and the means by which cultures have utilized
theirthermal properties, or have devised methods or structures
to modify climate. Broadly speaking, most natural materials
used for mass construction have comparable properties with
those ofturves. Densely packed material, such as rammed earth,
functions as a heat store and hence slowly transmitsthe heat
from outside to inside, where it is radiated into aroom. There are
many qualifierstothis principle, however, dependent onthe
season, the intensity of solar radiation, the daily length oftime
that.a wall is exposed to it, the placement of the wall in relation

2: Wood stocksfor 3: An Inuit family sits

house. Small windows, ofsealskin. Cooking
abalconyand helps raisethe internal

climate modifiers

temperature

to themovement ofthe sun, the curvature or inclination ofthe
exposed surface, partial ortemporary shading and many other
minorfactors which relateto the specific circumstances. Even
thetype of soil, the size ofthe particles, the degree of compression,
thethickness ofthe wallandthe amount of retained heat prior
to re-exposure will affectthethermal performance of an earthconstructed dwelling.*

Many ofthe same principles apply to stone, though kinds
of stone differ more markedly in their variety. Clearly, what is
advantageous in some temperate climates may be unwelcome iin
tropical conditions; thetransmission of heatthrough the walls
of a building is often undesirable inthetropics, ifthenights are
not cold. Re-radiation ofthe heat received may be preferred, and
adome or vault which presents a larger external surfacethan a
flat roof, allows more space for heatto rise and more surface for
radiation into night air. Mass timber walls of horizontal logor
blockbau construction absorb and retain heat more effectively
than timber frames with panel infill of wattle-and-daub.5

The forms of buildings, which have already been discussed
with regard to the materials used in their construction, may also
be examined fortheir climatological performance. Few may be
as remarkable as Inuit, who can raisethe internaltemperature
of an iglu by means of low-level ‘cold-sink’ approach tunnels,
insulating linings of caribou hide and sealskin over the snow
wallandthe iqlig bench, the warmth of blubber lamps and other
devices, as were summarized in Chapter One. Extreme conditions
require extremely efficient solutions, the great A-frame structures
in Malakwa and other parts of British Columbia which receive
heavy snowfall, being a case in point. It has been shown howthe
greatthatched roofs oftheGokayama region in Japan were
similarly builtto shedthe heavy snows, whileaccommodating
the silk-worm industry; in the vernacular there is little likelihood

131

## S. 69

ofwasted space. Other instances may not be as dramatic,
butthey are just as appropriate and effective, likethe deep
and overhanging eaves of Alpine and central European houses,
andthe devices used to protect gables and walls. Certain
examples, which have already been noted with referenceto
other features, may warrant further consideration in relation
to their climatic context.

Take pile structures, for instance. We have seen howthese
may beimmersed inthe beds of lakes, rivers orthe sea-shore,
often for ease of accessto an important resource, such asfish,
andthe desire to preserve and protectthe stock. Defence against
bitinginsects and mosquitos, against snakes and animals, and
against hostile tribes or colonists, are amongthe contributory
factorsto the decision to build houses on piles.® Pile dwellings
also have particular thermal properties, though these differ
according to where in the world they are located. In cold or wet
climates, orthosethat are both cold and wet, thecreep ofcold
from permafrost, or of risingdamp, may do damageto structures
built on the ground and make internal conditions unpleasant.
Building on piles allows ventilation beneathtthe buildingand
reduces direct contact with the ground. Pile dwellings are often
to be found in tropical climates, and in the forests ofthe Amazon
orsoutheast Asia. Herethe ventilation effect is also important,
butthe piles may be raised much highertto gain the benefit of
greater air circulation.

Though we may accept, oreven experience, the climatemodifying qualities of some buildingtypes, the question remains
asto whether there is supporting evidence of their success, or
failure. Only a small percentage of buildingttraditions have been
subjected to scientific analysis: the corbelled stone dwellings of
Europe and southeast Asia are one instance. Inthe hills ofJudea
and Samaria, 10,000 corbelled stone huts were reported by ZviY

D Ron, who measured the climatic performance ofanumber
ofthem. In this region, daytime highs may be as much as 95° F
(35°C) butthenightsarecool, droppingto 66° F(18°C),sothe
‘diurnal-nocturnaltemperature range‘, ortheextremes within
twenty-four hours, lies between 54°F (12°C) and 59°F(15°C).
Humidity, which affects comfort conditions, may range from as
littleas 30 per cent duringthe dayto as much as 90 per cent at
night. After summer readings weretaken inthehutsandthen
analysed itwas foundtthatthe daytime internal temperatures
were much lower than outside- as much as 52° F (11°C) difference
in the July readings. But at night thetemperature was higher
than that outside, where nocturnaltemperature ranged between
36°F(2°C) at its lowest, and not higherthan 50°F (10°C), because
ofthetransmission of heat internallythrough the mass ofthe
stone walls. In daytime the humidity levels were 30 per cent
or more higherthan outside, but they were lower at night,
becoming uncomfortable only inthe high summer. InJulyand
August, however, many farmers raisedtthatched, open-sided
sleeping shelters on top oftheir corbelled stone huts.?

Inthis, asinthe other examples, thetypical shelter
performed well in relation tothe climate for most oftheyear,
but during extreme periods of heat or cold beyond its range, an
alternative structure was built. Itis analogousto atemporary
change in one’s customary clothing-thewearing of a balaclava
helmet perhaps, during a bitterly cold spell inan otherwise
temperate climate. Unlike hats or gloves, parts of buildings
cannot easily beworn or discarded to suit climatic changes,
though there are plenty of examples oftemporary appendages
which meet occasional needs-the awning overthe shopfront
or window, the curtain hung overthe inside ofthe doorto reduce
draughts. Most structures are too substantial, and often too
permanent, for major changes to be practicable; instead, the

buildings aremadetorespondtothe demands ofthe particular
climateto which they are subject. Such responses, of course,
varythroughoutthe world and specific solutionsto climatic
problems are often extremely localized.

Tents and shelters ofthe nomads may seem elementary
as structures and spartan as dwellings, but in terms of climate,
they are often most effective. Part oftheirsuitability is related
totheirimpermanence; asatentispitched ora brush shelter
constructed it can be placed to exploitthe prevailing conditions
tothe greatest advantage. It can be placed undertrees for
additional shade, sited to make the best use of breezes, ortucked
in the lee of a hillto evade fierce winds. This flexibility, which
can makethe best ofthe slightest undulations of landform
also extends to the structure itself-tent walls can beraised or
removed, openings can be made in brush shelters and others
closed, and a new site can be selected ifthe conditions warrant it.
Nomads may have a high tolerance of extreme conditions out of
necessity, forthere hasto be a ‘trade-off’ between their freedom
to moveandthe portability of their dwellingtypes. Many such
peoples have morethan one dwellingtype accordingtothe
season, butinthetransitional period between the change of
seasons the shelter may become uncomfortableto inhabit. As
we have seen, nomadic economies do not support very large
numbers of people, though inthe environments they inhabit,
nomads are often the only kinds of social group that can survive.
These interesting and instructive exceptions apart, the world’s
populations are sedentary. How then do peoples living in
permanent dwellings cope with climate?

Living in comfort
Comfort conditions, orthethermal balance between the body
andthat oftheimmediate environment, depend on many

factors. Some ofthese are biological, caused bythe metabolic
rate, the skin temperature andtthe capacity of perspiration to
bring down body heat. Others relate to the variable conditions
of agiven environment, such asthetemperature of theair, its
humidity, the radianttemperature ofthe sun, the ground, or
buildings, the movement of air, and so on. Generally, however,
when the skin temperature rises above 93° F(34°C) or drops
below 84° F (29°C) we begin to feel discomfort:® a moderately
clothed individual in humid conditions who is not engaged in
physical activityand who has a skin temperature of 85°F(29.4°C)
is likelyto feel comfortable, but in an arid area with low humidity,
tolerance may be higher. These, however, are figures for skin
temperatures and not forthe environment; what has been
termed the ‘effective temperature‘, takes into consideration

the combined factors of airtemperature, humidity andaair
movement in determiningthe limits for comfort. Experiments
in equatorial regions have indicatedtthat above an effective
temperature 0f79°F (26°C) some discomfort can befelt, and
that human efficiency drops noticeably in higher temperatures.?
Above 104° F (40°C) discomfort is experienced, yet much higher
levels are endured by the Australian aborigines who adjust
themselves to the heat by wearing virtually no clothes, building
wiltjas or sideless sheltersto provide shade, and remaining
inactive in the hottest parts ofthe day.'

Clothing is, for many societies, thefirst defence againstthe
weather, shedding rain, keeping feet dry and affording insulation
with layers of wool or fur. But in the African Sahel, where daytime
temperatures average 81°F-85°F (27°C-29°C), clothing is often
minimal because contact withthe skin causes discomfort and
interferes with the process of sweating by whichthe body
temperature is regulated. Where clothing is worn inthe desert,
as itis by Bedouin peoples, the white burnous reflectsthe rays of

storms and haze often obscurethe sun. There are hot seasonal

Courtyard climate

In some areas where nomads move with theirflocks other peoples
are sedentary farmers. Thisisthe casein the Matmata mountains
ofTunisia, North Africa, where Bedouin tents aresited in arough
and scorched terrain that is dotted with depressions-the
subterranean dwellings, or haush, ofthe Matmata Berber.
Essentially, these are shafts, some 35-50 ft (10-15 m) in diameter,
excavated intothe red sandstone and marlto a depth of 16-20 ft
(5-6 m), sometimes approximately circular, sometimes more
square in plan. Acurvingflight of rock-hewn steps oraramped
tunnel, offwhich are stores and donkey stables, leads down from
thedesert surfaceto the bottom ofthe shaft. The haush usually
hasa cistern to oneside and some have a well; cess-pits are also
to befound, serving several families. Openingstunnelled intothe
haush walls open out to bottle-shaped, barrel-vaulted chambers.
Often attwo levels, the upper chambers are used as cereal

stores. Furthertunnels have sleeping recesses and nichesto
accommodate personal belongings. Inside, the room temperature
is pleasant and when the desert sands become cold at night,
withinthechambers there islittle fluctuation. Asthesun passes
over, theshading onthewallstravels roundtherough textured
surface. The shaft acts as a ‘thermal lung’ exchanging warm air
with cool airin the course of twenty-four hours. Sun-heated
surfaces act as a heat-store slowly transmittingtheirwarmth
totherooms within sothat atnighttheinteriors are keptatan
ambient temperature. This principle, arrived at by excavation

in Matmata, isalso the principle behind the courtyard house."

Desert settlementstend to be clusteredtogether and
urban forms of compact housing are common throughout
Southwest Asia, as well as in many parts ofthe dry savannah
regionsto the south ofthe Sahara. The muslim preference for
domestic seclusion, particularly ofthewomen, plays an
important part inthe organization ofthevillages andtowns,
butthe climate also exerts powerful constraints on building.
Defence against solar radiation starts with the orientation ofthe
building, which is sited so asto expose as little as possible of its
external surfaces to the intensity ofthe sun. This is particularly
the case with the roof, which receives the highest levels of solar
radiation and so should have the minimum possible surface
commensurate with the necessary spans ofthe internal spaces.
Flat roofs or shallow domes are usual, the former built in several
layers of pole, mats and palm fronds, and coveredwith athick
dressing of compacted earth sothatthe heat gain is only slowly
transferred to the interior. Dense, thick walls ofstoneand mud
are invariably built, and houses are constructed close together,
casting deep shadows over passage-ways and lanes, andonthe
walls of adjacent buildings. There are few openings in the outside
walls, andthe houses frequently face in towards courtyards.
Though this can be attributed to the need for seclusion it is also
because large openings would admit direct sunlight and heat."

Courtyard houses have an ancient history: examples have
been excavated at Kahun, in Egypt, that are believed to be 5,000
years old, whilethe Chaldean city of Ur, dating from before 2000
BC, also comprised houses ofthis form. Essentially, the courtyard
house, which consists of rooms on three or all sides of an open
atrium, is associated with Arab culture, but its distribution
extends between Sal& and Marrakesh in Moroccotothe west, and
Indiatotthe east where the haveli, or atrium house, is common in
the cities developed under Moghul influence, such as Haridwar,

Jaipurand Ahmedabad. Others areto befound as farsouth as
Dar es Salaam and north in Bukhara, Russia.” Rural houses ofthe
Egyptian fellahin or ofthe peasants in Central Iraq, for example,
revealthe basic forms ofthe courtyard plan: long, singlestoreyed rooms on three sides of ayard, thefourth being closed
byawallas high asthe buildings. Constructed of mud, the walls
arethickestwherethey are exposed tothe sun."

Much attention has been givento the elaborate houses
built around therriads, or garden courts, ofthe wealthy merchants
of Baghdad, for whom the most lavish forms were developed. But
the dar, the paved court typical of the houses of working people,
is not only more numerous but displays the essential elements of
thecourtyardtype and its efficiency asa climatically appropriate
building. Inthe narrow and angled streets and alleys ofthe older
quarters ofthecitythe houses are hidden beyond blank brick and
timber walls. Onlythe decoration and scale ofthe upper-storey
window bays, and the elaboration ofthe portals indicatethe
relative status ofthe occupants. Entry is gainedthrough a heavy,
carved door which opens onto.a reception corridor, whichturns
through aright angle, concealingthe courtyard beyond. Aguest
room offtheentrance corridor is sited over a basement, the sirab,
which is used as asummer room. Servicerooms, akitchen, and
alarge domed and vaulted living room also surroundthe open
space. Oppositethe guest room, isthe iwan, or covered dining
loggia. Columns support the tarma, a balcony room above used
for light meals and sleeping, which sometimes has an additional
salon that may overlookthe street. Aroundtthecourtyardatthe
upper level runs a gallery with ornamental balustrade which
gives accessto the bedrooms. Often constructed with atimberframe system, these rooms are segregated; on onesideare
quarters for the male members ofthe family, onthe otherthe
'harem for women and children.’ Baghdad houses are closely

Atrium houses with local variations areto befound in
Arab and Persian communitiesthroughout the region, butttheir
plans and space usage may vary considerably. Some are singlestoreyed, some are several storeys high, but, in principle, every
atrium performs a climatically similar role. At midday thesun
may reachtthe courtyard floor butthethickness ofthe walls and
the adjacent buildings prevents excessive solar heating. Cool air
inthe rooms is drawn into the courtyard, and, as it warms,
begins to rise, causing convection currents. Though these
continueto circulate duringthe afternoon, the lower angle
ofthesun creates deep shadows. When the sun sets, the
temperature drops rapidly and air currents circulate within the
courtyard and filterthroughtto the rooms, which remain cool
until the following afternoon. As the warm courtyard floor
andtheflatroof radiate heat at nightthey are often used for
sleeping in the summer, but in the winter shutters are closed
andthe neat storage effect ofthe walls is utilizedto raisethe
internal ttemperatures of the rooms by re-radiation.?'

The Iraqi badgirisatowerwith a row oftallslots, or vents,
which admittthe prevailing wind, generally from the north, and

deflect it down the shaft. High pressure on the wind side and low

pressure on the leeward side ofthe house ensure the movement
oftheair, which is channelled to an opening atthe bottom of
theshaft. Though it is cooled bytheside walls ofthesshaftthe
air may still bewarmerthan the airwithin theroom, but its
movement assists the evaporation of perspiration onthe skin
surface, and hencethe reduction of body temperature. Throughdraught is effected by corridors into which the exhaust airis
directed. To assist the cooling process, water vessels may be

placed inthe shaft atthe opening intothe room, or straw matting,

soaked at intervals in water, may be hung overthe vent opening
to cool by evaporation. Alternatively, trays of charcoal, wetted
at intervals, may be placed atthe bottom oftheshaftforthe
same reason. Deflected into a basement, asinthe Baghdad
house, the draught fromthe badgirmoves staleand humid air
in the room, and brings down the night-time humidity which
may reach 75 per cent, to a pleasant 35 per cent; afactorthat
seems to be more significant in terms of comfortthan a
reduction in airtemperature.23

Evaporative cooling is achieved in other ways: small pools
in courtyard gardens both cool and humidify dry air passing over
them, while mats of palm fronds, hung over windows and kept
moist by intermittent soaking, can coolandhhumidifytheairin
the same way, with the additional advantage of filtering desert
dust. The cleansing effect of sprays or fountains, which trap dust
particles as well as coolingtthe.air, is also considerable, but
normally only the wealthier families can indulge in this luxury
and fountains are seldom seen in ordinary dwellings. Trees are

23: Decorative
“hit-and-miss’
brickwork (headers
are omitted) creates

<circulate. Agra, India

important modifiers of climate and, as well as providing sshade,
are efficient in cleaningthe air. Moisture in plants, shrubsand
trees helps reduce airtemperature by the process of evaporative
cooling, whilethetranspiration of the leaves also assists in
loweringthetemperature while increasingthe humidity. Shade
trees can substantially cool the air beneath them and trees near
houses allow the heavier, cooler airto pass to the buildings while
interceptingthe hotter air at foliage level.?+

Consequently, in courtyard houses trees are valued and,
similarly, trees along a street add greatly to comfort conditions.
That villagers in north India are well aware of this, is evident from
the careful planting of young saplings, the protection ofthem
from cattle and goats with cylindrical, perforated walls of mud
bricks, and, of course, their cultivation to produce fruit. Vines,
likewise, provide both fruit and shade when trained over atrellis,
pergola or window opening. When grown beside a house, as
they are frequently in Greece, Sicily and other Mediterranean
countries, they contributeto thermal regulation by reducingthe
radiant effect of stone walls, and by creatinga cool space between
foliage and wall surface. Itshould be added that inmoretemperate
climates deciduous trees intercept the sun’s rays in thesummer
and permit them to pass through the leafless branches in winter;
in hot and dry countries evergreens are favoured precisely because
they do not havetthis seasonable variability.?5

Many measures that contributeto an improvement of
comfort conditions in arid climates are even simplerthanthe
planting of vegetation, and more immediate in theireeffect.
Cross-ventilation can create athrough passage of air; itisnot
sufficient, however, to have one side of a loggia open ifthere is
no meansfortheairto escape or be drawn through the space by
another opening. In Indian village homes, openings are often to
beseen inthewallsthat allow airto passthrough and are situated

14

## S. 74

142 Coping
with climate

at levels wherethe draught is most convenientfor its occupants,
sitting or at rest. Such openings are an inducementtotthieves,
even where there is littleto steal. ‘Hit and miss’ brickwork, where
bricks are omitted alternately or in successive courses, hasthe
same ventilating benefits but creates openings that aretoo
smallfor wayward hands. In many instancesthetechnique has
ledto inventive decorative brickwork, which is alsoto beseen
on parapet walls where roofs are used for sleeping.

One ofthe most widespread and economicaltechniques
for arid climate modification isthe coating of wallswith a
reflective surface. Ground chalk mixed with thin glue size
(whiting) ora solution of quick-lime and size (whitewash) painted
over walls and roofs can have dramatic results in reflectingthe
sun’s rays. Experiments were conducted in Khartoum on amud
layer roof, 7 in (17 cm) thick, and on an identical surface painted
with several coats of whitewash. When thessolarincidence
reached a maximum of 131°F (55°C) andthe airtemperature was
92°F(33°C),itwas found that the maximum surface temperature
ofthe exposed mud roof reached 145° F (63°C). The effect of
whitewashing on the internal temperature wasto bring it down
some 18° F(10°C).2 Further experimentswhich havecompared
the effectiveness of painting with other forms of roof covering
have been very instructive. Red paint reflected 26 per cent of
solarradiation, and absorbed 75 per cent, whereas whitewash
reflected 80 per cent of solar radiation and absorbed only 20 per
cent. This accounts forthe habitual whitewashing of walls in
the valleytowns ofthe Mzab. Inthe Greek island villages ofthe
Cyclades where the practice at times seems obsessional, the
frequent overpainting further increases the density ofthe
reflective area and contributes to its efficiency.

Agreat many people use galvanized sheeting, or
‘corrugated iron‘, fortheirroofstoday, mainly dueto its relative

simplicity of handling and fixing, and its comparative cheapness

Nevertheless, in spite ofthe need for re-painting, whitewashing
remainsthe simplest, cheapest and most effectivetreatment
for making surfaces highly reflective in a hot climate, though
the benefit needs to be considered against the problem of
heightened glare.

One beneficial characteristic of galvanized sheet iron is
thatitisthin; hence, although it transmits heat quickly, it also
loses it speedily and has no heat storage capacity. In general,
light and thin construction which does not store heat is bestina
hot and humid tropical climate as, without amarked drop inthe
night temperature, there can be no heat emission from amass
construction. As we have seen, in southeast Asiaandthe Oceanic
regions, building in light materials like bamboo is customary,
and shade is achieved by using materials such as palm fronds.
Itisimportantto maintain a flow of airto prevent the build-up
of heat, normally by means of cross-ventilation, which, in Pacific
island communities, may be maintained by careful sitingand
open side walls. Systems of rolled straw or cane mats which can
be furled or unfurled are often usedtto protectthe occupants
against inclement weather.?® Roof forms often needto be
resistant against wind-storms and hurricanes, so it may seem

143

## S. 75

betterto keep a low profile and build a shallow pitched roof which
the wind can pass over. Unfortunately, such aroof canactasan
aerofoil, rather likethewing of an airplane, and can be suddenly
lifted offthe main supports in a hurricane. Which brings usto
other problems generated by extreme climatic conditions.

Dwelling and disaster

Even if many of the effects of climate upon houses can be
substantially reduced by building form, details and modifications,
there are some that are difficultto control. Damp may penetrate
from outsidethe walls, or rise from the foundations, walls may
be proneto condensation, ortimbers may rot or decay overtime.
Some ofthese problems have to be endured; others may be
overcome with skills which may haveto be introduced from
other cultures:trenching around wallsto inhibit creeping of
moisture, laying damp-proof courses of slate slabs and protecting
theexposed ends oftimberjoists. Remedial actions can betaken

when necessary, such as the replacement of weak and broken
timbers, ortherepair of mud walls which have been cracked by
the strains of expansion and contraction caused by high diurnal
range and extremes of humidity. Many conditions are recurrent
and dealingwiththem becomes a part oftheannual cycle of
work: elephant grass is stored forthatching repairs in the African
Sahel, for use after short seasonal rains have brought aboutthe
almost inevitable collapse ofthe cover; stone rollers are kepton
flatmud roofs in Turkey or Afghanistan sothat weaknesses and
cracks may berolled out. These are problemsthe indigenous
builders have learned to accept; their periodic occurrence is
anticipated and there are measuresthat can betaken to deal
with the inconvenience or damage caused.?

Disasters are another matter. Drought, such as Afghanistan
has experienced inthe early 2ıst century, causes great hardship,
starvation, and consequently, considerable loss of life. Still more
serious was the drought of 2001-2 brought about by the El Nino

phenomenon, which is created by irregular fluctuations in
atmospheric pressure and thetemperature ofthe Pacific Ocean’s
surface and sea. Unprecedented rainfallinthesame region
accompanied the return of El Nifo in 1997, when ten million
people were affected, and 15,000 died, including many who
had prepared for drought. Though it does not seriously harm
buildings, long-term drought can affectthe growth of resources
such as grasses, that might be used fortheir repair, and itsaps
the energies of those who build or maintain them.3°

Floods are far more devastating, especiallythosethat occur
as a result ofthe movement oftropical cyclones (typhoons or
hurricanes) overthe sea andtowards land. Atmospheric pressure
drops asa storm gathers, causingthe seato rise, whilethewind
draws up the waves still furtherto cause penetratinginundation
of low-Iying lands. Inthe face of high winds, many dwellings in
southeast Asia and other hot-humid tropical regions afford some
protection. Roofs are built at pitches that deflect prevailingwinds
without being lifted off in a gale. Light frames of bamboo and
roofs of palm leaves are not essentially dangerous when blown
down, buttyphoons may ttear down palm trees and rip leaf cover
to shreds, greatly limitingthe materials available for rebuilding.

Thetorrential rainsthat accompany hurricanes can have
other devastating effects, saturating the soil on hill-slopes which
have been deforested and cleared for farming, swelling rivers
that flowto the flood plains where the alluvial soils makethem
attractivetothefarmers, and creatingimmense mudslides.
Though deaths from the inflow of high seas are relatively few,
10,000 people were killed and over 80,000 rendered homeless
when Hurricane Mitch struck Honduras late in October 1998.
Gigantic mudslides set in motion bytherains looseningthe soil,
contributed substantiallytothe loss of lifeand homes, as
structures were destabilized or submerged in liquid mud and

29: Adestructive
landslip was caused by
an earthquake in Santa
Tecla, El Salvador, 2001.
Aerial view showing

torn apart bythe winds. The largest slip occurred ontheLas
Casitas volcano in northwest Nicaragua, smothering Posoltega

floods in Tegucigalpa, where dams and reservoirs were breached

Yet, within afew days the people were clearingthe debris, saving

Hurricanes may reach speeds of 300 kphaatthe ‘eye’ of
thecyclone, and tropical cyclones cause damage to buildings by
virtually explodingthem. The increased loads ofthewind speed
on one side ofthe building create low-pressure areas onthe side
and opposite walls, the roof rips off, and the suction pullsthe
building asunder. In areas where buildings are of light frames,
fatalities from damage to buildings may not be high, though
flying debris can maim and kill. In some instances devastation
and loss of life on an almost unimaginable scale have occurred
asaresult ofacyclone, likethat which devastated south-east
Bangladesh in April 1991, killing 140,000 people, of whomthe
majority were drowned. Alethal surge wave, six metres in height,
was thrust inland by the cyclone until it was deflected by rising
ground. The returning wave surge took the lives of many who had
survived its initial sweep across the Bay of Bengal. Onlythe flood
of November 1970 was more lethal.3?

Some regions are notoriously subject to disasters: islands
intthe Caribbean, for example, orthe Philippines, are both prone
to hurricanes, floods, and earthquakes. Earthquakes are not
hazards that can be directly attributableto climate, but as
environmental catastrophes they can cause immense damage
and casualties. Like most other cataclysms, earthquakes occur
largely, though not wholly, inthetropical zones, their incidence

147

## S. 77

148 Coping
with climate

primarily related to the peripheries ofthe continental plates.
Movement ofthese plates causes massive pressuresto build up,
often alongfault lines on the earth’s crust, that are released
through earthquakes. But seismic hazards are unpredictable and
although methods for ascertainingthe potential danger spots
and predicting possibletimesthat a new disaster may occur have
largely improved, they are not wholly reliable. Moreover,
earthquake shocks may be felt at irregular distances from the
epicentres and the transmission of seismicshocksthrough the
mantle is affected by geological factors that may be highly
localized, often resulting in tremors being felt from different
directions. Buildings that may shake, but remain standingwhen
struck by shocks in one direction, are likelyto collapsewhen
multiple movement is experienced, as walls are pulled apart and
roofs cave in. Casualties from disintegratingmasonry and
collapsingmud and adobe walls are likely to be high, exacerbated
bythefalling of rooftimbers and mud layers, while the selfsame
stone rollers used on roofsto smooth out and seal cracks become
instruments of death in earthquakes.3

Fatalities can be on an appalling scale but few, if any, are
known to have been more terriblethan the 1976 earthquake,
magnitude 7.8, which hit Tangshan, northeast China. Close on
a quarter of a million people died, two-thirds of whom were
victims of building collapse and falling masonry, the dust from
crushed adobe walls suffocating a large proportion oftheir
number. The extent of destruction was massive, includingthe
water and sewerage systems, and the statistics of injuries and
disease, including dysentery, typhoid, encephalitis and enteritis,
astronomic. As workers in amining and steel producing
industrial city, most ofthe men and theirfamilies losttheir
homes but, while relief workers slept in tents, they built
temporary structures in parks and empty sites. They were

advisedto build low masonry walls on which wood planks were
raised, with corner posts supporting grass thatched and asphalt
roofs. Later, disaster-resistant government housing was
introduced, with guidance forthe local builders.3+

It is frequently the poor who suffer most from the effects
of floods, windstorms and earthquakes; theirs arethe houses
that are likely to be built on the least stable ground, the hillslopes, ravines and the low-Iying coastal lands proneto
inundation by the tsunami. Travelling at hundreds of milesan
hourandrisingto 30 metres in height, theseterrifying seismic
ocean waves are generated by earthquakes in the Pacific. Against
disasters of this scalethereislittlethatthe people can doeither
to anticipatethem orto defend themselves, theirfamiliesand
their dwellings. In some cases, people who are obliged for
economicreasonsto build on unstabilized land, orwhose houses
are built of earth, stone and timber, which are notoriously
dangerous in disasters might be expected to have developed
structures which can survive such cataclysms.

With limited resources available and in unpredictable
conditions, builders in stone and adobe have had difficulty in
developing earthquake-resistant structures. Timber frames are
safer if well-jointed, though wall infill is still likelyto collapse. A
successful anti-seismic combination of stone, earth andtimber
is used by sedentary Tajiks inthe mountain regions ofthe Hindu
Kush. Heavy stone walls on the ground floor arethe support for
pakhsa or mass earth construction on the floor above. Poplar
poles provide a framework for athird-floor level, builtinthe
lighter-weight senjmethod, with diagonal bracingforthe upper
storey ofthree-storey houses. Vertical poles havethin sun-dried
bricks laid between them in chevron form, the pole frame
supporting a relatively light layered pole, brush, reedand mud
flat roof.35 Another system widely used in Kashmir is known as

30, 31: The senj (Tajik,
left) and tag (Kashmiri,
right) methods of
anti-seismic
construction

32: The dwellings of
12,000 refugees in

the Ogaden, Ethiopia,
incorporate traditional
frame-and-mat
structures

taq construction, in which the weight ofttransverse beams is
carried by stone piers. At each floor levelthe main beam supports
the end of floor joists, which in turn take a further beam bearing
the brick infill ofthe level above. The bricks are weakly mortared,
permitting movement in an earthquake, andthis hasthe effect
of dampeningthe shock.3*

Againstthe annually recurrent rigours ofthetropical
climate most buildingtechniques are a defence but a major
earthquake may not recur in any one person’s lifetime, even in
seismic regions where tremors are more frequent. Nevertheless,
there isasteady climb in the frequency of natural disasters, of
which some may be attributedttothe effect of global warming on
climate change. The unprecedented growth inthenumber of
casualties and destruction of dwellings in the cities of Asia and
Latin America can be directly relatedtothe rapid expansion of
megacities, and the lack of preparedness by city governments.
As complex asthe problems of hazard prediction and community
vulnerability may be, the indigenous occupants of vernacular

houses are more likely to be victims of disasters than those with
theresponsibility of solvingthem.

Culturesthat are proneto frequent recurrent disasters
have, insome cases, developed strategiesto cope withthem, or
have devised buildings that are resistanttottheir impact. Others
have developed forms that fail easily but not lethally, and can be
swiftly re-erected. Concerned asthey may bewiththe more
immediate problems and tasks related to difficult climatic
conditions which are nonetheless the norm, many cultures focus
on managingtheirtime andtthe landsto offset the risk of, for
example, crop failure. Unpredictable ‘acts of God’, such as
tsunamior volcanic eruptions, are of such magnitude, and often
ofsuch a rarity, as not to entertheir reckoning. Catastrophesthat
are beyond their power to anticipate or influence are, for many,
‘as Allah wills’ Tothem, it is far more important and more
realisticto get on with being participating members oftheir
societies and ofraisingtheirfamilies inthe dwellingsthat are
thefocus of their daily lives.

Northern Ghana

Spatial relations

Varying from one culture to another accordingtotheireconomic
priorities, the cycle ofthe working year may be marked by
considerable changes inthe range oftemperature, length of
days, exposure to sunlight or precipitation of rain andsnow as
the months flow into seasons. In regions wheretthe seasonal
phenomena are less distinct, societies may be subjectedto
regularly recurring rains, monsoons, searing winds or periods of
stationary temperatures and high humidity. We have seen how
the buildings of societiesthe world over have been developed

to meetthe diversity of climate conditions, often creatinga
more acceptable micro-climate within. The annual cycles ofthe
climates in different regions also have a profound influence on
the nature ofthe economies they support and in which members
ofthese societies are engaged, this too is expressed in building.
The shelters ofthe pastoral nomads, or of farmers on marginal
lands, differ significantly from those erected by sedentary
agricultural peoples intemperate climates.

Between harvest and planting, old houses may be
repaired and new ones built; when the crops are nearing
fruition, field huts may be built and occupied; when fresh fodder
is needed, transhumance may be necessary. Secondary seasonal
dwellings may be used when the climate is too severe for all but
the hardiest or hungriest hunters, and families may passthe
season by drawing upon the stores of food laid down when the
hunting, the gathering orthe harvesting was good.'For billions
of people such ways of life are, in effect, the extensions ofthe
fundamental daily cycle of fire-making or stove-firing, food
preparation, cooking and eating, the fetching of water and
fuel, the care ofthe dwelling, working at home in the practice
of crafts and light industries, and the periods of leisure spent
visiting and entertaining, in sexual relationships and sleeping.

In their various ways each aspect oflifehas a bearingon
the dwelling, as the centre ofthe daily activities, orasthehome
to which those engaged in working outside it return. Frequently
the definition ofthe domestic space reflectsthis-separate male
and female areas, related, but by no means always equitably,
to their respective roles within the family. In many societies,
apart from the responsibility forthe bearing and raising ofthe
children, women are engaged in the domestictasks relatedto the
careofthe family and maintenance ofthe home, andthese do
not necessarily bring equivalence of status between the sexes.?
Itisfrequentlythe role ofthe maleto defend the family, to hunt,
to pasturethe animals, to labour in the fields, to engage in
traditional crafts, to build the houses, to market produce, and
to be employed as a wage earner or as a migrant worker, though
women often have an inordinate share in such occupations
in addition to their other work.

Male or female, for every individual in society the cycles of
days, months, or seasons are embedded in the cycle of life itself.
Whereasthe days andtheseasons are shared asthey recur, the
life-cycle of the individual overlaps the different stages of maturity
ofthe other members ofthe family andthe community. Some
societies have institutionalized the unfolding oftheyearsthrough
an age-set system, where groups of approximately identical
age proceed through life together, sharing activities, rituals and
habitations, as do young male Maasai.3 Almost all societies have
institutions of marriage whereby the union of husband and wife

isformally recognized through ceremony and perpetuated by
obligations, and by the bearing and raising of children.
Forthefamily the dwelling isthe focus of its identity as a
collective unit. The biological relationship of parents andtheir
offspring is expressed in the nuclear family, which, inmany
parts ofthe world, constitutes the basic domestic group. Often,
though, it is projected backtto one set of grandparents, to form
a stem family ofthree generations livingtogether. Brothers
orsisters ofthe parents, and their spouses and children, may
expandthe unittoan extended family, which can easily result
in ascore of adults and children living in one dwelling, and may
often include many more. Generally such an extended family is
related to a lineage or line of descent, which may beon the male
side ofthe family (patrilineal) or on the female side (matrilineal)
or, with some complications, through bilateral descent.+
Traditional Serbian society is representative of a patrilineal
society, in which descent and inheritance is of considerable
importance. Farm buildings in central Serbia share many features
with other Balkan traditions. In this mountainous region the
house is often built on a stone plinth, which serves as a store or
byre and acts as a levelling structure for the timber baulks. It may
bessited on a south-facing hill-slope, either alongthe contour,
or projectingfrom the slope with access ramps bankedtothe
entrances in the long dimension. These open on the main living
space, which may have asmall central hearth, or kuca, aword
that also means ‘household’. Though all domestic activities take
place here, it is sparsely furnished with small stools of barely
ankle-height on which the family members crouch bythe open
fire. Smoke fillsttheroom, dispersing slowly through thewooden
cowl inthe roof. The loft may be partially ceiledto provide extra
storage space or even asecondary soba or bedroom, and is
traditionally accessed by a ladder fashioned from a forked log

Even in the poorest houses the interiors arethe female domain
and are carefully maintained; asthe Serbian proverb explains,
“inthehousethewoman isthe head and the man a guest.

The vamilija, or common descent group, has assumed
much ofthe former functions ofthe zadruga, thetraditional
Serbian patriarchal household unit, which might have several
related married men within its compass. The zadruga has
become more of a marketing cooperative, butthe patrilineal
vamilija persists as adomestic group, with close links between
brothers reflected in their joint inheritance of land and property.
Even though daughters may marry out ofthe group, the blood
ties with their brothers remains a strong, even sacred one.

The elected head ofthe Vamilija may live in the principal farm
building, a two-storey, tiled-roof structure of squared logs, often
with an ajator a planked timber verandah supported by posts
and carved wooden consoles, an outside flight of stairs andthe
symbolic kuca inside. His rooms may have more furniturethan is
usual, with chests of drawers, atable and chairs with lathe-turned
legs and even an embroidered tablecloth. Woodtechnology
predominates, the granaries and stores being of squared timber
construction, as arethe separate dairies, though the timberframed corn crib is wattle-clad. A few outbuildings have a
thatched roof, held down by a pole frame, but the peasant houses
have roofs of wood shingles and, in some cases, kiln-fired tiles.

Acomplex and detailed kin terminology is employed for
every generation and member ofthe vamilija, yetwhen the head
of one such group - essentially a zadruga - displayed his own

Descent lines are an important aspect ofinheritance, and
influencethe passing on and division of land and property.
Traditional practices link past generations with those ofthe
present and the future, such asthe mutual aid system, pzajmica.
Lineage members frequently live in close association, ifnot
together in one building, at least inthe same village or locality,
and may be called upon to assist in house-building and other
activities requiring many hands.7

Anumber of lineages who believethemselvestto berelated
and who share their origins in acommon, but often mythical
ancestry, form a clan. Clans may live in proximity, but even ifthey
are dispersed, individuals may retain theiridentitywiththeclan
oftheir birth.® It may be seen thatthe complexities of kinship
could result in considerable pressure on larger settlements and
dwellings. Moreover, incest would be inevitable iftherewere no
measures to control marriage and interbreeding. Some cultures
are ‘*endogamous', requiringthat an individual marries within
a specific group, but ‘exogamy', orthe system that obliges
individuals to marry outside the group (often within specific
limitations of village or ethnicidentity) is a safeguard against
inbreeding. It may applyto one sex only-a daughter isexpected
to marry into afamily within, say, a particular village. Anewly
wed couple is by no means always free to decide wherethey wish
to live. Some societies are ‘virilocal’, expecting a son to reside
closeto his father or his male relatives on his father’s side; others
are ‘uxorilocal', requiringthatthe couple live near, orwith, the
wife’s mother or matrilineal kin.

These complexities may not bereadily apparenttothe
observer, butthey have undoubted implications on settlement
patterns, which extend far beyond the typological description of
their plans. For example, the Yao are a people of Malawi and its

in Malawi. The Yao are patrilateral lineages

adjacent states, numbering some two million. Their history isa
