ofthe nineteenth century leadingto their adopting agriculture.
The Yao are matrilineal and generally uxorilocal, the males living
exogamously in their wives’ villages, though relationships
between kinship groups are maintained by a sorority system in
which a man cares forthe welfare of his sisters, daughters and
grand-daughters. Many ofthewomen are descendants ofthe
enslaved wives of past chiefs, and this generates patrilateral links
within the maternal village. In form the village may consist of
clusters of cylindrical huts in apparent concentric rings, but
Clyde Mitchell’s research revealed the underlying pattern of
dominant matrilineages and ofthe patrilaterally-linked lineages,
by which the settlement functioned.!°

Monogamy, the union ofasingle male and a singlefemale,
isthe most common form of marriage, but polygamy, or plural
marriage is also widespread. Generally ittakes the form of
“polygyny’, witha mantaking a second or third wifewho will be
separately provided for, though often withinthesame dwelling
unit. This is the case with the Bafut males oftheCameroon, where
some ofthecommoners take a third wife, while noblemen might
have more than twenty." The attendant problems of many
children and mouthstto feed may be offset among some cultures
bytheincrease inthedomestic labourforce. Nevertheless, relative
wealth and large dwellings are implied by such multiple unions.
All systems of kinship have far wider implications in terms of
hierarchies and authority, of political systems and ideologies, of
social organizations and community interactions, of class and
caste, of gender roles and relationhips and much else, all of
which are ultimately relevant to settlement andto dwelling.

and society may be directly expressed in the physical structure of

the dwelling, with levels and rooms differentiated in accordance
with the priorities and distinctions of a specific kinship system.
But they are also respected in single-cell dwellings, with little or
no structural distinctions. Waitabu is a village on the small island
of Taveuni in the Fiji group, which shares the typical Melanesian
hot-humid climate. Rainfall is moderate, but Fijiissubjectto
tropical cyclones which sometimes reach hurricane force. For
this reason, traditional houses have steeply-pitched roofsthat
deflectthewinds and preservethethatch -iftheroof is lifted
offin ahurricaneit is customary forthefamilytotake refuge
under it wherever it falls until the storm is over. Taveuni has
asubsistence economy and there is no money exchanged in
Waitabu -wealth is measured in whales’ teeth which are also
used for gifts. Consequently, community labour is by obligation,
Tuesday being set aside for work within the village.

When a new vale, or dwelling, is needed it is built by
theyoung men ofthe tokatoka or lineage group, assisted
sometimes by other members ofttheir metagali, the larger
exogamous kin group. Araised yavu, or dwelling site, is
traditionalto afamily and remains its sole property, and it is
herethattthe earthfast corner posts are set. Wall plates are
lashed in placeto take the principal rafters, which support
theridge pole, significantly called the ‘rat scrambler”. Vertical
bamboo splines are bound with coir (coconut fibre cordage) to
horizontal bundles of reeds, and linethe inside walls, which
have an outside layer ofwoven coconut fronds. Bush vines are
used to lash the members, but interior exposed fixings may
be decorative and elaborately lashed with sennit (plaited
palm leaves). Recent houses tend to have lower-pitched roofs
and corrugated iron is replacing palm and other forms of
thatch, which may be more durable, but is potentially lethal
in a hurricane."

The main entrance in the gable wall facingthe sea opens
ontotthe interior space, which is conceptually divided into four
zones in the long dimension, two that are private, andtwothat
are less so. Fromthe gable entrance, thefirstoftheseisthe
female area, formerly used for cooking, but now closestto
the kitchen, which is externally situated beyond the valeon
the fringe ofthe village. Beyond this isthe men’s section, of
somewhat higher status, wherethe household head sitsatthe
top right, with any guest opposite him. Apandanus mat spans
both areaswhen the customary meal of taro, vegetable and fish,
is consumed, but each sex keepsto its zone. Beyond, thereisa
change of level, marked by a plinth and by entrance doors on
either side of it, which areseldom used except bythe family head
and his invitees. The front part ofthis higher level is a private
area where chiefs and honoured guests may be received with
appropriate ceremonial, including drinking from a bowl of kava.
Atthe back isthe logi, thetotally private zonewherethe
members ofthe family sleep and household effects are kept.

Althoughtthe people of Taveuni are Methodists, with a
chapel in most villages, the fear of magic persists. Formerly,

a fire was kept burning in the valeto keep away malignant spirits
andwitchcraft. Thefire also indicated that a household was in
residence and the valewell looked after, and hadthe additional
advantage of hardeningthethatch andto some extent, repelling
insects. Under missionary influence, the kitchens were moved
from within thehousetothe edge ofthevillage, and kerosene
lamps are usedto represent the fireand exorcism is called upon
to repel evil spirits. Admission to the house is by invitation only,
following an external conversation ratherthan requeststo enter.”

children, and giving a place ofimportancetothe household head.
When spaces need to be differentiated within the dwellingthere
are alternative ways to definethem. Subdivision of acircular hut,
though not infrequent, can create rather inconvenient and often
unventilated spaces; the alternative, common in much of Africa,
isto build huts for particular functions within an enclosed
compound. Each unit is in effect aroom, andthewholecompound
constitutestthe dwelling. Thisisthe casewith the Tswana of
Botswana.'*A Tswana dwelling may comprisethree orfour huts
within ayard, which is bounded by a hedge of rubber plants or
poles. Within thisis a carefully maintained and swept area of
beaten earth, the lolwapa (or lelapa), with its opening facingthe
main entrancetotheyard. Inthis area, which acts as aforecourt,
guests may be received; an apron tothe main sleeping hut whose
entrance faces it. Other huts may also open ontothe lolwapa or
be dispersed within theyard.

Tswana huts are circular in plan and built of mud, often
of wet brick, raised by the women in layers that are sometimes
reinforced at intervals with poles. Aring of about fifteen forked
poles, placed around the hut and made earthfast bythe men,
takes a circleof beams on which are rested and lashed with barkstringthe converging rafters ofthe conical roof; overthisthe
battens and grassthatch are laid. Asthe wall is not load-bearing,
aspaceis created betweenthewallandtheroofto.afford
ventilation. The outside walls may be lightly decorated with
finger markings inthe mud plaster and painted in earth browns,
red orlight orange, perhaps with an upper band of white. Inside,
the hut may also be painted andthe floor may have amud plaster
finish, topped with cow dung and polished as a hardening layer.’

The main hut may be occupied bythe parents or, ifthe
husband is away working in South Africa, by the mother with her
young children. Older children or unmarried daughters may sleep‘

in other huts, which can be built as needed. Absentee sons may
also build ahut within the yard, frequently favouringamodern
design, incorporating a rectangular plan and a hipped roof covered
with corrugated sheeting. An open kitchen is usually found in
theyard, with nearby storage spaces for spare building materials,
grass and feed, chicken pens, asmall kraalfor calves, a vegetable
plot and, perhaps, a covered granary with deeply-shaded eaves.
Anytrees are retained for their shade.' Additional structures in
the compound may include a pit latrine within awooden shelter,
afenced ablutions area forthe olderwomen and daughters, and
sometimes a shelterfor beer-making and selling, or for some
other modest enterprise. Not allthe family may reside at home;
sons and grandsons may beatthe cattle post orinthehutsat
the family fields where sorghum and maize are grown.

Within, the dwelling huts are furnished with wooden beds,
a platform for storing sorghum sacks, wooden or card-board
boxes for additional items, a shelfortwoforthe cups, buckets,
pottery or plastic bowlsthat make upthe domestic utensils and,
ifthe family can affordthem, atable, oneortwo chairsand even
asmallwardrobe. Maintainingthe house requires repainting
once ortwice a year, frequent resurfacing of the floors with cow
dung and polish, repair of erosion cracks in the mud walls and,
every few years, replacement ofthethatch. These jobs are
undertaken in the quiet period afterthe gathering of the harvest.
They aretheresponsibility of the wife, who swiftly invites
censure from other women ifthe huts and lolwapa are not kept
in good repair.

Livingroom

Although the compound system of individual units dispersed
within a defined open space is extremely flexible, the majority
of peoples prefer ‘living under one roof', an indication perhaps

13: Huts, shadettree,
and lolwapa ofa
Tswana homestead

14: A Tswana woman
in the lolwapa of her
home at Lethlakane,
Botswana. Symbolic

onthe house wall

of close familial relationships and mutual dependence. For
instance, in the verdant green, undulating but rough landscapes
of Irelandthe houses lie low beneath theirthatched roofs. The
poorest and simplest houses had onlytwo rooms, butthree or
more ranged in line were also common. Varying in depth from as
narrow as 10 ft (3m) totwice that size, the houses were
rectangular in plan, with thekitchen and hearth both physically
andsymbolicallyatthe centre. The broad hearth was setina
transverse wall, often with asmoke canopy intheform of a
truncated pyramid made of wattles built over it. The front plane
ofthecanopy might rest onthe brace-tree, a cross beam which
ran from the back wallto a wattle-and-daub scroop or ‘jambwall‘, setat aright-angletotthefire, giving protection from
draughts coming from the front entrance, and providinga back
supporttothe bed settle.® Asmall peep-hole was cut intothe
jamb-wall, sothat incoming visitors could be observed. Insome
regions ofthe north-west of Ireland, an ‘outshut’ extension
containing a bed was built, with a sloping roof, which was added
to increasethe width. This also permitted sleeping within reach
ofthewarmth ofthe open turffire.'?

Behindthe cross wall ofthe hearth a door ledto ‘the room’
or parlour, considered to be ‘above’ the kitchen and generally
placed to the west of the building. This was little used in practice.

Like many parlours and salons it was primarily for ‘best’; for special
occasions such as Christmas, and for celebratory, or ceremonial
functions, includingthe laying out of adeceased member ofthe
family. Recently, many parlours have been partitionedto make
second bedrooms. Oncethe old habit of sleeping bythefireand
onthe bed settle had given way to the use of conventional beds,
the room ‘below’ the kitchen - generally ontthe left ofthe
principal entrance - was used as a bedroom. Alternatively, itwas
divided in two, being used as astore or ‘larder' attherearandasa
small bedroom atthe front, which is similar to surviving presentday Irish farmhouses.

Overthe years most houses have had additions, including
aporchto keep outthe draughts atthe front ofthe house, anda
stable ora byre, and sometimes both, each under its own roof
extension, built against the end gable. Often a house built inturf
orclaywould have a stone extension, or in recent years, one of
concrete block under a corrugated iron roof. The spaces were
therefore differentiated by both subdivision and addition, or
sometimes by alteration, such asthe closing in of part oftheroof
space witha ceilingto make a half-loft. Today the heart ofthe
house and the focus of all its life is stillthe kitchen. Any tables
aresmalland placed against the wall, often beneath awindow
oreven,asa fallingtable’ folded up against the wall. The middle
space ofthe room is left clear of chairs untilthey are needed.
This allows free movement to and from the hearth, and also
permitted easy access when the family gathered for evening
ceilidhs, or song and storytelling sessions. The kitchen and its
furnishings are thewoman’s domain, scrubbed, swept and
polished as evidence of her caring.

The dresser opposite the fireplace held a display of
glittering china cups and other ceramics. Ifthestairsto a
half-loft were installed against the down-side partitions,

the dresser would be placed against it but still opposite the
hearth, sometimes providing additional support for the halfloft floor. Alternatively, it might be placed nexttothe box bed,
onthesame wall.?° Traditionally, the butter churn was placed
beside the hearth, and boles were recessed in the wall beside
the fireplace for the safe-keeping of personal articles: knitting
forthewomen on the left ofthe hearth, the men’s pipes on the
right. Alarge pot for hot water was always hung overthefireto
be ready forthe succession of meals and teas. It often hung
from asuspended chimney crane that swung out from thefire
following the arc of the sun. New houses were built onlywhen
marriage made them necessary; until recently, inheritance
confirmed the continuity of use ofthe buildings, which are in
many cases centuries old.?' Many hundreds of Irish farmhouses
were demolished in the last quarter of thetwentieth century,
asthe traditional buildings were rejected for new designsthat
reflected the growing economy. With them an important part
ofthe heritage was lost.

In contrast with the slow accretions ofthe Irish
farmhouses, the dwellings of the forest regions ofthe west
Malaysia peninsula are incremental, new sections beingadded
to meet changing family needs and economies. Traditionally
the Malay house was a timber-framed structure which, inthe
older examples, was often made ofteak. Other hardwoods that
are resistant to termites, or bamboos, have been employed as
teak has become more scarce and expensive. Young couples are
not obliged to live nearthe parents of either side but, withthe
inducement of family land on which to build, and help with the
materials, most would settle close to one or other. The majority
of households consist ofthe nuclear family (parentsand
children), but two or three households may commonly live in
proximity, especially ifthey are cultivating rice.

IX jemoran ibu
open social space

20: Example of a Malay
house inwhichthe
component structures
are allspanned by one
large roof. Pahang
province, Malaysia

Raised on piers, which rest on blocks of stone or hardwood,
the house consists of several elements linked together in various
arrangements. Dimensions ofthecomponents usedin the
framing are based on the arm-span, forearm, palm width and
similar measurements of the wife.?? With the assistance of
relatives and friends of the family, local carpenters construct the
main frame ofthe house using mortise-and-tenon and lapjoints,
tightened with wedges. Nails and bolts are not used, and this
system allows for the dismantling of partsorthe whole ofthe
house for relocation, or as giftsto their offspring. The basic unit
is the ibu rumah (literally, mother house) with a steeply-pitched
roof thatched with atap, or palm leaf. Thisisthe main sleeping
area forthe parents, whiletheir unmarried daughters retire
beyond a screened partition orformerly, to the atticwhich was
ventilated by the gable ends.

Floors and walls are of woven split bamboo, sometimes
stained and oiled to bring outtthe pattern oftheweave; though
lapped boards or wood panels are also used. Offtheibu ramah
and slightly below it and beneath broad eaves, isthe serambi
gantung, a partially open platform with a lean-to roof, which
actsasa reception area for guests, a place for feasts and prayers,
andasleeping area forthe male children. When it is walled in
to become part ofthelivingspace, afurther railed entrance
platform and social space might be added. The serambi gantung
isan important space, and particularly evident inthe houses
ofthe Minangkabau of the Negri Sembelan region, their saddle
roofs and upswept gables symbolizing buffalo horns, though
they also function effectively in exhausting warm air.?3

Onthe other side ofthehouse and at a lower levelisthe
ibu dapor, or kitchen platform, covered by a lean-to roof. Ifit
was attachedtothe gable end, it was known asthe ‘suckling
elephant’ (gajah menyusu). Often the kitchen is aseparate

structure, which is linked to the main house by a selang, orshort

cross-ventilation. Anumber of regional variations, asin Kelantan
and Terengganu, are to be found, some with elegant flights of
stairs. Today, corrugated iron sheeting and timber plank walis
have often replaced the traditional materials. Whilethis has
made them more resistanttothemonsoon rains, it has often
meanta considerable loss of internal comfort conditions.

Adaptable and extensible single-storey or split-level houses
can be economical to build, beingadded to when resources
permit and sufficient land is available. Yet houses of morethan
one storey have anumber of advantages: where agricultural land
isscarce they consumeless of it; wheretimber for roofs is limited
in quantity, length or strength, they require a smaller external
surfaceto coverthe house, relativeto the volume of living space.
There are fewer external walls in relation to thenumber of rooms
through which to lose heat, and upper-level rooms can benefit
from any breezes. Butthese advantages may be offset bythe
greatertechnical problems involved in their construction, which
often requires the assistance of specialists, while more floors
require more internaltimberto span rooms. Status and fashion
also play their part; tall buildings are often esteemed merely for
theirscale and are sometimes built high in a spirit of competition
with other builders and families. As is oftenthe case inthe
dwellings ofthe world, each culture develops a trade-off of
benefits against costs.

Kütahya province in western Anatolia, Turkey, isa hilly and
in some parts mountainous region, relatively well-watered and
with extensive forest cover which supports several hundred
villages of peasant farmers. Inthis region the dominant house
type is oftwo storeys under a pitched and tiled roof. Rather
roughly made, the walls have eithertimber frames with adobe or
stone infill, orthe wattle-and-daub construction of Iraqi origin
still known as bagdadi. The living space is on thefirst floor; below
arethe storage rooms andthe stable for winteringthe cattle
whose body heat helps keep warm the rooms above. Abroad
ladderleadstothe centrally located sofa (known asthe salonin
this region), alarge communal space with a balcony opentothe
weatherat oneend. Used as a reception area for special guests
(casual visitors are often not even invited into the ground floor),
itisalso the place where shoes are left, grain sacks are stored,
handtools are kept, food is prepared, cooking may be done and
meals are eaten. The salon has rooms on either side, each taking
acornerofthebuilding; and atransverse corridor extension of
thesalon has a storage closet atoneendand a garderobetoilet
closet atthe other.Thetoilet is as private as possible; no doors
open ontothe corridor off which itis situated. Excretafallstothe
animal dung heap below, and the mixture is consideredto be an
excellent and inoffensive manure. Western Anatolian houses of
the Kütahya province type are spatially differentiated laterally
through the depth ofthe building, longitudinallythrough their
width, and verticallythroughtwo floorsandthe loft space.

Turkey is officially a secular state but in the rural provinces
many people are devout Muslims with an ‘extended family’
structure. Inone room livesthe patriarch and his wife; his eldest
son, his wife and their children in another; asecond son and
family may occupy another or, iftoo young for marriage, the
sons and daughters will sleep separately in other rooms. What

appears onfirstsightto be a large house may have morethan a
score of occupants. Privacy is valuedandthe doors closeonthe
rooms of each family, being arranged so as to avoid overlooking
theentrances to otherfamily rooms.

Village houses are built where possible on a south-facing
slope, forthough it ishot in summerthe winters can be bitterly
cold. They are situated so that no windows overlook those of
another house, andthere are no windows in the rear elevations.
Beneath the windows on two sides of each livingspaceruns a
wooden sedir, or box-couch, where the men sit and sleep; women
and children sit ontherug or linoleum-covered floorwherethe
whole family takes its meals. Inone wall isa central fireplaceand
flue, flanked on either side by arecess covered with curtains or
doors. One is used for storing bedding, the otherfor ablutions. A
few houses may have iron beds and an iron stove may replacethe
fire intherooms, but other furniture is minimal, save for a lavish,
plastic-veneered, glass-fronted buffet, the modern equivalent of
the dowry chest, inwhich family treasures and souvenirs are kept.

The pattern of life is changing slowly, though itstiming is
influenced by weather, resources and prices atthe market. Inthe
springthe family may make essential repairs to their dwelling,
and may even dismantle a substantial part, or all, ofthestructure
to rebuild it. Building skills are expected of everyone, but some
villages are noted forthe quality of theirtimber framing. Twostoreyed buildings are not exceptional and in a few parts of Turkey,
in particular the conserved north-western village of Safranbolu,
some houses are three storeys in height.?° But this is unusual:
only per cent of Turkey’s rural houses are ofthree storeys or
more, over half oftherural houses being on one floor. Comparative
statistics are lacking but it is very likely that this is lowerthanthe
world average; single-storeyed dwellings probably represent
over 70 per cent ofthetotal. As in many extended family systems

Given space, there are ways in which even larger
populationsthan the extended family can beaccommodated
under one roof: the longhouse is one method. Distributed
discontinuously through south-east Asia, from Miri in Assam
through Malaysia and Indonesia, what is generallytermed
the ‘longhouse’ is a community dwellingtype of impressive
proportions, which shares little but the classificatory name with
the Dartmoor longhouse. Asian longhouses are not only much
wider but are also of far greater length. Structuresthat are
over 300 ft (100 m) longare known, though some of much greater
length have been recorded inthe past. They find their highest
concentrations among the diverse peoples of Borneo, though
in some areas they have all but disappeared. Longhouses of
earliertimes were massively built, some raised on ironwood
piers that weighed uptothreetons each. Butthere are many
regional and localtypes, from the umu ofthe Kenyah of Sabah,
which is constructed with a stepped succession of roofs, to
that ofthemountain-dwelling Kelabit, whose longhouse is
conceptually divided into ‘hearth-groups’ identified with
conjugal couples.2® Such variants are indicators of social and
cultural distinctions between the various peoples, and area
reminder that in all examples cited in this book there are building
types and sub-types with specific characteristics that reflect
small, but in local terms significant, differences between
broadly-related peoples and communities within a culture area.

## S. 85

Most numerous amongthe cultural groups of Kalimantan
and Sarawak arethe Dyak peoples who together number in
excess of a quarter of a million. The majority live by rivers, using
their boats for communication. Cultivators of wet and dry padi
(rice), the most prominent Dyak people, the Iban, also grow
rubber, while many collect and sell rattan or other jungle
products. An Iban village will normally consist oftwo orthree
longhouses. Whether a longhouse is constructed oftimber or
bamboo, orthe boards that form the walls are slats or of sawn
wood, the Iban longhouse takes a consistent form. Raised on
piers with its main supports risingto the ridge, which may be
30 ft(9 m) above the ground, the structure consists of a platform
with along pitched roof over differentiated spaces. The tempuan,
a broad passage, runsthrough the centre of the longhouse, with
one edge beneathttheridge, forthewhole length ofthe building.
Apart from beingtthe principal circulation corridor, it is also used
bythewomen for pounding padiwith poles and mortars.

Offto.oneside ofthetempuan aretheliving spaces or
apartments, and each bilek, with its own door openingontothe
corridor, is lined with sleeping mats and large jars. Anotched log
ladder leads to the sadau or loft storage area above, wherethe
padiis kept in large baskets, spare mats and containers are
rolled and hunting equipment is stored. It may also be used as
a sleeping place forthe young girls. Behind the bilekisthe dapor,
orkitchen, which, because of therisk of fire, is sometimesto be
found detached from the main dwelling space, but stillonthe
raised platform. Opposite the apartments and across the
tempuan isthe ruai, abroad boarded work space, where looms are
set up for the weaving of intricately patterned and dyed pua
cotton cloth, and where mats, fish traps, and rattan containers
are made. Itis also usedforritual dances and the playing ofthe
gong sets at night, as well as for meeting and conversation. Atthe

outer edge and underthe eaves ofthe covered part ofthe
structure isthe pantaiwhere guests and young unmarried men
retire. At many pointsitopens onto a broad verandah, the tanju,
which is used for drying padi, andwherethewhole longhouse
community gathers inthe cool ofthe evening. Rain falls during
the afternoon most days - an annual rainfall of 150 inches is
common and daytimetemperatures are high.??

Membership ofthelonghouse community impliesthe
acceptance ofthedecrees ofthetuairumah, orheadman, and
meeting many social obligations includingrecognition and respect
fortherights of others, and appropriate usage oftheinternaland
external spaces. It also means observingthe correct rites as decreed
bytheritual head ofthelonghouse, the tuai burong, or‘bird elder‘.
Tothelban certain birds are incarnations of gods. When seen they
are believedto begivingwarnings orcounsel, anditistherole of
thetuaiburongto interpretthese appearances and other auguries
ofthesuccess of every activity, includingtthe building of anew
longhouse. Fromthe cries ofthenendak bird he decides whether
a selected siteis auspicious. Asthe moon is waxing, women beat
gongs and other lineage members repel evil spirits withomen
sticks. When thetime comes for occupation, offerings, sacrifices
andtheritualconsumption of specially cooked rice must be
performed to ensurethe future security ofthecommunity.3°

Inthe past, allthe occupants of one longhouse were
probably members ofthe same clan or lineage, but it isnow
possible for members of other familiesto join a longhouse, or for
membersto leave one asthey will, subject tothe agreement of
thetuairumah. Ifa family wishesto move, itis required to leave
the main structural members, but cladding materials, including
theroof covering may be removed for use in another structure. A
young family may wish to be more independent while remaining
inthe longhouse. Additional living quarters may be built atthe

end ofthe house, increasing the length by a bay, orthe width of
an apartment. This will also require additionsto the tempuan,
verandah and other balancing elements, andthe moving of
access ladders. Beneaththe longhouse the space between the
pillars is used for pig pens, for keeping fowI and native stingless
bees, and forthreshing padi. Iban longhouses are therefore
spatially differentiated alongtthe length of the building on one
side by apartments, in depth by functions, and vertically by
making spaces above and below accessory to the living level.

With regardto their spatial utilization, the dwellings of
cultures throughout the world have many aspects in common,
but when their disposition and integration within buildings is
considered in addition to their use, their variety becomes
apparent. Often the spaces occur within a single volume,
differentiated as we have seen, with walls, screens, curtains
orchanges of level and surface. Some societies demand little
oftheir houses; others require more: rooms for bathing or
excretion, lobbies or hallsin which to receive visitors, private
spaces in which to work or public ones inwhichto conduct a
trade, as well as special rooms for ceremonials or prayer.

Defining spaces

Numerous dwellings have porches, verandahs and upper-storey
balconies, which make spatial connections with the world outside
while preservingttheir functional purposes as extensions ofthe
domestic living space. How societies regard the relationship of
internalto external space is often a measure oftheimportance
thattthey place on privacy. For many, thethreshold orthe main
doorway marks the limits beyond which the visitor may enter
only by invitation. In certain instancesthereisatransitional zone
between the public and private spaces, while in others, relatively
free movement between and within spaces is acceptable. This

may change at different times of the day or night, or freedom of
access may be permitted onlyto.a specific sex or age group, orto
members ofthe same lineage. The relationships of internaland
external space through use are indicative, therefore, of much
more thanthefunctionstowhichtthespaces are put.

Every culture has its particular expectations of its dwellings,
and arranges domestic spaces in ways that relateto its social
structure, to itstraditions andtothe organization ofthe daily
lives of its members. An analysis of dwelling patternsthat
compared built forms only, and which identified spaces according
to designations within the structure, could well overlookthe
spontaneous or unconstrained spillover of activities into the
external space. Flat roofs, for example, are used for dryingfruit,
for airingwashing, clothes and bedding, for sleeping, or for
movement from one dwellingto the next. Similarly, such an
analysis might not acknowledge the claims made by some
societies on external space for functions which, among others,
are customarily found within the building -outdoor hearths and
cookingfacilities, stores, cribs, fuel piles and allotment sheds,
the yards used for sleeping on hot and sultry nights, the use of
fields and bush in lieu of latrines, the drawing of water from a
well, fountain or standpipe and much else.

As we have seen inthe examples cited, not onlyinthis
chapter but elsewhere in this book, the ways in which dwellings
are organized and used, andtheforms and plantypesthat
they assume, are expressive of the specific cultures that have
developed them and whose ways of lifethey accommodate
and serve. Nevertheless, it is evident, too, that kinship patterns,
environmental conditions and economic systems have a
considerable bearing on the physicalform and spatial organization
ofthe dwelling. With the growth of families, whether nuclear
orextended, the care of young children and the infirm, and

the death ofthe aged, the demands on the dwellingto meet
achanging family size and structure are considerable.

Various solutions tothese problems exist, such asthe
capacityofthecompoundtypetorespondtofluctuationsin
family numbers by the addition or demolition of individual
domestic units, asin the instances oftheFijians and the Tswana.
There arethetwo- orthree-roomed houses oftheSerbsandthe
Irish, with outshuts or lofts adapted where necessary. Orthere is
the large-scale, multi-celled dwelling, such asthe western
Anatolian house, which accommodates the maximum
anticipated size ofthe extended family with the likelihood of
redundant spaces at various times. Again, there isthe mediumsized, basic structure forthe nuclear family, which is adapted or
extended to meet family growth, likethe Malaysian dwelling, in
contrasttothe multi-family bileks ofthe Iban Dyaks in their
village-scale longhouses. And, of course, there isthe selling of a
house as it becomes unsuitable with increase or decrease of
family size, and the purchase of another. Ofthese systemsthe
latteristhemost common intthe west, sellingand repurchasing

beingthe least immediately responsive andthe most expensive
option, requiring loose kinshipties and greater mobility. In
societies where attachmentto ancestral lands and close kinship
ties are profound, other options are more appropriate.

In factthough, no dwelling tradition is isolated from
its surroundings; each isa constituent part of itsenvironment,
sometimes territorially determined by walls, hedges or
thickets, sometimes bythe sweeping or maintenance of
adjacent space considered bythe occupantsto be part ofits
domain, or simply as required by custom. However the dwelling
ismadeto adaptto the family structure and its evolving needs,
and whateverthe organization and utilization of its internal and
external spaces may be, itembodiesthe values ofthegroupto
which it belongs. In many cases the significance of specific
locations within a site may not be apparent, excepttothosefor
whom the places have vital spiritual associations. At certain
stages inthelife cycles ofthe occupants and intthelifeofthe
dwelling itself, these are frequently given special emphasis in
meaning, ceremonial, symbolism and aesthetic enrichment.

## S. 87

## S. 88

170 Houseand

cosmos

House and cosmos

Societies of all kinds and in every part oftheworldneedto
account forthe nature oftheirenvironment and man’s perceived
place inthescheme of things, in orderto give meaning, purpose
and structureto living. The origin ofthesun, moon, rainand
earth, of animals and plants; and the mysteries of conception,
birth, life and the destiny ofthe souls ofthe departed are
commonlyatthe centre of man’s religious beliefs and practices.
Fearingthe unknown, masteringthe known; the world is made
somewhat more comprehensible ifthese observed phenomena
are brought into a system. Correspondences between
phenomena may then be perceived, influences deduced from
them and their apparent meanings become relevant. Forms are
modelled or carved in ancestral likenesses, dances are patterned
onthemovements of animals and birds, devices are painted and
masks wornttotapthe spiritual essence. Rituals are performed,
voices aremasked and hallucinogens taken, trialsendured and
ceremonials observed to make contact with thesupernatural.
Andthe contexts for so many ofthese are mankind’s constructed
symbols ofthecosmos, thefoci of ritual and reverenceinthe
stupa, thetemple, thesshrine, themosque, thechurch, the chapel
-or, initshuman-scale simulation of the universe, the dwelling.'

Building and meaning

Spread over a vastterritory stretching east morethan 2,000 miles
from the Caspian Sea to central Mongolia, yurt-dwelling peoples
includethe Turkomen, Kazakhs, Uzbeks, Kyrgyz and numerous
Mongoltribes. Some herd Bactrian camels, and, inthe mountains,
yaks, but all are fine horsemen. They depend largely on sheep

for milk, wool and, to some extent, meat, owned either by
themselves or by wealthy families. Theirtiesto lineage and clan
are strong, some peoples ofthe more western regions being

Previous pages: A
Kyrgyz yurtonthe

1: Erecting a Kyrgyz
boz oy. Oncethe kanat
orwalllattices arein

formerly politically organized in ‘hordes’. Adaptingtheyurttothe
harsh climatic conditions of steppe, gravel desert or mountains,
several million people stillfollowthetraditional nomadic pastoral
way of life, in spite of governmental attemptsto implement
resettlement schemes.?

The yurt-a Turkish word for ‘dwelling’-is known in
Afghanistan asthe kherga, in Russia as kabitka, and in Mongolia,
where it achieves its most remarkable forms, asthe ger. Among
allthese peoples the structural principles underlyingthe yurt
are basicallythe same: the dwelling is circular in plan with walls
made of an open, loose lattice frame of willow wands which may
beexpanded to buildthe house and contracted for carryingto
the next site. The cross-overs ofthe wands are lightlythonged
with rawhide. Each lattice, or khana (kanat), is made up of some
33 wands, and half adozen khana will produce a yurt about 16 ft
(5 m) in diameter. Light poles that have been preformed intoarcs
by steaming are lashedtothe frame, which is further stabilized
by webbingtension bands, and the poles inserted into a centre
ringorcrown. In Mongoliathe poles are straight andthecrown
is a heavy, wheel-like element with morticeandtenonjoints,
but elsewhere lighter crown hoops are used. Juniperwood door
frames provide a secure fixingforthe khana; the door may bea
feltmatthat can berolled up, a solid carved wooden door, ora
pair of doors with leather hinges. Once the frame is in placethe
yurt is covered with thick woollen felt mats made bythewomen,
which are secured with webbing bands or ropes. Kyrgyz boz oy of
white feltmay be decorated with applique designs in brown felt,
while Turkomen yurtwalls are covered with reed mats, held in
place and enriched with a broad, patterned tension band.

Spatially, yurts are strictly divided by systems which have
similar organization, though sometimes differently orientated;
Turkomen entrances face east, but other yurts customarily face

south-west. Thetransition from exteriorto interior ofthe yurt is
symbolically important, while inside a clockwise path must be
taken. The interior is divided into quadrants; the southern, or
entrance zone is ofthe lowest status, beingthat ofthe herders;
whilethe position opposite, inthenorth quadrant, isforthe
shrineand for distinguished guests. The quadrant on the lefthand side oftheentranceisthe male domain ofthe family
head, with saddles, weapons and huntinggear;thewomen and
children sleep opposite with cradle and churns. Chests for rugs
and clothing, boxes for valuables and utensils, as well as small
tables and vessels for food and mare’s milk also have their
assigned positions.

Atthecentre ofthe Mongolian geristhefire, regarded
by many Mongols as a protective deitywho must not be
disturbed. The ‘square ofthe hearth’ within the circleoftheger
follows Buddhist symbolism, thesquare and thecircle being
representative of male and female; the five eastern elements of
Earth, Wood, Metal, Water and Fire are symbolized respectively
bytheearth floor, thewooden hearth frame, theiron tripod and
the kettle of water overthefireinthe hearth. Above, the roof of
the dwellingsymbolizes the sky, the open smoke hole being seen
as the ‘Eye of Heaven’* The gerembraces other values, however,

KItecessfor,

Indian) nations, with a population in excess of 175,000, they are
mainly dispersed overthe reservation. The majority live in modest
‘outfits”, with a loose grouping of habitations, smallholdings,
and corrals. Whilenotnomadic, they traditionally movedto
another habitation for part oftheyearwhenttakingtheir animals
to winter grazing. Single-storey houses including log cabins,
adobe walled structures and government prefabricated units are
to beseen onthereservation, butthe dwellingthatisthefocus
of Navajo family life isthe hogan. Itis present in every homesite,
eventhough other buildings are erected nearby, andisthe
centre of family and spiritual life. Like their counterparts in other
societies, Navajo children build ‘play-house’ hogans, often on a
toy scale, adding ramadas or unwalled shade shelters of sticks,
grass corrals and clay sheep and horses. Butthough the play
hogan helps reinforce the behaviour patterns and values that
they are acquiring, the children learn most from being within
thesingle-celled hoganitselfand through beingthe centre of
important elements in Navajo religious life.

Oldest ofthe hogan types isthe ‘forked stick’ structure,
based onatripod offorked poles against which others are laid.
Aporch extension totheentrance, with asmoke-hole opening
facing forwards above it, was sometimes used, the pole frame
thickly plastered over with mud. Rarely used now, the basic form
oftheforked stick hogan is still usedforthe small ‘sweat-house’
in which ritual cleansing sweat baths or hot-stone saunas are
taken. Horizontal log construction is also used bythe Navajo,
though the logs may only be notched at their intersections. As
the hogan is notionally circular, the logs are laid in an hexagonal
arrangement to shoulder height, afterwhich the corners are
spanned in successive tiersto produce a ‘cribwork’ domical form,
which may be left open atthe peak, or can be closed. Interstices
between the logs are packed with earth or stones.

Another form isthe 'many-legged’ hogan made up of
between six and eight encircling forked poles holding a ring-beam
of logs which support a cribwork roof of similar construction.
Further poles placed vertically are used forthe walls. These
require access to wood, but resources are limited in some parts
ofthereservation. Consequently, a large number of other hogan
types has been noted: built of planks, of coursed and uncoursed
sandstone and, more recently, cinder blocks.’ Tothe observer
the structure may seem crudely made, especially when itis
compared withthe fine quality of Navajo woven blankets and
rugs, oroftheirturquoise and silver jewellery.

When the hogan is built it is consecrated with the
Blessingway Chant, which evokes the first hogans built bythe
Holy People, Hastyeyaltiand Hastyehogan, gods of Sunrise and
Sunset. Thewords indicatethatthesymbolic connotations are
very different from the physical reality:

Built of afterglow

Standeth hishogan

The hogan blessed

Built of yellow corn

Standeth hishogan,

The hogan blessed.

Built of gems and shining shells
Standeth his hogan,

The hogan blessed.

Blessingway rites occur at leasttwice a year in most
hogans, and often more frequently, as for example, when a
member ofthefamily departs for along period. Withinthe hogan
thespace is physically undivided, butto the Navajo it is symbolic
ofthe cosmos: the floor, slightly dished, is directly relatedtothe
earth, which is female, whilethe concave interior oftheroof
corresponds to the sky, which is male. Sacred pollen issmeared
onthe house posts which symbolizethe four polesthat support
the sky. Inhogans where there are no vertical poles, itisthe
cornersformed bythe hexagonal constructionthat are
symbolically significant. Alwaystothe east, the entrance pole
is dedicated to the Earth Woman deity; thatto the west being
that ofthe Water Woman, the divinity ofthe Water World. Corn
Woman’s poleliestothe north;and the south hogan poleis
dedicated to Mountain Woman. Many Navajo divinities are
female, and even thoughtthe household head is male, the people
are matrilineal and matrilocal.

On enteringthe hogan men are expectedtositonthe
north side, facing south, and women sit onthe south side, where
theloom and cooking utensils are kept. Belongings arehung
on pegs and notches orare placed in crevices and ledges inthe
structure. Inthe middle isthe hearth, which symbolizesthe nadir
andtthe centre ofthe world. Tothe west ofthe hogan andfacing
theentrance, isthe place of honourforthe patriarch or matriarch,
and for visiting medicine men, or hosteen. Movement through
the hogan must follow the ‘sunwise path’. On entering, one
encirclesthe hearth onthe south side in a clockwise direction,
passingviathenorth sideto exit bythe east-facing entrance.
Behaviour within the hogan is marked by prohibitions: itis
permissable to speak loudly when passing bythe north side or
vociferously outside the hogan, but not inside. One must not
look into the hogan without permission, but once invited may

inspect every detail. No matter how crowded the hogan is with
sleepers, thereclining bodies must never be stepped over, northe
hearth crossed.

When a curing ceremonial takes place, such asthe Beauty
Way or Mountain Way Chant, asmooth sandfloor inthe centre
ofthe hogan (normallythe hearth) is prepared fortheritualsand
the making of the likaah, or symbolic ‘dry-painting), in coloured
earths. Lasting several days, the ceremonial culminates with the
sick person, often a child, sitting in the centre and infusingthe
healing powers oftheinvoked Holy People. It is possiblethat
children have a psychosomatic disposition to be ill in orderto
experience the unity of kinshipamongthe witnesses ofthe
chant, and are similarly disposed to get better after. But in any
eventthe hogan isthe centre ofthe ceremonialandthe place
from which all good emanates to counter the hostile spiritsthat
exist in the desert. Asthe symbol of life it is also witnessto
death; when an occupant dies, the body istaken outthrough
an opening made in the north wall, and the hogan is abandoned
and allowed to collapse; with death the spirit and material of
the hogan rejoin the earth.? Like other single-cell dwellingsthe
hogan is spatially differentiatedthrough custom, by gender,
by hierarchy, by ritual and by symbolic association and not by
physical partitions.

Thoughtthetime-spans involved are immense in terms of
human history, correspondences between the symbolism ofthe
hogan andthat ofthe yurt, let alone those of many othertypes of
Asian and Amer-Indian dwelling, are provocative. Now living in
Arizona, the Navajo came from the north, probably arrivingin
Colorado by the twelfth century." It is importantto notethatthe
Navajo (or Dine) speak an Athapaskan language, having broken
away from the main cluster of Athapaskan-speaking peoples
who inhabit northern Canada, Yukon and Alaska. These

territories are closetothe Aleutian ‘land-bridge, by which Asian
peoples are believedto have crossed into the Americas a dozen
millennia or more ago." Their descendants penetrated Central
America and populated much of South America, wherethe
cosmic symbolism ofthe dwelling persisted, sometimes in
combination with zoomorphism, orthe employment of animal
forms and metaphors. Thisisthecasewiththe Tukanoans, one
ofthe Amer-Indian peoples ofthe upper Amazon region.

Though many tribes have been assimilated into the new
Latin societies of mixed race, in Amazonia andthe remoter
regions of South America tribes of Amer-Indians retain their
identities, their life-ways, and beliefs. Aterritorythat stretches
between Brazil and Colombia isthe home of many Indian tribes
such asthe)ivaro, Yanomamo and Yukuna. Asubstantial
proportion ofthese peoples live in large, communal buildings
sometimes referred to as longhouses’. Following one of Claude
Levi-Strauss’s observations, these have been studied as ‘house
societies’. By no means uncontentious, the concept ofthe house
society raises many issues related to kinship, hierarchy, corporate
identity, feudalistic systems and much else, as anthropologists
debatethe validity ofanew classification.”? Inthe present
contextthe complex ritual and spiritual life of a house society
of Tukanoan Amer-Indians, has been studied by Christineand
Stephen Hugh-Jones.

Manioc cultivators, the Tukanoans live in patrilinealand
exogamous groups onthe banks ofthe Pira-Parana River.
Their community houses are of considerable dimensions, and
thatched with palm leaves on palm slats, with eaves that sweep
closetothe ground. Each roof frame is supported by eight major
house posts placed on either side of a central male-female axis.
The male end ofthe house, which is also where visitors are
received, faces east and is clad with bark paintings;the female

end is apsidal, with a central door, and faces west. Inside, nuclear
families ofthe male lineage occupy walled-off rooms on either
side ofthehouse and towards the rear, female, end. In each
compartment hammocks are slung around cooking hearths and
doors open bothtothe central space andtothe manioc gardens
outside. Inthe centre ofthe longhouse isthe main social area,
which isembraced by a ceremonial dance path. Here, themen
gather nightly, young male initiates sleep and ceremonials are
held. When major ceremonials, such astthe initiation rituals
Hewi, take place, duringwhich the chanting, flute-playing
young males paint each other black and arewhipped bythe
shaman to promote strength, they are hidden fromthewomen
byarattan screen.

All Tukanoans believe themselves to bethe children ofthe
Primal Sun, but each group also traces its originto a mythical
ancestor, one ofthe largest species of snake, theanaconda. In
mythicaltimes, anacondas swam west uptherivers from the
Water Door ofthe Milk River (the Amazon) stopping atthe
‘waking-up places’ wherethe ancestors ofthe Tukanoans danced
and established their homesites. The anacondas’ journey eastto
west correspondedtothe path ofthe Sun, and accordingly, the
male entrance, facing east, represents the Water Door by which
theancestral anacondas entered. The house as abody hasthe
entrance as its mouth, butitis also perceived asthe entranceto
the womb; whilethe rear doorway is regarded asthe anus. Birth
is associated, as might be expected, withthe ancestral ‘wakingup houses’, but also with the manioc gardens where birth literally
takes place. The gardens are reached through the family doors
and the woman’s door, butthewomb interiorofthe house,
the family apartments, the heat ofthe cookingfires (which is
associated with female sexuality), the nourishment ofthe
gardens and oftheforest beyond, are all seen as female andthe

ofritualand ceremonial; therectilinear structure ofthe front of

Water Doorand symbol of sexual penetration; theriverlanding
andthepaththatlinksitwith the dwelling are allmaleand

Alonghouse is also perceived to symbolize the world; the
roof as sky, the house-posts as mountains, thefloorasthe Earth,
andthegrave, the Underworld. The upper half ofthe multilayered cosmic gourd with its arboreal ‘tree-skin’ and successive
‘skins’ of birds, rainclouds, mist, moon, sun, stars and ‘sky-skin’
ofthe First Being, which theshamans, or priest-healers, pass

abovethe plane ofthe Earth. Below itisthe Underworld of
negative forces, ofthe River of Death, but also ofthe Sun’s River
bywhich the sun travelstto its daily regeneration inthe east, and
of other mythological associations. When a member ofthe group
dies, the body is placed in a coffin madefromthehalves ofa
canoe and interred in achamber off a deep hole below the floor
ofthe house. Whilethe spirit oftheancestorremainsin the
house, the body decays in its grave, butthen the soul enters

a beautifully painted ancestral longhouse, visible onlytothe
shamans andthe dead, wherethe ancestors perpetually dance.
When the name ofthe deceased isgivento anewborn childthe
spiritis happy for it lives again, the soulthrivinginthe continuing
ritual oftheancestral house."

Symbol systems
It may be seen from these Asian and American examples, thata
people may develop a symbol system that includes virtually all
their experience of their known world and embraces their history:
myths are symbols of their unknowable past. Through the myths
and legends, ritual lore, epic songs and religious customs of a
society, its values may betransmitted, communicated, repeated
and learned. Asthe roof underwhich so much ofthisisactedand
re-enacted intthe daily, yearly and life cycles, the dwelling readily
becomes the symbolic model ofthe greater universe oftimeand
spaceinwhich they areseento exist. The concept ofthe ‘symbol’
is often a vague one, and its distinction from a ‘sign’ in certain
usages may be unclear, so a few words should bespenton
explanation asto how the terms are employed here.

Asign is used to denote something -an object, a situation,
an event, specifically and unequivocally; signs under such
circumstances do not carry any additional associations. Symbols,

17: Corner of a Kabylie
room where the ‘east’
wall oflight meetsthe
‘south’ hearth wall. A
quernis placed below
thegrain jars. Painted
decorations recallwoven

18: Entranceto a Kabylie

totherightleadsto
the dark, lower and
‘natural’ end ofthe
dwelling. Maatka

ontthe other hand, are usedto connote meanings in additionto
those which they may depict;they have and inspire meaningful
associations. In this respect signs are distinct from symbols,
butasign can be interpreted asa symbol while it performs its
function of signification. Earlier, we briefly noted howthe
threshold or doorway distinguishes the external from the internal.
Intthe course of use, the very presence of adoorway denotesan
entrance, and in this sense it functions as assign. But it can

also become a symbol of entry or privacy, and may have many
additional symbolic meanings of, say, sexual penetration or
birth. As a sign it has only one specific meaning; as asymbol
itmay have a much larger sphere of associations.

Both sign and symbol, to be recognized and understood,
must exist within systems. The doorway exists within thesign
system of door denoting entrance, window denoting opening,
roof shape denoting house. But as symbols, the windows may
connote release, broadening perceptions, eyes ofthe spirits; the
roof may connote protection, the sky, the deities in their celestial
abode. Again, signs exist effectively in the present: they denote
here and now. Symbols exist inthe past, the present, or may
imply the future. In asense symbols aretimeless, though being
non-specific overtimetheirmeaning may change, gaining or
losing in force or effectiveness, and taking on new levels of
connotation.'s

Male and female, left-hand and right-hand parts ofthe
house have been noted in several examples in preceding pages.
Sun and Moon, night and day, good and evil, and life and death
are dualities within the experience of usall. So, too, arethe
spatial dichotomies of earth and sky, above and below, frontand
back, inside and outside and, as we have seen, these are often
related symbolically." This ‘binary principle’ may be extended
in numerous ways: by comparisons, contrasts, antinomies,

homologies, parallels and polar oppositions which are
nonetheless on the same metaphoric ‘axes’. How such dualism
is manifest in the dwellingwas demonstrated by Pierre Bourdieu
in his analysis of the Berber houses in Kabylie, Algeria, which
also indicated the contradictions that may arisewhen the
symbolism is applied literally.

Asimple structure of undressed stone, the Berber house
consists of one rectangular room divided a third ofthe way
alongits length bya cross wallto make a dark, flagstoned stable
at a lower level and a higher, better-lit, living space with a floor
polished with black clay and cow dung. Amain ridge beam,
which spans both spaces, is supported atthe dividing wall by a
pillar; in Berber symbolism the protecting, strong beam is male
andthepillarisfemale. The dark end ofthe house iswherethe
water vessels are placed and, inthe stable, wherethe animals
are kept; it is associated with sexual relationships, birth, sleep,
and death - with what is ‘animal’. In contrast, the higher living
space is associated with allthat is noble:thehonour ofthe
household head, his protection of his wife’s virtue. As asymbol
of his patrilineal kin, his rifle is bythe hearth atthe north gable
wall,wherethere are recessestotake household utensils and
grain vessels.

Inthelongwallonthe east facade isthe main entrance,
which is male: opposite isthe female entrance, smaller but set
inthe wall and illuminated by the main entrance. Known as ‘the
Wall of Light’ it is where the loom is situated, the loom beingthe
symbol ofwoman’s creative and domestic activityandofthe
values that her husband protects. Itis beforetheloomthat a
bride is presented, behind the loom where she sits after marriage,
andthere wherethe umbilical cord of her child is buried. Yetthere
isa problem, for her wallisthe west wall, though its eastern face
isthe surface that is illuminated. Internally, therefore, the west

importance ofthe dwelling as microcosm, such as Lebeuf’s
research ontthe Faliofthe Cameroon highlands. Better known,
and frequently cited, isthe work of Marcel Griaule and Germaine
Dieterlen and their colleagues concerningthe culture ofthe
Dogon people who liveon, ornearto, the Bandiagara escarpment

morethan three decades; even so, it was somefifteen years before
the Dogon revealed the essence of their cosmological ideas."
Inthe complex Dogon mythology the Creator God, Amma,
generated within infinite space a primordial egg with a double
placenta in which werethe Twin Nommo, sons of God. One twin,
Yurugu the fox, broke from the egg, bringing with him as hefell
a piece ofthe placenta, which became Earth. Eventuallyinthe
creation myth Yurugu cameto stand forthe dry, the uncultivated,
the uninhabited, the night and disorder, while his counterpart
Nommo becamethe symbol ofthe ordered world, ofthe day, the
sky, fertility, the cultivated and the inhabitable. The concept of
twinness, essentialto Dogon mythology isa recognition ofthe
duality of so much inthe world of experience, which in many
other societies, has become a fundamental ordering principle
intheir created world, including their dwellings.?°
PartoftheappealoftheDogon conception of both village
and dwelling was its anthropomorphism. The parts ofthe ginu
da, the big house of alineage cluster are relatedtothose ofthe
human body. Sothe head is associated with the kitchen, with
thehhearth stones asthe eyes, the dembere or central room with
the belly and theflanking goat houses and stores culminating
intowers, with the limbs. The breasts are symbolized by jars
of water, the sexual organs by grinding stones, andthe whole,
graphically expressed by Griaule, represents a man Iying on his
right side in the act of procreation.?'! However, Dogon compounds
do not correspond in plan to Griaule’s drawing, as comparison

Orientations

Many other kinds of symbolic system related to the house exist,

some peculiarto one culture, others borrowed or assimilated.

Amongthe Sakalava, atribe on the west coast of Madagascar

who, like other Malagasay tribes may be of Indonesian descent,

the plan ofthehhouse is used as an astrological calendar, which

is of Chaldean origin. Theterms used are of Arabic extraction,

andto confuse the issue of its source further, connections have j
been noted with some aspects ofthe Japanese house. Asimple
rectangular house with a central pole supportingtheridge, the
Sakalava dwelling is oriented north-south. The months are
identified with the corners and two points on each side ofthe
house, commencing at the north-east with Alahamady, or Aries.
Each corner hasthree days associated with it; each wall point has
two (twenty-eight in all), sothatthe zodiacal calendaris also a
daily one. The points ofthe zodiac determine the position of
household articles: Adaoro for example (Taurus) isthe household
head’s sleeping place; Asombola (Virgo) is relatedtothe Arabic
as-sumbula (grain) and isthesite ofthericemortarandthe
winnowing pan.?

We can only guess how old such symbolic associations
with the building may be in these and other societies; there is
reason to believe that many may link with the remote past of
tribal peoples, and have been passed on orally by successive
generations. Inthe previous chapter we considered use related to
spatial organization inthe Malay house, butthe building ofthe
house means more than this, beingaccompanied by extensive
rituals and symbolism. Although the Malays today are largely

Muslim, they retain many practices and beliefs oftheirformer
animist religion, includingthe propitiation ofthespirits ofearth,
water and forest into whose domains the kampung, or village,
intrudes.?*Raised abovethe ground and situated in a defensive
clearing, the house is separated from the dangerous spiritsofthe
Jungle. Nevertheless, charms and spirit traps are hungaaround
the dwelling as a further protection from their power. Apawang
(magician) builds a ritual hut and performs rites to drive off
malignant spirits when new ground isto be prepared for building.

First, however, it is necessary to select asitethatis
auspicious, permittingthe appropriate orientation ofthe house.
Uneven ground is avoided, forsmall mounds are believedtto be
the abodes of hostile beings. The colour ofthe soil, the inclination
oftheland, the orientation ofthe dwelling have allto be
determined by bomoh (animist priest) and pawang working with
the guidance of astrological charm books. Oncethesite has been
selected and cleared, atree is carefully selected and felledto
provide the main poststo support the dwelling. On the principle
of‘onetree, one house’thetimber ofthetreeis divided inthe
sawpitto producethe nine poststhat are basictothe construction.
The kiang seri, thefirst pillar, is then positioned on the south side
ofthe house, after a dedication ceremony and feast. Offerings of
iron, copperand hardwoods are placed in the hole;the house
spiritwould reside there, whilethe post is capped wth red, black
and white symbolic cloths.#5 Other posts are placed in the precise
relationshipthatthey hadinthetrunk ofthetreeatthetime
of division. With the participation of relatives and friends, the
house is erected in propitious months and days, with completion
ofthe building marked by due ceremonies and feasts.

In societies where a literate tradition developed and where
mysticrrites and observances became increasinglythe esoteric
province of alearned priesthood, traditional customs were

written down. During the process some may have become

less flexible, enteringthe canons of religious doctrine, and by
application, of building. This appearsto have been the case

with Hindu architecture, where ancient practices were noted,
especially from the members oftheguilds, andas earlyasthe
sixth century AD recorded intthe sastras, or manuals, ofthesilpins,
thetemple craftsmen. One ofthe Vastu Sastras, the Manasara,
laid down the proportions fortemplesandtowns andtherules
of building in the finest detail.

The silpin sastra gave rules governingthe plan ofthe
house, recommending positions forthe courtyard, the respective
rooms of men and women, theroom for receiving guests and
storerooms, thekitchen and cooking space, andthe bathing
place. The house was interpreted anthropomorphically,
conceptualized in human terms asthe organs ofthe Purusha or
the Primal Being, whose head, arms, legs, stomach and sexual
organs had their parallel in the organization of the dwelling.?5
Such treatises were directed mainly atthetemple priests and
atthewealthy and literate, often being passed on, many times
removed, throughtthe assimilated knowledge ofthe craftsmen
andthe propitiatory practices ofthe village priests, to influence
the dwellings of ordinary people.?7

Building a house in Andhra Pradesh today involves
religious observancesthatcommence with the selection of
thesite. Having divined the most auspicioustime and day for
building, which is generally considered to be between February
and April, the pundit, a Hindu priest, enjoins the family in prayers
atthesite. There hemarksthe positions ofthe corner poles, and
coconuts are broken inaritual intended to ensurethe safety of
the building. The householder digs the hole forthe centre post
andthe coconut milk is poured into it. Nine coloured bean seeds,
signifyingthe planets, are buried inthe hole, invokingthe

Therules ofthe sastras are followed bythe unlettered
village craftsmen who buildthe houses and raisethe steeply
pyramidical bamboo roof frames. Ahouse is required to be
symmetrical, with a central doorway and well-made door. From
onetothree poles should supporttthe ridge, and the plan must
theoretically conform to specific measurements: 12ft (4m)
square, 12ft by ıgft(4m by 6m), and so on. Village craftsmen
generally adheretothecanon ofthe sastra, though with
variationsthat conform with local preferences astothe choice of
proportions used. Thewomen decorate the ground in front ofthe
houses daily with alponas, complex patterns of rice powder, asan
offeringto Laxshmi, the Goddess of Wealth, and other deities.?

Whilethe cosmological meaning ofthe individual
dwelling is our concern here, the dwelling is very frequently
situated in a village ortown that may have its own expanding
spheres of meaning, in which the mosque, church, shrine or
temple isthe spiritualfocus. Bali, one ofthe many islands of
Indonesia, isa mere 80 miles in length. Hometothe only Hindu
people in a predominantly Muslim nation, itisfamous forthe
beauty of its landscape, its people, their music, dancingand
ceremonials. But much is screened from the eyes ofthe
casual visitor. Straight and blank brick walls edge the karang,
the houseyard orcompound, and beyond its narrow entrance
ontthe seaward side, ascreen wall effectively hidesthe
domesticlife within.

thenorth-east, facingthe sacred volcano, Gunung Agung.

during the melaspasin, the dedicatory ceremonial, and in all

members ofthesame clan or kinship group. One oftheseisthe
“origin’ compound, and will housethe principal ancestraltemple
oftherelated families.3°

Within the private domain oftheir dwellingsthey may
feel secure and intimate with their family, but Balinese
villagers are intricately bound into the structure oftheir
communities in complex ways, that involvethe recognition of
status and, within their class, manifold time-consuming duties
andresponsibilities. Each household must be represented by
both aman and awoman (usuallythe wife) on the banjar
(hamlet) council, which establishes rules of behaviour and ritual
observances and determines the location of new dwellings.
Every banjarhas mutual aid groups and a sabak, or irrigation
society, which maintains the provision of water fortherice
terraces. Many other groups, clubs and societies existand a
Balinese commoner may be a participatingmember of uptoa
dozen ofthem. All are ultimately linked through thetemple
system, the pamaksan, to which every Balinese owes allegiance.3'
Itisestimatedthatthere are some 20,000 temples, one for
every hundred people (notto mention the 300,000 household
and innumerable wayside-shrines). Allthetemples require
ceremonials and duties including ritual cock-fights, the music
ofthe gamelan orchestras oftuned gongs, and the beautiful
and elegantly costumed stylized dancing ofthegirl-child
temple dancers, whose every finger or eye movements, let
alone their poses and steps, have symbolic significance.3

Another system, similar in some aspects, inthat itis
also appliedtotemples, but very different in others has long
been in use in Japan. Hogaku (Direction-Angle) is an orientation
method also based on the use ofthe compass and linked with
astrology. Directions are associated with Go gyo, thefive
elements, andwiththe Zodiac. Together they are employed in

determining house orientation, or Kaso. A binary principle,
known as Inyo, from Infemale, and Yo male, appliesto
orientation andto other conceptual dualities relatedtothe
form ofthe building and its details. The birthdate and auspicious
signs relatedtothe owner, theshape ofthesite, the location of
the house centre are all considerationsthat reflectthe
beneficent and malignant, positiveand negative forcesthat
influence the precise determination ofthe plan and situation
ofthe house.3

Each ofthe basic twenty-four divisions ofthe Hogaku
compass offered certain axioms that advise on dwelling form
and plan. So, a Shinto shrine or a gate situated attheinpropitious
north-east would bring misfortune, but a hillock, which acted as
ascreen, would be lucky. Correspondingly, apondtothe southwest could mean intemperance ofthe household head, a lavatory
sited there would bring constant disease but a garden ortrees
planted would be benign. But a Shinto shrineto the north-west
would bring divine protection, while a storeroom would be
beneficial because prosperity flows north-west alongthis axis.
However, anumber ofthe Hogaku rules could conflict with good
practice -if alavatory was sited in the most propitious position
(approximately north-west by north) with a hill-slope rising
tothenorth, then awellsitedtothewest ofthe building in a
position which could bring domestic peace would nevertheless
be proneto seepage from it. Perhaps because of a growing
awareness of such problems some details of Hogaku have
declined in use, but its broad principles are still observed in rural
districts and an expert in the system is capable of weighing up
the relative merits of different locationstothe satisfaction of
house-builders.3+

Generally, Hogaku is site-focused, while feng shui places
greater emphasis on the environment. Ofthe systems for

31: Thesabak, or |
irrigation society in |
which all families are

Balinesericeterraces

identifyingthe most auspicious sites for buildingand forthe
location of the parts ofthe dwelling, the Chinese form of
geomancy, feng shui (Wind-Water) is among the most complex.
Itwas developed asa consistenttheoryinthetenth century AD,
when different forms of geomancy were brought together in

a complicated multi-plate and subdivided site compass. The
Chinese binary principle of Yang (male) and Yin (female) pervades
all concepts ofthe universe. However, they are not equal, forthis
would imply opposition; Yang is dominant inthe ideal state.
Astrology is closely linked with feng shui-the constellations,
theChinese Zodiac, the calendar of days and months, the Four
Seasons, the Five Elements, the Ten Heavenly Stems, the Twelve
Earthly Branches, and the Trigrams and Hexagrams oftheIChing
(the ‘Book of Changes’), allhave Yang and Yin attributes, which
interact, wax and wane.

Important inthe practice of feng shuiisthe conducting of
ch’i, the Vital Breath, tothe dwelling, and the avoidance of Shaor
Noxious Vapours. Ch’iemanates from the Dragon Kings, whose
lairs are in the mountains, and follows rivers and meandering
routes. Straight lines are abhorrent, forthey permitthe entry of
the ‘secret arrows’ of demons. Mountains are Yang, and are also
the abodes ofthe Gods; thecomplementary undulating hills are
associated with the Tigers and are Yin; an ideal site for building is
where the ‘White Tiger ofthe East’ andthe ‘Blue Dragon ofthe
West’ are symbolically united. Usingthe feng shuicompass, on
whose plate astrological signs are inscribed, the Hsien-Sheng,
or feng shui master, ascertains the strength or weakness of Ch’i
flow, the Dragon’s pulse, the influence of Heaven, andthe
‘health’ ofthe Earth. Geomancy is used both forthe dwellings
ofthelivingand those ofthe dead, forthecontentment ofthe
ancestors in theirtombs will cause them to be well-disposed
to the living.35

“ i a

LT Bla N

Baffling in its complexity to all but a master, feng shuiin
application is often extremely practical, informed by centuries of
accumulated wisdom. A desirable dwelling site is south-facing,
protected from the north, on well-drained and sloping land,
served by gently flowing water, and accommodating breezes.
Whentthere is norising ground behindthe housetothe north,
the Hsien-Sheng may recommend the planting ofascreen of
trees. One is advised notto build over buried wells or dead ttrees,
andto locatetoilets with pigsties on the south-east corner above
the slope. Windows, door openings and furniture, notto mention
trees and garden features should be positioned sothatthech’i,
the flow ofthe life force and of air, is neither permittedto
stagnate nor so ventilated that the breezes disperse it. Bedrooms
should not connect with, or be located over empty spaces and
principalrooms should face south. Obviously, not all conditions
can be met, especially in the location ofthe houses ofthe poor.
Propitiatory offerings and auspicious colours may be usedto
counter adverse forces and mirrors placedto deflect ‘secret
arrows’. To retain a Hsien-Sheng may be beyond the resources of
many families, but popular manuals, illustrated with cartoonlike drawings and in virtual comic-strip form are available which
give good advice inthe context of feng shuisymbolism, tothe
house-builder and home-maker.3

Cosmos and community

Among indigenous cultures that had been dominated by colonial
powers and were disposessed or relocated, the values ascribed

to their dwellings sometimes declined. This was the case with
numerous Native American peoples who had ceased to usetheir
traditional house types bytthe earlytwentieth century. Though
some Chippewa domical-framed wigwams, Hidatsa earth lodges
and Apache wickiups were still being built inthe 19205 the

majority had long been abandoned, likethe frame houses ofthe
Kwakiutl, Haida and other First Nations peoples ofthe Pacific
coastal region of north-west Canada. Following a policy ofthe
“assimilation’, the destruction of ‘native’ material cultureandthe
elimination of expressions of tribal identity was encouraged by
the Canadian government, tothe extentthat some Haida houses
were burnedto the ground by Indian Agents in the 19308.
Similarly, the conical pole and tent-cover tipiofthe Plains was
greatly reduced in use, although a few Blackfoot still lived in
them intthe 1930s. In spite ofthis, the tipiretained its cosmic
spatial connotations and many tipis were raised at pow-wows,
orinter-tribal meetings throughout the century. Sweat-houses

intheform ofa small traditional dwelling are still in useforritual
cleansing, whiletemporary ceremonial lodges forthe Sun-dance
are still erected by some Plains peoples.7

Even ifthe house has lost much of its meaningforthe
individual family, there has been a significant increase inthe
number of ceremonial structures, councillodges, cultural and
arts centres, schools and other community buildings on Native
American reservations inthe United States, as has been
documnented by Carole Herselle Krinsky. Though the majority are
architect-designed, in some cases by Native American architects,
they have been significant inthe renewal of cultural identity. This
has not been sustained in housing however, with large numbers
of houses in a state of serious deterioration and a massive
shortfall inthenumber provided bythe Department of Housing
and Urban Development. Expectations of housing provision adds
tothe problem and, in spite of longttraditions of self-building
there appearto be few inducementstocopewith the housing
shortage by such means.3®

Among the Kwakwaka’wakw - speaking peoples ofthe
Pacific north-west of Canada, includingthe Kwakiutl, itis said
that “the house holds thettribes in its hands”. While there has
been no return to traditional domestic housing, there has been
aremarkable revival inthe building oftraditional longhouses
by tribes who are reclaiming their sense of identity. Magnificent
framed structures of cedar, some withtheir broad facades bearing
totemic images painted in black, red and white with pigments
made from coal, berries and clam shells, have been built since
the mid-1950s. Other First Nation bands such asthe Qua’utsun
(Cowachin) of southeast Vancouver Island orthe Gitanyow
(formerly Kitwancool) ofthe upper Skeena region in northwest
British Columia, have constructed new longhouses for community
useandasthefocus oftheir renewed life. Overthirty ofthe

Gitanyow gyetim gan, or ‘totem poles’, stand in front ofthebig
house, facingthe Kitwancool river. As Glen Williams, hereditary
chief ofthe Gitanyow nation which is related tothe Tsimshian,
explained, “we are revivingoureconomy based on thetraditional
skills we have maintained here: carving, the arts, fishing, food
preparation an intimate knowledge of ourterritories.”39

Comparable in scale with the Canadian long-houses were
the great meeting houses ofthe Maori of Aotearoa (New Zealand),
though bytthe twentieth century the building of traditional
‘sleeping houses’ had waned. There had been considerable variety
in the plans ofthe houses with rectangular, circular or oval plans
recorded. Whilethey could be simple structures many were
carefullythatched and occasionally, had finely detailed front
elevations. The customary kainga, or settlement would have
several such sleeping houses. In front ofthe chief's house was
the marae, orsacred ground, where a meeting house would also
be constructed. Following colonization oftheislandsinthe
mid-nineteenth century the chief’s house became increasingly
important, takingon the combined roles of guest-house, church
and community meeting house. New meeting houses continue
to be built, the necessary skills beingtaught inthe whare
wananga, or native college.#°

Unlikethe Native American social buildings inthe
United States, which sometimes echo thetraditionalforms
but are generally built with modern materials and construction
techniques, the Maori meeting houses are impressive structures
built with the use of traditional materials and methods.
Conformingto a pattern in their rectangular plans and sweeping
pitched roofs, the symbolism of the houses is anthropomorphic,
each house representingthe common ancestor ofthe
community. Onthefacadetherichly carved barge-boards are
conceived as arms, terminating in rararapa, orfingers, whilethe

ridge is regarded asthespine, the rafters and wall slabs as ribs.
Within, the ridge is supported bytwo substantial carved posts,
that atthe front representing Tane, the Life Giver, whilethe rear
post is sculptured to depict Hinenuitepo, or Death. Ancestral
figures of each lineage are richly carved in the vigorous styles of
the regional traditions, whilethe rafters above them are painted
with flowing shapes and clear colours which, insome cases
record the fortunes of each family. Between the ancestral figures
are tukutuku, or panels of grasses and reeds woven bythe
women. Other buildings, including a cookhouse, banqueting
room, sleeping houses and stores may also definethe marae,
butthe carved meeting house istthe most important, being
regarded astthe living spirit ofthetribal group, and being directly
addressed by its given name in ceremonials. The symbolism of
the carvings and paintings is both complex and protected, their
deeper meanings seldom disclosed.#

Many ofthesymbolic aspects ofthe buildings discussed
in this chapter are reinforced through myths and legends which
encapsulate the values ofthe societies concerned. They are
mysteries, encoded in symbols, which are onlycomprehended
through the acquisition of esoteric knowledge which may be
inaccessibleto allbuttheshamans, medicine men or priests. At
successive stages in life, some of the veils overthese mysteries
may be unfolded, perhaps with the aid of hallucinogen-induced
visions and dreams. The traumas of circumcision and initiation
to manhood may be followed by other 'rites of passage’ in
successive phases of life untilthe elders with their accumulated
age and wisdom pass on their understanding to succeeding
generations. The buildings they inhabit may symbolizethe
seemingly inexplicable, but iftheir meanings remain implicit
rather than explicit, the cosmic connotations ofthe dwelling
may be further expressed in shape, form, colour and decoration.
