Decorated dwellings
‘Remember, the path of culture isthe path away from
ornamentation towards the elimination of ornament’, wrote
the radical architect Adolf Loos in 1910. For Loos, who was writing
inthe wake ofthe effusions of Art Nouveau, ornament was a
“crime”. His attacks upon its use, his denigration of ‘the Papuan’
who ‘covers everything within his reach with decoration’ and his
advocacy of ‘pure form alone’ exemplified in his own buildings,
were highly influential on early modernist architects.'
Nevertheless, some, likethearchitect Bruno Taut, saw much to
praise intheformalism of Japanese traditional architecture, even
iftheir pilgrimages to the East didnottakethem to Papua New
Guinea, Indonesia, orevento China.?

Some 300 kilometres south-west of Shanghai liesthe
Huizhou region of southern Anhui province, where, inthe Yi
and Shexian counties, the fields of padi, vegetables, cereals and
tobacco are meticulousiy maintained. Oxen and buffalo draw
thewooden ploughs in the valley mud-fields, while familyteams
plant bunches of padi rice in shallow terraces ofthe lower hill
slopes. Below the ranges of wooded hills, scores of villages and
smalltowns are grouped nearthe Liang River and other
tributaries ofthe southern loop ofthe Yangtze, their low-Iying
white and purple-grey masses generally facing south or southwest. Approached by aroad that may follow theline oftherriver
orthefoothills, the villages are often announced by a stone
memorial archway, ten metres or more in height, with richly
carved panels bearing motifs and inscriptions, supported by a
row of four columns. Beyond, the village houses are one or, more
frequently, two storeys in height, onlythe Ancestral Hallsand
temples risingabovethem. Narrow lanes paved with granite
blocks wind through the villages, often alongside watercourses
in granite channels bridged by slabs.3To some extentthesiting

Rendered and painted white, brick walls flankthe sides of
the houses, rarely interrupted by windows or any other openings,
largely as a precaution against the spread of fire. Changes of level
astthey indicatethe heights oftherooms within, givethe walls
astepped contour defined bytiled or carved brick copings.
Descending in steps the wall-copings with their risingterminal
finials are compared with horse-heads, and are much admired.
Only asingle doorway on thefront facade wall may break the
white planes, its singularity emphasized by a large lintel stone
surmounted by a hood of carved brick and sometimes with a
tiled coping, the different details identifyingthe occupying
families. Double doors, barred at night, open ontoasmall
courtyard, which may have awide rainwatertrough, sometimes
with fish and plants in it, spanning much ofthe rectangular
space. Beyond arethetwo orthree bays ofthe house, marked by
astepped plinth. Thefirst bay isopentothecourtand may be
used for relaxing, for raising silkworms in large trays, orfor handcraftinganything from eel-traps to a new plough. Flanking
corridors lined with screens may leadto a staircase risingtothe
rooms on the upper floor, ofwhich some share a verandah
that opens on tothe narrow court. Afurtherroom may serve a
ceremonial or guest reception function, but in the larger houses
aformal hall defined by light screen walls may occupy a bay

between them.5 Each bay is delineated by a row of four columns,
the outer ones being shorterand closeto the walls; in spite of
theirexternalappearance the walls arenot loadbearingandthe
columns alone support the structure ofthepitched roofs which
riseto 30 ft (9 orı0 m) attheridges. The majority ofthese houses
are constructed usingtthe tailiang system, with massive
transverse cedartie-beams between columnsthattakethe main
purlins, these ‘moon beams’ being slightly curved and thicker at
the centre, the ends being supported by brackets carved in floral
shapes. Short struts support smaller beamsto createthe slightly
curved, shallow pitch oftheroof.®

Introduced with the Ming Dynasty in the fourteenth
century AD, and adopted bythe Qing Dynasty inthe seventeenth
century, the houses were builtto asimilar plan, usingthe same
structural system which local carpenters could learn to erect.
Variations camewith thewooden walls, the placing of stairsand
the partitioningofrooms, each requiringwooden screens which
were carved and assembled in a seemingly infinite variety of
patterns and designs: some geometric, some symbolic, some
figuratively carved with landscapes, trees, birds and animals.
Duringmuch ofthe Ming period residents were only permittedto
paint the interiorwoodwork black, but inthe Qing period natural
wood could be used andthe carvings tended to be more intricate.
Occasionallythetransverse beams were sculpted with recesses
bearing mythical or genre scenes in bas-relief.?

Many ofthe houses were owned by farmers, butthe
fertility ofthe land and the craft skills ofthe populace ledtothe
rise of amerchant class, who builtthe larger houses. Extended
family occupancy was usual until the October Revolution; in
subsequent years numerous houses accommodated two or
three families, and still do. Duringthe Red Guard era some of
the houses were vandalized and carvings defaced or destroyed,

but byfarthe majority of carved panels survived by being
temporarilyremoved or reversed.® Today, the villages of Huizhou,
with their white walled and dark purple-grey tiled roofs, reflected
inthe streams and pools are clustered harmoniously before
carefully nurtured fields and against the backdrops ofthenhills.
Within, the variety of screens and carvings is continually
animated bythecchanginglight and shadows. Perhaps if Adolf
Loos and his contemporaries had seenthem, the remarkable
synthesis of clearforms and finely crafted decorations might
havetempered their opinions. Who knows? The course of
modernism could have been different.

Coloured walls

To western European eyes, some forms of indigenous building
are of great beauty, well-regarded forthe evidence of a highly
developed design sense, or fortheirrefined craftsmanship and
attention to detail. Architects, in particular, admirethe ‘vernacular’
for its demonstration ofthefundamental organization of form
and space in satisfyingthe demands of function. Notraditional
dwelling has been more attractive to architects, and indeed,

to tourists, than the ‘cubic’ whitewashed houses ofthe Greek
islands. Since they were endorsed by Le Corbusierthey have been
admired for the purity of their ‘primary forms’?

Sugar white againstthe indigo sea, Paroikiaon the
western bay of Paros is almost a clich& of a Cycladic island
township. In classical times it was the source of Parian marble
and was attractiveto invaders, and from 1537 to 1830 it was under
Turkish rule. A castle built by the Franks once dominated the
town; now it is almost completely obliterated by the white, flatroofed, originally one-roomed dwellings, or monokhoro, ofthe
old kastrothat set a consistent scaletothe community. Today,
the village houses are generally of two storeys, built of slabs of

grey-green schist. Doorways and windows were formerly framed
inthe local marble, which was also used for steps thoughtthe
internal floors were often of clay. Cypress limbs span therooms,
and layers of seaweed, reeds and beaten earth mixed with goathair, provide an insulating, weather-resistant surface forthe
roofs.'? At street level there are stores forfishingtackle, stables
for donkeys and recesses for wells, fountains or even small
kitchens, with stone hobs to take waiting pitchers. Steep flights
of external steps lead to upper rooms between the white walls of
the narrow streets, and the doors, window-frames and balconies
are painted in blue and sea-green, a legacy perhaps, ofthe
Ottoman period.

Paroikia houses are closely packed together and even if
openings occasionally reveal small, basil-scented courtyards,
there is little room for expansion. Consequently, some houses

have been extended by constructing an arch overthestreet with
a room ortwo above it. Other dwellings span the streets
unconventionally; alivingroom on oneside, a kitchen recess on
the opposite side, and bedrooms atthe upper levels of both. This
is dueto the system of ‘partible inheritance’ when property is
divided between the members ofthe next generation, following
the decease of the owner. Large paving slabs are edged in white,
marking outterritory and appropriating external livingspaceto

may be undertaken inthese claimed spaces, andsometimes a
cul-de-sac is sealed off by a low wallto indicate the extension of
a dwelling."Though it has suffered and gained from tourism,
Paroikia is still a fishing port out of season, with asmall square
where old men sit drinking ouzo, a quay where nets are repaired,
andchurches and wells that act as social centres. Formany a
western architect the simple, flat-roofed, lime-washed, Cycladic
vernacular displays a formal unity, which has also conditioned
tourist perceptions, the appeal ofthe ‘white villages’ ofthe
Mediterranean being exploited in countless travel brochures.
Residents are now obliged to keep their walls white, with thick
layers of lime-wash applied almost dailyto areas where walls or
even paths are constantly soiled.'

Painting the external walls of a dwellingisamongthe
simplest and yet most arresting forms of decoration. Among the
colour-washed houses of much of Central and Southern Europe,
the earth colours, which includethe ochres and siennas, raw or
burnt, are frequently employed with subtlershades achieved by
mixing them with white. Adding handfuls of chestnut or laterite
soils can also produce warm hues and pink washes, which may
beenhanced by contrastingthe colours of doors and window
frames. In parts of Bohemia, villages comprise anumber of

10,11: Homes onthe
island village of
Burano, in

the Venice Lagoonare
painted with bold, clear

colours. Italy

with a church and, sometimes, a smithy. With domestic quarters
on oneside of acourtyard and afacing row of stores and stables
onthe other, each pair of building masses is linked by a unifying
facade in which the main gates for cart access and smaller
doorways are incorporated. Modified architraves, pilastersand
classical details or mouldings are delineated against a background
of yellow ochre or muted pink, often with an inscription orthe
date of building, the initials ofthe owner or builderandtools,
plants or other symbols moulded in relief and painted white."

Similartreatments areto be found in Hungary and Austria,
while further south inthe Venetian lagoon the island of Burano
is notable forthe vibrancy ofthe colours of its houses. An island
of poorfishermen, it had a population in the mid-eighteenth
century of about 6,000 people living in cottages, many having
singlerooms only on each of two floors. Built of brick, with tiled
pitched roofs and tall chimneys, they generally had symmetrical
facades, sometrimes with adormer window orfrontal chimney
and with steep stairsatthe rear. Lined along canals spanned
by bridges, they remain substantiallythesame, but increased
prosperity following the provision of public services has enabled
the islandersto purchase their cottages, andto decorate them in
strong, plain colour fields.'+

Such use of colour on houses is common in many countries,
particularly among those of Hispanic origin or influence. In
Venezuela, for example, contrasting or complementary colours
are used for door frames, window grilles and relief mouldings.
The originality ofthe decoration by the householders of
Maracaibo, however, did not savethe central barrio of El Saladillo
from destruction in thename of urban development.'; More
fortunate were the painters of houses in Mexico whose daring
juxtaposition of colours is justly celebrated. Inthevillages and
townships of Puebla, for instance, many houses, shops and

churches have their walls painted in clear, bright pigments. With
access to cheap commercial paints, Mexican householders make
extensive use of rosa mexicano, an intense ‘shocking pink’, often
in association with deep bottle green. The brighter viridian green
is also popular, even forthe painting of graves, while purple,
orange, chrome yellow and bluesthat range from cerulean to
ultramarine, are also widely employed. Traditionally, a layer of
mud plaster would be applied to a wall before it was finished with
paint. With access to store-bought plaster and canned paintsthe
adjacent colours can bevibrant, especiallyonthe colonnaded
sidewalks that frontthe row houses of Tlacotalpan in Veracruz.
Such use of colour among Mexican vernacular decorators was
influential on such architects as Luis Barragän and Ricardo
Legorreta, though the painters were doubtless unaware ofit.So
important isthe use of colour on the dwellings ofthe peoplethat
Mexican migrants in California have maintained thetradition,
most notably onthesmall houses and businesses ofthe San
Francisco Bay suburb of San Pablo."

Inthe majority ofthese cases the areas of colourhave
been onthe facades of small, often one-roomed dwellings,
apparentlysymbolizingthe inhabitants’ collective sense of
identity through the practice, asserting their individuality in
thehuesthey have chosen and, in effect, claimingthe space
beforethem. Other cultures have been emboldened to explore
a diversity of motifs and patterns on their house walls, evolving
forms of decoration which accord with the aesthetic norms of
their group while permitting greater freedom for personal
creativity. Such isthe case with the Ndebele ofthe Transvaal
whose former rondavel dwelling has been replaced, under urban
influence, by a rectangular unit. Incommon with other peoples
of southern Africa, amongthem the Sotho, the Pediofthe
Transvaal, and the Tswana of Botswana, the Ndebele have

retained the lapa (cf. Tswana lolwapa), or court, servingasan
external reception apron beforethe dwelling huts. Divided by a
wall,thecourt hasa rear cooking place and a front area, which

is surrounded by stepped seating platforms designed to getthe
benefit ofsummer shade and winter sun. Usuallythe entrance
isemphasized by moulded clay piers or by raisingthe level ofthe
wall, ziggurat fashion, in a succession of steps.

Both the front ofthe house andthe outside walls ofthe
lapa are painted in strong colours, the external facade wall
contrasting with the more sober grey and white chevron patterns
ofthe lapa interior and the brown or grey walls ofthe less
important sides These retain the monochromatic character of
the earliest phase of Ndebele mural art. Slaked lime is used for
the ground-work of white and a mixture of clay and soot is
prepared forthe strong bands of black which delineate bold
designs on the vertical surfaces. Earths provide the basic palette
of red-brown and ochre, with Rickett’s Blue, a pigment usedto
heighten the whiteness of domestic laundry, mixed with chalk
whiting or used neat.” A powerful symmetry is evidentinthe
patterns, many of which derive from woven textiles. African
peoples likethe Ndebele andthe related M’Pogga are noted for
theirinventive and colourful beadwork, bracelets, armbands,
collars, headbands and aprons. Stepped patterns produced by
the careful stringing of coloured beads were enlarged onthe wall
surfaces. Having no effective binder some ofthe colours were
fugitive and deteriorated on exposureto seasonal rains. Asthe
maintenance of a clean and decorative lapawas regarded asa
virtueamongthe Ndebele, thewomen annually restored their
paintings in early spring or in anticipation of pubertyritesor.a
wedding festival. New designs were intermittently introduced,
with motifs which, by the 1950s, increasingly reflectedthe
proximity of Pretoria. Letters from automobile licence plates

enjoyed a brief fashion, butthe most persistenttthemes were
based on urban Victorian architecture. Domes and cupolas, towers
and gateways, seen on the Indian mosque and the Town Hall in
Pretoria, which displayed amarked centrality, proved popular."

Inside, the houses were normally simply furnished, but
bythe late 1970s imitation full-size dressers, fitted with drawer
knobs, displays of plates and other china, even paper doilys, which
were based on the furnishings in the kitchens of white homes
where some ofthewomen had worked, were homogeneously
moulded in clay and brightly painted. Some measure of social
aspiration may bereadintotheseandthe changing motifs, but
the Ndebele women drew inspiration from many sources.
Traditional lapa decoration passed from motherto daughters,
respectingthe ground rules of white field, black outlines, clear
colours and pervasive symmetry which form the constituents
ofthe Ndebele visual language." Nearto Pretoria, the Ndebele
settlements were engulfed by the expansion ofthe city, likethe
beautiful ‘Speelman’s Place‘, whose inhabitants were resettled in
Native Trust lands in the mid 19505. Many Ndebele became, inthe
parlance ofthetime andtheregion, ‘detribalized‘, absorbed into
the city, adopting western economy. Decades later, the rural
Ndebele ofthefragmented settlements of Bophuthatswana still
maintain thetraditional painted lapas, but there is littleroom,
time or inclination for pride in decoration inthe ever-expanding
shanty settlements of Kwandebele outside Pretoria.

Motifs and symbols

Colouristhe simplest form of decoration, but in many cultures
three-dimensionalornamentation isemployed, executed with
skilful craftsmanship and in fine detail. Where such decoration
isto befound may be conditioned by many factors, not leastthe
materials employed in building. Some, like palm trunks, cannot

be carved while certain timbers are better carved onthe length
than on their ends, though the projecting ends of joists will often
be sculptured in facial or animal likenesses. Frequently, exposed
structural elements like brackets or consoles, columns or posts,
may be notched, incised or painted. Carving oftimbers used in
building can reduce the weight ofthecomponents-butitcan
also weaken them; the same may be said of stone, which may be
too hard, like granite, ortoo granular in texture like some sandstones to lend itselfto the edge tool; others may besmooth and
dense like marble or alabaster and can promote fine and detailed
workmanship. Often the material itself suggests decorative
treatments in grain or vein. Reeds and grass when stained in
different colours and woven into mats and small panels, create
patterned and textured surfaces. With the use of modular
elements such as coursed stone and brickwork, or interlocking
faience and tiles, many patterns may be devised. Clay may be
moulded and plaster worked in relief, such treatments being
particularly suited to large planes and surfaces - walls, floors
and, with greater difficulty butto maximized effect, ceilings.
Craftspersons need to have command of their materialsandtheir
tools, but above all, ofthe devices, motifs and qualities ofthe
cultures to which they belong.

Surprisingly little research has been undertaken onthe
aesthetics oftribal, peasant and folk societies in decorating
buildings, with Marian Wenzel’s study of house decoration in
Nubia, or Anthony Forge’s investigation of Abelam motifs on cult
houses in New Guinea beingamongthe rare exceptions.?° A
considerable literature on their arts exists, but it is usually described
andanalysed essentially in western terms and evaluated by western
criteria. Some cultures and cultural complexes share a predilection
for usingthe building as a vehicle for visual embellishment, though
this isfarmore characteristic of south-east Asia than it is of, say,

South America. Many societies in Indonesia have richly decorated,
finelyworked and extravagantly formed dwellings. The Bataks of
north Sumatera, who number about half a million, are divided into
tribal groups whoseterritory is on arrelatively high plateau (4,000
ft) overlooked by volcanoes; fresh waterand abundantfishingin
Lake Toba contributeto its favourable conditions. The Toba Bataks
tothesouth ofthelake arethe most populous group.

Strongly hierarchical, thetypical Batak huta (village) had
an aristocratic clan who were direct descendants ofthefounder
and his family, and their indentured serfs. Local wars have
destroyed many villages and houses, which were often situated
on hill-tops or are partially protected by swamps and ramparts
on which bamboo thickets were grown for further protection.
Within thethicket, the huta comprised two rows of buildings
facingthe halaman, or village plaza, which is always oriented
east-west. Onthenorth side, facing south, arethe houses, or
rumah, and oppositethem the soporrice barns, which in recent
years have frequently been adapted as houses. Steeply ridged
and pointed roofs project overthe gablefronts ofthe dwellings
giving shadeforthe men repairing nets andwomen weaving
clothinthe halaman.

In spite of being massively constructed, the saddle-back
roof and curving eaves ofthe Toba house convey an impression
oflightness. When a new building was to be built anumber of
available hardwoods was felled for various parts, after appropriate

offerings and rituals had been made. Selected posts were placed
in order of qualitythat reflected the hierarchy of spaces within
the house. Shorter posts secured with pin-and-hole fixings
helped support the floor, which rested on longitudinal beams,
some over 30ft (10 m) in length, and equally substantial cross
members.?' Planked panels were fixed overthe external gable
end which comprised three levels, including a stair and balcony;
other panels were attachedtothe sides ofthe house. These panels
took the decoration, which, in the best examples were finely
carved with spiral andfoliated volute patterns, painted in black,
red and white. Female breasts andtthe boraspati, a local lizard,
were also carved on the facade panels as fertilitysymbols,
together with heraldic motifs. Heads of buffalo, or of mythical
creatures, amongwhich the singa dragon isthe most important,
were carved and painted in black, red and white atthe gable apex
orthe corners ofthe house frame as protective devices.

Atthe sides ofthehousethe panels were not carved but
were painted, often with foliage in the form of elongated running
spirals, but also with smallssecular scenes and figures. No
decorations wereto befound atthe back ofthe house, where
the kitchens and domestic spaces were situated and, unlike
thefacade which was paid meticulous attention, were poorly
maintained. The interiors ofthe houses today are often carelessiy
kept, the latrines mere holes in the floor and thelighting levels

low. Batak houses are meant to beseen and admired from the
halaman, the hierarchies within the village expressed inthe
scale ofthe houses, in the quality oftheworkmanship, and
even intherestrictions on decoration of surfaces, imposed by
the village heads.??

Throughout southeast Asia and Melanesia, including
New Guinea, related dwellingtypes are to be found built on piles,
with saddle-back roofs and projecting ridges, often protecting
decorated gables. Many have crossed poles atthe leading edges,
which are sometimes decorated with foliatedforms, animal
heads or buffalo horns, and variants may be traced in cultures
as far apart as northern Thailand and south-west Sulawesi.
Sulawesi (Celebes) isthe home of many peoples includingthe
Sa’dan Toraja, whose houses with immense roofs look rather like
opposing rows of giant birds that have just alighted in the forest.
Toraja houses are ranged hierarchically opposite their rrice barns,
the internal spaces shared between a number of related families.
However, formuch oftheyearthe lipu, or ‘mother village‘, is
occupied principally by the elderlyandthe very young, where
the older children and active adults sleep closeto their crops
in simple field shelters. Ceremonials and funerals are always
conducted within the lipu, which is regarded with respect.
Smiths and other craftsmen, the noise of whose work might
alarm the guardian spirits, are obliged to stay outside the village
except, of course, when a house is being built.3 It has been
argued that the ingenious buildings, with their boat forms,
curving timbers and sail-like roofs are echoes of ancient
maritime traditions which, in some degree, is reflected intheir
terminology. But it has also been shown thatthereare close
correlations between thetechnologies and forms of pile houses
with oversailing gables that linkthrough chains of diffusion,
to ancient traditions in mainland Asia.?+

23: The ‘tree of life’

High in the gables ofthe impressive tongkonan, or
ancestral ‘origin’ houses, are painted sun motifs crested with
facing cockerels, while below them on the structural beams and
opening panels are decorations in black and red, with delineation
in white or yellow. These surfaces are embellished with a variety
ofrepeat patterns, some relatingto basketry, others suggesting
derivations from interlaced leather and woven fabrics, but
most having associations with flora, fauna or other natural
phenomena. These patterns repeat certain widespread motifs,
such asthe spiral, with many volutes and parallel colour bands,
which are often in groups ofthree pairs, sothatthe edge loops
are similar. Alternatively, they may be reflected, with one spiral
the mirror image ofthe other, orthey may beopposed and
reversed. While such a motif may refertothe growth of riceorthe
movement ofatadpole, having connotations of energy orvirility,
with subtle adjustments of colour banding and enfolded devices
itmaysymbolize a balanced load and hence, equality and shared
responsibility. Other motifs include pointed ovals, with designs
identified as buffalo ears or cockerel feathers.25

Some decorations are highly abstracted butthe motif of
the sacred water-buffalo symbolized by its horns is recurrent,
and powerfullyemphasized by thetiers ofthe horns of sacrificial
buffaloes mounted on the house-posts ofthetongkonan.
Comparative studies have revealed remarkable links between
design motifs of far apart cultures: the four-spiral pattern, the
bull’s head, a figure standing between bull’s hornsto be found
on Toraja houses is also to be seen on dwellings ofthe Ao Naga in
Assam, two thousand miles to the north-west. The characteristic
roof-types are depicted on the bronze drums oftheDongson
culture, an archaic civilization of Indo-China (2400-1900 BC),
which appearstto have been the fulcrum forthe dispersal of
cultural traits that survive in Indonesia. Even more remarkable

perhaps, isthat they also relate to late Bronze Age designs of
Caucasian origin.25 The sources, and possible diffusion, of
decorative motifs and theirsymbolic significance to different
societies is a complex subject, involving among other factors,
the experience and perception and values attached to perceived
phenomena, such asthe sun, moon and stars, plants and flowers,
andtheforms of living creatures. Atree may be depicted asthe
"Tree of Life‘, while a central pole might be associated withthe
Axis Mundithatlinksthe heavenly and earthly domains.

Simple geometries, such asthe cross, thecircleorthe
diamond may be divided or multiplied, and inthe process may
acquire symbolic connotations, such as the serrated shapes
associated with fire, and curved chevrons with water.?? Attimes
the production of an artefact may inspire decorative motifs,
likethetwisting ofthreads, the plaiting of stringorcane, orthe
weaving of cloth and the stringing of beads. Such may bethe
case with thetwo-dimensional, two-colour paintingon the
interior walls of Kabylie dwellings which recallthe motifs of
woven tent cloths. But Bourdieu, whose analysis ofthe binary
symbolism oftthe Kabylie house has been discussed in the
previous chapter, appearsto have ignoredthe forms and
decoration of the house interiors, ofwhich hemade no mention.2

Ritual and ceremonial play a great part in the perpetuation
of ancient beliefs and withthe symbols associated with them.
They do not always remain the same, nor dothesymbols stay
constant; the ‘migration of symbols’ or ‘symbolic drift',asthe
shifting ofthe meaning of symbols with culture change or
diffusion istermed, is acommon phenomenon.?? What weare
seeing isthe overlay of many principles:the perception and
interpretation ofindividual and collective experience, the
association of colours and shapes, the use ofschemata based
on anthropomorphic, zoomorphic and phylomorphicshapes

derived from those of flora and fauna, the influence of animist
beliefs, the adoption of ancient and later religious symbolism,
the application of rule systems, the persistence of patterns
derived from weaving or plaiting. Together they constitute for
each society a system of visual communication of signification
and meaning, bythe use of decorative elements. Each dwelling
conforms with the design ‘language’ ofthe people, but, likethe
individual who uses his personal ‘speech’ to express ideasthat
can be shared by others within his community, thechoice and
combinations may differ occasionally.3° New ones may be
acquiredand assumed by associetyto contributetothe language,
butthe persistence of aspecificrange within the rule system of a
tradition acts asa bond within a largercommunity that expresses
its values, lineages, hierarchies, history andfaith. Thefactthat
correspondences between the Toradjaandthe Batak areshared
by many other southeast Asian peoples is evidence of a'metalanguage’ of design which, likethe Finno-Hungarian and Indo-
European linguistic complexes embraces the languages of many
societies, each helpingtto reinforcethe common identity of
thosewho share it.

While nota rule, it is frequentlythe casethat decorative
treatments are richest intemples, mosques and churches.
Decoration isemployed as an indication of respect, oras
supplication, orto appeasethegods. Deep carvings cast sculptural
shadows that enhancethe forms employed, andthese can be
enriched with semi-precious stones and inlays that shine
lustrously in flickering candlelight, addingto the atmosphere of
mystery and rreverence. Palaces and houses ofthearistocracy, the
gentry orthe ruling classes may also be lavishly decorated. Clan
and cult houses of noble families and founding lineages may be
elaborately carved and painted with symbols. In some societies
such &lites are closely associated with religious authority, and

the kind and quality of decorative embellishment is usedto
reinforce the relationship. Artisthehandmaiden of both hierarchy
and divine authority. On the dwellings ofthecommon people
decoration may often be used, but sparingly and discreetly,
theirmembership ofthe society beingaasserted through design,
but relative status can sometimes be inferred from its
comparative complexity.

Such isthe case with the dwellings ofthe Hausa whose
decorative motifs are sometimes incised, but, intheirmore
striking and traditional examples, are modelled in relief. An
agglomerate of peoples who were gradually welded into one
state, the Hausa have had a history ofathousand years. Their
territory extended from the Ayr mountains ofthesouthern
Sahara to what is now central Nigeria, andthough they were
defeated in the Fulani jihad of 1807 and fell again tothe British a
century later, the Hausa remain one ofthe most powerful of all
African peoples, with some 15 million speakers of their language.
Rural Hausa live in gida, or farm compounds, and dispersed
settlements of earth-wallhuts, either circular or square in plan,
theformerwith thatched conical roofs.

Untilthe nineteenth century, therural Hausa remained
animists, even though Islam had reached Hausaland in the late
fourteenth century AD, eventually being converted bythe
teaching of travelling merchants, pilgrims and clerics. The Hausa
were largely an urban people; their birni, orwalledtownships,
enclosed fields which could support them in times of siege.
Gradually gaining in power, such cities as Katsina, Kano and
Zaria became important tradingand manufacturing centres.
Trade contacts were established with other Sudanic emirates
and sultanates. Today, there are strong formal links in the
architecture of such towns as Zinder and Agades in Niger. A
feudal system with ruling aristocratic families was expressed

27: Painted decoration

on a Kano house-front.

The ‘rabbit’s ears’ at

in the palaces ofthe emirs, who owed allegianceto the Caliphate

leatherwork, textile dying or butchery.

Hausa dwellings are constructed of tabala, pear-shaped
lumps of cured lateritic clay mixed with straw, which are laid in
rows, base down, to produce thick walls, or azara. Lengths of
thefan palm, which is resistantto white termites, are used for
reinforcement andto shape internal archesto support roof
domes. Roofs are also constructed from lengths of palm to form
atight mesh over which raffia palm mats and earth can be laid.
Traditionally, the huts were circular in plan, and a double-cylinder
of linked units was a unique feature of Hausa vernacular
architecture. Underthe influence of Islam from thenorth and
west, square dwelling units became increasingly popular, replacing
circular ones when these eventually collapsed. Generallythe
Hausa compound is surrounded by a walland has an entrance
unit, the zaure, which leadstothe shagafa, the hut where guests
may bereceived. Beyond this isthe household head'’s sleeping
hutandthe heart ofthecompound, the cikin gida, wherethe
wives and children live.32

Great buildings in the Hausa cities, such asthe Friday
Mosque at Zaria orthe Kano Palace, were richly decorated within.
Walls, panels and ribbed arched ceilings being both sculptured in
relief and elaborately painted in abstract loops, whorls, chevrons,
and bosses, every inch ofthe surface of the reception rooms
beingtreated in this way. Such decorative interiors are known
to have existed in the early nineteenth century and may have
been derived fromthenomadic tradition of tent hangings.3
Yetthe characteristic external house decorations of Hausa cities
developed in the twentieth century, when attitudesto azzikl,
orthe gaining of prosperity and respect, which had formerly

28: View overthe

includingthe zaure
andtheshagafacan
be discerned

required restraint and propriety, were relaxed. Displays of success
by merchants andtraders were condoned, and inthe1930s many

beseeninthecities. Overthe earth walls mud plasterwas moulded
into the desired shapes by hand, and coated with a waterresistant layer of a local cement made from mud, dung and lasomixed animal hair and dye-pit residues. The pattern was scribed
with the fingers, andthe unwanted areas scooped away sothat
the device stood in relief against the background. Itwas finished
with a local cement and whitewashed or painted in earth colours,
orsometimes with commercial paints.

With aa restricted budget a householder might havethe
doorway decorated, and might have the door itself made bright
with decorative panels cut out of biscuit tins. Patterns around
thewindows and the moulded seats by the door would follow,
but if hewas wealthy enough he could havethe entire facade
decorated. The motifs employed appear to have been pre-Islamic
in some instances, but the dagi, the ‘Northern’ or ‘Endless
Knot’ possibly of Roman origin, was widely used, as was the
volute. Some of the patterns relatetothoseontheriga, the
asymmetricallyembroidered Hausa robe. Others appearto
be derived from leather horse trappings. Bythe 19405 a few
representational motifs began to appear: swords, saddlebags,
rifles, clocks, bicycles and motorcars reflected the owner’s
prosperity; even thetrains and aeroplanes in which hemay have
travelled were used in later designs. A less expensive sgraffito
(scratched) technique was developed with the use of ‘land-crete’
and soil-cement blocks, patterns being scratched ontheir
outside faces. Sgraffito patterns were soon being usedforan
overalltreatment, with geometric designs became popular in
the 19605. Not only merchants but successful karuwa, the city’s
prostitutes, decorated their houses with zanen gida; itbecame a

31: Decorative
treatment onthe

buildings of San’ais
more controlledthan
inthe rural regions.
The mafrajor reception
room has the finest
detail. Smoke holes
indicate the situation
ofthekitchens. San’a,
North Yemen

symbol of success and status, though by the 1970s such displays
ofimmodesty were becoming less popular.3*

Decoration on dwellings as an indication of relative status
isby no means uncommon, butthe distinction between the
houses ofthe urban merchants of Kano andthose ofthefarmers
ofrural Hausaland is marked. In Yemen the distinction is not as
clear:the houses of ruralfarmers may be somewhat lower, and
perhaps, less richly decorated but there isa continuity ofform
and treatment that indicates the basic unity ofthetradition.
Ina largely dryand mountainous country, agriculture is
dependent on the management of water resources. The wadis
that bisect the midland and highland regions are fertile, and
skilful conservation techniques that include cisternsand
conduits, canals and extensive terraces, have supported large
populations for millennia. Beyond the highlands where mountain
ridges run north with peaksrising to 10,000 feet (3,000 m) isthe
semi-desert eastern plateau. Apart from the coastal strip all
regions have tall houses, approximately square in plan, which
derived from the surveillance towers builtto guard crops and
villages from the raids of warring tribes. In the midlands they
are mainly built of stone, but intheeastern highlands andthe
plateau, mud orfired bricks are common, and insome areas a
combination of lower levels in stone and upper storeys of mud
or brick is common.>5

Usually dominated by the houses of sheikhs, villages
cluster onthe hilltops in the highlands, and many towns are
scattered through the eastern plateau. With such a dispersal
there are numerous variants but most tower houses conform
to a consistent type usually ofthree or four storeys; sometimes
reaching seven in thecities. Atthe ground floortheentrance
space also serves as a store with stalls for sheep and goats and,
in rural areas, a stable. Thefloor above isthe main living area,

the divan, where the family weddings andfestivals are held, and
visitors received. Above arethe private levels, with rooms for
women and children, and the dark smoke-blackened kitchen.
Finest ofthe rooms isthe mafrajon thetop floor, wherethe
household head and male relatives smoke the water pipeand
chew gat. Niches are set inthe walls for domestic objects
throughoutthe house, carpets and rugs are spread overthe
stone or packed-earth floors, and low mattresses surroundthe
rooms for seating. The walls are white with gypsum plaster,
which is also moulded overtthe irregular ceilingjoists, and
decorative panels of carved alabaster may be found in wealthier
homes. Abovethesshuttered windows, thefanlights may beina
mosaic of coloured glass which tintsthe interior, and at night,
illuminated from within, enriches the street.3°

Much oftheelaborate decoration to befoundonthe
exterior of Yemen houses derives from the materialsthat are
utilized and the form and functions of elements in the buildings.
Both stonework and fired brick are used decoratively. Blocks of
stone are dressed only on the facing surface, sometimes with
diamond designs; within thewallthe rough shapes are locked
into rubble masonry and mortar with lime and mud. Friezes and
string courses markingtthe floor levels are customary, often with
an alternating chevron pattern of bricks or stone dividingthe
walls of multi-storeyed houses in decorative bands. Generallythe
doorway is emphasized and may have a suppressed orflattened
arch, with a stilted arch rising above it; the pierced lunette
between both ventilating and dimly illuminating the interior.
Ontthe exteriorthe coupling of shuttered windows with thin
alabaster lunettes or takhrim above, is expressed by unifying
frames of whitewash and plaster in low relief. The glowing shapes
and multi-coloured glass ofthe takhrim are made by laying
gypsum plasteron a board and cutting out the design before it

## S. 107

sets. Pieces of coloured glass are held in position with wet plaster
andthe decorative lunette plastered into the stonearch.

Many elements give additional decorative relief, among
them the shubaq, a projecting box of stone orwood, which is
pierced to promote air circulation and permitswomen to see into
the street without being seen. Porous jars are keptin them as
cooling devices, for drinking water andto humidify the air within.
In most Yemen tower houses the upper storeys arethe more
elaborately decorated, and the windows are largest forthe
mafraj. Insome areasthe windows are surrounded with relief
panels scribed with designs; many ofthe patterns relate closely
to those on rugs and fabrics, though sometimes Koranic
inscriptions are cut intothe plasterwork. Elsewhere, the walls
are painted with decorations which, inthe plateau city of San’a
sometimes threaten to smotherthe forms in controlled

gingerbread and confectionery patterns. Ostentatious, if
arresting, these arethe houses ofthe wealthy; they contrast
with the restraintofthewhite parapets and ochre hues ofthe
northern mud city of Sa’dah, orthe inventive, exuberant but
modest decoration on the dwellings ofthefarming villages
and ruraltowns.7

Anumber ofthe peoples who have been discussed here -
the Toba Bataks, for example, have cultures threatened by similar
forces, and their splendid decorative buildings are waning rapidly
as population pressures are forcing large numberstto settleinthe
cities. Nevertheless, even iftheir architecture has suffered in
quality, urbanized Toba Bataks in Medan have strengthened
their kinship ties and formed clan associations that still perform
appropriate rites forthe construction ofthe city dwelling. The
Toraja have been at once, more ambitious and more traditional,
adheringto their customs in building while increasing their
scale.3® As for Kano and San’a, inthe mid-1960s Kanohada
population of300,000; bytheend ofthecenturyithad doubled,
with Kano state having nearly six million people. The growth of
San’a was even more marked. With the cessation ofthe Civil War
in 1969 itexpanded rapidly and bytheyear 2000 had a population
of over half a million, embracing distant villages in modern urban
sprawl. Neither old city has survived this growth unscarred but
they are substantially intact, with modern city developments
besidethem. Beyond the spacious, but often climaticallyand
culturally unsuitable concrete houses of the wealthy, within
their walled and steel-gated compounds, arethe earthen houses
and hastily-erected huts of new migrants. Both cities are
experiencing, on acomparatively small scale, the kinds of urban
expansion and pressurethatarechangingtheshape ofthe
world’s cities and which are creating new problems in the rural
areas, asthey become depopulated.3?

and diversity ofthese

Goingtotown

Inthe Kathmandu Valley region of Nepal, live half a million
Newars, farmers who have been blessed with rich and productive
phosphatic blue clays and peat deposits, or ko, which were used
as fertilizer. The mountainous landscape is extensivelyterraced
andthe intensive cultivation producestwo crops a year-ofrice
and grain. Because the population is considerable, Newar villages
often have morethan 2,000 residents, whiletowns like Kirtipur
have more than 15,000, with densities of 500 people per hectare
not uncommon. Their syncretic religion combines Hindu
elements with Buddhism, the Siddartha Gautama, or Buddha,
being recognized as the seventh incarnation ofthe Hindu God
Vishnu. Situated on a long hilltop site, thetown of Kirtipuris
lozenge-shaped within the protective mandala of many Hindu
temples and Buddhist stupas which, with their plazas, or chowks,
are important social centres withinthecommunity. Thereligious
symbolism ofthetown’s plan is conceptual ratherthen precisely
mapped, the sacred sites beinghonoured by the daily pujarites
and offerings ofthe people. Narrow streets followthe contours
and wind overthehilltop, linked by steep lanes and flights of
steps. Tothe southern edge ofthetown isthe Kasi market;
another, the Nayaa market, is disengaged from thetowntothe
east butisclosetothe university.!

Clustered in tols, orenclaves, each ofthe households
maintainsa kheba, or kitchen garden, which provides vegetables
and also, if necessary, room for further building. Whetherthey
are in the villages orthetowns Newar houses are similar, being
generally constructed on three orfourfloorsto conserve landand
to permit extended families to live in close proximity. In Kirtipur
they aresome 20 ft (6 m) deep with aspine wall, with
symmetrical facades, and are frequently built in rows. Often the
rearisextended around a smallcourtyard to make an enclosed

dwelling complex shared by the fukee, ahouseholdthat may
comprise several families ofthesame caste and clan, who
acknowledge one head.

Built on deep and solid foundations of stone, thefacade
ofthe house is offired brick, but forthe internal cross and spine
walls sun-dried, unfired bricks are used. On larger housesthe
windows are elegantly framed with richly carved wood, and
carvings enrich other details. Affected by risingdamp, the ground
floors are used as byres or, when opened up and benefitingfrom
better ventilation, as shops. Steep, open-tread stairs, each step
dedicatedto.agod, risetightlyatoneside and can be closed off
atthe landings with heavy, sculptured doors. The matan, or first
floor, is used for sleeping and storage, its windows fitted with
carved latticesto repel malignant spirits.

Aboveisthe chota, the main living floor, which has
columns instead ofthespine wall, giving considerable space.
Well-illuminated with large windows, which provide good crossventilation andthrough which the good spirits can pass, it is
where weaving and household craft activities take place. Inthe
loft, nearesttothe gods, arethe kitchen andthe household
shrine. This area, which is restrictedttothe members ofthe
household, sometimes has a secluded roofterraceopentothe
sky. Roofttiles, bedded on sterile clay, sweep upwardstothe
ridge.°Though there can bea functional explanation forthe
levels ofthe dwelling, itisnotablethatthetransformative and
irreversible processes like spinning, weaving and cooking are
closestto the domain of the benificent spirits.

Anew house is built on the advice of an astrologer-priest
who consultsthe Vastu-vidya for guidance on thesiting, the
timing of construction andthe necessary sacrifices that will
ensure the health, wealth and long life ofthe occupants, provided
they maintain their ritual puja. Each day commences withthe

women taking offeringstothe nearesttemple or shrine of Ganesh,
the protector oftheir tol. Household gods are also worshipped,
with appropriate offerings and the burning of incense.3 Kirtipur
has some ofthe features of both avillageand atown, inmany of
the ways of dwelling and living, custom and community. Aspects
ofthetraditional rural way of life persist, but its proximitytothe
larger cities of Bhaktapurand Kathmandu means thatthechoices
offered bythetown are greater. So too, are the difficulties, for
either rural or urban living has its problems.

In Kirtipur, all residents ofthetown conformtothe
strict caste system. People inthesame tolare generally ofthe
same caste, though the kahajan, the farmers of the upper low
caste, predominate and are dispersed through thetown. Other
clean castes live in specifictolsbutthe unclean castes and
particularly, the phore, the sweepers, liveinasector ofthatched
earth dwellings outside thetown limits. Traditionally, the phore
collectthemanure and clean the streets, for Kirtipur has almost
no sanitation apart from a fewtoilets owned by Brahmins. Side
‘latrine alleys’ become extremely unpleasant and dysentery,
worms and other diseases are rifeamongthe children who
play in them. The poor conditions are exacerbated bythe
dependency ofthetown’s inhabitants on a score of unreliable
stand-pipes for water, which are pronetto breaks and pollution.
Some phore work for specific wealthy families, while others
attempt to escapetheir unclean caste status by seeking
alternative work in anothertown.*

Though the focus of amarket town is basically commercial,
the concentration of activities and people offers farmoreto.a
rural populace;the town contrasts with thevillagebythe
duplication of services and resources, and the greater measure
of choice that it affords. In thevillagethe potter orthe basketmaker may satisfy localdemand, but inthetowns and cities

from national and international sources are on display and

With theirtraditions of cultivation and production,
accomplishment in building construction andthe economic use
of materials, spatial organization in relation to social and familial
norms, symbolism ofthespaces and levels, and decorative
enrichment of details, the Newar of Kirtipurand their dwellings
typify many ofthe qualities of vernacular dwellings. Butthere
exist increasing pressures ontterrain and resources, particularly
those caused bythe appropriation of athird oftheirlands bythe
university. As a result, many people have migrated from Kirtipur
(and othertowns) to the big cities of Bhaktapur, Patan and
Kathmandu -the smallest ofthese, Bhaktapur, trebling its
growth in lessthan a decade. This qualifies it for city recognition
bythe United Nations’ statisticalterms, which rateasacitya
place with above 100,000 people. But cities are not merely defined
by numbers; the city is identified more bythe kind of government,
social structure, occupational differentiation andthe kind and
quality of life experienced within it, than by its size.5

With their high densities of both inhabitants and their
dwellings, ‘pre-industrial cities’ depend fortheir existence on
many factors, from a constant supply of fresh water and food
to the maintenance oftrade and communication routes forthe
transport of essentialsthatthe city cannot provide, andthe
technology to transform raw materials into products. These
necessitate the protection of territory, property, supplies and
lives, and have implications forthe relative values of commodities,
the system of laws and the means for enforcingthem, andthe

215

## S. 111

216 Goingtotown

consequent stratification of society. Yet, increasingly, the larger
urban centres exert their fascination forthe members of village
communities that are not self-sustaining, there being strong
economic inducements to movetothe bigtowns and cities.

And countless millions of people, like the Newars of Nepal,

are doing so.

Crisisinthe cities

The problems ofthecities are also the problems oftherural
areas; the realization that the world’s cities are growing by

at least 50 million people a year inevitably draws attention

to the urban catastrophes confronting us in the future. Rural
development is probably less considered than isthe urban crisis
and insufficient attention is giventto the other half ofthe world’s
population. Here, no lessthan inthecity, land reform is urgent.
Every aspect ofthe housing problem inevitably has its economic
andits political dimensions, which are obviously beyond the
scope ofthe present book. Intheirenormitytherights, needs
and contributions oftheindividual household orthesmall
community within the larger urban or national plans, may be
subordinated, if not overlooked.

Itis unpalatable but inescapable, thatthe main problems
ofeexpanding cities- hopelessness and poverty-are duetoa
multiplicity of inter-related factors. To summarize briefly, if
crudely, these include the inequalities of land ownership, the
exploitation ofthepoorbytheveryrichandthecontrol of industry
and manipulation of markets by multi-national corporations.
Inessence, the problems oftheccity arise fundamentally from
problems of western-styled capitalism and increasingly from
thecompound negative effects of globalization.® These have
resulted in gross inequalities in wealth, as is evidenced in cities
as diverse as Cairo and Mexico City. There is littleindication ofthe

8: Squatter housing
onthe banks ofthe
Calcutta canal. Rural
techniques of building
have been used. Most
ofthesquattersare
outcaste Harijans who
collect large quantities
of dungto sell as fuel
in the form of cakes.
India

so-called ‘trickle down’ effect, where both rich and poor gain
relative benefitfromthe commercial success of large corporations
and multi-national enterprises. Housing, orthelack ofit,
demonstrates this in hundreds of cities across the world, where
much ofit is peripheral, makeshift, marginalized, self-built from
urban detritus and perpetuallythreatened with demolition.
Calcutta, for example, almost defies description: every
definition ofthecityisto befound within it, every characteristic
is present in its vast size, high densities, heterogeneous
population, diversified structure, social stratifications and sheer
numbers of people. It has fine administrative buildings and
museums, large public spaces, light and heavy industry and
many citizens with considerable wealth. It also has, for hundreds
ofthousands of people, probably the worst living conditions
in the world; and ateeming population of slum dwellersand
squatters whose numbers increase literally by hundreds every
day. ‘Hooghly-side‘, the metropolis of greater Calcutta which
spreads west and north alongthe Hooghly river for 65 kilometres
(40 miles), provides atenth of India’stotal industry and mining
and employmentfor a million workers-athird in Calcutta itself.
Indiaisthetenth industrial nation in the world, with its greatest
concentration of industry in the northeast. ‘Growth corridors’
alongtherailroads and canals mark the expansion ofthecities
andthelinkingofthe centre in an increasingly vast conurbation,
between which arethe farms and villages of West Bengal.?
Ofthe Calcutta Urban Agglomeration’s population of
nine million, well over four million (of whom morethan half are
migrants) are in the Metropolitan Howrah-Calcutta twin cities
area. Sinceits role as administrative centre forthe British East
India Company began and, until 1912, asthe capital of India,
Calcutta has always attracted large numbers from rural areas,
particularly from the neighbouring states of Orissa, Uttar

Pradesh and Bihar. Rural poverty in Bihar state, with a population
of over 85 million, is even more serious than in West Bengal.
Nearly 60 percent oftherural population live in poverty, half of
the poor own lessthan 4 per cent ofthe land. Even before
Calcutta ceasedto bethe capital, more than half its population
came from other states.’ Followingthe partition of India,
between 1940 and 1956, fourto six million refugees poured into
India from East Pakistan, a quarter ofthem settlingin Calcutta.
Five million more refugees arrived as a result ofthe Civil Warand
still other influxes have followed the floods in Bangladesh.
Lyingintheswamp-lands atthe head ofthe Ganges Delta,
Calcutta’s physical location is not healthy. Butthe sacred river
gave trading access to the Indian hinterland, from which many
migrants came. Rural West Bengal remains under-developed,
with caste-ridden villages and strict rules of ritual purity and
pollution. Land-owners, includingthe Brahmins and other high
caste households within thirty miles of Howrah and Calcutta,
have educated members who work daily in the city, commuting
bythe unbelievably over-crowded trains. Butthe Muchisand
Santak atthe bottom ofthe caste structure, often landless and
desperately poor, work as sharecroppers and day-labourers in
their villages. Frequently, work is only obtainable for four or five
months in theyearand starvation threatens most of West Bengal’s
70 million people." Land reforms in 1955 limited holdingsto
25 acresanda homestead but on these small farms there aretoo
many people living ontoo little, unproductive land. Thousands
of young men leave forthe city where their lives may be less
constrained by stiflingtraditions and where they may gain
meagre benefits from the spin-offs of industry and commerce.
Many of Calcutta’s immigrants are seasonal, returningtothe
farms when work is available, especially for harvest. More migrants
are male than female- employees inthejute mills leave their

wives and children behind, resulting in a seriousimbalanceinthe
urban population.

Bytheyear200o the population of Calcutta within the
city was rated at 4.3 million, butthis was lessthan halfthe
million estimated as living inthe metropolitan area. How have
these huge numbers been assimilated by the city and in what
kinds of dwelling do they live? Hundreds ofthousands live in
bustees, or self-built settlements. Some oftheseare officially
registered and sanctioned, being on land owned by landlords,
but with houses built by Thika tenants. These tenants build two
orthree houses, occupying one and renting outthe othersto
migrants and refugees, these bustees being eligible for
improvement support. Scores of unregistered refugee colonies
also spring up, sometimes securing larger plots and perhaps
some access to services such as a standpipe or public latrine,
orbytapping a power line. Immense numbers of peoplelivein
settlements that have been illegally built and with no direct
access to services whatever. Their ‘squatter’ dwellings are built
of sticks and mud, covered in jute wrappings, cardboard or waste
materials held down with oldtyres. Usually enclosing a single
space, they stand in thefilth of excreta and mud; squalid in the
heat, unimaginably unpleasant in themonsoons. Every vacant
lotisfilled. Huts linetherrailroads, perch on the edge ofthe
Calcutta canal and sprawl in a limitless, heaving sea of greybrown detritus beyond the station at Howrah.'?

Yet those who live in the squatter settlements are notthe
most disadvantaged oftheresidents of Calcutta. Closeto a
million people livewithout a dwelling of any kind; they arethe
pavement dwellers who sleep on the steps of shops, inthe station
precincts, on rooftops, on traffic islands or onthe sidewalks.
With no cover but a cloth in which to wrap themselves at night,
whole families liveand somehow raise children. Squatters and

217

by new arrivalstothe

## S. 112

pavement dwellers have set up enterprises selling matches or
pencils, cleaning shoes, or, with bathroom scales, weighing
passers-by. Others live by scavenging, turning over piles of refuse
and waste for recycling materials or, ifthey are near water, as
dhobis, orwashermen. Many collect dung from the old cattle
roaming loose inthe streetsto make and sell dung-cakes for fuel,
as used in their villages. While in numerous squatter houses -
of which at least one in ten isa workshop - families work on
contract, makingthe cupsforrrailroad chai (tea) from playing
cards and paper bags from magazines or computer print-outs.®

With little or no sanitation, and with watertanksthat
are used for washing, drinking and washing clothes, disease is
inevitable. An infrastructure appropriate fortwo million people
hasto support a population of morethan fivetimesthat. Yetthe
squatter settlements and the bustees provide a foothold forthe
low-caste or Harijan migrant, andtheconditions of livingare often
littleworse than inthe village. Opportunities for making some
kind of living may arise, however minimal, even of establishing a
small business. The Thika tenant system encourages small-scale
enterprise, while the bustees afford basic livingaccommodation
closeto places of work. Faced with the rrealitythatthe bustees
were not being replaced by new low-cost housing (the city built
only 9,000 replacement housing units over a fifteen-year period),
ambitious Bustee Improvement Schemes were initiated.
Between 1971 and 1979, over 1.5 million slum dwellers benefited
from better sewerage and services. Butthe communities were
not involved inthe decision-making and subsequent maintenance
has been poor. Meanwhile, many thousands of migrants
continueto flockto the improved locations.'+

If conditions of homelessness appear in sharpest reliefin
Calcutta, the problems of Mumbai (Bombay) are no less massive,
northe housing any better. Incountriesthroughoutthe

10: Astreet market

where urban migrants
who maintain contact
with their rural village in Mumbai (Bombay)

11:‘Dhobi Wallahs’ or
wash-men, many of

relatives, sell fresh orCalcutta, dothe

India wealthier people of
the city. India

developing world and increasingly, onthe doorsteps of Europe,
squatter camps and illegal settlements have developed around
cities. In a rapidly urbanizing world the dwellings ofthe new
migrants, as much asthose ofthe underprivileged rural poor
andthe problems ofthe homeless, present an unprecedented
challenge. Just how many people in the world aretrulyhomeless
inthe sense of beingwithout a dwelling of any kind is unknown;
the organizers ofthe International Year of Shelterforthe
Homeless (1987) estimated that 100 million people had no home.
The actual number may well have been severaltimesthat and
certainlyistoday. Asforthe people who live in inadequate or
sub-standard housing this runs into billions depending upon the
criteria used to defineminimal standards. Though inadequate
housing in theseterms isto be found in ruralareasthroughout
the so-called ‘developing world’, conditions are most extreme

in the expanding cities.

Why dothecities grow at such rates? Intthefirst place, their
growth is not solely dueto immigration, forthey would grow
inevitably by ‘natural increase”. Although family sizetends to drop
in the industrially and technologically developed world, in areas
where infant mortality rates are high large families arethe norm.
More children may mean more mouthsto feed, but they also
mean more potential earnings forthe family and an insurance
against old age. Even where health standards have improvedtto
the point that fewer children die in infancy, the habit of having
large familiesandthe approval given to the bearing of many
sons persists, contributingtothe growth in population. Lack of
knowledge of contraception methods, oraccesstothem, and,
perhaps more important, religious and social inhibitions against
them, are contributory factors, while in some societies polygamy
further accounts forthe driveto increase, flourish and multiply.'

Peasants comingtothecity bring village values withthem

which may persist inthenew communities for a longtime;
large families, customary in rural areas, seldom diminish when
peasants settleinthe city. Whythen, dothey migrate? Naturally,
some reasons are specificto each migrant family butthere are
othersthat can begeneralized. Significant amongthese arethe
‘push’ factors-thethrust from the rural areas caused bythe
local pressures that threaten their survival. Technological
innovations on the farms have meant that tractors have
increasingly replaced horses and oxen, and machines have
replaced manual labour. Mechanization has been paralleled by
the ‘Green Revolution’ ofthe1960s andthe nurturing ofhigh
yield crops, whilethe increased use of pesticides and artificial
fertilizers has meant that production has often been improved
bytheelimination of inhibitors; increased yields may mean that
more people can be fed - but with less dependence on large
numbers of peopleto dothe work. The Green Revolution has
not always been successful and production has sometimes been
reduced, with inevitable consequences. Genetic modification
may exacerbate them further, while attemptstto build in
obsolescence in ‘GM’ crops could result in catastrophic rural
impoverishment. There are other ‘push’ factors, such asthe
famines of Ethiopia, the West African sahel and Afghanistan
caused by decades of drought and global warming. Advancing
deserts created by overgrazing and overcultivation, theinundation
of lands by vast floods and other environmental hazards have
also forced peasants and farmers to migrate to the cities."
Compared with the pressures of underemployment,
exploitation and landlessness in rural areas the attractions ofthe
city are magnetic. Cities mean jobs, which, iftheimmigrant is
lucky, has the appropriate skills, or is prepared to accept minimal
wages and conditions of work, may mean steady employment
in afactory or industrial plant. Industries engaged inthe

production of acommodity need raw materials, packaging,
despatch and much else; the ‘multiplier effect’ issuchthatthe
presence of one industry generates others in an expanding spiral
ofsupplies and services. Somewhere, at the bottom oftheheap
perhaps, there arejobsthat some ofthe newly arrived immigrants
may obtain. Ifthey are not within the ‘formal sector’ of officially
recognized, endorsed and taxed enterprisesthen many may
find them in the ‘informal sector’ of unofficial, casual, spin-off
endeavours. We have noted a few in Calcutta. They may range
from the preparation and vending offood andtea, the cutting
of hairorthe polishing of shoes, which require little capital
investment, to the salvaging, stripping, recycling or collecting
and re-selling of waste products from the factories. Such
activities are ‘marginal’ and they do not constitute adequate
employment. Butthey can and sometimes do, grow into small
enterprises and recognizing this, some aid organizations have
assistedthem. Sothe ‘pull’ factors that attract migrants and
which also include the entertainments, the chance encounters;
thefreedom and, if desired, theanonymity ofthecity,

are considerable.

Ittakes nerve to migrate, courage to chance a new life
with no assured job and no dwelling. In their various ways the
poor have surmounted these problems, often exploitingthe
specific circumstances of a particular urban context. Of course,
they are heavily circumscribed by limited opportunities, lack
of access to power, the unavailability of landandthe cost of
transport. Thepoor mustliveintheforsaken areas, the exhausted
and depressed ghettos ofthe old quarters, orthey must settle,
usually illegally, on the fringes of the city. This condition of
peri-urban living does not mean that all migrant settlements
are reduced to a single state of poverty and uniformity. Insome
settlements dwellings are largely constructed of bidons- cut and

Morocco. Kerosene cans are also employed in this way, producing

and used also for roofing. But in some settlements, sheet
corrugated iron is ubiquitous, as it is in Tondo, Manila; in others,

and mud, asthey are inthe Mathare Valley, Nairobi.

In 1930 Nairobi had a pre-war population of around 36,000
butittrebled in size duringthe Second World War. Amodern city
with industries which employ morethan 40 per cent ofall
industrial workers in Kenya, it attracted migrants. Following
Independence they flowed in from rural areas and by 1970 its
population was half a million; many built homes in peri-urban
settlements includingthose in the Mathare Valleyto the northeast ofthecity.Achain of illegal villages grew from 3,000 t0
60,000 people in six years and continuedtto expand rapidly. More
than half ofthe settlers were Kikuyu, though only inthe outlying
villages of the valley did they retain theirtraditional rural
dwelling, with its strong cylindrical walls of cedar posts and
thatched roof. Closertothecity, the coastal ‘Swahili’ dwelling
type, with rectangular plan, pitched roof and wattleand daub
walls, replaced it. Some dwellings were of the four-room plan
with a central corridor, which permitted incomes to be made
from theletting of rooms; others had rows of rooms under a
single roof of corrugated iron, flattened cans orthatch. Small
shops opened off many ofthe dwellings and most had guard
dogs and chickens sharing the living space." Exceptionally high
densities, comparable withthose in Bogotä or Alexandria were

reached bythe early 19705, with over 1,300 personstothe
residential hectare (2.5 acres).

Acommon reaction of city councilsto growing peri-urban
settlements isto destroy them and this was the fate of Kaburini
village near the city cemetery, which was burned down by City
Council askaris, or native police.” The bulldozing of squatter
settlements on the excuses that they are ‘breeding grounds for
vice and crime‘, ‘insanitary', ‘degrading‘, ‘unsightly’ (and visible
to tourists) and other familiar epithets, is no solutiontothe
problems ofthe expanding cities. Mathare was popular because
itwas only five miles from the city centreand condoned perhaps
because it was out of sight. Many attempts were made bythe
community to obtain safe water, but althoughtthe villagers
brought their own pipesto within ayard ofthe city supply, for
years the city authorities refusedto make aconnectiontoan
illegal settlement. Eventually, in 1969, the connection was made
and a modest water tax was imposed by the settlersthemselves
which helped pay forthe village police force.

Unable to obtain City Council and central government
recognition which would lead to better facilities and a proper
infrastructure, many settlers formed themselves into co-operatives
(orsmall share-holding companies) and purchased Mathare land
on which to build ‘company housing‘. Asthey were unableto meet
the council’s required standards of services most commenced
without planning controls, building with pole structures clad with
timber planking and finished with waste engine-oil-a deterrent
to termites.?° Like millions of ‘'homeless’ people in other parts of
the industrially developing world, they demonstrated their need
forasstake inthecity, by their resourcefulness and their capacity
to organize themselves. Settlers are interested in the future of
their communities and will build their own dwellings even when
land, resources and services are minimal.

## S. 114

222 Goingtotown

Onealternative to squatter settlement isto provide land
and essential services on which the homeless may build their
own dwellings. Such ‘Sites and Services’ (S&S) schemes were
officially supported by the World Bank in 1972. Kenya had already
experimented with S&S projects as early asthe1950s and several
were initiated in Nairobi in the 19705. The largestwasthe Dandora
Community Project. This was aimed atthe lowest-income sector
ofthe community, ofwhose meagre incomes a quarter was paid
back on monthly charges forthe sites and services. Several
options were available but typically, a residential plot of100sqm
to 160 sq m would have a ‘wet core’ (toilet and shower) with water
and sewerage connections, access to roads, collection of refuse
and street lighting. Ascore of demonstration plots showed how
dwellings could be built to incorporate the service facilities.
Primary schools, two health centresandtwo community centres,
asports complex and a market were planned.

There were 26,000 applications forthe 6,000 sites;
householders were required to have lived fortwo years in Nairobi,
butto own no plot or property, to pay a deposit of KSh 400
within two months andto pay the fees for service connections.
Sites with awet core orwith the addition of a kitchen and store
were offered with different advances, repayment beingatthe
rate of 8.5 per cent interest. This represented some 30 per cent
ofthe householder's income, but default was negligible.
Construction of phase one ofthe Dandora project involving 1,000
serviced plots, began in November 1976. Technical advisers were
on handto instruct families in necessary building skills and to
help organize building groups. Within eighteen months all
households had completed at leasttwo rooms, livinginthe
kitchen oratemporary shelter untilthey were ready for
occupation, Many houses were substantially built in stone or
cement block - durable materials acceptabletothe City Council.

Asecond phase was completed late in 1982, with the benefit of
lessons learned from the problems that had arisen in phase one.?'
Dandora has been a model for further projects, the diversity of
houses self-built by householders or paid labourers offering
some relieffrom the monotony ofits relentless grid plan.
Meanwhile, inevitably, the Mathare Valley and other squatter
settlements continued to grow, eventhough new self-build
projects were planned and commenced.?

‘Sites & Services’ schemes potentially make it possible for
householdsto buildthe dwellings they want, largely intheform
most appropriate to their cultural requirements, with proper
servicing and often, community facilities. In practice, some
schemes aretoo rigidly controlled bythesite layout, servicing
systems, plotsizeand bythe regulations exercised bythe local
governmentfortheir potentialto be fully realized. Community
involvement inthe planning stage is often minimalandthe
means to facilitate community development without restrictions
given little consideration. S&S schemes range in kind from a
minimally ‘pegged-out’ lotwith shared standpipes and sanitation,
tothe core unit, orthe core unit with additional kitchen orliving
space, such as at Dandora, to shell or ‘envelope’ dwellings,
serviced and roofed but capable of subdivision or extension.
Again, thetype of service may differ accordingto the locality;
earth closets in some, water-borne systems of sanitation in
others, on-site or off-sitetreatment of human and household
wastes and garbage collection, separation and disposal. Other
considerations includethe nature and surfacing of pedestrian
access, the degree of need for vehiculartransport now orinthe
future and access to public transit systems. Furthermore, there
arethe physical conditions relatingtothe nature and stability
ofthesite, soils, gradient, vulnerability to inundation or seismic
activityand much elseto be cconsidered.23

Because ofthese complex issues most S&S projects

Andthere is a key question which even S&S projects do not.
resolve: what happenstothosewho aretrulyhomeless and
jobless-thosetoo poor evento beeligible? A policy which
partially meets this problem is that of Settlement Upgrading (SU).

Upgrading

One example of settlement upgrading isthe Kampung
Improvement Programme (KIP) in Jakarta, Java, Indonesia, initiated
in 1969 when a survey revealed that in a city of 4.5 million people
(which doubled bythe end ofthe century), over 65 per centhad
notoilets, 80 per cent no electricityand 90 per cent no water
connections. Kampungs are villages, butthe kampungs of Jakarta,
Surabaya and elsewhere, are urban settlements largely situated
on swampy land proneto serious flooding and occupied by lowincome families. Lessthan a quarter ofthe houses have solid
floors and walls; the majority have bamboo walls and roofs, with
floors of earth. Specialto Jakarta isthe pondok, a form of rooming
house run by tauke, an immigrant-owner, who economically
boards, feeds and services migrants, often from his own village,
making his profit from wholesale purchasing.*# In thefirst phase
ofthe Improvement Programme, 87 kampungs involving over

a million people were connected with water supply, tube wells
being drilled where possible. Asystem of drainage canalsto
mitigate the flooding followed the improved roadsandthe
concrete paths serving individual houses. Communal sanitation

anda system of garbage disposal were also introduced. Asecond

with schools and health clinics being built employing local

the programme and not merely the relatively more affluent.5
While the KIP projects were not concerned with housing,
it was found that, with increased security andthe upgrading of
their settlements, families gradually improved their dwellings.
No taxes have ever been exacted from the residents, buttheyare
expected to make voluntary contributions to maintenance and
garbage disposal. However, the programme has been criticized
forwhat some see as its low-level provision and for its ‘top-down’
policy and limited community involvement -any discussion on
decisions is lefttothe camat and lurah, or local leaders, to
organize.2° Unquestionably, theimpact oftheKIPonthe
kampungs is significant and its methods of settlement upgrading
are now applied to 200 cities in Indonesia; meanwhile, attracted
perhaps by the KIPcommitment, the population of Jakarta has
grown to eight million. Asthetwenty-first century commenced
all but 20 per cent ofthe urban population remain in the lowincome category. Approximately 80 per cent of allhousing is
stillthe responsibility of individual households andthough the
balance is being met by public housingthis means that houses
are either self-built or, where families can afford it, with some
assistance from contractors or paid labourers. When the benefits
ofimproved roadways, walkways and drainage channels have
been demonstrated many kampungs have initated theirown

Certain Kampung Improvement Programmes, notably in
Jakarta in 1981 and Surabaya in 1986, receivedthe Aga Khan Award
for Architecture.2? However, there are many criticisms of S&S and
SU/Improvement schemes that have been made by subsequent
researchers, though some ofthese are prejudiced or clouded by
political and ideological disputes. Some argue that, in effect,
theschemes acceptthe status quoandtendto consolidate it,
thatthey exploitthe labour ofthe homeless and encourage
governmentsto abrogate their responsibility to ensure that all
people are properly housed. Further, there isthe problem of
creating over-dependent communities, who learn to expectthe
stateto provide them with housing. Accustomed to building
their own dwellings in rural contexts and keepingthem in order
in accordance with the norms oftheir culture, there are many
who do not recognize or whofailto acceptthatthey should
maintain their houses and the services they have received. On
the other hand, the desire to stabilize and improve the initial selfbuilt dwelling with more permanent materials is notable in many
cases, though even a supposedly impartial researcher may write
disapprovingly of ‘primitive structures of wood or bamboo’ that
exist beside structures that conform to ‘formal standards’.
Asthe settlers obtain ameasure of employment andtheir
comparative security is assured, there is a generaltendencyto
substitute the initial materials with more permanent

components. In Mexico City, for example, the first elementary
constructions built from scrap have been steadily replaced,
in some instances byfired brick. The structures have been
regularized to reflect and conform with those ofthe established
suburbs ofthecity, thereby not onlyimprovingthe quality ofthe
buildings and the conditions of living within them, but making
a statement about the settlers’ new urban identity.3°

Every peri-urban (or ‘squatter‘ ‘illegal’, ‘informal’ or
‘spontaneous’) settlement is special in certain respects. But
there are some overriding issues that are broadly applicableand
ofthese, from the settler’s point of view, security oftenureand
titleto the land are probably the most important. These are
unfamiliar concepts in some parts ofthe world. Traditional views
of land ownership may present considerable problems, which
cannot be overcome-any morethan canthe application of
housing standards - by rigid adherence to European systems.
Nevertheless, when it is possible, the guarantee of security
encourages sound building in durable materials and permanent
residence, though security oftenureandtitlehave had certain
and in some cases, unforeseen, outcomes in many cities. Most
persistent oftheseisthe selling-off ofthesite, ora part ofit,
to other settlers. By so doingowners may receive a desirable
monetary return, even ifthe sale results inthe loss of astake
in the city, orin increasingthe density in whichthey live.
Alternativelythe site may be divided, orthe building enlarged,
to gain a modest income from the provision of rentable
accommodation for new settlers. Asisthe case in the gecekondus
(or ‘built overnight’) housing of Ankara, Turkey, wherethe
squatters, who formed three-quarters ofthe population of
2.5 million, settled on government land before eventually gaining
titleto their plots and being recognized as citizens, and not as
squatters. Once secured, somesites may besoldto.alocal
