The growth of cities in the second half ofthetwentieth
century, though proportionately small and manageable in North
America and most of Europe, increased in many cities ofthe
world ataratethat was far beyond the predictions of any urban
authorities. Säo Paolo, the capital city of Brazil,hadatotal
population offewer than three million in 1950; bythe end of
the centurythe metropolitan area had grown six-foldto some
18 million. Some authorities placethe figure even higher,
exceeding that of Mexico City with its metropolitan area of more
than 20 million inhabitants. Comparable isthe Egyptian capital,
Cairo, which has a city population of ten million, but with some
16 million people living inthe metropolitan area as awhole.

The conditions under which vast numbers live are extreme, the
conspicuous affluence of the wealthy being in marked contrast
to the sufferings ofthe reported million who live in the grid-like
walled cemeteries like those in Khalifa, known as ‘The Cities of
the Dead”. Unauthorized high-rise housing, raised by unqualified
builders and dangerously insecure, fronts the cascades of

city garbage which are collected, sifted through and burnt
bythe zabaleen. Onlywhen comparedtothe desperate
conditions ofthe ‘supersaturated’ Nile strip, in which so many
ofthe fellahin, or peasants live, can the reasons for movingto
such circumstances in the city be understood. However, the
distinction between the city of Cairo and its satellite villages can
be hardto make as the process of urban expansion engulfsthem.

Most retain much ofttheir rural character and social structure,
migrants from outlying districts chosingthe neighbourhoods
where others from their villages have already settled, those from
the Deltatothe north tendingtto bring their families with them.?

Youngtowns
In asense, the homeless who have been solving their housing
predicament by buildingtheir own dwellings have carried onthe
vernacular tradition of using available resources, even ifthose
accessibletothe poor inthecity are usually limited to the waste
ofthe affluent. Eventually they have been ableto improve their
homes. Long-established settlements that have been provided
with services have become indistinguishablefromthe customary
low-income housing ofthecity. This isnotto saythatthey are
necessarily well-designed, or make the best use of sitesand
resources. Norisit an aesthetic argument for regarding squatter
settlements as a ‘new vernacular’ even though most may be said
to have been self-built.3 Ifthe waste products and discarded
materials ofthecity areregarded asthe ‘local materialsand
resources’ some may consider these factors as justifyingsuch an
argument. However, though some settlements may have a phase
when traditional houses are built on the fringe ofthecity, the
majority of squatter houses are erected without atradition. It
istrue that families may moveto thecity in phases, the earlier
settlers advising the next generation to move in. Again, some
settlements have been established for nearly half acentury and
have hadtime enough both to draw upon their former traditions
andto establish their own, as have many in the barriadas of Lima.
Lima, the capital city of Peru, isthetarget city of migrants
from all parts of the country. In 1980, (by which time much ofthe
considerable literature on its housing had been published, Lloyd,
1980, cites 230 of the ‘more accessible’ references), and the

population had reached 3.5 million; twenty years laterthis figure
had doubled to 7.2 million. Most ofthe migrants were Quechuas
from the rural regions, who had suffered the decline of
agriculture and ofthe mines and the misery ofthe militant years
ofthe ‘Shining Path’. Others however, were emigrants fromthe
callejones, inner city single-storey rental units. Purpose-built by
alandlord oneitherside of an alley serviced by asingletapand
perhaps a latrine. Orthey migrated from the corralones, sum
buildings made of waste materials crammed into a walled
compound on rented plots, evicted when the landlord wished to
develop the site. Those who lived in the inner city slums generally
have employment servicingthe needs of the wealthy residents
of Miraflores or other suburbs. The majority of migrants however,
settled intthe barriadas, often by means of an illegal, collective
“invasion’ of government land in the desertic plains surrounding
the city. The initial settlers, the ‘bridgeheaders’ to use Turner’s
term, would mark outthe plots on the land, erect estera (mat
shelters) ortentsto establish their claim and would be rapidly
Joined by others- more than thirty-fivethousand have been

At least forty informal settlement districts, latertermed
pueblos nuevos, or ‘youngtowns’, spread from Lima, their age
indicated by the degree of advancement ofthe building stages
andtheir recognition as part ofthecity bythe provision of

with adobe bricks, later developing their houses with fired bricks
delivered on sitewhen, having secured jobs, they can afford

Alarge proportion ofthe populace oftheyoungtowns
havethe power ofthe vote, ofwhich fact no political party is
oblivious. In consequence, repressingthe establishment of a
new settlement would be an act of political suicide, even though
there would be some justification - one being lack of water, for it
never rains in Lima and the only riveristhe Rimac. Nevertheless,
the example of Lima, which may have the largest owner-built

With the accumulated knowledge and experience gained
in some settlements bytwo orthree generations over a period
offifty years, there are indications ofthe emergence of new
vernacular architectural traditions, which are significantly
different in the cities of Asia, Indonesia, Africa or Latin America.
Often the differences aretraceabletothetraditions ofthe
migrants’ places of origin, butthey are also reflections ofthe
character ofthe urban centrestowhichthey have migrated and
of which they are now a part. Yetitcan be argued that, regardless
ofthe proliferation of such schemes, the settlements are

occupied by squatters, the houses are planned without
professional advice, they are built with notechnical sophistication,
they are insanitary, unhealthy and inappropriate fortheraising
of children, they aggravate the problems of providing public
services andthey place excessive burdens on civil administrations.
The problems ofthe expanding cities andthe degreeto which
governments and civic authorities should, orindeed can, provide
suitable alternative housingatascaleandataratethat copes
with the influx of migrants, areimmense if, and when, they are
confronted. Clearly, it may seem, such housing does require

the intervention of architects, planners and designers. Butthis
assumption provokes a number of questions.

Can the professional designer, often withouttraining in
any ofthe issues, produce schemes which can as effectively meet
the needs of an ever-expanding population? Can prefabricated or
system-building facilitate construction and development inthe
quarters or wards of a settlement? Arethe planning concepts and
regulated designs onesto which people of diverse cultures can
conform? Do values and conventional mores persistthatcan be
accommodated or can adapt to more efficient and formal plans
and structures? There is an area from which some indications
may be drawn and that is the provision of housing followingthe
impact of a natural disaster. Though rapid impact disasters may
not becomparable with the cumulative problems of inner urban
and peri-urban expansion and densification, the measures that
haveto be taken may not be dissimilar.

Emergency shelter

Peoplewho are suddenly deprived of ahome may readily accept
any form of shelter. This is evident in theterrifying aftermath of
a natural disaster, when hundreds, thousands, even tens of
thousands of people may lose their dwellings, if notttheir lives.

In such circumstances the provision of shelter is a major priority.
While it is hard to draw direct correlations between disastersand
time of year, heavy seasonal flooding or the onset of winter
mean that disaster victims have to be protected swiftly. Tents,
of course, are the most immediate form of shelter provision
that can be dispatched and raised quickly, butthey offer poor
insulation from the climate, none from damp or cold rising from
the ground. Fornon-nomadic peoples unaccustomedtothe
specialized way of lifethat tents demand, they are only ever
regarded astemporary while waiting forthe opportunitytoreturn
to their homes. Despitethefright and horror which may initially
be associated with the home-site and official perceptionsthat
emphasize the risks of further collapse or recurrence ofthe
disaster, the majority of victims soon desireto return, to rebuild
andto take up living where life has been violently interrupted.
This often means that any housing provided hasto be seen as
being semi-permanent, ratherthan temporary - many workers
in the field believingthatthere is no such thing astemporary
housing, though, for it may be relocatedand brought backtothe
home-site, wherethe temporary dwelling becomes permanent.
Anumber of emergency shelters have been professionally
designed and commercially produced to meetthe need for a
swift provision of protection. Butthe production ofthe units
contributes to the economy ofthe donor society and seldom
helpsthe economy oftthe stricken one. Like many forms of ‘aid’
they stillhaveto be paidfor bythe aid organizations, andthe
suppliers are frequently inthe more prosperous West. They
usually have high unit costs and coupled with the expenditure
ontransport, may cost more per square metrethan fully-serviced
housing. Five hundred polyurethane ‘igloos’ designed bythe
West German Bayer Corporation and developed withthe Red
Cross were donated followingthe Nicaraguan earthquake of 1972.

migrantstothe‘young

Bayer domes had been used previously followingthe
Gediz earthquake in Kütahya province, Turkey, in 1970, when the
inhabitants ofthetownship of Akgaalan were rehoused in them.
The delay was shorter, but it was stilltwo months untiltheshelters
arrived. Later, when permanent apartment-styled housingwas
builtthe peasants were gladto usethe domes as stores, chicken
houses or stables- all necessary amenities which the postdisaster housing did not provide. They were not the onlyform

of donor-housing; two-storeyed hexagonal prefabricated

units encircled with reinforcing hoops were given bythe
Austrian Government. Bizarre inthe context ofthe Gediz
peasant environment they were greatly disliked and remained
uninhabited.3 Nevertheless, the main responsetothe
earthquake was the provision by the government of permanent
housingto accommodate the estimated 70,000 homeless. Over
1,000 people had been killed, 15,000 houses destroyed or heavily
damaged, two towns and over 300 villages affected. The supply
of housing units was undertaken speedily-one new village

was completed within two months and all were finished in
twenty. The housetype was a single-storey compact design of
four rooms, built either by contractors to standard plans, or
prefabricated; the village layouts were drawn uptto ensure swift
communications and safety inthe event of another earthquake.
Yet, a dozen years later, whole villages stood empty, while in

## S. 118

)

others the prefabricated houses had been heavily adapted and
extended, or used as stores.

Asthe research project on the long-term cultural
implications ofthis policy revealed, the reasons were complex.
In some cases where the houses were unoccupied, they had
been sited where there was no water, orwere placed too far
from the farming land. But forthe most part they were simply
inappropriate to the life-ways ofthe people of Kutahya province
(whose dwellings have been described in Chapter Seven). The
‘disaster housing’ was designed for occupation by nuclear
families, but the peasant families were ofthe extended typeliving in the prefabricated dwellings either meant over-crowding
to accommodate the family, orthe break-up ofthefamily
system.#° Similarly, thelack of asalon and reception space meant

thatthere was no transitional zone inwhich to receive guests or
visitors; most families built an extension to the entrance forthis
purpose. There were more intimate problems in a society which
placed great emphasis on privacy, such asthe exposure to view
ofthetoilet, and screen walls were generally added on. Though
they were modified to makethem more convenient to live in, like
other adaptations and extensions, includingthe outbuildings
thatthe peasant farmers required, the alterations madethe
house more vulnerable in the event of another earthquake.
Most peasants would have preferredto have hadthe money
orthe materialsto repairtheir old houses, orto build new ones.
Itwould have been cheaper and culturally more effective. In
these communities, the people all have building skills adequate
forthe purposes.#

Such an example could besaidto be out of date; that
lessons have been learned from the mistakes of previous decades.
Regrettably, this is seldom so. Disasters strike most frequently
when they are not anticipated and when neitherthe populace
northeadministrations are prepared. Such wasthecaseinthe
small but densely populated Central American country of
El Salvador. Torn apart by civil war inthe 1980s, the country’s
5.4 million people were further devastated by successive
earthquakes in January and February 2001, when half ofthe
nation’s housing was damaged or destroyed. Faced with the
scale ofthe disasterthegovernment soughttto provide
temporary housing forthe 175,000 familiesthat had been
rendered totally homeless. Materials were obtained withthe
support of many countries, including sufficient corrugated
sheet metalto have paved the way to Argentina and immense
quantities of lighttimber framing.

Nailedtogether and clad in sheet metal a housing unit
covered 16 square metres (approx 172 square ft), andalthough

small, they offered a littlemore spacethanthecramped
conditions in urban one-room apartments that many families
had been usedto. With the assistance of aid agencies, the
houses were constructed by the army in four months. Itwasa
considerable achievement - but duringthe heat ofthedaythe
internal temperatures ofthe unserviced metal shelters, or
‘microwaves’ as the families callthem, reaches 66°C. Inrural
areas athousand small units were built of concrete blocks by a
Catholic Relief agency and other such services also helped house
the homeless. As noted above, the government houses are likely,
as are most ‘emergency shelters’, to become permanent, even
ifvirtually uninhabitable. Though the wet season was
approaching, a genuinely temporary solution usingthetents
of aid agencies would have allowed time for a more carefully
planned programme, using local resources and skillsto be
prepared and implemented.#?

Local knowledge, skills and motivation persist even after
a disaster, rebuilding often being more therapeutic and positive
than submission to the decisions and directions of official
bodies and external agencies. Majorflooding occurred, inthe
upper reaches ofthe Indus valley, Pakistan, in 1988, when a
thousand villages were submerged by the swollen waters ofthe
Ravi, Jellum and other rivers. The Rajput village of Jubbo, noted
for its katchi abadi, or earthen houses with moulded details,
sculptured columns and walls modelled with symbols, was one
ofthemanyto be inundated.*# AJubbo dwelling consisted of a
vera, or houseyard, with a number of carefully kept single-storey,
flat-roofed kanra rooms, with stores, stables and domestic
spaces. Having been solidly built of compressed mud on rrising
ground above theriver Ravi, Jubbo had sufficient land forthe
pasture of its cattle, which at night weretied to postsinthe
dawala, a large village open space. Jubbo lay beyond the bund,

orlevee wall, that surrounded and protected the city ofLahore
butit was close enough forthevillagers to take refuge within its
perimeter, ifthey wished to receivethe city’s protection. Jubbo’s
villagers, under the direction oftheir zemindar, chose notto do
so anditwasassumed thatthe village had succumbedtothe
flood and all its members lost.

Yetthis was notthe case. When the flood waters rose and
the inundation oftherising ground was imminent, thezemindar
ordered the cattle in the tawalato be turned loose. In other
villagesthe cattleremained tethered totheir posts and alltold,
50,000 were drowned asthe waters covered them, butthe
zemindar’s presence of mind saved his villagers’ livestock and
enabled themto supply milktotheirown and othervillages :
when the floods subsided. Though no word ofthefate of Jubbo
had been received, itturned outthat within a matter of four
months several ofthe families had rebuilt their homes on the
original sites, while otherswere steadily working on their own.
When a chliani or kitchen was built by one ofthewomen, the
symbols of fire and water were moulded before any ofthe
construction was built, whilesymbols ofthe Tree of Lifeand
other devices indicative of gratitude for survival and trust in
God were moulded on the walls.++

One of countless communities that have reconstructed
themselves after a disaster Jubbo is indicative ofthe drive of
affected people to rebuild both their lives and their dwellings.
Compared with the many reports on housing after disasters
prepared by aid agencies, few instances of self-help resuscitation
have been documented. An effect ofthis isthe perpetuation of
post-disaster housing provision, ratherthan the support of local
community endeavour. Assistance is certainly necessary:
without stabilization the earthen walls would again collapse
ina future inundation. Oftenthe nature of anatural disaster

232 Goingtotown

necessitates management on a massive scale, involvingthe
rescue, hospital treatment, re-uniting and feeding of hundreds,
even thousands of victims. Draconian measures may be necessary,
butthis should not be madethe excusefor extensive mass
housing of standard components laid out in relentless, military
grids that ignorethe buildingtraditions, the spatial organization,
values and norms ofthe stricken settlements and their
communities. Though responses sensitiveto local needs may
take longer, with genuinelytemporary and recoverable shelter,
advice and assistance could be given to support vernacular
know-how in rebuilding, which can ensure the continuity, rather
than the disruption, of lives and dwelling traditions.

This is notto arguethattthere are no examples of
appropriate and successful post-disaster housingthat have
been effective in these respects. In fact, some have existed for
alongtime. In 1956 a major earthquake struck the eastern
Mediterranean, includingtthe island which may have well have
been the Atlantis of legend: Santorini in the Cyclades. Subject
to massive volcanic eruptions, the explosion at Thera (Santorini),
100 miles to the north, about 1450 scis believedto have beenthe
cause oftheeclipse ofthe Minoan civilization of ancient Crete. If
the 1956 earthquake was not of comparable scale it nevertheless
caused considerable damage, with large villages like Pyrgos
losing a great many buildings. In this instance, most ofthe kasteli,
orringed uppertown was destroyed, the buildings in dangerous
condition being considered irreparable and capped with concrete
for safety. Below the kasteli, however and in a dozen other
settlements on the volcanic incline and rim where collapsing
buildings dragged down those below them, large numbers of
traditional dwellings were replaced with new structures.#

Traditionally, houses on Santorini had a rectangular plan
which was spanned by aflat roof, often with a parapet, orbya

was one ofthe.areas

Cyclades, Greece

long barrel vault. Volcanic stone of various kinds was used forthe
construction, boulders of black stone being used inthe walls, a
more workable light red stone employed in vault construction,
pumice used for both vaults and flat roofs and pozzuolana, or
tephra, mixed with slaked limeto produce a strong cement for
finishing roofs. Formerly, to frame a vault, horizontal beams
were notched into semi-circular gable ends, laid with sticks and
skimmed with plaster. When dry, a 25cm thick layer of pozzuolana
cement was poured over itto complete the vault. Later, inthe
1920, blocks of pumice stone were usedto constructthe vault
and subsequently finished with the pozzuolana cement.# That
this change inthetraditional construction did not affect spatial
organization appearsto have made the post-1956 introduction
of concrete vaults, of similar design and proportions, acceptable.
Undertaken by young architects in the Housing Department
ofthe Ministry of Works, the design was carefully based on the
tradition, with old, damaged houses being repaired where
possible. Building loans for new houses were sometimes
mis-managed by civil authorities, butthearchitectural intentions
were largely met.”

Santorini’s reconstruction programme whichtook nine
yearsto complete, is not without its critics, partly because many
ofthevaults have been built over, alteringttheir form. However, in
the majority of instances the original vault may still be perceived,
whilethe adaptations meet a specificdemand inthecommunity:
namely, to build adowry house for each daughter. The plain that
lies on the long rear slope ofthe island is dotted with dowry
houses, butthe construction of others attachedto orabovethe
domestic vaults is far less consuming of land and in keeping with
thetradition. Other extensions have been builtto provide
accommodation fortourists, buttheintroduction oftheconcrete
vaults has been compatible with the retention oftraditional

forms while meeting the need to satisfy new functions.#
Itistruethatthetimetakento complete the final stages of
the project was over-extended, butthe attention to detail in
producing an appropriate, yet flexible, design has ensured for
the dwellings a long and enduring life which can continueto
accommodate change.

Afurther criticism ofthe Santorini programme relatedto
the organization ofthe houses. Though their new houses were
sometimes built in rows of fiveor more vaults, there were many
that were in pairs or singly constructed, enablingthe design to
adjusttothe steep inclines and irregular sites ofthe Santorini
volcanic rim. Some ofthe new houses in the villages of Pyrgos,
Emborian or Oie, were constructed in rows of fiveor more vvaults,
occasionally with rows in parallel, though the broken gradients
ofthe sites made this difficult. Intervention in the planningand
housing areas by professional designers almost invariably leads
to plans and methodsthat are regimented, with spatial designs
whose grid-plans follow minimal service lines ratherthan
respondtothe socio-cultural needs ofthe affected communities.
Asthese approaches are perceived in relation to impact
disasters, the distinction between the positions ofthe victims
of catastrophe and ofthose who plan forthem are highlighted.

Problems of intervention, cultural misinterpretation and lack of
awareness on the part of members of relief agencies, including in
particularthe designers of post-disaster housing, are apparent.
When designs are made and implemented without givingthem
attention, the potential for serious, even permanent sociocultural damage is considerable.

With the combined effects of globalwarming, increased
densification of cities, irfesponsible building methods, ecological
changes brought about by deforestation and many otherfactors,
natural disasters affecting both urban and rural locations, are
likely to be of ever increasing scale. Eight million people were
rendered homeless and 110,000 were killed when two cyclones
devastated the Indian state of Orissa. In January 2001 an
earthquake of a magnitude of 7.7 struck the western state of
Gujarat, when 20,000 people were killed and half a million
dwellings destroyed, mainly inthe Kutch region, while forty poorly
constructed housing blocks collapsed in the city of Anmedabad.
Vernacular buildings are vulnerable, but so arethe buildings that
are intendedto replacethem. Recognition oftheshortcomings
oftemporary and permanent re-settlement, oftheneed for
integrated safety features in new building and retrofit in others,
rather than rehousing to standardized plans is long overdue.+#

## S. 121

Enduring dwellings
Few readers are likely to have seen an Eskimo iglu. Butthis
book opened with thesnow-house because, as a stereotype
it was familiar in illustrations and, in its authentic form, it
clearly demonstrated how resources, technology, climate,
environment, settlement patterns, lifestyle, valuesand
meanings all play their part in shaping a dwellingtype
appropriateto a particular culture. However, dramatic changes
have cometothe Arcticin the past thirty years. In many ways,
Inuit culture has been altered: rifle, skidoo (motorized sledge)
and frame house have replaced harpoon, husky team, and snowblock iglu. The hunters ofthe white desert have been invaded by
hunters of black oil. The Inuit have been drawn into an economy
based on national and commercial interests, ratherthanttheir
own. Yet, the north is still under-developed and for many people,
neither adequate job opportunities in modern technology
exist, noran appropriate development oftheir own indigenous
culture has evolved.!

Their dwellings illustrate the situation. Asthe skidoos
enabled hunters to depart and return in aday ortwo, the skills
in building iglus died; and as industries were established and
framed houses were imported, the Inuit adopted the new house
types. These brought with them many problems: dwellings built
on permafrost lose their heat rapidly through thefloor, the
permafrost melts beneath them and causes subsidence. Houses
can be built on gravel plinths or on timber piles- butthetimber
also has to be brought in. People need to heatttheir houses and
this means the consumption of kerosene, inevitably expensive.
Frame houses forthe lowest economic sector costtwice as much
asthey do in southern Canada or in the United States; hunting
seldom meets the additional living costs and over halfthe Inuit
population is on welfare.

Unable to purchase frame houses, many build theirown
from packing cases, tar-paper and material jettisoned bythe
construction and oil companies. But there islittle servicing for
many oftheir communities, and seldom any system forthe
treatment of wastes, which cannot be disposed ofinthe
permafrost. The Inuitfound the ‘modern’ houses extremely
uncomfortable, and running with condensation. In Alaska, some
were covered completely with sods to provide insulation sothat
they appeared like awkward versions ofthetraditional house;
others were lined with cardboard and floored with linoleum.
Sittingonthefloorwhen eating or preparing skins is still
preferred, but itis very cold. Inthe1960s, prefabricated ‘Eskimo
Units’ were sentto settlements like Sugluk onthe Hudson Bay
but, although they were subsidized, no families could paythe
$1,000 cost. Additionally, having chemical toilets but no water
supply, they were inconvenient. Larger houses costing eight
times as much were later available, but few Inuit were ableto
paytherent, sowhen they can, many families still preferto use
traditional tents inthesummer months.

In attemptstto please the Inuit some public buildings -
including a cathedral - have been constructed of concrete in iglu
form. Perhaps it is not surprisingthat some 25,000 Canadian
Inuit fought for, and finally succeeded atthe end ofthetwentieth
century in establishing, their own self-governing homeland,
Nunavut (Our Land) beyond thetree-lineinthe North. Inthe
1980s and 90s some forty Government Reports on the National
Inuit Housing Consultation Project were published on such
aspects as sustainability in Arctic housing, problems of mass
building in energy consumption, and the potentialfor core
housing. Inthe Final Report of2001it was estimated that some
8,800 housing units would be required in the followingfive
years, 5,500 ofthem in Nunavut. Therole ofthe Inuit Tapirisat

of Canada was defined, includingthe objective of gaining
traditional knowledge andthe understanding ofthe Elderson
past housing and future needs.? It is not unusual forone
generation to become interested in the values and way of life of
the grandparents’ generation, but whethertheir questionnaires
to the Elders and others will derive any lasting benefit from
former building traditions remainsto be seen.

During the past half-centurythenomadic groups ofthe
world have been in sharp decline, even though they are frequently
the only people who are ableto surviveinthe regions they have
occupied and utilized for generations. The only peoplethat is,
apart from the wealthy timber, minerals and oil companies eager
to exploitthe natural resources, against whom there may be
few restrictions. Such groups as the Bedouin ofthe Arabian
peninsula, orthe Punan of Malaysia areamongthenomadic
cultures, of which only relatively few survive. Ofthese, the
Gabbra orthe Turkana and similar nomads oftthe north of Kenya
and parts of Somalia and Ethiopia continue theirtraditional way
oflife, butthis may be for a limited period only.3Thetwenty-first
century may wellseethe end oftraditions which have lasted for
literally thousands of years, and with them perceptions of life
and knowledge ofthe environment that will be irrecoverable.

Dwellingtypesthat evolved over millennia are
disappearing fast. In many cases, alternative housing has been
provided but it has often been a poor match to requirements.
Australian aborigines, for instance, are widely dispersed but
thoughtthere are local tribal differences in the construction and
form oftheirwindbreaks and shelters, they all servethe function
of protection from the weather and the intense heat ofthesun.
Even attimes oftheyear when physical shelter for such protection
is not strictly necessary, aborigines still build a dwelling; most
oftheir daily activities occur outside the structure but within

the dwelling place.* However, asthe environmental psychologist
Joseph Reser has shown, the provision of permanent houses has
not been successful either in meeting their social and cultural
needs or in bringingthe improvement in healththat modern
sanitation and facilities might ensure. Many aborigines are
seekingto return totheirhomelands, their own valuesandttheir
traditional dwellings.5

Elsewhere in the world building traditions continue among
many cultures, though often in the poorest nations or regions
of Africa, the Indian subcontinent and much of Asia. To countries
that are industrializing or are ‘modernizing’ with ttheintent
of participating in the global economy, vernacular traditions
are often perceived as an embarrassment. Destruction seemed
inevitable but forthe growing recognition ofthefactthat
tourists from the West frequently enjoyed visiting traditional
communities. They even stay in simulated regional houses -
though with ‘all mod cons’ (modern conveniences), of course.
Followingthe repression ofthe Maasai and the loss oftheirlands
to the safari trade, minimal but humiliating relief hascome for
someinthere-enactment of their way of lifeforthe cameras of
tourists. Similarly, the people popularly known astthe ‘Bushmen’
andlaterreferredto astheSan, the Basarwa were, until recently,
nomadic hunter-gatherers in the Kalahari desert of Namibia and
Botswana. Their kua, ortemporary shelter of brushwood, was
mainly used to protect children and food-stores. Now settled,
much oftheirincome comes from performing their former lifeways for tourists, who are accommodated in ‘native huts’ built
speciallyfortheir use. This is not anew phenomenon but one
which has been experienced by peoples of Oceania, and
Indonesia. Isthe future ofthe vernacularthereforeto be one
of escape forthe city dwellers, ‘bolt-holes’ for executivesand
financiers, and picturesque settings fortourist vacations?

So compelling isthe lucrative presence ofttouriststhat
eventhe most respected of environments may becompromised
in their interests, the housing beingthe most expendable
element. Inthe late sixteenth century the Safavid Shah Abbas
made Isfahan his capital, instituting a programme of building
and development that continued forthirty years. His planner,
Sheikh Behai, created a new maidan, which is stillone ofthe
world’s largest urban public spaces. At oneend, opposite a
splendid mosque, the Kaiserieh Gate markedtheentranceto a
new bazaar, formally planned but ingeniously linked withthe
meandering old bazaar.’ These were the greatest years of Isfahan’s
history, when allthe arts flourished beside its commercial
activity. Some 1,600 caravanserais and 300 hammams (public
baths) were in use, besides numerous mosques and madressehs,
while its network of routes and lanes made Isfahan bazaar worldfamous. Between them werethe houses ofthe merchants, with
great garden courts, and separate secluded anderun, courts for
thewomen and kin. Nearby were the houses of artisans, rooms
and storestaking up irregularities sothat asmall courtyard could
be square, with acentral poolandatree.

Many merchant houses are in disrepairtoday, butthe
artisan dwellings are still in use. Approached by twisting lanes
between high walls, an unobtrusive doorway opens onto.a
small reception chamber. Beyond, amodest dwelling may have
a kitchen, latrine, several personal rooms and a joint family
room opening on asingle court. Service lanes facilitatethe
evacuation ofthelatrines, but lanes builttotake camel trains
and donkeys cannot take vehicles.® Underthe late Shah’s
programme of modernization, draconian measures introduced
broad thoroughfares, intended to bringtourists rapidlytothe
Masjid-i-Jame, the city’s oldest mosque. Hundreds of houses
were destroyed, the new roads slicing across the bazaar,

Destruction of vernacular dwellings may not be as brutal,
noraas dramatic, as that perpetrated in Isfahan, butthere are

many instances of destruction in the interests of‘modernization”.

Among the most notorious was the systematic demolition of
Romanian villages under the orders ofthe Communist dictator,
Nicolae Ceausescu. It was his intention to erasethe past,
developing avenues flanked by housing blocks which would
accommodate dispossessed villagers and obligethem to follow
the dictates oftheregime. With the excuse thatthetradition had
been well documented, the Open Air Museum near Bucharest
being amongthe most comprehensive and highly respected in
Europe, many villages were devastated and untold numbers of
dwellings demolished.' Fortuitously, the collapse of the Soviet
states in 1989 halted the process, and much of Romania’s rich
vernacular traditions remain." Sometimes the reasons forthe
neglect or destruction of vernacular buildings are ideological,
but more frequently they are relatedto commercial interests.
While vernaculartraditions were still being practised in
Hungary, Poland and other Central and eastern European
countries inthe 1920s and 30s, few persist today. Nevertheless,
millions of vernacular dwellings are still lived inthroughout
Europe, even if others have been abandoned asthecities have
attracted their rural populations. Many villages inthesouthern
Pyrenees of Spain, and hill-towns in France and central Italy,
have been deserted by theirformer occupants who have left for
thefringes of Barcelona or Naples. Their migration has been
instrumental in providing second homes for northern Europeans
willingto tacklethe complexities of ownership involved.
Decline in vernacular building practices is not new; inthe UK, the
Netherlands, France and other Western European countriesthey

ceased with the mid-nineteenth-century expansion of heavy
industry and the development oftherailroads. Mine and
factory owners developed mass housing for workers, while
suburbanization motivated independent building contractors
and eventually ledtothe imposition of building standards.
Together with the planning laws enacted in the mid-twentieth
century, a necessary degree of control was brought about, after
the environment-consuming recklessness of ‘estate’ and
‘ribbon’ development. Legislation greatly inhibited self-buildin
Britain, though it is now permitted when produced by housing
co-operatives and is acceptable to local planning authorities.”
self-built housing is still permissable in many states ofthe
United States, wherethe continuity of vernacular traditions in
horizontal log construction, or adobe in semi-desert regions, has
been notable.' Nevertheless, the appeal of vernacular building
in Britain is strong, if sentimental, with the ideal ofthe ‘cottage
with roses round the door’ still a dream of many city-dwellers.
There is little doubt that traditional dwellings have
meaningformost cultures, though withthe decline ofthe
building ttraditions in some, this may often besymbolic of an
all-embracing nationhood that places importance on the homes
ofits ordinary people, as well as on the edifices of its most
privileged elites.'5 Yet national interests can still be insensitive to
those of indigenous peoples, asthe capital territory of Canberra,
Australia bears witness. This development is on ground sacred
totthe Aborigines and has ledto permanent protest inthe form
ofacamp-site declaredto bethat of the Aboriginal Parliament.’

Conserved in open air

Faced bytthe reality of disappearingttraditions both in the process
of building new structures and as a result ofthe demolition of
vernacular buildings to make space for new structures, such as is

8: Thirty houses that 9: Adecorated village

thedressofthat

period. Turku, Finland

evident from Ireland to Japan, many people have been anxious to
conserve what can be saved while there is still an opportunityto
do so. In the West, vernaculartraditions represent a link with
the past, with thellives of parents, orwith the rural origins of
families. Some people may visit restored houses out ofnnostalgia,
while others derive satisfaction from the comparison oftheir
present well-beingwith the apparent simplicity and discomfort
of former dwellings. Similar motivations inspired the creators of
the so-called ‘open-air museums’ of vernacular buildings. These
were frequently the products of personal obsessions, such asthe
‘Skansen’ at Stockholm, opened in 1891, and the ‘picture-book’
founded by a dentist, Anders Sandvig, in 1904 at Maihaugen,
Norway. Others followed, likethe Openluchtmuseum at Arnhem,
which endeavoured to ‘present a picture ofthe daily life of
ordinary people in this country as it was in the past'.7

By 1980, over 500 open-airmuseums had been established
in Scandinavia and Eastern Europe, with a handful in Britain and
Germany but none in France, Spain, Portugal or Italy.'® Some are
clustered in village form, others arranged along pre-determined
routes, and most projected ‘a snapshot of life in formertimes’
with furniture and utensils appropriateto an era, such asthe
mid-eighteenth century. In some, dummy figures freezethe
interior at a moment in history; in others, volunteers in period
clothes perform household tasks for visitors, many being
schoolchildren. When rescue ratherthan refuge istheforce
behind acquisition it is generally the most singular, impressive,
finely detailed or curious buildingsthat aretransferredtothe
site and preserved for posterity. Vernacular dwellings appear as
artefacts from history, relevant now only as educational vehicles.
Selection of exceptional examples may ensuretheir protection
but, by implication, may causetherejection ofthose of lesser
quality-afactorthat can condition attitudestothe demolition

of less notable buildings. Nevertheless, for many vistorstothese
museums, andtothe 250 such sites inthe United States, they
satisfythe desireto find roots.” The opening of similar museums
has now spread to India and Indonesia, Japan and China, Nigeria
and Kenya, Oceania and elsewhere, several gaining greatly from
the skills and knowledge of veteran builders. The museums may
engender pride and respect amongtthe people oftheirrespective
countries and regionsthat visitthem, butthey are also subject
to the same isolation from the reality ofthe present andthe
future. As buildings they exist like specimens in the display
cases of a regular museum.?°

The conservation of vernacular dwellings remains a
serious issue. Listing and grading buildings for preservation can
ensure their individual protection on site, but may not preserve
their neighbours. Collective conservation without ghosttown
sterility is demonstrated by the Elbe museum, Moravia, inwhich
part ofa working village has been conserved for visitors, or bythe
fishing village of Enkhuizen in Friesland, where conserved houses
open to visitors are dispersed within the living community,
Tusson village in the Cevennes, France has been conserved
through local initiative, the various buildings opentothe public
helpingthe community to thrive.2' Whilethese villages exemplify
the past, the dwellings having been built by former generations
ofthecommunities, they also communicate a sense of recent
history and potential sustainability.

Villages in several countries have been conservedto
protectthem from insensitive development. Some have been
recognized as World Heritage Sites, likethe Native American
pueblo, the ‘Sky Village’ of Acoma on asandstone mesa inthe
New Mexico desert, reputedly occupied forathousand years.
Partially destroyed by the Spanish in 1598, it was rebuilt and has
since changed little, apart from the erecting of an adobe Mission

church.?? As dry farmers, the Acoma people believethat
successful cultivation is dependent on aritual cycle, essential
to which are kivas, or sacred ceremonial chambers, from which
emergethe kachina deities.3 Ranged in parallel steppedterraces
ofmesa stone bonded with sage-brush ash, earth and water, their
dwellings were formerly accessed by ladderstotrap-doors inthe
roofs, as arethe six kivas still. Most houses now have street-doors
and, often, aluminium-framed windows, stucco walls and milled
timber beams. Living on a reservation of a quarter of amillion
acres underthetheocratic rule ofthe cacique, the Acoma people
adhere to their customs, values and beliefs. Below the mesa, the
desert villages of McCarty’s and Acomita absorbtthe population
growth.2# In the mid-1930s measured drawings ofthe pueblo
were made but, following recognition as a World Heritage Site,
theterraces have been 'restored’ by refacing in adobe block,
ratherthan by usingthetraditional method. Though it remains
the centre ofthe ceremonial life, Acoma is otherwise virtually
deserted except for conducted tours, the visitors’ fees
augmentingthe sale of pottery.?5

Obtaining recognition as a World Heritage Site demands
considerable effort. Hongcun and Xidi, two villages inthe
Huizhou province of southern Anhui, China discussed in Chapter
Nine, were nominated by the National Administration of
Cultural Heritage for inclusion in the list drawn up in 2000.26
To qualify, villages must be of historic significance, have
buildings of high quality, and of international importance.
Conservation commenced in the early 1980s, roads and river
dams being renovated and the waterway dredged, while
ancestral halls, temples, gardens and amemorial arch were
restored inthe ensuing years, with a view to makingthem tourist
attractions. Protective measures included laws governingthe
preservation areas and control of development. Ground water

Both villages have been restored with care, but not
without a loss of authenticity asthe agricultural population
prepares for the large numbers of visitors anticipated. Xidi and
Hongcun may meetthe World Heritage Site requirements, being
restored and polished as rare gems, but there are several other
villages, Tang-yue, Tang-Mo and Zhifeng among them, which
meritsuch attention, and many morethat would benefit from
it.22Thatttwo have been selected for specialtreatment,
marginalizes the others who will get little spin-off from the
tourist coaches bringing visitors to Hongcun and Xidi. Instead,
they are proneto loss of confidence in the knowledgethatthey
have been passed over. Asforthetourists, thelisted villages will
have irresistable appeal for some, but the pleasures of discovery
will be limited. A programme of community involvementand
restoration trainingthat was eventuallytothe benefit ofall
villages in the province would have had greater long-term
advantages. Broad policies of conservation could have been
adopted with the participation of localcommunities, instead of
special treatment being given to individual buildings on grounds
that emphasize their distinctive features to alien art historians
or conservationists. Measures taken to conserve the generality
of dwellings need not be agreat drain on the public purse. Elders
with knowledge oftherituals, techniques and forms of building
could be encouraged to transmit them to younger members of
theirfamilies and communities. Local resources could still be
employed and nurtured for future use, if due consideration is
given to the materials and technologies, design and construction
abilities that are available.

Ultimately, however, itisnotsimply a question of
restoration, conservation or repair ofthefabric of buildings and
their physical environment, or of regional skills. Of greater
importance, ifseldom referred to, isthe conservation ofthe
culturesandthe economies oftheresidents. Inthe application
for inclusion oftwo Chinese villages on World Heritage List it is
acknowledged the education level is ‘rather low’ andthe labour
force diminishing, with an assurance that ‘the government is
taking measures to enhance local economy and to stabilize
social structure’? No details ofthe measures are given butthe
statement indicates an awareness ofthe issue. Asisthe case
with the open-airmuseums, the ‘folk art’ criteria, which place
buildings on a notional scale of merit, usually works adversely
forthe majority. In both groups it is largely a matter of image;
one of nostalgic ‘picture-books’ ofthe lives and dwellings of
former generations inthe case ofthe museums, and one of
rare and distinctive ‘world class’ locations inthat ofthe
Heritage Sites.

By ‘listing’ and grading individual buildings for
conservation (as inthe UK), many buildings have been saved
from demolition.3° Nevertheless, thetotal number of buildings
which have received these various official accolades is
infinitessimal when compared with the hundreds of millions
of vernacular dwellings inthe world which are virtually
unrecognized, undocumented and generally disregarded.
Moreover, inthe longterm, the conservation of individual
buildings without the participation ofthose directly affected
will lead to further abandonment, with the inevitable decline
ofthe vernacular and greater pressure on thecities. This may
be problem enough, buttheimplications are far greater when
the scale of world population increase inthecoming decades
is brought intothe equation.

The other half
It used to be claimed repeatedly that by the beginning ofthe
twenty-first century half ofthe world’s population would be
urban, living in cities. In fact, the repetition of this statement
meantthatthe considerable variability ofthe rate of growth, and
the proportion of ruralto urban populations in specific national
orrregional contexts, is compromised. United Nations figures
indicate that in Asia, in spite ofthe expansion of such cities as
Bangkok or Jakarta, 65 per cent ofthe population ofthe
continent is rural. On the other hand, there are some forty-two
cities inthe world with a population exceeding five million, more
than half ofthem being in Asia. Over athird ofthe population
ofthese-considerably more insome instances-liveinthecity
slums and the peri-urban squatter settlements briefly
discussed in Chapter Ten.?'Theccity oftoday is already beset
with difficulties; the city ofthe future will be facing problems
ona disastrous scale.

Ifhalfthe world’s population is urban and livingin
cities then, by definition, halfthe world’s population is not.
Fundamentally, the problems ofthecity arethe problems ofthe
rural regions. Itisthe neglect ofthese areas, the industrialization
of agriculture, the acquistion and exploitation ofthe land,
whether arable or forest, and other such factors which have
driventhe poor tothefringes oftheccity, and will continueto do
so increasingly within the next few decades. What is beingdone
to resuscitate the rural regions? Even though bringing renewed
life to rural settlements could do much to alleviate pressure on
the world’s cities, the question is seldom addressed. The seats of
government andthe decision-makers of nations are city-based
and many havellittlecontact with rural areas, exceptto usethem
as playgrounds, as places for release from urban stress, or as
venues for vacation homes. Awareness oftheplight ofrural

15: In this inner city
slum, 700,000 closepacked squatter
houses are cladin
corrugated iron
sheeting. Tondo,
Manila, Philippines

communities changed temporarily in parts of western Europe
with the presence and fear of disease among livestock, butthe
drastic measures taken to eradicate disease showed little
sensitivitytotherural contexts. Thismay be regarded as
irrelevanttothe housing and environment issues on aglobal
scale, but it is indicative of official attitudes.

Urban growth in much ofthe world is already reaching
unmanageable proportions, projections forthe future
being almost beyond credulity. And most governments,
administrations, educationalists and even media pundits, prefer
nottothink about it. Considerthe facts, and the projections
related to population increase: attheturn ofthetwenty-first
century the world population is believed to have reached six
billion. This represents an unprecedented acceleration oftotal
growth, the population in 1900 being around 1,500 million,
taking a half-centuryto double, and anotherto double again.
Projections for growth in thefirst half ofthe new century are
exponential, theworld population being expected to increase
by 50 per cent infifty years, to reach 9,000 million by 2050 AD.

Many factors contributetothese figures, includingthe
gross birthrate of nations and thefertility rate, or births per
child-bearingwoman. Similarly, the falling death rate, including
that of infants, and greater longevity of both sexes, each
contributetothetotal survivingatany one time. Improved
medical treatment, but alsothe spread of contagious diseases,
the increasingly serious scale of disasters, which largely affect
thepoorratherthantherich, areamongthe many factorsthat
have bearing on the figures. Assuming that the world’s peoples
are not annihilated by nuclear or stellar disaster, the problems
arising from, or exacerbated by, this massive increase inthe
world population haveto befaced. The basic question is: how
can such an increase in the world’s peoples be met interms of

food production, water supply, waste disposal-and most
importantly, dwellings? The need for appropriate housing for an
additional three billion people isnotan issuethat can be evaded
orignored any longer.

Notthe least of the difficulties is how to define what
constitutes acceptable standards for housing, forthat which is
culturally appropriate in one context may beinadmissible in
another. There are many conditions to be considered, population
density amongthem. Frequently, this reaches 1,000 persons per
hectare but in Bangkok or Karachi, the density may betwice as
great. Inadequacy in housing provision is often relatedtothe
number of habitable rooms used by a family, tothe number of
persons per room orthe number of square metres of occupiable
space per person; but ‘net’ densities based on spaces withinthe
building are different from the ‘gross’ densities of land occupied.
Another factor isthe definition ofthe materials used in building
construction, many local authorities and financing bodies
consider dwellings to be inadequate if they are not constructed
of ‘durable materials’. The problem isthat a definition ofwhatis
‘non-durable’ depends on perceptions that are highly subjective
and, like so much else in this field, often reflects the culture and
conditions of the observer rather than those of the observed.?+

Perhapsthe most consistent measure of inadequacy in
housingrelatestothe provision of basic services. Ifthe installation
of electricity, adomestic water supply, efficient sanitation and
disposal of waste are considered to be necessary in the definition
ofthe acceptable minimum, nearly two-thirds ofthe world’s
housing does not meet it. A World Health Organization (WHO)
survey in 1970 revealed that over halfthe urban population ofthe
developed countries did not have connections to a water supply
and iin the developing countries the situation was far more
serious. The WHO reported in 1975 that, excluding China, 85 per

cent ofthe rural populations in ‘developing countries’ had no
organized system of human waste disposal, and estimatedthat
over three-quarters ofthe combined urban and rural population,
nearly 1,200 million, lacked adequate sanitation. In 1977, the
World Bank’s projected estimates for the provision of safe water
to the developing world was in the region of $6o billion and for
adequate sanitation systems as much as $200 billion.

Morethana quarter ofa century laterthesituation has
deteriorated further, with disastrous implications forthe health
and life-expectancy ofthousands of millions of people.3° Even
wherethere is agreement on housing densitiesand occupancy
rates, on admissable building materials, and services required,
there are serious problems when rehousing projects are planned.
These arerelatedtothe cost and availability ofland, the price of
building materials, the methods of funding projects, issues of
self-help or contracted labour, controls and restrictions, supply
of essential services and maintenance. Isthere any alternative
to the slow, costlyand seldom wholly successful process oftotal
rehousing, with its regimented rows of dwellings, which conform
to militaristic grid patterns to facilitate servicing, but which do
not even keep pace with population growth?

There is an alternative, onethat could reduce expenditure
dramatically, which could ensure the architectural identities
of widely differing cultures, and which could build on existing
knowledge and experienceto cope with population growth.
Itwould necessitatethe continuation of many building practices
butalsothe introduction of culturally compatible changes.
Briefly, such an alternative would be rooted in the positive
support ofregional vernacular traditions withthe full participation
ofcommunitiesintheshapingand construction oftheir
dwellings. It is likely to meet with considerable opposition,
orto besummarily dismissed, forthere are many problems

Material considerations

Inthe course ofthis book we have considered anumber of
themes related to the worldwide phenomenon of dwelling
through necessarily brief case studies. The examples have been
chosen from personal experience and enquiry, and have been
selected to be as representative as this constraint permits ofthe
many issues, both material and cultural, that have bearing on
building traditions. Some may be explicit and others implicit: for
example, itis clearthat landscape and environmental conditions

play a considerable part in the selection ofthesitesfor dwellings.

These may be assessed by leaders in some cultures, and codified
in others. But whether they arethe outcome of agreement
between nomadic groups, orthe collective decision of
households within aterritory,theeconomythat.a culture
pursues for its survivaland continuity is vital.

Decline of an economy may be brought about by climatic
changes, over-intensive use ofthe land, population growth,
dependence on monocultural production, the exploitation of
labour andthe withdrawal of markets, or many other pressures
that may result in enforced migration tothe urban fringes of
the world’s cities. Which is why any serious reversalofthe
trend towards ever-expanding squatter and slum settlements
must depend on the resuscitation of ruraleconomies orthe
development of decentralized systems of production and
distribution. Obviously there are implications for housingand
growing housing needs, whetherthese beinthecity, inthe
suburbs in the peri-urban settlements or inthetowns, villages,
hamlets and settlements oftherural regions. These vary inthe

light- and shade - of national and global politics and policies,
but the houses, the homes, the dwellings of billions of people
are deeply affected both by their implementation and bythe
assurance of the future security oftheircommunities.

There are numerous problems confrontingthose who
seekto achieve a more culturally sensitive, economically viable,
practicably sustainable future forthe built environments beyond
thecities, ofthe world’s diverse peoples. These may relateto a
variety ofspecific climate or environmental conditions, orthe
nature of particular resources. If, for instance, the importance
oftherenewability of resources is considered, the state ofthe
forests must be addressed. This most valuable of renewable
building materials is not being adequately replaced in the
quantity needed by plantations and reforestation. Trees need
time to grow. In some cases, a matter ofa few decades will see
saplings reach maturity, asthe peasants of Western Anatolia
are fully aware, when they plant poplar and larch in groves
maintained for their use in future building. In other instances
thegrowth oftrees, includingthe oaks and many tropical
hardwoods, takes too longforthetimberto be harvestedin a
lifetime. The felling oftropical forests and their saleto the west
forimmediate profit but without thought forthe future, has
been culpable in Indonesia and Malaysia. Similarly, the clearance
offoreststo open the landto pioneer settlers in Brazil, orfor
large-scale ranchingforthe burger business has been both
thoughtless and in violation of indigenous rights.3®

Roots of trees draw immense quantities of waterfromthe
land, butthey also hold it and stabilize it; deforestation in Nepal
has released more water into the Ganges and has seriously
contributed to the annual inundation of Bangladesh. Timber
species are vulnerable in themselves, some being attacked by
termites or eaten by insects, timber structures attract rodents,

17: Houses constructed
of soil block stabilized
with the addition of
sump oil from heavy
vehicles. Thehouses
were intendedto
indicate possibilities
to local self-builders.
Near Khartoum, Sudan

birds and parasites which contributeto disease. Wood can be
subjectto wet rotand dry rot, and appropriate preventative
treatments needto be applied. Additionally, there isthe question
of vast quantities oftimber being consumed for fuel. Whilethe
regeneration oftheforests may be undertaken over an extended
period, the rapid growth of eucalyptus can provide an important
source for both round and sawn timber. Many ofthe problems of
cultivating, logging, drying, preserving and sawing eucalyptus
have been addressed and solved, so that a species, E. Globulus,
formerly used principally for firewood, is now in considerable use
as a building material with potential for further development for
low-income housing.3? Bamboo by comparison, has already been
extensively used for building over many generations, its many
species and performance characteristics being developed and
exploited from Colombia to Japan. Nevertheless, although
bamboo grows in tropical and sub-tropicalforests acrossthe
world, it is seldom used for building in Africa. In view of its
remarkably rapid growth, tensile strength and dimensional range
bamboo may have considerable potential in that continent, as
has been demonstrated in Asia and Latin America.4°

Earth, orwhen mixed with water, mud, isthe most
ubiquitous building material in the world, half of all existing
dwellings being constructed of it; some regional estimates place
the percentage far higher, asisthe case in India. Its effectiveness
as a buildng material dependsto a considerable extent onthe
soil structure and particle size prevalent. ‘Semi-permanent’
materials like mud and mud brick can be made more durable -
as land-crete‘, using 1:12 mixture of cement and laterite soils;
“asfadobe‘, combining asphalt with earth and adobe blocks; and
with waste engine oil used as an emulsifier and binding agent in
earth bricks. Compressing earth blocks with manually operated
equipment, the Cinva-Ram invented by a Colombian, Raul

Ramirez, made one block at atime, though more recent manual
systems have a better rate of production.# Nonetheless, the
electrically powered system used for ramming earth walls-such
asatthe Eden Project in England - is questionable. Thetrade-off
between costs of labour-intensive hand production, manuallyoperated machines and powered cement block-making
machines which may greatly improve output - but which
require fuelto run andare liableto breakdown - needsto be
carefully evaluated.#

Where top-soils needto be saved and other ecological
issues arise related to the conservation of other resources,
including water and plants, a cost-benefit analysis may have
to be applied. Adobe blocks on the other hand, can be massproduced, as in New Mexico, where some four million adobe
bricks are made annually by home-builders, and as many more
by commercial enterprises, including that ofthe Native Products
Division ofthe Eight Northern Pueblos Council.# There are
numerous organizations committedtothe useand advancement
of earth construction, such as CRATerre in Grenoble, France,
which have publications and a service to advise on such issues.4+
Unfired brick hasthe virtue that it uses no fuel, and is returnable
to its original state if desired. Fired brick, onthe other hand, can
consume considerable quantities offuel, though the use of such
waste products asriceand corn husks to heat brick and lime
kilns has proved effectivewhen there are adequate quantities
available. Comparatively quick and easy to lay and bond with
mortar, brick is used extensively wherethe clays are appropriate,
asin India and Nepal.#

Though stone in terms of craftsmen-hours is expensive
to dress, it is preferabletothe inauthentic crushingand
reconstitution of mock-stone. Rubble-stone remains a practical
materialwhich can be bonded with mortar and used with quoins,

or dressed corner-stones for greater strength. Short layers of
bricks may likewise be used forthis purpose. Broken and rubble
stone may also be held in wire mesh containers, or gabions,
which can be stacked and used as a mass walling material.
Where building stability is uncertain, as in seismically prone
parts ofthe world, decks of concrete, orreinforced concrete
framing systems may be desirable, the upstanding reinforcement
ofasingle level permittingthe later addition of an upperfloor.
Retrofit, orthe post-construction installation of protective
methodsto stabilize or reinforce walls and roofs can be
difficult.* A variety of methods for strengthening structures by
diagonal or corner bracing and protection from collapsing walls
and ceilings by internal membranes, can be incorporated during
the building process. On such technical advice, and onthe use
of appropriatetechnologies in development situations, there
is considerable literature.#7

Of paramount importance inthe housing ofthe future
isaccess to services. Those systems by which energy and vital
resources may be supplied and wastes removed: electricity, water
and sanitation beingthe most prominent. Electricity isthe most
flexible, capable of being supplied by cable or wire above ground
orbelow ground, to streets and to individual dwellings. Relatively
inexpensive, it can provide high levels of safety in street lighting
and opportunities for a variety of cooking, heatingand
entertainment facilities even fortheeconomically disadvantaged.
But it still has high costs, and alternative methods of generating
electricity by manpower, windpower and waterpower have been
devised. Water supply is variable, being excessive in some
regions and minimal in others, precipitation, acquifers and
stream-flow of groundwater, differing accordingtotopography
and geophysical phenomena and the hydrologic cycle.
Techniques of watergathering, from desalination to condensation

can be applied in extreme circumstances, but the problem of
pollution may necessitate sanitation protection or storage
facilities. Cisterns, underdrainage systems and filtering methods
may be economically installed using appropriate technologies.
Similarly, the disposal of wastes and faecal matter have been the
subject of research and composting latrines and non-water
borne systems developed.#

Adoption ofthesetechnologies can be accepted whenthe
benefitsto health and life expectancy are communicated; the
greater problem isthe reluctance of many administrationsto
invest in such methods of servicing, orto assist communities in
making or obtaining and installingthem.

Vernacular traditions have adapted where social mores or
the introduction of new and economical building methods have
warranted change. One method ofreducingthe cost of buildings
isto employ fewer imported materials - steel, concreteand
glass. Soemetimes the imported material is more effective inthe
long-term because it may reducetime and money spent on
maintenance - corrugated iron roofs area good example. The
unsightliness ofthe rusting material is widely deplored, and
as has been noted, its capacity for reflecting solar heat is
considerably reduced by rust. Though easily affixed it is unable
to ‘breathe’ and is also extremely noisy in the heavy rains of
monsoon periods, addingto the discomfort ofthose living under
it. Forthese reasons, many Malay houses have a layer ofthatch
overthe corrugated sheet metal roofs.5° But, as itswidespread
use in Australia indicates, with diverse detailsto cope with
climatic problems, and careful maintenance ofthesurface and
edges, it can be employed with the simplicity and efficiency of
traditional materials. Corrugated and galvanized sheet metal
has been extensively used in most parts ofthe world for well over
a century, and plywood sheets for almost as long. Often such

22: Cistern, Punta del
Estacio, Murcia, which
holds sufficient water
forthe needs ofafarm
duringthe arid
summer. Spain

249

## S. 128

250 Enduring
dwellings

materials are less appropriate than traditional ones, plywood
being light and easy to fix, but subjectto delaminating in damp
climates. Concrete also has its considerable shortcomings,
being proneto damp andgrowth of algae, thermally poor and
lacking resiliance.5'

Traditional materials, using vernacular technologies are
generally appropriateto local conditions, being drawn forthe
most part, from available resources. Palms, including pandanus
andrattan, and many species of grass and reed are both
extensively used and renewable, if, as isthe case with wheatstraw,
mechanization has not destroyed its employment, inthis
instance forthatching. Their use also has important economic
implications, being obtained with theminimum of
transportation costs and, frequently worked by hand, involve
relatively little use of high energy consuming fuels. Vernacular
architecture is therefore ecologically sensitive and with regional
resources being carefully nurtured, is, as it has always been,
economicallyand environmentally sustainable.

Technology transfer

With the emphasis on ‘modern’ materials and the contempt with
which traditional ones are often held, support for the use of local,
renewable, accessible and economic materials is desirable - but
not atthe cost of adverse conditions, for example, the felling of
trees that may leadtoflooding. Where certain materials have
been expended or have been exhausted by excessive use or by
natural causes due, perhaps, to climate change, ‘technology
transfer’ isimportant.

Adesign team, like the Development Workshop, which
has undertaken community projects in many parts ofthe Middle
East and Africa, acknowledging Hassan Fathy’s inspiration, has
considerably advanced the use of available skills and materials,

23: Malay housewitha 24: Augustus Street,

corrugated iron roof Toowong. Wood framed
thatched overto house with surrounding
improve insulation and balconies, roofed with
reduce noiseinthe bright new galvanized
rains. Near Malacca, metal, which reflects
Malaysia solar heat. Australia

particularly in rural areas. In particular, the ‘Woodless
Construction’ program employingthe Nubian vault technique
noted and used by Fathy, for building in regions where soils are
suitable but where timber is no longer available. This programme
of ‘technology transfer’ has been extremely successful in
southern Algeria, Niger and Chad and has been adopted and
extended by traditional builders in these regions. To aidthe
processes of information and technology transfer, technical
notes on, forinstance, the use ofindigenous materials for roofs
orwalls, or methods of installing non-water borne sanitary
facilities are published by the United Nations Centre for Housing
and Settlements, Nairobi, the Building Research Establishment
in England, and research centres in anumber of other countries.

In spite ofthe information resources based on the
outcome ofresearch such advice on ‘intermediate’ or ‘appropriate’
technologies may still reach only asmall percentage ofthose
involved in housing. As we have seen, inthe processes of
building, inthe organization of space and the patterns of daily
use, the dwelling is expressive ofthe values of its occupiers.
These need to be articulated and accommodated in the building
of new dwellings or intheadaptation and upgrading of
traditional ones. Unfortunately, the research publications and
technical datathat support the architect/enabler are not
paralleled by similar support as a result of anthropological field
work. Consequently, government agencies, local authorities,
contractors, technical advisors, engineers, architectsand
planners, frequently plan communities and design housing on
pragmatic data with little or no knowledge of indigenous
dwellingtypes and patterns.

Nevertheless, negative resistance to vernacular building
methods and materials persists, even when augmented by
so-called ‘modern materials’ where circumstances warrant it.
