Largely, the issue is one of prejudice; ignorance and hostility
to what have been regarded as ‘bush’ or ‘backward’ cultures,
antipathy to vernacular architecture and the use of traditional
resources and techniques, and fears of being ‘held back’ from
modernizing. But it is also one of superior and patronizing
attitudes, which are expressed inthe concept of housingthe
‘masses’. Increasingly, theimpoverished rural peoples ofthe
world are expected to conform to ‘mass housing’ and ‘low-cost
housing', without regard fortheirfamily sizes and affiliations,
their use of spaces, their values, norms and beliefs, let alonetheir
capacity to shape their own environments, which are äll
expressed in their traditional dwellings. -
Attheturn ofthetwenty-first century numerous ‘new
millennium’architectural conferences were held in all parts of
the world, but while the ‘sustainability’ ofthe city was the buzzword and theme, little was discussed concerningthe increase
in peri-urban populations.55 The architectural debate largely
avoided the issue of in-migration, but where it was acknowledged,
the design solution persistently cited was a return to high-rise
blocks. Eventhough they have proved to be extremely
unsatisfactory-inthe condition and neglect ofthe buildings,
the failure of prefabricated systems, but especially socially -
there were few who recognized their failings. Psychological
aspects of occupancy were neglected, such asthe need for social
contact and exchange thattheimposed anonymity oftheform
ofthe high-rise developments inhibited, the limitations on
circulation and freedom of movement, the detachment from
theland andthefear of assault in the corridors, stairwells and
elevators. The fact that so many, from Pruitt-Igoeto North
Peckham inLondon, have hadto be demolished was ignored.
Cosy euphemisms and metaphors like ‘the global village’
have deflected attention and assuaged consciences, the concept

of‘globalization’ obliterating urban and rural distinctions by
embracingthem in one phenomenon. This has done nothing

to confront the problems ofthe exponentially expanding cities,
northose pressures exerted on the vastly greater, but widely
neglected, non-urban regions. To a great extentthe solutions

to immediate housing needs areto be met by national
governments and local authorities, engineers and builders, many
of whose attitudes and values have been shaped in Western
moulds. Whatever their political shade, their commitmentto
high technologies for prestigious buildings seems universal. As
forthe shelter ofthehomeless, housing of replicated kinds is
customary. Standardized in plan and structure, multiplied and
arranged in geometric settlements for ease of service runs and
drawing-board formalism, such bureaucratized, centralized
mass-housing solutions are not designedto berresponsive either
tothe cultural patterns of established traditions orto emerging
aspirations. They neither utilize local skills nor make intelligent
use of received knowledge. Instead, family and individual
spatial allocations are drawn from standard designs, ratherthan
from the analysis and understanding, of specific physical,
psychological and socio-cultural needs.5®

Transmitting tradition

Itmay beargued that many ofthe dwellingtraditionsthat have
been discussed in this book are of peoples of small populations,
whose cultures are anachronistic inthe new millennium. Indeed,
it suits the Interests of many governments and multi-national
corporations, notto mention a nascent mass-housing industry,
to maintain such a position. Nevertheless, the diversity of
dwellingtypes and traditions directly reflectsthe diversity ofthe
cultures that buildthem. Some ofthese are notablefortheir
spatial organization, fortheirscaleand forthesize ofthe nuclear,

stem or extended families, or even entire clans, thatthey may
accommodate. In certain instances there are differentiated
spaces that arise from gradients of privacy or role, whether or
not these are defined in terms of kinship, gender, age, status or
the rreception of guests. Likewise, there arespaces that are
considered to be moresacred than others, where degrees of
purity or profanity are expressed in the location of building parts
and functions. Andthere are numerous cases where such
distinctions are not readily apparent but are defined by custom
and learned behaviour. Designated areas forthe hearth, forthe
consumption of food, for sleeping, for craftwork, for relaxing or
for prayer may exist in specific traditions. Itshould not bethe
purpose of housingto reduce or destroy the values and functions
that different cultures embody in their dwellings, but ratherto
respectthem -ifindeed, ‘housing', or external intervention in
the production of dwellings, is to be justified.5

How are these values to be determined and understood?
How, in fact, are they to beaccommodated in new housing
without doing damagetothe culture concerned, or experiencing
the rejection of new or adapted buildings because they are
culturally inappropriate? Anthropologists may be ableto reveal

orexplain aspects of traditional custom and personal or group
behaviourthat are peculiarto a society. Yet, unfortunately, their
frequent lack of awareness of, or interest in, its buildings ortheir
construction, may prove to be a limitation. Moreover, ifasthis
book has indicated, vernacular building traditions differ as do
the culturesthat create and build them, how can their diversity
bereckoned with, and accommodated? While there is no simple
answertothese points, itisthefull participation ofthe members
ofeach culture concerned that is vitalto the satisfactory
development of dwelling, in both senses, inthefuture.

Itwould doubtless be valuable ifthere were a grand theory
that accountsforallthetypes and conditions of vernacular
architecture, which explainsthe nature, function and associated
values inthe dwellings of all cultures, no matter whattheir
environments, their social and family structures, their religions
and beliefs might be. Ifthere is anything in common with all
these ‘traditions’ it liesinthetransmission and passingonto
subsequent generations oradjacent cultures ofthe ‘tradita”.
Tritely termed ‘handing down’, it involves relatively little material
transmission, though heirlooms and certain possessions
including rritual objects, clothing or jewellery may be physically

## S. 130

passed to inheritors. Of particular importance with referenceto
dwellings arethe selection ofthesite, and where applicablethe
fields of influence of beneficent spirits, the consecration or
otherritual observances at commencement, the layoutandthe
spatial organization ofthe building, and the finding, obtaining
and preparation or transformation of materials. These may
betransmitted by engagingtheyouth oftheculture, inthe
processes, but atall stages, instruction inthe appropriate
observances is shared across the generations.5®

By no means do all societies have religious orsymbolic
connotations in allthey do or build, butthe correct procedures
areimportantto each. The processes of construction, from the
digging ofthe foundations orthe insertion of major supports, to
thetopping out oftheroof ortheaffixing ofthefinal finial, may

be age or gender defined, but instruction of youth is
fundamental. We have seen this in the building ofthe Maasai
enkang orthe building ofthe Malay house, but itiscommonto
theraising of all dwellings, the most personal and intimate of
structures. The methods oftransmission are variedandthough
oral means are understandablythe most common, they range
from invocations and prayers to chants and songs. Music may
accompany building, settothe rhythm of movements asisthe
case in many parts of West and Central Africa, while work songs
of many kinds co-ordinate group production of woven materials,
orthe driving of piles orthe splitting oftimber and stone.”
Many aspects ofthe process may be learned by rote, with grades
and measurements mentally recorded. Measurements may
frequently be derived from human proportions, by divisions

and multiples of digits or armspans.

Thoughthe means and content of verbal transmission has
been studied little, it includes the use of proverbs and aphorisms
orsayingsthat reinforce good practice. Maxims or phrasesthat
remind craftspersons of details oremphasize values in working
materials and finished surfaces are common. Poems and
panegyrics, myths and epics set standards and expectations of
quality as well as giving, through heroic examples, confirmation
ofthe origins of practices and performance of skills. Many of
these are formulaic, sothatthey may be learned and memorized
with little difficulty, and can also be repeated as corrections or
reminders. Correct terminology, which may extendtothe
identification of materials, components and parts, jointsand
connections, techniques andtools, are also the means whereby
alterations, extensions and variants are communicated.

This also appliesto theterminology of processes, by which
instruction in using new materials orthe adoption of innovative
techniques can be compared with traditional practices. These
will also be taught by direct instruction orthrough assignments
of graduated difficulty, while verbal and physical correction is
paralleled by praise and approbation. Non-verbal means of
instruction are customary in many aspects of building
construction, such asthe demonstration oftechniqueswhetherthese are of lashings, the application of paintorthe
stretching oftent covers.

In non-literate societies graphiccommunication may be
employed to convey plans or details, whilenumerals may be
cut with an axe-blade to match pre-fabricated parts. The use
oftemplets or patterns by which mouldings may be drawn is
customary among plasterers and masons, and manuals depicting
details and methods to achieve them have long been in use in
China and Japan. Most vehicles of communication are direct

with ‘consoles’ or

from The Balkansto

between parents and children, master craftsmen and
apprentices, elders and novices, often involvingmembers of
three generations. But stem transmission by descent has its
parallels in lateral or spatial transmission by diffusion. The
synchronic passing or copying of buildingcomponents may
account for the wide distribution of certain features, such as
the roof truss orthe post-and-console. Though all methods
suggest intention and training, the role of play and mimicry in
thetransmission of skillsandthe use or respect ofspaces is
significant. By many such means the complex of traditions
which are embodied in the dwellings of aculture, andthe
nuances or latitudes by which each building within atradition
exhibits individual features, are acquired, learned and
transmitted to others overtime.s°

External influence andtechnological training are not
inimicaltothe passing on of traditions, for change and
development areto be perceived inthe majority oftraditions in
building. Their acceptance may require acquistion, testingand
assimilation or rejection, depending on the balance of benefit
and interference that Innovations and introductions may
effect. To the dramatic changes in environments, economies
and employment with which thousands of cultures have been
assailed inthe past half-century, there have been differing
responses. Some have adapted, many have adjusted, while
others have succumbed, butthe scale of change forallhas had
no precedents in living memory. Sothe questions remain as
to howto meet housing needs in theimmediate future, what
the professional architect can contributeto the solution, what
disciplines, if any, can co-operate inthe daunting but essential
undertaking, and what part vernacular architecture traditions
andtheirtransmission have to play in the provision of
millions of dwellings.

Epilogue: dwelling, design and development

Considerable concern has been raised bythe Chinese
government ’s implementation ofthe ‘Three Gorges’ dam project
on the Yangtze River. Closed in 1997 to all river transport, thedam
project will flood immense areas of land when it becomes
operational in 2008. Apart from environmental damage, thedam
will costthelivelihoods, the villages and dwellings of hundreds
ofthousands of families. It seems that some measure of relief for
the latter was being contemplated for, early in 2001, a brief note
inthe building press reported that an invitation had been
extended bythe Chinese to a British firm to assist inthe design
ofhousingforthe1.2 million people who will be displaced by

the building of the dams.' The firm of consulting engineers
announced their planto produce over a million ‘homes’to.a
standard design inthe form ofterrace housing-not based on
theterracing ofthe valleywherethe Yangtze flows, but onthe
eighteenth-century innovation of ‘terrace', or ‘row’ housing. This
system, introduced extensively in London, involved the erection
of continuous rows of houses sharing party walls, generally at
leastthreefloors in height, in addition to a basement and
frequently, an attic storey. Should this seem irrelevant, the
firm’s website publicity uses as examples illustrations of terraces
ofthe Holland Park, London type, in which a single apartment

at 2000 AD prices cost over a million pounds, and asinglehouse
severaltimesthat figure.?

However, the houses are not to be based on the Belgravia
or Kensington example but, it is claimed, on the Chinese ‘shophouse”. Such two-storey shop-houses were probably developed
bytheChinese in Malaysia and Singapore. Pre-engineered in a
Chongging shipyard, the modular ‘proven’ components ofthe
six-storey houses will be dispatched for erection on site. ‘Fullspan, non-load-bearing partitions’ are promisedanda choice

With internal rather than trans-global expenditure it could have

Dwellings and design

This book has been about vernacular dwellings, with examples
drawn from all continents. It does not claim to be comprehensive
oreven fully representative, forthere are many thousands of
culturesthroughout the world, and all havetheir own architectural
traditions. In addition, there are innumerable dwellings which
have been constructed by their owners as ‘one-off’ buildings that
do notreflect a particulartradition. They includethe eccentric
houses in North America and Australia built of unconventional
materials, such as the ‘bottle houses’ made of beverage, alcohol
orembalming fluid bottles that function as brick-like units
mortared together. Others for instance, in parts of Africa and
Central America have been built of beer orssimilar cans, filled
with sand or gravel. These and other buildings made of recycled
materials, such as automobile parts and broken pottery, built

by individualists without referencetto others, are frequently
identified as ‘primitive’ or ‘naive‘, but are also sometimes
recognized as artworks.* Original and unique, these are not
vernacular dwellings, inthe sense of their being created bythe
people in accordance with traditional norms.

Nevertheless, in certain instances there have been signs
ofan emergent tradition, as builders have related more closely
with the houses of others: the ‘baches’ (probably from ‘bachelor
houses’) of settlers in rural New Zealand and later, of self-built
coastal, second homes. Of similar intention werethe converted
railway carriages and owner-built homes on the ‘plotlands’
of East Anglia and southeast England largely constructed by
working families from London in thefirst half ofthetwentieth

century.5 Many ofthese were ofthe ‘summerhouse’ type,

which correspond with the ‘chalet gardens’ of Amsterdam or
Copenhagen, and the ‘Kleingarten’ of contemporary Berlin.°
More standardized intheirforms and dimensions for reasons of
transportability on national highways, as well asthe location of
services nodes, are ‘mobile homes’. Rarely moving morethan
once -from the manufacturerto the purchaser’sown orrented
site-themobilehome can sometimes be clad, fitted or
decorated to the buyer’s requirements. On site it is often
extended with car-ports and verandas but also with ‘add-ons’to
provide more room.” Some measure of self-build is permitted in
relationtothe popular architecture of contractor-built suburban
housing, butthis is rarelymorethan a garden shed or workshop,
planning approval being required in Britain and elsewhere for
extensions and loft conversions.

Such examples may not be representative of vernacular
architecture, astheterm is used forthis book, butthey are
closely related, being indicative ofthe widespread desireto
create and build one’s own dwelling, which is often limited more
bylegislation than by ability. It is not imperativeasitisin much
ofthe world, where building a dwelling is a matter of necessity,
andthe means whereby the know-how is acquired isttraditionally
passed on. As | have endeavoured to show, the vernacular
dwellings of mankind representthe complex interaction of many
aspects of culture essentialto specific societies. Yet vernacular
architecture is not confined to dwellings: barns and byres,
granaries and grist-mills, shrines, shops and schoolhouses -
thelist offunctionaltypes aroundtthe world is considerable.

The organization of settlements andtheir relationshipto
territory and site, social structure, economy, markets and
communications are of basic importance.? Ultimately, however,
it isthe dwellings of humankind that predominate- allother

|

buildings depending for their functions onthe occupiers ofthe
world’s houses and residential structures.

Dwellingtypes are influenced bytheresourcesthat are
availabletoa culture atthetime of building, and the skills and
technologies employed in obtaining, making and building
with them. Over generations numerous means have been
evolved, to aidthe adjustment to extremes of environment by
modifying climate within a building while meetingtheneedto
accommodate the particular living patterns of specific cultures.
Community structure and familytype make demands upon
spatial relationships, whilethe cycles ofthe day, the seasons,
the working year and ofllife itself, profoundly affectthe use of
internal and external space. Societies and individuals attach
significancetotheir houses that relateto their value systerns,
ranging from personal identification withthehome, tothe
cosmic symbolism ofthe dwelling, its location and orientation.
We have seen that, inthe processes of building, inthe
organization of space, the subtleties of detail, even inthe
disposition of domesticarticlesandthe patterns of daily use, the
dwelling has significance. Sometimes explicit, sometimes wholly
implicit, this may be expressed in built form and details, in spatial
organization, orthrough sacred and secular symbolism and
decoration. But in every case, these factors reflectthe behaviours,
customs and beliefs, and the hierarchies, values and aspirations
ofthe members ofthe cultures concerned. They needto be
articulated in the building of new dwellings orthe adaptation and
upgrading of traditional ones in each and every cultural context.

General assumptions and blanket solutions to housing
needs are likelyto create problems of mis-match and even total
rejection. Unfortunately, government agencies, local authorities,
contractors, technical advisors, engineers, architects and
planners frequently plan communities and design housing on

4: Raglan, New 5: Lightly framed 6: Asubstantially built
Zealand, where large bambooandthatch house ofthe Dai people
numbers of baches store houses ofthe Dai (related tothe Thai) of
or self-built batchelor permit ventilation and Southern Yunan, China.

China ofbamboo

pragmatic data with little or no knowledge of indigenous
dwellingtypes and patterns. Admittedly, itis easy to laythe
blame on the authorities, architects or anthropologists forthe
general lack of awareness of the diversity ofthe dwellings ofthe
world, for ignorance of mankind’s housingand for a sheer lack of
concern over homelessness. Whoever is responsible, this brings
usto the issue ofthe design of houses for peoples of another
culture-sometimes, indeed, of another continent. Interms of
its material valuethe house is frequentlythe most expensive
item inthe property oftheindividual or family. Its value lies not
onlyinits resale or rental potential but also in the expenditure of
effort, skill and personal involvement ofthose who have been its
builders as well as its owners. A bond is established between the
builder and his dwelling which is far more profound than the
Teutonic concept of heim/home, exploited intheimagery and
media of northern Europe and North America.

There are many reasons why alien-designed, massproduced and pre-packaged housing fails. Accustomedto a
different relationship between internal and external spacethan
that of a conventional Western house, a people may experience
a disconcerting loss of ‘environmental control‘, which comes
with being unableto relocate or alter the dwellingto suit specific
social or climatic conditions, asthey have been accustomedto
do.? To live in a Western-styled house requires the fragmentation
of personalspaces andtheadoption, at least in part, ofthe
life-ways of another culture; though to adopt them may mean
the loss of cultural and personal identity. In a period of
“globalization‘, however theterm may be interpreted, it may be
argued that what is suitable for one culture- almost inevitably
Western - is appropriate for all. The popularity of certain
convenience and take-away foods, articles of clothing, or items
ofimmediate communication may beinstanced as evidence.

## S. 134

262 Epilogue

Againstthetendency to obliterate cultural diversity, many
peoples are seekingto return to their homelands, theirown
values and theirtraditional dwellings. This has been evident
since the collapse ofthe Soviet Union, which caused the partition
of countries such as Czechoslovakia and Yugoslavia into separate
states, with the independence movements of peoples likethe
east Timorese, and many conflicts, from Northern Irelandto
Palestine and Israel. Howeverthey may be resolved, they
underscore the desire of cultures to retain their own identities
and theirterritories, to shape their destinies and determinetheir
own solutions to problems of economy, employment, education
and housing. Yet, for many other people, there are no homelands
to whichtthey can return. Often their only recourse, as we have
seen, has beento migratetothecityandto endeavourto
establish some kind of home on the urban fringes.!°

Though the larger settlement forms oftown andcity
embrace both housing and homelessness in complex ways, only
inthe post-Second World War years have escalating population
growth, mass migrationtotthecities, andthe decay of urban
living under extremes of economicand spatial pressures obscured
cultural values and blunted perception ofthem. Inasense, the
homeless who have been solvingtheir housing predicament by
buildingtheir own dwellings have carried on the vernacular
tradition of using available resources, even ifthose accessibleto
thepoorinthecity are usually limitedtothe waste ofthe affluent.
Eventuallythey have been ableto improve them materially and
some long-established settlements that have been provided
with services have become indistinguishable from the customary
low-income housing ofthecity."Thisisnottosaythatthey are
necessarily well-designed, orthatthey makethe best use of sites
and resources. Istherethen, a placeforthe anthropologist or
thearchitectto sharetheirtraining, knowledge and experience

7. New housesina
refugee camp, with
bamboo frame, and
mats of diagonally
woven bamboo secured
byalattice of split
poles. Dacca, Pakistan

to the benefit ofthe least affluent sectors ofthe community,
rural or urban?

Even if anthropologists give littleindication of interest
in the fabricorthe construction ofthe dwelling, they have
published much research on aspects of culture, such as kinship
studies or symbolicrituals, which directly relateto the dwelling
and illumine aspects of its use of space. These may also reveal
interactions within or between communities relatingto
endogamous or exogamous marriage practices, to affiliations
and lineages, to hierarchies and social organizations which
may not always be visually apparent but which are germane
to behaviour in the settlement and beyond. Extensivethough
anthropological and ethnographical literature may be, a
considerable amount is available only in specialized professional
publications, or in monographs of limited circulation, and most
ofit remains unseen by architects, let alone by politicians, or
apparently, bynon-governmental organizations.

This is highlighted by the publication ofthe Sphere Project
in which ‘minimum standards in disaster response’ have been
defined by a great many NGOs. Included are ‘Minimum
Standards in Shelter and Site Planning’ (embracing ‘Household
Items’ and ‘Clothing’) drawn up by a consortium in which the
Lutheran World Federation is prominent. Guidance on postdisaster assessment recommends making a ‘profile’ of victims,
which should note ‘whether people are rural or urban’, their
“traditional lifestyle in terms ofpublic/private use of space’, and
their ‘traditional building skills and construction methods’. No
further information is provided on whatthese mean or howthey
are evaluated. Using as a ‘key indicator’ thatthe ‘covered area
available per person averages 3.5 t0 4.5 sq m’ only site planning,
in relation to climatic conditions and the supply of plastic
sheeting for shelter, is given more than cursory attention. There

8:ACalcutta bustee

9: An upgrading

which has been programme bythe

is no discussion of cultural differences asto needs and values.
Norareany references to anthropological studies or vernacular
architecture traditions, not even to analysis of former postdisaster housing, cited in the bibliography."

Dialogue, exchange of information and research and,
above all, joint research undertakings between disciplines are
essential ifinformed and balanced recommendations
concerning housing and its servicingareto bemadetothose
with the authority and resources to apply them. An architect who
functions as an ‘enabler' and who works in an advisory capacity
with the members of a community is likely to be more effective
than one who attemptsto meet the problems of large-scale,
low-cost housingfrom aremote distance. This would seem to
bea special breed of professional, though a number of architects
andfirms function in this manner, helping buildersto useand
develop their skills, andto make the best use of vernacular
techniques and indigenous materials, some of whom have
been recognized by the Aga Khan Award for Architecture.3
Assisted self-build projects in various parts oftheworld
have made effective use of local materials and indigenous
techniques, improved by experimental methods, though often

forced to accord with inflexible grid plans. With thetransfer of
information and technology gained in such projects itmay be
possible to share successes, guard against failures and anticipate
future developments.

Nevertheless, the problem of housing a further 3 billion
people before the middle ofthe twenty-first century hasto be
confronted. This is in addition to the need for maintainingand
improving those dwellings that are in peri-urban settlements,
that are already under construction, in the course of being built
incrementally or, that remain and can be supported intherural
regions ofthe world. The implications of planningand land
tenure, of resources and expenditure, of credit against self-help
labour, and of efficient servicingthatresponds to cultural needs
ratherthan imposes alien design concepts must all betaken into
account. And so mustthe education ofanthropologists and
geographers, financiers and administrators, and above all, that
ofarchitects and planners who may well beresponsible for
assisting and supporting self- and community-builders. It will
betheir knowledge, abilities, motivations and concernsthat
willbefundamentalto the creation of appropriate dwellings
throughout much ofthe world inthe foreseeable future.

## S. 138

270 Glossary zu

Glossary
Note: Terms In this Glossary are briefly defined in accordance with how they are
used in the text ofthe book. Many may have other meanings and most willhave
fuller or more complex definitions, which may be found in specialized works of
reference.

aborigines Thefirst or most ancient people ofa region, eg.of Australia or
North America
abutment Awallor bank receiving the lateral thrust pressure ofan arch or vault
adobe, atob Compressed earth, normally in the form of bricks or blocks.
Arabic origin
A-frame Apitched roof frame in direct contact with the ground rather than
with walls
age-set Aclass of people of similar age, generally living together and sharing
duties
aggregate Stones or other loose but hard material added to a mixture of earth
orconcrete
airshaft Asmall court which permits airto ventilate rooms on several levels
alp Mountain meadow. In popular usage, a high mountain, or mountain range
ancestralhall Community building dedicated to, or respectingthe ancestors
andtheirteachings
animism The beliefthat allliving things have a spirit or soul
anneal To fire, fuse, bake or glaze by heating a material such as steel or glass
anthropology Literally, the science orthe study ofhumankind
anthropometry Measurement ofthe human body, often applied to domestic
and furniture design
anthropomorphism Human form or attributes expressed in site, architecture
ordesign m
apex Apeak or pointedtop, for example of a spire, roof or mountain
apse, apsidal A semi-circular bay or recess, usually with a half-dome roof; ofan
apse-like form
arcade plate The horizontal plane which supports a row ofarches ralsed on piers
arch Curved construction spanning an opening, often made ofwedge-shaped
elements
architrave A beam spanning between columns and resting on their capitals
armature Framing system which may be covered with mats, leaves or other
cladding
asfadobe Earth which is mixed with asphalt when making adobe blocks.
ash-house Stone structure in which fire ashes are kept for eventual use as
fertilizer, for hardening earthen floors or in making soap
ashlar Construction of dressed or regularly cut and smoothed stone
atrium The main hall ofa Roman building, with a partiallyopen roof andan
impluvium (a rectangular basin to collect rain-water). Applied to halls of similar
form and use
auger Carpenter’stool for boring holes, with a T-handle and screw pointed shaft
autochthonous Related to the original inhabitants ofaregion
axonometric Aform of graphic projection from the plan set at 45° inwhichthe
vertical and horizontal dimensions can be measured. An Illusion of threedimensional form isconveyed
bache Self-built house, generally on thecoast, New Zealand
badgir Awindscoop or wind-catcher, Iragiterm
bagdadi Timber frame with long braces and earth brick nogging. Turkey, but of
Iraq origin
balloon frame Timber frame system employing light (2x 4 inch) members which
could be pre-fabricated and ‘inflated,' or assembled on site. Important inthe
development of vernacular architecture in the USA
bamboo Agiant grass ofthetropics, extensively used as a building material
banjo-plan Of acircular plan, with a linear extension or approach structure
bargeboard Boards followingthe edge ofaroof gable protectingrafters or
purlin ends
barrel-vault A vault of semi-circular section. Also called a ‘tunnel vault’
barriada Squatter settlement, Spanish Latin America, especially Peru
barrio Settlement ortown quarter, generally applied to low-income sectors.
Spain
bas-relief Sculpture, usually in picture form, including a background and with
figures carved into the surface in shallow depth. French origin
bast Flexible fibrous bark, especially ofthe limetree
batten Anarrow strip of board, usedto secure ahhatch, or in large numbers for
flooring
batter The inclination of asloping wall or buttress built to resistthrust
baulk Asubstantial length oftimber, usually a squared and trimmed log
beehivetype Inthe form of atraditional parabolic-domed beehive made of
twisted straw rope. Now obsolete
bilek Domestic unit within a long-house. Malaysia/Indonesia
binarism Perception and classification of human, natural and other phenomena
by their opposites (male/female, left/right, sun/moon, etc)
blacktent Thetent of nomads in South-west Asia and North Africa, covered in
strips ofwoven black cloth sewn together
blade Asplit log offering a flat surface against which another may be placed, as
in acruck frame

blockbau Horizontal log construction. German origin

bole Recess or small opening in a wall, for example, a bee-hive (external) ora salt-
Jar (internal). Ashuttered bole may also be madetto provide ventilation

bond The placing of bricks or regular blocks of stone in the construction ofa wall
so that they interlock. Stretchers are laid horizontally in the plane ofthe wall,
while headers are placed across its depth, ensuring that joins of adjacent
courses do not coincide

borie Corbelled stone dwelling or store. French

borrow pit Apitorbasin dug intothe ground from which earth for wall-building
is obtained

bow-drill A.drill which is operated by the movement of a bow, the cord of which
istwisted around thebit

box-frame Assembly of lengths of timbertto form the basic skeleton oftimberframed walls, frequently in cubic or rectangular box form

brace Acomponent placed diagonally across a corner, employing the principle
oftriangulation in orderto stabilizethe structure

bracket Brace or console usually made of two pieces of wood or metal joöinedto
form a right-angle, one following the vertical side of a post, the other
supportinga beam

bresummer Alarge horizontal timber beam, especiallyone usedtospana
fireplace opening

brick, burnt Brick of earth or clay which has been ‘fired’ or baked in akiln

bund Levee or embankment built as protection from floods. Probably of Hindi
origin

bungalow Single-storey house developed in the earlytwentieth century and
derived from a traditional Indian dwellingtype in Bangalore

buron Mountain hut forthe accommodation oftranshumant herders. France.
See transhumance

bustee Squatter settlement in an Indian city, such as Bombay or Calcutta

buttress Aconstruction, generally of brick or stone, erected to give support to
awall

byre Stable for cattle, especially in Scotland

cabane Small house or field hut built for and by French agricultural workers

cacique Native American chief. Latin America. Spanish origin

campesino Peasant farmer, Central and Latin America. Spanish origin

cantilever Structural principle by which abeam supported or pinned at oneend
only can extend out to support a load above, such as a projecting upper storey

caravanserai Inn orstaging post on a caravan route, accommodating merchants
and animals. Often abbreviated as serai. Persian origin

caste The hierarchical ordering of social or occupational groups of people within
a society. Formerly India and Nepal

catenary Acurve, asformed by a chain suspended at its ends from two opposite
points

catslide Monopitch roof extended from one side of a pitched roof and inthe
same plane

centering Awooden frame or formwork over which an arch or vaultis
constructed and which is ‘struck’ (removed) when the work is complete

centralplacetheory Thetheory developed by Cristallerto explain the spatial
relationship of a market town or other ‘central place’ to its satellite settlements

chalet Originally a Swiss peasant or herdsman’s hut, theterm was later applied
to asmall house with Swiss features

chamfer Formed by removing the arris, or sharp edge of a stone block or squared
wooden beam

chevron Design or motif ina V-shape

chink, chinking The filling of gaps between logs or other structural members
with earth or pieces of stone

chowk Urban space orwidening of a road. Indian

circumpolar Appliedto lands and cultures in arctic North America, Europe
andAsia

cistern An excavated or constructed reservoirfor rainwater which may be below
ornearadwelling

cladding The use of anon-structural, wind or waterproof, insulating layerto
covera roof (eg with tiles or slates) or a wall (eg with wood lap-boarding)

clapboard Wall cladding with overlapping narrow boards, which may bethinner
alongthe upper edge

clasp To hold a beam or other building component with another, shapedto
receiveit

climate zone Identification of a region in which all parts share similar climatic
characteristics (eg relative humidity, rainfall, temperature range)

cob Built with lumps of clay or earth mixed with a natural aggregate. Originally
aterm used in Devon, south-west England, but now more widely employed

coir Fibre of coconut husks manually twisted to make cordage or rope used for
lashings and the making of mats

cold-sink Topographical or artificially constructed hollow which retains cold air

collar, collarbeam Abeam above atie-beam and nearthe apex of a roof-truss
which contributes to stability by triangulation

compound Adefined area which may contain anumber ofhuts, granariesand
‚other features used by a household, applied particularly in Africa and India

compression Concentrated weight, as opposedtotension. Compression
strength referstothecapacityto.accept a weight orload

conduit Achannel or narrow canal in which water is conducted. Also appliedto
similar channels carrying electric cables or otherlinear systems

cone, conical The form produced by the rotation of atriangle, with theapex at
the centreanda circular base

cone-and-cylinder The combination of a conical (cone-form) roof surmountinga
cylindrical dwelling or storage unit. Applied to many such structures in Africa
and India

connotative In semiotics, that which implies additional or symbolicratherthan
specificmeaning *

conservation The policy and practice of protection of a building (or complex) and
keeping it in its present state without risk of decay, destruction or neglect.

console Brace or bracket construction, usually in the form of a single member
placed above a post or column instead of a capital, to support abeam

coping Thetop course of a masonry wall, often with a slope and slight overhang
to cast off rain-water

coppicing Nurture ofthe coppice or copse (asmall area oftrees), particularlythe
cutting ofyoung trees nearthe ground to promote growth for making poles

corbel Stone or short length of timber projecting from a walltotakea load, such
astheendofabeam

cordage Collectiveterm forlengths of string orrope

corral Atimber (or stone) stockade enclosing a ‘fold’ or‘pound’ in which animals,
such as horses, are kept

cosmic, cosmos The concept ofthe world, orofthe entire universe, as an ordered

and harmonious system. Among many cultures symbolized in the order of a
dwelling and its parts

course Alayer of masonry which may rest upon another layer. The succession of
layers may be placed intheform ofabond

courtyard plan Arrangement of a house or of a group of buildings whose walls
face onto.an open space, or courtyard

cowl Device in a form reminiscent ofa monk’s hood (cowl) placed on achimney
to deflect smoke, ortoturn with thewindto aid ventilation

creep Slow movement, as of soil on a slope, or penetration of damp in a wall

erenellated Intheform ofthe battlements on a fortress, with crenelles or
indented spaces, alternating with merlons, orrraised defensive sections.
Apattern ofthisshape

<ross-cousins Children of siblings of opposite sex

cross-vault The meeting oftwo vaults, normally at a right-angle, forming a cross
on plan

crownpost Aking-post or central post in a rooftruss connectinga tie-beam or
collar-beam with the ridge, a ‘crown’ being formed where brackets or braces are
also employed

cruck, cruck frame Traditional structure of pairs oftimber blades (crucks),
erected as inverted chevrons in bays, to provide a house frame. British Isles

cucina Kitchen. Italian

culm Section ofthejointed stalk of abamboo, cane or other such grass

cupola Small dome on a pierced cylindrical or polygonal drum, usedto crown
aroof

dais Raised platform for a high table or seat ofhonour

dapor Kitchen building or extension, Malay

dar Courtyard in a house. Arabic origin

daub Mud orcclay, as appliedto a wattle panel to infill a wall

deconstruction Dismantling of a structure. Also a philosophical dismantling of
theoretical constructs that require examination or replacement

demotic Vernacular of, by or belongingto the people

dendrochronology Dating oftimberbythenumberandtypeoftreerings, and
the establishment of acomparativetime-sequence by this means

denotative In semiotics, that which signifies or stands for something specific

dentilcourse Series ofsmall square blocks, liketeeth, as used in classical
cornices

oneday

to make a hydrate of lime. Used for limewashing walls, orwhen mixed with

next wall section

rawhide Untanned leather. Strips may be used for binding or making hinges

section Inarchitectural graphics, a vertical ‘slice’ through the building, as

break away

large building components. The lashing counteracts the possibility shearing

slates ortiles

sill, cill_ Horizontal element below a door orwindow, or supporting atimberframe

Yucatan, Mexico

structures, including its dimensions, altitude and topographical features

skillet Long-handled iron pan or pot, used for frying or other cooking

skin Said ofthe outer wall or cladding of a building

slater Craftsman who clads a roof orwallwith thin, overlaid pieces of slate

slats Thin lengths ofwood used to make a screen orto support other materials

sod Topsoil and its grass or vegetation, cut in largeturves

soddy House built ofsods, laid as bricks but with the vegetation still attached.
Western USA

solar Upper level constructed at one, or both ends of an open hall house for use
as sleeping quarters

solarheating Heating of a building by direct radiation from the sun, or by
harnessing, storing and converting the energy from sunlight

sole-plate Horizontal timber supporting posts and studs of atimber frame. See
sillbeam

soug Covered street bazaar, Middle East or North Africa

spalling Shearing or breaking of a layer of plaster, rendering, or mud from a wall

speicher Store-house. German origin

spine-wall Central wall of a buildingwhich may risetotheridge

splines Slats, or sliversofwood

squatter settlement Illegal ornon-formal settlement, generally on the outskirts
ofacityand occupied by the poor or underprivileged, having dwellings of
makeshift materials

squinch Corner detail of diminishing arcs, an alternativetothe pendentivein
transmittingthe load of adometoa cubic base

staddlestones Mushroom-shaped assembly of a stone pillarand a flat topstone, usedto support thecorners of agranary and to repel rodents

stay Support or brace, generally of wood, between two wood components

steppe Extensive treeless grasslands in Russia and Siberia

stilt, stilt dwelling Narrow post with a projecting bracket or other support.
Dwellings may be raised on a number of stilts over water or marshlands

storey Lateral division of a building. Most vernacular buildings are of one, two
orthree storeys, or layers of accommodation

strap-drill Drill operated by a strapwound around the drill shaft

stretcher Brick laid horizontally inthe plane ofthe wall

stringcourse A band of brick or stone set in a wall, sometimes bearinga moulding

structuralism In anthropology, atheoretical position which seekstotracethe
underlying ‘structures’ of society. In architecture, a philosophy of structure as
the essence of building

structure The system by which a building is constructed, includingthe scientific
principles by which its stability can be ascertained. Also appliedto the totality
ofabuilding

stud Vertical timber in atimber-framed building, stud construction having studs
at regular intervals within the overall frame

stupa Buddhist shrine, normally in domical form with finial. South Asia

subsidence Sinking of a building or a part ofit, dueto weakness in theground or
failure ofthe foundations

subsistence Basic survival economy, without surpluses

substructure Underlying structure, usually referring to supports below floor
level. Also applied metaphorically to basic but hidden features

superstructure The main, visible part of a building above foundation levelwhich
is constructed to accommodate its principal uses

sustainability Theprinciplethat the continuity oflife, ofnatural resources and
of settlement should be ensured without further harm to the environment

sweathouse Native American covered framework or small unit in which
cleansing rituals by means of steam baths can be conducted

swish West African method of building mass earthen walls in a succession of
‚lifts’, or raised sections. Asante, Ghana

syncretism Meeting and combination of religions or belief systems

tailiang Traditional post-and-beam construction. China

tamp To pound or compress earth. Also the name of the instrument used

tarpaper Literally, tarred paper, often figured with simulated brickwork patterns,
usedforan economic cladding

taxonomy Theory and principles of classification, which may includetypologies

technology transfer Sharing of aspects ofbuilding technology with cultures
who are not familiar with them

tenon The‘tongue’ orflat extension inserted into the mortise slot inamortise- '
and-tenon joint

tension, tensility Stretching, or capacity to withstand such strain

tenure Title orrightto hold land

terrace, terracing Cultivation system in hillyor mountainous sites in which
stepped, level fields are obtained by constructing and backfilling walls

terrace housing Houses constructed in rows and sharing ‘party’ walls. Also
referred to as a ‘row house’

tesserae Pieces of marble, stone, ceramic or glass used to compose a mosaic
design, motif or picture, usually as a flooring or wall decoration

thatch, thatching Roof cladding of grass or reeds, palm, mongongo or other
leaves, laid in overlapping layers. Infrequently used to cover walls, oran entire
building

thermallung Principle of heat exchange which can keep courtyards cool in hot
regions, eg ofthe Middle East or North Africa

Third World Under-developed countries, not members ofthe Western
industrialized First World orthe Communist Second World. Now an obsoleteterm

threshold Literally, a board across a barn entrance to hold threshed corn. Widely
applied to the step of a doorway marking the transition from outside to inside

through-stone Stone block in astone wall laid at right anglestothefaceofthe
wallto connect both sides. Use similarto that of header bricks

tie,tiebeam Connecting beam between the bases of principal rafters, resting on
orcloseto the wallplates

tiles Flatorslightly curved plate, usually of fired clay, secured to laths withwooden
pegs and laid as a roof cladding system. Other tile profiles have been used

tile-hanging Wall covering oftiles, affixed to laths on atimber frame in
overlapping rows. Used as wall cladding on surfaces exposed to persistent rains

tipi Conicaltent formed by arring.of poles crossingatthe apex and covered with a
canvas tent cloth. Native American, Plains

toolkit Range oftools including percussion, cutting, smoothing, drilling and
othertypes, held by a carpenter orjoiner. Figuratively used for allthetools used
byaculture

topography Description, which may include graphics and mapping, ofall
features of a landscape. Also used to referto such features

tor Apeak ofexposed graniteon amoor, or undulating open land. West of England

trabeated construction Post-and-beam construction, commonly timber but can
applytostone

transhumance Seasonal removal of herds to other pastures, to permit recovery
ofgrazed land

trapezoidal Quadrilateral form, with two sides parallelto each other

triangulation To make in triangular form, and thus to provide rigidity, asinthe
use ofacornerbrace

trickle-down Theory that design features, uses, economic advantages, etc
slowly flow from the *higher' social strata to benefit the ‘\ower’ strata

troglodyte Cave dweller. From the Greek

tropic Strictly, between the Tropic of Cancer (23°.27 North) and the Tropic of
Capricorn (23°.27 South) or extremes of the sun’s northern and southern
solstices. Loosely applied to regions with hot, humid climates

trulli Houses or house clusters with conical domed roofs of corbelled stone,
constructed by trullisti. Apulia, Italy

trundlebed Supplementary bed on wheels which may be stored and drawn from
undera larger bed. Used for children orwhere accommodation is limited

truss, roof-truss Timber framework, eg oftie-beam and principal rafters in aroof
which is self-supporting and carries purlins and ridge. May include king-post,
braces and other beams

tufa,tuff Porous rock formed from volcanic eruptions. Hardens on exposuretoair

tundra Infertile Arctic regions with frozen ground. Sami (Lapp) origin

typology Research, identification and listing by type. This may be offunctions,
forms, plans, details or other features in common

ümgebinderhaus House with the ground floor externally braced by timber
arcades which also support shallow jetties. Eastern Europe

undercroft Acryptoroccupiable space beneath a building undifferentiated
structure. Sald of.a construction in which the walls and roof are not.
differentiated, ieadome, or where the structural difference of parts is not
evident

unfired brick Adobe or hand-moulded bricks which have not been baked

ustad Master craftsman in Muslim building

uxorilocal Livingatthehome ofthe wife

valley In landscape, the lowland between hills. By extension, in buildingthe
meeting between two inclinations of adjacent roofsto form a valley gutter

vault Arched roof or ceiling, usually of brick or stone. A groin vault is created by
the meeting oftwo barrel (or tunnel) vaults. Diagonal ribs may strengthen and
define vaults

velum Amembrane or thin layer. Applied to tent-cloths

vihar Asmalltemple or place of Hindu worship

virilocal Living atthehome ofthe male or son ofthefamily

voussoir Wedge-shaped stones usedto form an arch. In brick arches thewedge
shape is made withthemortar

wall-plate Beam placed alongthetop ofawalltotaketheends of principaland
common rafters

wattle-and-daub Interwoven lengths of pliable wood, eg hazel or willow usedto
fill a wall panel (wattle), and packed with moist earth or clay (daub)

webbing Strong fabric woven in strips or bands. Used to support tent cloths orto

hold down yurtfelts

wedge Tapered peg ofwood or metal usedto tighten a joint, orto split atimber

wet process Use of mortar, damp clay or other means of construction employing.
water, as distinct from a dry process, eg dry stone walling with no mortar

whaikaro Carving traditions ofthe Maori. New Zealand

whalebone frame Bones of whales which have been usedtto provide a basic
domical or literally rib framework, which can be covered or clad. Arctic regions

wharepuni Pitched roof house ofthe Maori

wigwam Domical mat or bark-covered dwelling. North-Eastern Algonquians,
USA. Incorrectly but frequently used to describe the tipi of the plains peoples

wind-brace Brace usingthe principle oftriangulationto stabilize a structure
which may be subjected to strong and destructive winds

wind-scoop Roof construction inthe form ofatower, which, by means ofan
opening facing prevailing breezes, circulates air via its shaft to cool theinterior
ofabuilding

wing Supplementary buildingwhich may be addedto one or both sides of a
larger house or other building, but having Its own roof

withies Rods of willow or osier used for making wattle

wohnstube Main living or all-purpose room, Germany, Switzerland

yard Asaspace or place, an area for domestic use, often behind the dwelling. As a
dimension, approximates to a single pace, orthree feet (nearlya metre)

yield Thetotal production ofacrop, or ofa defined area of land, overone
growing season

yurt Nomadic structure consisting of lattice walls and ribbed dome roof, capable
oferection and dismantling and carried by pack animals. Term of Russian origin

zabaleen Underprivileged people living by refuse collecting. Cairo, Egypt

zadruga Clan or group of related Slav families, working as a unit underthe
direction of a patriarch. Serbia and other Slav countries

zemindar Leader or chief of.a Pashtun village. Pakistan

ziggurat Astepped pyramid, or design in this form or silhouette

zodiac In popular use, thetwelve major constellations of stars, assigned
symbols, names and monthly periods of the year. Believed to influencethe
fortunes of people on Earth

zukuri Style or buildingtype, used as a suffix, eg gassho-zukurl. Japan

## S. 147

288 Credits

‚Author’s Acknowledgements

Tolistthe names ofall those people in so many
countries who advised, who acted as hosts and who
supplied information that relatedto this book would
double its size. Their help is not forgotten, and noris
that ofthe many people with local knowledge and
personal experience of building and livingin the
vernacular architectures of cultures all overthe
world. To be practical | am listing herethenames of
those who gave detailed information and advice,
who often helped facilitate the research, and in
many cases who were guides who accompanied me,
and translated for me on field trips. Some were
architects, others were academics, many were
colleagues or present and past students, and quitea
few were anthropologists or specialists in other
disciplines. Though the field work in European and
Mediterranean regions, inthe Commonwealth and in
North America was usually unaccompanied, their
support and guidance in the countries noted was
invaluable.

I am indebted to Kaj Andersen (Kenya), June
‚Anderson (USA), Yasemin Aysan (Turkey), Ali Bakhtiar
(Iran), Simon Bronner (USA), Jasmin Cheema
(Pakistan), Jorge Gonzalez Claveran (Mexico), Jeff
Cook (USA), Miles Danby (Ghana), RMW Dixon (Fiji),
Rosario Encarnacion-Tan (Philippines), Aydin Germen
(Turkey), Jorge Gonzalez-Claveran (Mexico), Richard
Hayward (Peru), Theo Hunter (New Zealand), Guyula
Istvänfi (Hungary), Michael Lloyd (Ghana, Costa
Rica), Francesco Javier Lopez (Mexico), David Lung
(China, Hong Kong), Toru Mitsui (Japan), Hubert
Murray (Kenya), Chotu Padamsee (India), Johnny
Parth (Austria), Milan Prodanovic (Serbia), Mauro
Rahardjo (Indonesia), Jai Sen (India), Josef Vareka
(Czech Republic), Wan Saleh Wan Ibrahim (Malaysia),
Wang Degang (China), Wang Wen-Oing (China),
among many others

While the majority of the photographs in
this book were taken by myself or by Val, whileon
field trips, | greatly appreciate the help of many
friends and specialists whose photographs have
been important in illustrating the dwellings of a
number of cultures. They are listed separately,
together with the drawings made by Stuart Parker in
addition to my own. Of particular importancetome
has been the support of colleagues inthe Centrefor
Vernacular Architecture Studies at Oxford Brookes
University, including Lindsay Asquith, Rosie Latter
and Marcel Vellinga. Their participation, together
with that of lan Davis, Maurice Mitchell, Antonia
Noussia and Richard Twinch on projects and field
studies conducted with students on the Centre'sMA
Course in International Studies in Vernacular
Architecture, in Spain, Italy, Greece, Slovenia and
elsewhere has been invaluable, whilethe
participation of students from all continents has
been a constant inspiration.

In conclusion | wish to express my gratitude
to lona Baird, architectural editor at Phaidon Press,
for her patient editingof mytext, to Sophia Gibb and
Sarah Hopper for picture research and to Roger
Fawcett-Tang for his thoughtful design ofthis book.

Paul Oliver
Oxford, 2002
